89. A 57-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 3-month history of a nonproductive cough and intermittent flushing of her face and neck. The flushing occurs three times daily and lasts approximately 30 minutes; it is not associated with eating or drinking. Ten years ago, she underwent a cholecystectomy for cholelithiasis. She takes no medications. Her last menstrual period was 5 years ago. She does not smoke or drink alcohol. Her temperature is 36.9 °C (98.4 °F), pulse is 92/min, respirations are 12/min, and blood pressure is 100/60 mm Hg. Examination shows no abnormalities. An x-ray of the chest shows a 3-cm mass in the left upper lobe. An increased serum concentration of which of the following substances is most likely to contribute to this patient’s symptoms?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. ADH (vasopressin)</br>b. Cortisol</br>c. Follicle-stimulating hormone</br>d. Histamine</br>e. Norepinephrine</br>f. Parathyroid hormone</br>g. Thyroid-stimulating hormone</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Histamine  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – 57-year-old woman with a 3-month history of non-productive cough → points to a primary pulmonary lesion.<br /></br>  – Recurrent episodes of facial and neck flushing lasting ≈30 min, NOT related to meals → classic for carcinoid syndrome originating in the lung (flushing from intestinal carcinoid usually appears only after hepatic metastasis).<br /></br>  – 3-cm left-upper-lobe mass on chest x-ray → typical size/location for a bronchial carcinoid tumor.<br /></br>  – No other systemic findings (no diarrhea, no wheezing yet) but flushing alone is enough to suspect release of vasoactive amines.  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives: flushing, lung mass.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no alcohol/food trigger, no menopausal changes (post-menopausal 5 y ago), no smoking (argues against squamous cell carcinoma-related PTHrP).  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Identify paraneoplastic syndromes associated with lung tumors.<br /></br>2. Flushing suggests vasoactive mediators such as serotonin, bradykinin, or histamine (all released by carcinoid tumors).<br /></br>3. Serotonin is not an answer choice; histamine is.<br /></br>4. Other answer choices (ADH, ACTH/cortisol, PTH, TSH, FSH, norepinephrine) cause distinct syndromes that do not primarily manifest as flushing.<br /></br>5. Therefore, histamine excess best explains the patient’s presentation.  </p></br><p>Take-home: Bronchial carcinoid tumors can secrete serotonin, kallikrein (→ bradykinin), and histamine, producing episodic flushing, diarrhea, and wheezing.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Carcinoid tumors = well-differentiated neuroendocrine tumors (chromogranin-A+, synaptophysin+).<br /></br>• Ectopic production of:<br /></br>  – Serotonin → vasodilation, flushing, diarrhea, wheezing, right-sided heart lesions.<br /></br>  – Histamine &amp; kallikrein → potent vasodilators that accentuate flushing.<br /></br>• When tumor is in the lung, secreted amines enter systemic circulation directly (no first-pass metabolism by the liver), so even a solitary pulmonary lesion can cause full carcinoid syndrome.  </p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>1. Confirm diagnosis:<br /></br>   – 24-hour urinary 5-HIAA (5-hydroxy-indole-acetic acid) ↑<br /></br>   – Serum chromogranin A ↑<br /></br>   – CT chest / Octreotide scan to localize disease.<br /></br>2. Management:<br /></br>   – Surgical resection if localized.<br /></br>   – Somatostatin analogs (octreotide, lanreotide) to control hormone release.<br /></br>   – Telotristat for refractory diarrhea (serotonin synthesis inhibitor).  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Bronchial Carcinoid FHiSH”<br /></br>F – Flushing<br /></br>H – Histamine release<br /></br>S – Serotonin release → Stool (diarrhea)<br /></br>H – Heart (right-sided valvular lesions)  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. ADH (vasopressin) – Produced by small-cell carcinoma → causes SIADH → hyponatremia, seizures, confusion, NOT flushing.<br /></br>b. Cortisol (via ectopic ACTH) – Also from small-cell carcinoma → Cushing syndrome (central obesity, moon facies, muscle weakness).<br /></br>c. Follicle-stimulating hormone – Ectopic production is rare; would cause ovarian/testicular enlargement or menstrual changes, not flushing.<br /></br>e. Norepinephrine – Pheochromocytoma (adrenal or extra-adrenal) → episodic hypertension, headache, diaphoresis; mass would be adrenal, not pulmonary.<br /></br>f. Parathyroid hormone – Squamous cell carcinoma → PTH-rP → hypercalcemia (stones, bones, groans). Flushing is not typical.<br /></br>g. Thyroid-stimulating hormone – Seen in some pituitary adenomas; produces hyperthyroidism symptoms (heat intolerance, weight loss), but not isolated flushing in the setting of a lung mass.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Episodic facial flushing in a patient with a solitary lung mass is classic for a bronchial carcinoid tumor; excess histamine (along with serotonin/bradykinin) mediates the flushing episodes.</p>
90. A 72-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 4-year history of nonproductive chronic cough. The cough primarily occurs during eating or drinking; he has frequently coughed up undigested food and pills. He has not had nausea or vomiting or the sensation of food stuck in his throat. He has required admission to the hospital twice during the past 2 years for treatment of aspiration pneumonia. He has arthritis and hypercholesterolemia. His only medication is lovastatin. He does not smoke cigarettes or drink alcohol. Vital signs are within normal limits. During the examination, he is coughing continuously. Examination shows no abnormalities except for a grade 2/6 systolic murmur. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Carcinoma of the esophagus</br>b. Gastroesophageal reflux disease</br>c. Lower esophageal (Schatzki) ring</br>d. Pharyngoesophageal (Zenker) diverticulum</br>e. Transfer dysphagia</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Pharyngoesophageal (Zenker) diverticulum  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key phrases &amp; clues<br /></br>• 72-year-old man (elderly = classic age group)<br /></br>• 4-year history of non-productive chronic cough that is “primarily during eating or drinking”<br /></br>• Regurgitation of “undigested food and pills” hours after ingestion<br /></br>• Recurrent aspiration pneumonia admissions<br /></br>• No odynophagia, no true obstructive sensation while swallowing (often absent with Zenker)<br /></br>• Continual coughing on exam (suggests pooling &amp; spillage of oropharyngeal contents)  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives: elderly, regurgitation of UNDIGESTED food, cough/aspiration with meals.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no progressive solid-food dysphagia, no weight loss, no heartburn, no chest pain—features that would point to carcinoma or GERD are missing.  </p></br><p>Pattern recognition: Elderly + post-prandial cough/regurgitation + aspiration pneumonia ⇒ think Zenker diverticulum.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)  </p></br><ol></br><li>Symptom timing: Cough only when eating → problem is at the pharyngeal transfer stage, not in mid- or distal esophagus.  </li></br><li>Nature of regurgitate: “Undigested food/pills” hours later comes from a pouch that retains material; stomach contents would be digested/acidic.  </li></br><li>Complications: Recurrent aspiration pneumonia is a classic Zenker complication.  </li></br><li>Risk profile: Zenker occurs from cricopharyngeal muscle dysfunction → herniation at Killian triangle, typically in elderly men.  </li></br></ol></br><p>Take-home concept: Zenker diverticulum causes halitosis, nocturnal or post-prandial regurgitation of undigested food, chronic cough, and aspiration—usually without true esophageal obstructive dysphagia.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>• Etiology: Cricopharyngeal muscle dysfunction → increased intraluminal pressure → mucosal herniation through Killian triangle (between thyropharyngeus &amp; cricopharyngeus muscles).<br /></br>• False diverticulum (mucosa &amp; submucosa only) located just above upper esophageal sphincter.<br /></br>• Retention of food leads to fermentation → halitosis, regurgitation, chronic cough, and aspiration.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnosis &amp; Treatment  </p></br><p>Diagnostics<br /></br>1. Best initial/confirmatory test: Barium swallow (contrast esophagram) – shows posterior outpouching.<br /></br>2. Endoscopy is generally avoided initially due to perforation risk.  </p></br><p>Management<br /></br>• Symptomatic or complicated (aspiration, weight loss) → surgical cricopharyngeal myotomy ± diverticulectomy or endoscopic stapling (flexible endoscopic septotomy).<br /></br>• Small, asymptomatic pouches may be observed.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“Zenker = Zen cougher”<br /></br>Z – Zero digestion (undigested food)<br /></br>E – Elderly male<br /></br>N – No true dysphagia (or minimal)<br /></br>K – Killian triangle defect<br /></br>E – Esophagram best test<br /></br>R – Regurgitation/halitosis  </p></br><p>Picture a senior named “Mr. Zen” storing crackers in a pouch on his neck and coughing them up at dinner—visual memory palace for exam day.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Carcinoma of the esophagus<br /></br>• Presents with progressive solid-then-liquid dysphagia, weight loss, odynophagia, ± bleeding.<br /></br>• Regurgitation of undigested food and coughing immediately with meals is uncommon; aspiration more likely late.  </p></br><p>b. Gastroesophageal reflux disease (GERD)<br /></br>• Produces heartburn, sour taste, nocturnal cough. Reflux material is acidic gastric contents, not undigested solids.<br /></br>• Does not cause pouch-like retention or recurrent aspiration of solids.  </p></br><p>c. Lower esophageal (Schatzki) ring<br /></br>• Intermittent solid-food dysphagia that “sticks” in lower chest; usually precipitated by eating meat (“steakhouse syndrome”).<br /></br>• No regurgitation of undigested food, no proximal aspiration.  </p></br><p>e. Transfer dysphagia (oropharyngeal neuromuscular swallowing disorder)<br /></br>• Seen with stroke, myasthenia gravis, Parkinson disease. Characterized by immediate choking/coughing upon swallowing water or thin liquids.<br /></br>• Typically no late regurgitation of undigested food; neurological deficits are often evident.  </p></br><p>Key differentiator: Only Zenker provides the triad—elderly + regurgitation of undigested food + aspiration pneumonia.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Zenker diverticulum is a pharyngoesophageal false diverticulum in older adults that traps undigested food, causing regurgitation, chronic cough, halitosis, and aspiration pneumonia. Diagnose with barium swallow and treat surgically if symptomatic.</p>
91. A 32-year-old woman comes to the physician for a follow-up examination to discuss test results regarding HIV infection. Laboratory studies 1 week ago showed:</br>HIV antibody test                  positive</br>HIV Western blot assay          positive</br>CD4⁺ T-lymphocyte count       250/mm³ (Normal ≥ 500)</br>Plasma HIV RNA viral load      40,000 copies/mL</br>The patient feels well and is asymptomatic. She is sexually active with multiple male partners; they use condoms inconsistently. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows scattered enlarged lymph nodes in the neck and groin area. The most appropriate next step is prophylaxis against which of the following organisms?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Cytomegalovirus</br>b. Mycobacterium avium complex</br>c. Pneumocystis jiroveci</br>d. Streptococcus pneumoniae</br>e. Toxoplasma gondii</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>d. Streptococcus pneumoniae  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Newly diagnosed, asymptomatic HIV-positive woman.<br /></br>• CD4⁺ T-cell count = 250/mm³ (below normal but &gt;200).<br /></br>• No prior opportunistic infections are mentioned.<br /></br>• Question stem asks for “the most appropriate next step … prophylaxis against which organism?”</p></br><p>Key clue: CD4 count (250) is above the numerical thresholds that trigger antibiotic prophylaxis for the classic opportunistic infections (PCP &lt; 200, Toxo &lt; 100, MAC &lt; 50). Therefore, the only immediately indicated preventive measure is vaccination, most importantly the pneumococcal series.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Start ART in every newly diagnosed patient (implied but not among answer choices).<br /></br>2. Review OI (opportunistic infection) prophylaxis guidelines:<br /></br>   • CD4 &lt; 200 → TMP-SMX for Pneumocystis jirovecii.<br /></br>   • CD4 &lt; 100 &amp; Toxo-IgG⁺ → TMP-SMX for Toxoplasma gondii.<br /></br>   • CD4 &lt; 50 → Azithromycin or clarithromycin for MAC.<br /></br>3. Patient’s CD4 (250) is above all antibiotic-prophylaxis cut-offs.<br /></br>4. Universal immunizations for all HIV patients, regardless of CD4, include:<br /></br>   • PCV13 → then PPSV23 ≥8 weeks later (Streptococcus pneumoniae)<br /></br>   • Annual inactivated influenza, HAV, HBV, HPV, meningococcal, etc.<br /></br>5. Therefore pneumococcal vaccination is the next prophylactic step.</p></br><p>Take-home message: In HIV, vaccine-based prophylaxis (especially against S. pneumoniae) is recommended for ALL patients independent of CD4 count, whereas antibiotic prophylaxis is triggered by specific CD4 thresholds.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• HIV causes gradual CD4 decline → loss of cell-mediated immunity.<br /></br>• Risk of encapsulated bacteria (S. pneumoniae, H. influenzae) is elevated at any CD4 because antibody production is impaired and splenic function may be suboptimal. Conjugate (PCV13) and polysaccharide (PPSV23) vaccines broaden serotype coverage and boost T-cell–dependent B-cell responses.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>Pneumococcal prophylaxis schedule in HIV:<br /></br>1. PCV13 (Prevnar 13) IM once.<br /></br>2. PPSV23 (Pneumovax 23) IM ≥8 weeks later, then one booster at age ≥65 yr or ≥5 yr after the first PPSV23, whichever is later.<br /></br>3. Give regardless of CD4, viral load, or ART status.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Mne-MOON-ia”:<br /></br>• MOON = Minimum Of One Number (CD4 cutoff) Needed for antibiotics.<br /></br>  200: PCP<br /></br>  100: Toxoplasma<br /></br>   50: MAC<br /></br>• Anything ABOVE the moon (higher CD4) → think VACCINES, especially Pneumococcus.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Cytomegalovirus – Routine CMV prophylaxis is NOT recommended in HIV because ganciclovir/valganciclovir toxicity outweighs benefit; instead, regular eye exams are used to detect early CMV retinitis (risk rises when CD4 &lt; 50).<br /></br>b. Mycobacterium avium complex – Begin azithromycin (1 ×/wk) or clarithromycin when CD4 &lt; 50. Patient’s CD4 is 250.<br /></br>c. Pneumocystis jirovecii – Start TMP-SMX when CD4 &lt; 200 or if oral thrush/PCP history. CD4 is above the threshold.<br /></br>e. Toxoplasma gondii – Start TMP-SMX when CD4 &lt; 100 AND Toxo-IgG positive. Neither criterion met here.</p></br><p>Common pitfall: jumping to antibiotic prophylaxis purely because the CD4 count is &lt;500; remember the specific cut-offs. Always think vaccines first when counts are still above 200.</p>
92. A 72-year-old woman with hypertension comes to the office because of a 1-day history of episodes of shaking of her arms and legs. She has no other history of serious illness. Her only medication is hydrochlorothiazide. Her pulse is 74/min, and blood pressure is 128/82 mm Hg. Physical examination shows rhythmic, rapid, coarse shaking of all extremities, which the patient is able to suppress when asked to do so by the physician. There is no cogwheel rigidity, bradykinesia, or masked facies. Mental status examination shows a worried mood and constricted affect. She recalls two of three objects after 5 minutes. Which of the following is the most likely clinical course for this patient’s illness?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Development of a cerebral infarction</br>b. Development of a seizure disorder</br>c. Progression to dementia, Alzheimer type</br>d. Progression to Parkinson disease</br>e. Resolution of symptoms within the next few weeks</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>E. Resolution of symptoms within the next few weeks  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues:<br /></br>• “Rhythmic, rapid, coarse shaking of all extremities” – looks like a tremor.<br /></br>• “Patient is able to suppress when asked” – TRUE neurologic tremors (Parkinsonian rest tremor, essential tremor, cerebellar intention tremor) cannot be willfully stopped on command. Voluntary suppression is classic for a psychogenic (functional) tremor or an anxiety-related exaggerated physiologic tremor.<br /></br>• No other neurologic signs – no cogwheel rigidity, bradykinesia, masked facies (rules out Parkinson disease).<br /></br>• Sudden onset, short duration (1 day).<br /></br>• Mental status: worried mood, constricted affect – suggests anxiety/stress.<br /></br>Together these point to an acute stress-related physiologic / psychogenic tremor that is transient.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)  </p></br><ol></br><li>Decide “tremor” vs seizure/myoclonus: rhythmic and suppressible → tremor.  </li></br><li>Decide type of tremor:<br /></br>   • Parkinson rest tremor – unilateral distal, “pill-rolling,” NOT suppressible.<br /></br>   • Essential tremor – action/postural, worse with movement, improves with alcohol, NOT suppressible.<br /></br>   • Cerebellar intention tremor – worse approaching target, associated with ataxia, NOT suppressible.<br /></br>   • Physiologic / psychogenic tremor – generalized, abrupt, emotionally triggered, can be suppressed/distracted.  </li></br><li>Psychogenic/physiologic tremors typically resolve when the underlying anxiety or stress abates → self-limited over days-weeks.  </li></br></ol></br><p>Core takeaway: A suppressible, whole-body tremor in an anxious patient is almost always functional and short-lived.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>Exaggerated physiologic tremor<br /></br>• Normal physiologic 8–12 Hz tremor is present in everyone; heightened by sympathetic activation (stress, anxiety, caffeine, β-agonists).<br /></br>• Mechanism: enhanced β-adrenergic activity → increased muscle spindle discharge.<br /></br>• Because the motor circuit is intact, the tremor can be consciously inhibited for a short time.<br /></br>• No progressive neurodegeneration.  </p></br><p>Psychogenic (functional) tremor<br /></br>• A subtype of functional neurologic symptom disorder (conversion disorder).<br /></br>• Features: sudden onset, variability with distraction, entrainment, ability to stop, large-amplitude coarse movements.<br /></br>• Often linked to recent emotional stress; prognosis is good with reassurance and stress reduction.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Work-up  </p></br><p>• Mainstay: reassurance, address anxiety (stress management, possible short-term, low-dose benzodiazepine or β-blocker if symptomatic).<br /></br>• No imaging or extensive labs are required if exam is classic and no red flags are present.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“STOP” for psychogenic tremor<br /></br>S – Sudden onset<br /></br>T – Tremor of entire body<br /></br>O – Obeys command (can stop on request)<br /></br>P – Psychic stress present<br /></br>If a tremor can STOP, it will likely STOP on its own.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Incorrect  </p></br><p>A. Development of a cerebral infarction<br /></br>• Stroke risk factors: focal deficits, vascular history. Tremor alone is not a herald sign. Suppressible “shaking” is not TIAs.  </p></br><p>B. Development of a seizure disorder<br /></br>• Seizure movements are involuntary, cannot be halted on command, usually followed by post-ictal state. No loss of consciousness, tongue bite, incontinence, or post-ictal confusion here.  </p></br><p>C. Progression to dementia, Alzheimer type<br /></br>• Anxiety-related tremor has no link to neurodegenerative cognitive decline. Mild recall difficulty (2/3 objects) is nonspecific and common in normal aging or anxiety.  </p></br><p>D. Progression to Parkinson disease<br /></br>• Parkinson tremor is typically asymmetric, rest-dominant, and accompanied over time by bradykinesia, rigidity, masked facies—all absent here. Ability to suppress argues strongly against Parkinson.  </p></br><p>E. Resolution of symptoms within the next few weeks (Correct)<br /></br>• Matches natural history of functional/anxiety-related tremor—good prognosis, self-limited.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line: A tremor that the patient can voluntarily stop, appearing in an anxious context and lacking other neurologic signs, is functional/exaggerated physiologic and will resolve spontaneously within weeks.</p>
93. A 68-year-old woman, gravida 2, para 2, comes to the clinic because of a 3-week history of progressive loss of urine control. She says that when she has the urge to void, she sometimes has urine loss before she reaches the bathroom. She now wears absorbent pads daily. She has hypertension well controlled with hydrochlorothiazide and enalapril and osteoarthritis treated with naproxen. Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination, including pelvic examination, shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: F</br></br>a. Addition-of-vaginal-estradiol-to-the-medieation-regimen</br>b. Initiation of oxybutynin</br>c. Placement of a pessary</br>d. Renal ultrasonography</br>e. Switching from hydrochlorothiazide to amlodipine</br>f. Urinalysis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>f. Urinalysis  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key findings: 68-year-old post-menopausal woman, NEW (3-week) onset, “progressive” urinary urgency with leakage before reaching the bathroom (urge incontinence pattern).<br /></br>• No pelvic organ prolapse, no neurologic deficits, normal vitals.<br /></br>• Medications: hydrochlorothiazide (a diuretic that can aggravate frequency/urgency).<br /></br>• Time course is short (weeks), so a transient/reversible cause must be excluded before labeling “idiopathic overactive bladder.”  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Anytime a patient presents with NEW urinary incontinence, screen for reversible causes.<br /></br>Step 2: The simplest, fastest screen is a urinalysis (UA): identifies urinary tract infection, hematuria, glucosuria, specific gravity (over-diuresis), etc.<br /></br>Step 3: Only after UA is negative do you proceed to targeted therapy (antimuscarinics, pelvic floor therapy, stop culprit drugs, etc.).  </p></br><p>Take-home message: UA is the mandatory first test for all new-onset incontinence to rule out infection or other transient causes (DIAPPERS mnemonic).  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Urge incontinence = detrusor overactivity ⇒ uninhibited contractions → urgency, frequency, nocturia ± leakage.<br /></br>Transient causes (DIAPPERS):<br /></br>• D – Delirium/Dementia<br /></br>• I – Infection (UTI) ← detected by UA<br /></br>• A – Atrophic urethritis/vaginitis<br /></br>• P – Pharmaceuticals (eg, diuretics, anticholinesterases)<br /></br>• P – Psychological (depression)<br /></br>• E – Excess urine output (hyperglycemia, DI)<br /></br>• R – Restricted mobility<br /></br>• S – Stool impaction  </p></br><p>Treatments/Diagnostics<br /></br>• First diagnostic: UA ± culture.<br /></br>• If UA positive → treat UTI → reassess.<br /></br>• If UA negative and symptoms persist → lifestyle (bladder training, ↓ caffeine), then pharmacologic (oxybutynin, tolterodine; β3-agonist mirabegron if anticholinergic intolerant).<br /></br>• Imaging/cystoscopy reserved for hematuria, suspicion of obstruction, or neurogenic bladder.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Before you prescribe, DIP the strip.” (Dipstick UA precedes therapy)<br /></br>Use DIAPPERS to remember reversible causes.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Vaginal estradiol: Helpful for stress incontinence due to urogenital atrophy, but UA still needed first; patient’s presentation suggests urgency, not stress (no leakage with cough/sneeze).<br /></br>b. Oxybutynin: Correct long-term therapy for idiopathic urge incontinence, but only AFTER reversible causes have been excluded with UA. Jumping to meds can miss a treatable UTI.<br /></br>c. Pessary: Used for stress incontinence from pelvic organ prolapse; exam showed no prolapse.<br /></br>d. Renal ultrasonography: Evaluates obstruction, stones, hydronephrosis; not first-line in uncomplicated new urgency.<br /></br>e. Stopping HCTZ: Diuretics can worsen frequency, but again reversible causes (UTI, glycosuria) must be sought first; abrupt discontinuation could worsen her well-controlled hypertension.  </p></br><p>Bottom line: Urinalysis is the obligatory first step in any patient with new-onset urinary incontinence.</p>
94. A 49-year-old woman comes to the physician because she has not had a menstrual period for 9 months. She has not had vaginal dryness or hot flashes. She has no history of serious illness or abnormal Pap smears. She has had a levonorgestrel IUD in place for 4 years. Her last visit to a physician was 3 years ago. She is 152 cm (5 ft) tall and weighs 54 kg, or 120lb; BMI is 23 kg/m2. Her blood pressure is 145/95 mm Hg. Physical examination shows no other abnormalities. On pelvic examination, the IUD string cannot be seen at the cervical os. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Measurement of serum follicle-stimulating hormone concentration</br>b. Pelvic CT scan to localize the IUD</br>c. Reassurance only</br>d. Switching to an oral contraceptive</br>e. Urine pregnancy test</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Urine pregnancy test  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues:<br /></br>• 49-year-old (still within reproductive age—average menopause 51 yr)<br /></br>• Secondary amenorrhea (9 mo without menses)<br /></br>• Levonorgestrel intra-uterine device (IUD) in place 4 yr, BUT no strings visible → possible intra-uterine displacement or expulsion → unreliable contraception<br /></br>• No menopausal symptoms (no hot flashes, no vaginal dryness)<br /></br>• Must always exclude pregnancy first in any patient with ≥ 6 mo amenorrhea who could conceive  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives: reproductive age, absent strings (contraceptive failure risk)<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no vasomotor sx to suggest menopause, no systemic illness  </p></br><p>Pattern: “Amenorrhea + any possibility of conception → pregnancy test is step-1.”  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)  </p></br><ol></br><li>Define the problem: secondary amenorrhea (&gt;3 mo in previously regular menses).  </li></br><li>Universal first test: rule out pregnancy (β-hCG) regardless of contraception method.  </li></br><li>Missing IUD strings raise suspicion of expulsion → increases pregnancy risk.<br /></br>Therefore the most appropriate NEXT step is a urine pregnancy test.  </li></br></ol></br><p>Take-home message: Pregnancy test is ALWAYS the first diagnostic step in secondary amenorrhea in women of reproductive potential, even when they report reliable contraception.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>• Levonorgestrel IUD mainly works via local progestin effect (endometrial atrophy, cervical mucus thickening).<br /></br>• String absence can mean: retracted into cervical canal, broken, intra-uterine malposition, or complete expulsion (≈ 5% within 5 y).<br /></br>• If expelled, fertility promptly returns → unintended pregnancy.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Pearls  </p></br><p>Algorithm for secondary amenorrhea:<br /></br>1. β-hCG (urine or serum).<br /></br>2. If negative → TSH &amp; prolactin.<br /></br>3. If normal → FSH ± estradiol to evaluate ovarian failure/menopause.<br /></br>4. Subsequent imaging/hormonal challenge as indicated.  </p></br><p>No imaging (ultrasound/CT) is indicated until pregnancy is ruled out.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“B-HCG FIRST”:<br /></br>B – Bleeding stopped<br /></br>H – Home pregnancy test<br /></br>F – First step<br /></br>I – In every<br /></br>R – Reproductive-age<br /></br>S – Scenario  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Serum FSH measurement<br /></br>• Appropriate only after pregnancy is excluded and TSH/prolactin checked. Premature to jump to menopause evaluation.<br /></br>• Lack of vasomotor symptoms also makes menopausal transition less likely.  </p></br><p>b. Pelvic CT to localize IUD<br /></br>• Imaging is unnecessary until pregnancy status is known. Ultrasound (not CT) would be initial imaging if pregnancy negative and IUD location needed.  </p></br><p>c. Reassurance only<br /></br>• Ignores potentially serious etiologies (pregnancy, especially ectopic).  </p></br><p>d. Switching to an oral contraceptive<br /></br>• Management, not diagnostic. Must first confirm she is not pregnant before prescribing estrogen-containing agents.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line: In every woman with new-onset amenorrhea who could conceivably be pregnant—regardless of age, symptoms, or contraceptive history—the first and most appropriate next step is a pregnancy test.</p>
95. A 19-year-old man, who is a college freshman, comes to the student health center because of a 2-year history of intermittent headaches that have been occurring more frequently during the past 6 months and recently have caused him to miss several days of class. He says the headaches affect either the left or right side of his head, and begin with “sparkling lights” in his peripheral vision and sensitivity to light and sound. The headaches often are brought on by strong odors, hunger, and alcohol consumption. The headaches also are associated with nausea. The patient says he often tries to “sleep them off.” The headaches previously occurred once every few months, but now he has as many as four headaches monthly. Acetaminophen has provided no relief. He has no history of serious illness and takes no other medications. Vital signs are within normal limits. Neurologic examination discloses no abnormalities. Daily use of which of the following medications is most appropriate to recommend to prevent or decrease the frequency of this patient’s headaches?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Dihydroergotamine</br>b. Metoclopramide</br>c. Oxycodone</br>d. Propranolol</br>e. Sumatriptan</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Propranolol  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues:<br /></br>• 19-year-old man with 2-year history of episodic unilateral headaches.<br /></br>• Classic migraine prodrome: “sparkling lights” (scintillating scotoma/aura), photophobia, phonophobia.<br /></br>• Triggers: strong odors, hunger, alcohol → typical migraine precipitants.<br /></br>• Associated nausea; patient prefers to sleep in dark room.<br /></br>• Frequency now ≥4 episodes/month and disabling (missed classes, acetaminophen ineffective).<br /></br>• Normal neurologic exam → no red-flag features requiring imaging.  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives: aura, unilateral throbbing pain, photophobia/phonophobia, nausea, clear triggers.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no focal deficits, no systemic signs, no sudden onset “worst headache,” no papilledema → favors primary migraine &gt; secondary cause.  </p></br><p>Pattern recognition: Migraine with aura that has increased to ≥4 attacks/month ⇒ time to start preventive (prophylactic) therapy.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning  </p></br><p>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Diagnose migraine with aura (ICHD-3 criteria).<br /></br>2. Determine need for prophylaxis:<br /></br>   • ≥4 attacks per month,<br /></br>   • or ≥3 attacks with significant disability,<br /></br>   • or abortive meds contraindicated/ineffective.<br /></br>   – Patient meets criteria.<br /></br>3. Choose first-line daily prophylactic agent: β-blocker (propranolol or timolol), antiepileptics (topiramate, valproate), or tricyclic (amitriptyline).<br /></br>4. Select among answer choices: Only propranolol fits.  </p></br><p>Take-home: For frequent or impairing migraines, daily β-blocker prophylaxis is guideline-backed first-line.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>Migraine = neurovascular disorder.<br /></br>• Cortical spreading depression → aura.<br /></br>• Trigeminovascular activation → release of CGRP, substance P → meningeal vasodilation &amp; sterile neurogenic inflammation → pain.<br /></br>Prophylactic β-blockers ↓ central adrenergic tone and dampen cortical excitability → fewer attacks.  </p></br><p>Risk factors for progression: stress, sleep deprivation, alcohol, missed meals—all present in college life.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests  </p></br><p>Acute (abortive): NSAIDs, acetaminophen, triptans, DHE, anti-emetics.<br /></br>Preventive (daily):<br /></br>• 1st line: Propranolol, timolol; topiramate; divalproex sodium; amitriptyline; CGRP mAbs.<br /></br>• 2nd line: Verapamil, gabapentin, botulinum toxin (chronic &gt;15 d/mo).  </p></br><p>No imaging needed when neuro exam is normal and headache pattern is typical.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“MIGRAINE ≥4” mnemonic for prophylaxis:<br /></br>M – More than 4 Monthly attacks<br /></br>I – Impairing quality of life<br /></br>G – Growing frequency<br /></br>R – Refractory to abortives<br /></br>AINE – Agents:<br /></br>  A = Amitriptyline<br /></br>  I = β-blocker (Inderal = propranolol)<br /></br>  N = Neuromodulators (Topiramate, Valproate)<br /></br>  E = mAbs (erenumab, etc.)  </p></br><p>Visual: Picture a knight “PROtecting” your brain with a round “β-shield” (beta-blocker).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Dihydroergotamine (DHE): Potent 5-HT1 agonist used as an abortive therapy for ongoing migraine, not taken daily for prevention; side effects (nausea, vasoconstriction).  </p></br><p>b. Metoclopramide: Dopamine antagonist anti-emetic used adjunctively during acute migraine to treat nausea and enhance GI absorption of analgesics. Provides no preventive benefit.  </p></br><p>c. Oxycodone: Opioid analgesic reserved for severe refractory pain; not recommended for migraines (risk of dependence, rebound headaches).  </p></br><p>e. Sumatriptan: 5-HT1B/1D agonist first-line ABORTIVE agent; taken at onset of aura/pain, not daily. Overuse causes medication-overuse headache.  </p></br><p>Key differentiator: Only propranolol is guideline-endorsed for DAILY prophylaxis.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Concept  </p></br><p>Migraine prophylaxis is indicated when attacks are frequent or disabling. First-line daily agents include β-blockers such as propranolol. Recognizing when to shift from abortive to preventive therapy—and choosing the correct class—is a classic NBME/Step-2 application.</p>
96. A previously healthy 9-year-old boy is brought to the emergency department because of acute pain and swelling around the right elbow since sliding into home plate during a baseball game 30 minutes ago. On arrival, his arm is splinted in newspaper and wrapped with duct tape. He is in acute distress. Examination of the right upper extremity shows edema and severe tenderness around the elbow. There is a distal right humeral deformity. Radial pulses are decreased on the right. X-rays of the right upper extremity show a transverse fracture of the right distal humeral metaphysis with posterior displacement of the distal fragment. This patient is at greatest risk for which of the following sequelae?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Acute osteomyelitis</br>b. Brachial artery injury</br>c. Fracture nonunion</br>d. Growth-plate injury</br>e. Ulnar nerve entrapment</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Brachial artery injury  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Mechanism/age: 5–10-yr-old child, FOOSH or fall while playing → think “supracondylar humerus fracture.”<br /></br>• X-ray wording: “transverse fracture of the distal humeral metaphysis with posterior displacement of the distal fragment” = classic extension-type supracondylar fracture.<br /></br>• Vascular clue: “Radial pulses are decreased on the right.” Radial pulse = distal flow from the brachial artery ⇒ suggests vascular compromise.<br /></br>These two clues together point directly to brachial artery injury as the most feared early complication.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify fracture type → supracondylar (distal humerus).<br /></br>2. Recall anatomy → brachial artery and median nerve lie in the antecubital fossa, just anterior to the distal humerus; easily tethered, kinked, or lacerated by a displaced fragment.<br /></br>3. Tie to physical finding → diminished radial pulse = warning sign of brachial artery injury or spasm.<br /></br>4. Prioritize complication by time sensitivity → vascular injury → limb ischemia → Volkmann ischemic contracture if missed.<br /></br>Take-home: In a child with a displaced supracondylar fracture, always assess pulses and motor/sensory function; urgent reduction ± vascular exploration is often needed.  </p></br><p>–––––––– Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Extension-type supracondylar fractures = 95% of cases. Distal fragment is pushed posteriorly; proximal fragment pierces anterior soft tissue.<br /></br>• The brachial artery can be:<br /></br>  – Intimal tear → thrombosis<br /></br>  – Complete transection<br /></br>  – Kinked by bone spike → spasm/occlusion<br /></br>• Untreated ischemia → compartment syndrome → Volkmann contracture (flexion contracture of wrist/fingers from forearm muscle necrosis).  </p></br><p>–––––––– Diagnostic &amp; Management Pearls<br /></br>1. Immediate neurovascular exam (radial pulse, cap refill, hand color/temp, median &amp; radial nerve motor-sensory).<br /></br>2. If pulses absent or Doppler weak → urgent closed reduction &amp; percutaneous pinning.<br /></br>   • Persistently absent pulse after reduction → vascular surgery exploration.<br /></br>3. Post-op: serial exams for compartment syndrome.<br /></br>4. Analgesia &amp; splinting with elbow at 90°, forearm neutral.  </p></br><p>–––––––– Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Supracondylar = SUPRA ‘Brach’ danger”:<br /></br>• SUPRA = SUPracondylar<br /></br>• BRACH = Brachial artery/Broken Bone Risks<br /></br>Visualize the distal humerus like an ice pick pointing at the brachial artery in the antecubital fossa.  </p></br><p>–––––––– Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Acute osteomyelitis – Develops weeks later, usually from open fractures or hematogenous seeding; no mention of puncture wound or infection risk here. Immediate vascular threat is more pressing.<br /></br>c. Fracture nonunion – Rare in healthy children because of excellent periosteal blood supply; union occurs rapidly with proper fixation.<br /></br>d. Growth-plate injury – Would require physeal (Salter-Harris) involvement; this fracture is metaphyseal, not epiphyseal/physis.<br /></br>e. Ulnar nerve entrapment – More common in flexion-type supracondylar fractures (distal fragment anterior); here fragment is posterior (extension-type). Median/interosseous nerves are actually at higher risk, but vascular injury still outranks them in urgency.  </p></br><p>Key takeaway: In any displaced pediatric supracondylar humerus fracture, immediately think “check the brachial artery.”</p>
97. A 17-year-old girl is brought to the physician by her mother because she is concerned that her daughter has never had a menstrual period. The mother reports that her own menarche occurred at the age of 13 years. The patient has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. She is in the 11th grade, receives grades of A’s and B’s, and sings in the school choir. She has never been sexually active. She is 173 cm (5 ft 8 in) tall and weighs 66 kg, or 145lb; BMI is 22 kg/m². Her blood pressure is 100/60 mm Hg. Breast development is sexual maturity rating (SMR) stage 5, and pubic hair development is SMR stage 2. Examination of the external genitalia shows no abnormalities. Insertion of a speculum into the vagina is difficult. The cervix cannot be visualized or palpated. The uterus is not palpable. Karyotype analysis shows a 46,XY chromosome. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. DNA hybridization of Y chromosome</br>b. Measurement of serum testosterone concentration</br>c. Hormone therapy</br>d. Gonadectomy</br>e. Neovaginostomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>• Gonadectomy (choice D)</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>1. Primary amenorrhea at age 17.<br /></br>2. Fully developed breasts (SMR 5) → indicates normal estrogen effect.<br /></br>3. Sparse/absent pubic &amp; axillary hair (SMR 2) → suggests absent/blocked androgen effect.<br /></br>4. Difficult speculum insertion, absent cervix/uterus → mullerian structures did not develop.<br /></br>5. Karyotype 46,XY.</p></br><p>Pattern: 46,XY patient with normal-to-high estrogen (aromatized from testicular testosterone), absent mullerian structures (testes made MIS/AMH), and end-organ resistance to androgens → Complete Androgen Insensitivity Syndrome (AIS, “testicular feminization”).</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning &amp; Core Take-away<br /></br>• In AIS the intra-abdominal or inguinal testes should be removed after completion of pubertal development because:<br /></br>  – They cannot descend through a blind-ending vagina.<br /></br>  – Risk of malignant transformation (seminoma, dysgerminoma, gonadoblastoma) rises sharply after puberty (~15–20 %).<br /></br>• The patient is 17, already completed puberty (Tanner 5 breasts) → time to do the prophylactic bilateral gonadectomy.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Gene defect in androgen receptor → tissues cannot respond to testosterone or DHT.<br /></br>• Testes produce:<br /></br>  – Anti-Müllerian hormone → regression of uterus, tubes, upper vagina.<br /></br>  – Testosterone → converted to estrogen peripherally → breast development.<br /></br>• Lack of androgen action → scant pubic/axillary hair, absent male internal &amp; external genitalia.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Pearls<br /></br>1. Diagnosis: clinical picture + 46,XY karyotype; testosterone levels are normal/high but not utilized.<br /></br>2. Management sequence:<br /></br>   a. Counsel &amp; psychological support.<br /></br>   b. Prophylactic gonadectomy after puberty.<br /></br>   c. Lifelong low-dose estrogen replacement (no progestin needed—no uterus).<br /></br>   d. Optional vaginal lengthening (dilators &gt; surgical neovagina) if dyspareunia or sexual activity desired.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“AIS = Absent Internal (female) Structures + Sparse hair”<br /></br>Use the phrase “GONE-ads once GROWTH is GONE” → remove the testes once growth/puberty is complete.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>A. DNA hybridization of Y chromosome<br /></br>• Karyotype (46,XY) is already known; adds no new information or change in management.</p></br><p>B. Measurement of serum testosterone concentration<br /></br>• Typically high-normal in AIS, but result would not alter the immediate need for gonadectomy.</p></br><p>C. Hormone therapy<br /></br>• Estrogen replacement is necessary only AFTER the testes are removed. Giving it now doesn’t address malignancy risk.</p></br><p>E. Neovaginostomy<br /></br>• Vaginal creation/dilation is elective for future sexual function. It is performed after or concurrent with gonadectomy, not the first imperative step.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Key Take-Home Message<br /></br>In a phenotypic female with primary amenorrhea, absent uterus, scant pubic hair, and 46,XY karyotype—think complete androgen insensitivity. After completion of secondary sexual characteristics, prophylactic gonadectomy is essential to eliminate the rising risk of germ-cell malignancy.</p>
98. A 90-year-old woman, who lives alone, is brought to the emergency department after her family found her lying on the floor of her home. She did not have loss of consciousness. She has hypertension and type 2 diabetes mellitus. On arrival, her pulse is 60/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 110/60 mm Hg. Examination shows an open fracture of the tibia. Antibiotic therapy is recommended with irrigation, debridement, and surgical stabilization of the tibia with an intramedullary nail. The patient insists that she will allow irrigation, debridement, and antibiotics but refuses the surgical stabilization and requests discharge to home as soon as possible. Her family provides documentation that the patient has appointed her son as her durable power of attorney. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Assess the patient’s decision-making capacity</br>b. Discuss the situation with the patient’s son</br>c. Petition the court to appoint a guardian</br>d. Proceed with the recommended treatment, including surgical stabilization</br>e. Stabilize the fracture with cast immobilization to abide by the patient’s wishes</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>a. Assess the patient’s decision-making capacity</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• 90-year-old patient with an open tibial fracture → standard of care = IV antibiotics + irrigation/debridement + surgical stabilization (e.g., intramedullary nail).<br /></br>• She explicitly accepts antibiotics/irrigation/debridement but refuses surgical fixation and wants discharge.<br /></br>• She has a durable power of attorney (DPOA) document naming her son.<br /></br>Key clue: The presence of a DPOA does NOT automatically transfer decision-making authority; it is activated only if the patient lacks decision-making capacity. Therefore, the first step is to determine whether she currently has that capacity.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>1. Autonomy is paramount when the patient has capacity.<br /></br>2. If capacity is uncertain, it must be formally assessed.<br /></br>3. A DPOA (or any surrogate) becomes the decision maker only after the patient is found incapable.<br /></br>4. Court involvement is the last resort, used only when no valid surrogate exists or when disputes cannot be resolved.</p></br><p>Take-home core concept: Always assess capacity before turning to a surrogate or overriding the patient’s expressed wishes.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (brief, for context)</br>Open (Gustilo-Anderson) fractures require:</br>• Immediate broad-spectrum IV antibiotics (e.g., cefazolin ± gentamicin)<br /></br>• Urgent irrigation and surgical debridement<br /></br>• Skeletal stabilization (ideally intramedullary nail or external fixator) to minimize infection, nonunion, and malalignment</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment / Diagnostic Tests</br>• Capacity assessment = bedside clinical evaluation (orientation, understanding, appreciation, reasoning, ability to communicate a choice).<br /></br>• If she has capacity → respect her decision, document counseling, offer follow-up care.<br /></br>• If she lacks capacity → the son (DPOA) makes the decision; typical care would proceed with recommended fixation unless he refuses.<br /></br>• No imaging or labs change the ethical algorithm; they are already indicated for fracture management, not for capacity determination.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“CAP before POA”:<br /></br>• Check CAPacity first; only then may the Power Of Attorney act.  </p></br><p>Four pillars of capacity (CURB):<br /></br>C – Communicates a consistent choice<br /></br>U – Understands information<br /></br>R – Reasons about options/consequences<br /></br>B – appreciates personal Benefit/burden</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>b. Discuss the situation with the patient’s son<br /></br>   • Premature. Son’s authority is contingent on the patient lacking capacity. Must assess capacity first.</p></br><p>c. Petition the court to appoint a guardian<br /></br>   • Used only if no valid surrogate exists OR there is an unresolvable conflict. The patient already has a DPOA, and capacity has not been evaluated.</p></br><p>d. Proceed with the recommended treatment, including surgical stabilization<br /></br>   • Violates patient autonomy if she has capacity. Even life-saving treatment cannot be forced on a capable patient.</p></br><p>e. Stabilize the fracture with cast immobilization to abide by the patient’s wishes<br /></br>   • Clinically suboptimal and still assumes she has capacity without verification. Capacity assessment is the prerequisite step.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>In any competent-appearing patient who refuses part or all of recommended therapy, the very first step is to formally assess decision-making capacity before involving surrogates or courts or disregarding the patient’s wishes.</p>
99. A 21-year-old man comes to the physician for an examination prior to employment. He feels well. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. He is in his first year of law school and sleeps 4 to 5 hours nightly; he reports feeling stressed frequently because of the demanding course load. He has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 2 years and drinks one beer daily. He is 178 cm (5 ft 10 in) tall and weighs 91 kg, or 200lb; BMI is 29 kg/m2. His pulse is 80/min, and blood pressure is 140/100 mm Hg. Examination shows no abnormalities. Serum electrolyte concentrations are within the reference ranges. Urinalysis shows no abnormalities. In addition to a subsequent blood pressure measurement, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Measurement of serum aldosterone:renin ratio</br>b. Doppler ultrasonography of renal arteries</br>c. Renal CT scan</br>d. Renal arteriography</br>e. No additional diagnostic studies are indicated at this time</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. No additional diagnostic studies are indicated at this time  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Young patient (21 y) with a single, mildly elevated office blood pressure (140/100 mm Hg).<br /></br>• No end-organ symptoms (headache, vision changes, chest pain, claudication).<br /></br>• Basic screen (electrolytes, UA) is normal; potassium is explicitly normal → no biochemical clue to a secondary cause (eg, primary aldosteronism).<br /></br>• Several lifestyle factors (obesity, smoking, alcohol, stress, sleep deprivation) make primary (essential) hypertension more likely.<br /></br>• Key phrase: “In addition to a subsequent blood pressure measurement …” – the question writers remind you that one elevated value is NOT diagnostic; confirmatory readings are already planned.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach recommended by USPSTF/JNC-8/ACC-AHA:<br /></br>1. Detect an elevated reading.<br /></br>2. Re-check BP on at least one more occasion (preferably ≥1 week apart) before labeling a patient hypertensive.<br /></br>3. Only AFTER hypertension is confirmed do you:<br /></br>   • Obtain basic labs (BMP, fasting glucose or A1c, lipid panel, UA).<br /></br>   • Screen for secondary causes if red flags present (hypokalemia, abrupt onset &lt;30 y, malignant HTN, renal bruit, etc.).  </p></br><p>Because this patient has only a single elevated value and no red flags, the correct “next step” is simply to re-measure at a later visit; no additional studies are warranted now.  </p></br><p>Take-home: Hypertension diagnosis = “Two readings, two occasions.” Avoid premature, costly or invasive work-ups.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (concise)<br /></br>• Essential HTN = multifactorial ↑SVR from genetics + environment (obesity, tobacco, alcohol, stress, poor sleep).<br /></br>• Secondary HTN in the young (renal artery stenosis, fibromuscular dysplasia, primary aldosteronism) is screened only when clinical/lab clues suggest it.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics (once HTN is confirmed)<br /></br>1. Lifestyle: weight loss, DASH diet, ↓Na⁺, ↓EtOH, regular exercise, smoking cessation, stress/sleep hygiene.<br /></br>2. Pharmacologic: thiazide diuretic (HCTZ), ACE-I (lisinopril), ARB (losartan), CCB (amlodipine) depending on comorbidities.<br /></br>3. Baseline tests: BMP, fasting glucose/A1c, lipid profile, UA for protein/hematuria, ECG.<br /></br>4. Secondary work-up targeted to findings (eg, aldosterone:renin ratio if HTN + hypokalemia).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>• “HTN needs TWO: two readings, two visits.”<br /></br>• “K-low → Aldo” – hypokalemia is the key trigger to order an aldosterone:renin ratio.<br /></br>• “Young + Bruit = Doppler” – abdominal bruit or resistant HTN in a young woman → think renal artery stenosis/fibromuscular dysplasia.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Measurement of serum aldosterone:renin ratio<br /></br>   • Indicated when HTN is confirmed AND there’s spontaneous or diuretic-induced hypokalemia, adrenal mass, or family history of early strokes. None are present.  </p></br><p>b. Doppler ultrasonography of renal arteries<br /></br>   • Used to screen for renal artery stenosis (fibromuscular dysplasia/atherosclerosis). Consider if sudden-onset HTN &lt;30 y, abdominal bruit, recurrent flash pulmonary edema, or resistant HTN — not seen here.  </p></br><p>c. Renal CT scan<br /></br>   • Helpful for anatomic evaluation of kidneys/adrenals after biochemical evidence of a secondary cause (eg, CT adrenal for Conn syndrome; CT angiography for renal artery stenosis). Premature now.  </p></br><p>d. Renal arteriography<br /></br>   • Invasive gold standard for renal artery stenosis, reserved for high clinical suspicion after non-invasive testing, or when planning angioplasty. Inappropriate as first step.  </p></br><p>e. No additional diagnostic studies are indicated at this time<br /></br>   • Correct: repeat BP later; if still elevated, proceed with standard initial evaluation.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Do not label or investigate hypertension on a single office reading. Confirm it first; then order focused, evidence-based tests.</p>
100. An 18-year-old man is brought to the emergency department 25 minutes after being involved in a motor vehicle collision. He was the unrestrained driver. He has shortness of breath and moderate left-sided chest pain. His temperature is 37.5 °C (99.5 °F), pulse is 110/min, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 110/60 mm Hg. Examination of the chest shows numerous abrasions and tenderness on the left. Breath sounds are decreased on the left. Heart sounds are normal. Abdominal examination shows no abnormalities. Pulse oximetry on 40% oxygen by face mask shows an oxygen saturation of 92%. An x-ray of the chest shows a left pleural effusion. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Ultrasonography of the left hemithorax</br>b. CT scan of the chest</br>c. Video-assisted thoracoscopy</br>d. Thoracentesis</br>e. Tube thoracostomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>• Tube thoracostomy  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Unrestrained driver … motor-vehicle collision”: high-energy blunt chest trauma → think lung contusion, pneumothorax, or hemothorax.<br /></br>• “Shortness of breath … moderate left-sided chest pain … ↓ breath sounds on the left … CXR: left pleural effusion”: Classic trauma pattern for an acute hemothorax (blood layering out as an “effusion”).<br /></br>• Vitals: tachycardic (110/min) but normotensive (110/60) → not yet in hemorrhagic shock, therefore have time for definitive tube placement.<br /></br>• No abdominal findings and normal heart sounds → effusion source is intrathoracic, not pericardial or intra-abdominal.  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives: unilateral dullness/effusion, chest wall tenderness.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no distended neck veins or tracheal deviation (rules out tension pneumothorax).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Hemothorax after blunt trauma demands immediate drainage to:<br /></br>1. Re-expand the lung, improving ventilation/oxygenation.<br /></br>2. Quantify blood loss (guides need for thoracotomy or transfusion).<br /></br>3. Prevent retained clot → empyema/fibrothorax.  </p></br><p>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Identify traumatic pleural fluid collection on CXR.<br /></br>2. In trauma, fluid = blood until proven otherwise.<br /></br>3. First-line management = large-caliber chest tube in the 5th ICS anterior-axillary line.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In any chest trauma with radiographic effusion, the next action is tube thoracostomy, not diagnostic taps or advanced imaging.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (Hemothorax)<br /></br>• Etiology: Laceration of lung parenchyma, intercostal/internal mammary vessels, or great-vessel injury.<br /></br>• Blood accumulates in pleural space → compresses lung → hypoxia, ↓ preload.<br /></br>• Up to 30–40% of circulating volume can hide in one hemithorax.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>1. Tube thoracostomy (28–36 Fr) = FIRST step.<br /></br>   – Immediate output &gt;1500 mL or &gt;200 mL/hr x 3 h → proceed to open thoracotomy.<br /></br>2. After chest tube: upright CXR or CT to assess re-expansion and residual clot.<br /></br>3. VATS (video-assisted thoracoscopic surgery) within 48–72 h if residual/retained hemothorax &gt;300–500 mL.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Blood in the chest? Put in the chest-tube, STAT!”<br /></br>FAST → FIND; CT → CONFIRM; Tube → TREAT.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Ultrasonography of the left hemithorax (eFAST)<br /></br>   • Useful in the trauma bay before a CXR, but we already have definitive imaging showing an effusion. Ultrasound does not treat or quantify blood loss.  </p></br><p>b. CT scan of the chest<br /></br>   • Gives anatomic detail, but delays life-saving drainage. CT is done AFTER tube placement to look for ongoing bleeding or associated injuries.  </p></br><p>c. Video-assisted thoracoscopy (VATS)<br /></br>   • Indicated for retained clot (&gt;48–72 h) or control of slow bleeding, not as the first action in the ED.  </p></br><p>d. Thoracentesis<br /></br>   • Designed for diagnostic sampling of small, non-traumatic effusions. A small-bore needle will not evacuate liters of blood and risks re-accumulation and clotting.  </p></br><p>e. Tube thoracostomy (Correct)<br /></br>   • Only option that immediately treats the problem and supplies diagnostic information (amount and rate of blood loss).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Blunt chest trauma + pleural fluid on imaging = assume hemothorax. Immediate tube thoracostomy is the standard initial management to drain blood, re-expand lung, and gauge ongoing bleeding.</p>
101. A 37-year-old woman comes to the physician for a preoperative examination 2 weeks prior to undergoing a splenectomy. She received the diagnosis of immune thrombocytopenic purpura 3 months ago. Her platelet count has ranged between 5000/mm³ and 20,000/mm³ for the past 2 weeks despite treatment with systemic corticosteroids and intravenous immunoglobulin for 2½ months. Her only current medication is prednisone. She has smoked one-half pack of cigarettes daily for 20 years. Examination shows scattered petechiae and multiple small ecchymoses over the upper and lower extremities. This patient should receive which of the following vaccines preoperatively?</br>Streptococcus pneumoniae Haemophilus influenzae Type b Neisseria meningitidis	Correct answer: G</br></br>a. No  no  yes</br>b. No  yes  no</br>c. No  yes  yes</br>d. Yes  no  no</br>e. Yes  no  yes</br>f. Yes  yes  no</br>g. Yes  yes  yes</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>g. Yes    Yes    Yes  (S. pneumoniae, H. influenzae type b, and N. meningitidis)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Planned splenectomy → permanent functional asplenia<br /></br>• Functional/actual asplenia = markedly ↑ risk of fulminant sepsis by ENCAPSULATED bacteria.<br /></br>• Key encapsulated organisms tested on NBME/Step 2CK:<br /></br>  – Streptococcus pneumoniae<br /></br>  – Haemophilus influenzae type b (Hib)<br /></br>  – Neisseria meningitidis<br /></br>• Timing clue: “2 weeks prior to splenectomy” → elective, so give vaccines ≥14 days before (allows adequate antibody response before spleen removal).</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Identify future asplenia.<br /></br>2. Recall core concept: spleen is critical for IgM-mediated opsonization &amp; clearance of encapsulated bugs.<br /></br>3. Prevention strategy = vaccinate against ALL major encapsulated organisms before spleen is gone (or ASAP after if emergency).<br /></br>4. Therefore patient needs pneumococcal, meningococcal, and Hib vaccines pre-op.</p></br><p>Take-home Message: Elective splenectomy patients should receive vaccines against S. pneumoniae, N. meningitidis, and Hib at least 2 weeks before surgery.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Spleen filters blood-borne pathogens via macrophages in splenic cords; produces IgM → complements activation (classical pathway) → opsonization (C3b).<br /></br>• Encapsulated organisms evade phagocytosis unless opsonized; hence overwhelming postsplenectomy infection (OPSI).<br /></br>• OPSI can kill within hours; mortality 50–70 % without rapid treatment.</p></br><p>Vaccines &amp; Dosing (adult schedule)<br /></br>1. Pneumococcal:<br /></br>   • PCV13 (Prevnar-13) first, followed ≥8 wk later by PPSV23 (Pneumovax-23); revaccinate PPSV23 5 yr later &amp; once after age 65.<br /></br>2. Meningococcal:<br /></br>   • MenACWY conjugate: 2-dose primary series (≥8 wk apart) then booster q5 yr.<br /></br>   • MenB (eg, Bexsero, Trumenba): 2- or 3-dose series depending on brand.<br /></br>3. Hib: single dose if not previously completed childhood series (ACIP: give to all asplenic adults).</p></br><p>Also recommended: annual inactivated influenza vaccine; Tdap per routine; COVID-19, etc.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“SHiN your Spleen” – Spleen guards against:<br /></br>S = Strep pneumo<br /></br>Hi = Haemophilus influenzae type b<br /></br>N = Neisseria meningitidis<br /></br>Lose the spleen? Shine them up with vaccines BEFORE 2 weeks!</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Yes/No/Yes (pneumococcus omitted) – Pneumococcus causes the majority of OPSI; omitting it is unsafe.<br /></br>b. No/Yes/No – Misses both pneumococcus &amp; meningococcus.<br /></br>c. No/Yes/Yes – Still lacks pneumococcal coverage.<br /></br>d. Yes/No/No – Ignores meningococcus &amp; Hib risk.<br /></br>e. Yes/No/Yes – Lacks Hib protection; adults born before 1990 often never immunized; still at risk.<br /></br>f. Yes/Yes/No – Omits meningococcal vaccine, a leading rapidly fatal pathogen post-splenectomy.</p></br><p>Common Pitfalls<br /></br>• Assuming adults don’t need Hib vaccine. If no documented complete series, 1 dose is advised.<br /></br>• Forgetting MenB component; both MenACWY and MenB are required for high-risk groups.<br /></br>• Giving vaccines after surgery when antibody response is blunted.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Key takeaway: Prior to elective splenectomy, administer vaccines against all three major encapsulated organisms—Strep pneumoniae, Neisseria meningitidis, and Hib—to prevent overwhelming postsplenectomy sepsis.</p>
102. A previously healthy 15-year-old boy is brought to the emergency department because of a 3-hour history of severe abdominal pain, nausea, and vomiting. He was kicked in the abdomen while competing in a karate tournament. He initially reported only mild pain but now rates it as an 8 on a 10-point scale. Abdominal examination shows diffuse tenderness to palpation that is most severe in the epigastric region. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Esophageal rupture</br>b. Gastric ulcer</br>c. Gastroenteritis</br>d. Hepatitis</br>e. Pancreatitis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e.  Pancreatitis  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>• Mechanism of injury – “kicked in the abdomen while competing in a karate tournament” → blunt abdominal trauma.<br /></br>• Time course – initially mild pain that becomes severe over 3 hours → evolving injury rather than pre-existing chronic condition.<br /></br>• Location – pain and maximal tenderness are “most severe in the epigastric region.”<br /></br>• Accompanying symptoms – nausea and vomiting are classic with pancreatic irritation.<br /></br>Key pattern: Blunt epigastric trauma + worsening epigastric pain + emesis ⇒ think traumatic pancreatitis (pancreatic contusion/laceration → enzyme leakage and inflammation).  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)  </p></br><ol></br><li>Localize pain: epigastrium suggests stomach, duodenum, pancreas, or distal esophagus.  </li></br><li>Incorporate mechanism: blunt trauma makes acute inflammatory or perforative processes from trauma most likely.  </li></br><li>Typical traumatic intra-abdominal organ injured by a direct blow to upper abdomen in adolescents = pancreas (retroperitoneal, compressed against vertebral column).  </li></br><li>Rapid onset severe pain + vomiting are cardinal signs of acute pancreatitis.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Blunt force directly to upper abdomen readily causes pancreatic injury → acute pancreatitis hours later.  </li></br></ol></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)  </p></br><p>• Etiology: In children/teens, the two leading causes of pancreatitis are blunt abdominal trauma and medications; gallstones/alcohol dominate in adults.<br /></br>• Mechanism: Shearing/compression of the pancreas against the spine → ductal disruption → leakage of activated pancreatic enzymes → autodigestion, inflammation, edema → severe epigastric pain radiating to back, nausea/vomiting, ± peritonitis if enzymes track anteriorly.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment Pearls  </p></br><p>Diagnostics<br /></br>1. Serum lipase (more specific) and amylase – typically &gt;3× upper limit.<br /></br>2. Abdominal CT with IV contrast if diagnosis unclear or to evaluate for ductal disruption, pseudocyst, hemorrhage.<br /></br>3. Ultrasound/MRCP if concern for bile duct pathology (less relevant here).  </p></br><p>Management<br /></br>• NPO, aggressive IV crystalloid hydration (e.g., Lactated Ringer’s), IV opioids for pain.<br /></br>• Monitor hematocrit, BUN, calcium, and vital signs for severity.<br /></br>• Surgical/IR consultation if CT shows duct transection, pseudocyst, or hemorrhage.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“GET SMASHED” mnemonic for pancreatitis causes; remember “T = Trauma.”<br /></br>Picture a karate T-kick (Trauma) “smasHing” the pancreas shaped like a boxing glove in the epigastrium → pancreatitis.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Esophageal rupture (Boerhaave)<br /></br>• Classically follows forceful retching/vomiting (sudden ↑ intra-esophageal pressure) – not a karate kick.<br /></br>• Presents with severe chest pain, subcutaneous emphysema, mediastinal crunch, not isolated epigastric tenderness. CXR shows pneumomediastinum/pleural effusion.  </p></br><p>b. Gastric ulcer<br /></br>• Chronic gnawing epigastric pain, often relieved or worsened by meals; unrelated to acute trauma. No sudden severe pain escalation within hours of an abdominal blow.  </p></br><p>c. Gastroenteritis<br /></br>• Diffuse crampy abdominal pain, diarrhea, low-grade fever; viral prodrome typically precedes. Trauma is irrelevant; focal epigastric tenderness is uncommon.  </p></br><p>d. Hepatitis<br /></br>• Presents with systemic symptoms (malaise, anorexia), RUQ tenderness, jaundice, elevated transaminases. No typical link to blunt trauma or isolated epigastric pain.  </p></br><p>e. Pancreatitis – chosen answer fits trauma mechanism, time course, and symptom cluster.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line  </p></br><p>In adolescents, a direct blow to the upper abdomen is the single most common traumatic precipitant of acute pancreatitis. Always think “pancreas” when an epigastric injury from sports or seat-belt compression is followed by worsening epigastric pain and vomiting.</p>
103. A 27-year-old woman, gravida 2, para 2, delivers a healthy 3345-g (7-lb 6-oz) newborn by cesarean delivery because of arrest of descent. Thirty-six hours later, she has chills, loss of appetite, and foul-smelling lochia. Temperature is 38.4°C (101.1°F), pulse is 112/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 90/60 mm Hg. Examination shows a clean, dry incision without erythema. There is no breast tenderness or erythema. The lungs are clear to auscultation. There is no costovertebral angle tenderness. The uterine fundus is tender to palpation. Leukocyte count is 14,000/mm³. Urinalysis shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Antibiotic therapy</br>b. CT scan of the abdomen and pelvis</br>c. Culture of the lochia</br>d. Endometrial biopsy</br>e. Transvaginal ultrasonography</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>A. Start broad-spectrum intravenous antibiotic therapy</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Timing: Fever 36 h postpartum (most cases of postpartum endometritis present 24-72 h after delivery).<br /></br>• Mode of delivery: Cesarean after labor → single strongest risk factor (≈20-30 % incidence without prophylaxis).<br /></br>• Symptoms/signs: Chills, foul-smelling lochia, uterine fundal tenderness, leukocytosis.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: Incision clean (rules out wound infection), clear lungs (rules out pneumonia), no CVA tenderness or abnormal UA (rules out pyelonephritis), no breast findings (rules out mastitis).  </p></br><p>Pattern = “Post-cesarean fever + tender uterus + malodorous lochia” → postpartum endometritis.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify postpartum fever source.<br /></br>2. Uterine tenderness + foul lochia in a post-C-section patient = endometritis.<br /></br>3. Standard of care is immediate empiric broad-spectrum IV antibiotics (clindamycin + gentamicin) that cover anaerobes, gram-negatives, and streptococci. Cultures or imaging are not required before starting therapy—the infection is polymicrobial and response to therapy, not culture, guides management.<br /></br>Take-home: Postpartum endometritis → treat first, ask questions later.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Polymicrobial (mixed anaerobes, gram-negative rods, and group B or group A strep) ascending infection of the decidua.<br /></br>• Risk factors: Cesarean (especially after labor or PROM), prolonged labor, multiple vaginal exams, retained products, chorioamnionitis.<br /></br>• Mechanism: Disrupted decidual barrier + necrotic tissue = ideal medium for ascending vaginal flora.<br /></br>• Clinical picture: Fever, tachycardia, lower-abdominal pain, uterine tenderness, foul lochia, leukocytosis beginning on postpartum day 1-3.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>First-line:<br /></br>• IV clindamycin (anaerobes + gram-positives) PLUS IV gentamicin (gram-negatives).<br /></br>• Continue until afebrile ≥24 h.<br /></br>Second-line/add-on:<br /></br>• Ampicillin added if no improvement after 24-48 h to cover enterococcus.<br /></br>Diagnostics: None required initially. Imaging only if no clinical improvement (evaluate for retained products, abscess, septic pelvic thrombophlebitis).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“C-section → CLINDA + GENT get her WELL”:<br /></br>• C-section risk<br /></br>• CLINDAmycin + GENtamicin<br /></br>• Give promptly to get her WELL (afebrile).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. CT abdomen/pelvis – Used if fever persists despite antibiotics to look for abscess or septic pelvic thrombophlebitis; not first step.<br /></br>C. Culture of lochia – Polymicrobial flora; culture rarely guides therapy and delays treatment.<br /></br>D. Endometrial biopsy – Unnecessary; diagnosis is clinical.<br /></br>E. Transvaginal US – Helpful if suspect retained products (echogenic intrauterine material), but uterine tenderness + foul lochia after cesarean with no bleeding pattern strongly favors endometritis; imaging not needed before empiric therapy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Key Takeaway<br /></br>Post-cesarean day-1–3 fever with uterine tenderness = postpartum endometritis → give IV clindamycin + gentamicin immediately; do not delay for cultures or imaging.</p>
104. A 37-year-old man comes to the physician for an examination required by his employer. He feels well. He has no known allergies or history of serious illness. He takes no medications. He has smoked one and one-half packs of cigarettes daily for 20 years and drinks six beers weekly. He has a sedentary lifestyle. He is 178 cm (5 ft 10 in) tall and weighs 82 kg, or 180lb; BMI is 26 kg/m². His pulse is 68/min and regular, and blood pressure is 128/84 mm Hg. The lungs are clear to auscultation; there is a prolonged expiratory phase. Cardiac examination shows no abnormalities. During the examination, the patient asks what he can expect if he quits smoking now. Which of the following is the most appropriate physician response?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. “Age-related decline in pulmonary function will be slower if you stop smoking.”</br>b. “In 5 years, your risk for lung cancer will decrease to that of a nonsmoker.”</br>c. “Your pulmonary function will not decrease further in the future.”</br>d. “Your risk for lung cancer will be the same as it is now.”</br>e. “Your risk for myocardial infarction will be higher than that of a nonsmoker for the rest of your life, but your risk for cerebral infarction will decrease.”</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. “Age-related decline in pulmonary function will be slower if you stop smoking.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• Key clues: 37-y-o man, 30-pack-year history, prolonged expiratory phase (early obstructive change). Otherwise healthy.<br /></br>• Question stem: “What can he expect if he quits smoking now?”—asks for realistic physiologic benefit of cessation.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>1. Smokers lose forced expiratory volume in 1 second (FEV₁) ≈ 60 mL/yr vs ≈ 30 mL/yr in nonsmokers.<br /></br>2. Smoking cessation does NOT restore destroyed alveoli, but the excessive annual loss stops; thereafter the slope of decline parallels that of a nonsmoker.<br /></br>3. Therefore the best counsel is: “Your age-related decline in lung function will slow.”</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Quitting smoking returns the rate of FEV₁ decline to normal, preserving lung function long-term, although existing damage is largely irreversible.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Tobacco smoke → chronic airway inflammation, mucus hypersecretion, protease release → accelerated small-airway narrowing and emphysematous destruction → rapid FEV₁ decline.<br /></br>• When smoking stops, inflammatory drive falls;
