protease–antiprotease balance improves → accelerated decline ceases, but destroyed parenchyma does not regenerate.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment / Prognosis Facts Worth Knowing</br>Time-course of risk reduction after cessation:<br /></br>• 20 min – HR/BP drop.<br /></br>• 48 h – CO level normalizes, taste/smell improve.<br /></br>• 2–3 wk – improved circulation, lung function begins to improve.<br /></br>• 1 yr – excess risk of acute MI halves.<br /></br>• 5 yr – stroke risk ~ nonsmoker; lung cancer risk ↓ but still &gt; nonsmoker.<br /></br>• 10 yr – lung cancer mortality ≈ 30–50 % of continuing smoker.<br /></br>• 15 yr – coronary heart disease risk ≈ nonsmoker.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“1-5-10-15 Rule”:<br /></br>1 yr MI cut in half,<br /></br>5 yr Stroke ≈ normal,<br /></br>10 yr Lung cancer 50 %,<br /></br>15 yr CHD ≈ normal.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>b. “In 5 years, lung cancer risk will equal a nonsmoker’s.”<br /></br>   • False: risk remains elevated for &gt;10–15 yrs.</p></br><p>c. “Pulmonary function will not decrease further.”<br /></br>   • False: FEV₁ still falls with age; only the <em>rate</em> normalizes.</p></br><p>d. “Risk for lung cancer will be the same as it is now.”<br /></br>   • False: risk begins falling immediately and continues to decline.</p></br><p>e. “MI risk will be higher than a nonsmoker’s for life, but stroke risk will decrease.”<br /></br>   • False: MI risk approaches that of a nonsmoker within ≈15 yrs, and stroke risk normalizes by ≈5 yrs.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>Quitting smoking does not restore lost lung capacity, but it converts the accelerated FEV₁ decline back to the normal age-related slope, markedly preserving future pulmonary function and reducing multiple disease risks.</p>
105. A 38-year-old woman comes to the office for evaluation of several red, painless lesions on her chest and abdomen that she first noticed 4 months ago. Medical history is remarkable only for occasional headaches, which she describes as generally right sided and throbbing, with associated nausea and photophobia. Ibuprofen partially relieves the pain. She also takes an oral contraceptive. Her last menstrual period was 3 weeks ago. She is sexually active with one male partner and they do not use condoms. She has smoked one-half pack of cigarettes daily for the past 15 years and she drinks one beer daily. Vital signs are normal. Physical examination discloses several small (3 to 5- mm), slightly raised, erythematous lesions on her chest and abdomen as shown. Which of the following is the most appropriate diagnostic study?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Cerebral MR angiography</br>b. Excisional biopsy of a lesion</br>c. HIV antibody test</br>d. Liver function tests</br>e. No diagnostic study is indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>e. No diagnostic study is indicated</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis – Key Clues</br>• “38-year-old woman” – adult onset<br /></br>• “Several small (3-5 mm), slightly raised, erythematous lesions” on the chest/abdomen<br /></br>• “Red, painless” and present × 4 mo<br /></br>• No systemic symptoms (no bleeding, weight loss, fever, pruritus, abdominal pain, jaundice, etc.)</p></br><p>Pattern: Multiple, pinpoint-to-pin-head, bright-red papules that appear in adulthood → classic description of cherry hemangiomas (Campbell-de-Morgan spots).</p></br><p>Pertinent negatives:</br>• No blanching spider-like telangiectasia reported → argues against spider angioma.<br /></br>• No purple/brown plaques → argues against Kaposi sarcoma.<br /></br>• No friable, rapidly growing lesion → argues against pyogenic granuloma.<br /></br>• No mucosal bleeding, epistaxis, or family history → argues against hereditary hemorrhagic telangiectasia.<br /></br>• Normal vitals and exam otherwise → no suggestion of occult liver disease, infection, or malignancy.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-wise thought process:</br>1. Skin lesion morphology &amp; size → small, bright-red papules = cherry hemangioma.<br /></br>2. Epidemiology → very common after age 30, increase with age, benign capillary proliferations.<br /></br>3. Natural history → do not transform, do not signal systemic disease.<br /></br>4. Management → reassurance; removal only for cosmetic reasons or recurrent bleeding.<br /></br>Therefore, no laboratory work-up or imaging is warranted.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Cherry hemangiomas are benign vascular proliferations that require no evaluation.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Etiology: Benign capillary hemangiomas of the papillary dermis. Exact cause unknown; age-related vascular proliferation.<br /></br>• Clinical course: Persist indefinitely; number increases with advancing age; may bleed if traumatised but otherwise asymptomatic; no malignant potential.<br /></br>• Distinguishing points: Bright-red to violaceous, dome-shaped papules 1-6 mm; most often on trunk; do not blanch completely when pressed (unlike spider angiomas).</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics</br>First-line treatment: None required. Elective options — shave/punch excision, laser ablation, or electrocautery — purely cosmetic. No imaging, labs, or biopsy are indicated in an otherwise healthy patient.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“Cherry at Thirty”: Cherry hemangiomas start after the third decade and are as harmless as the cherries on top of a sundae — just decoration, no work-up.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Why the Other Options Are Wrong</br>a. Cerebral MR angiography – would be ordered if you suspected hereditary hemorrhagic telangiectasia (telangiectasias, epistaxis, AVMs). This patient lacks mucosal lesions or bleeding history.</p></br><p>b. Excisional biopsy – unnecessary for typical-appearing cherry hemangiomas; biopsy reserved for atypical, changing, or pigmented lesions suspicious for melanoma or angiosarcoma.</p></br><p>c. HIV antibody test – bacillary angiomatosis or Kaposi sarcoma in HIV+ patients presents with violaceous/erythematous papules that often bleed; lesions here are classic cherry hemangiomas and patient lacks risk factors or systemic findings.</p></br><p>d. Liver function tests – multiple (&gt;5-10) spider angiomas, palmar erythema, gynecomastia could suggest chronic liver disease or hyperestrogen states (e.g., pregnancy, OCP use). The described lesions are too small, raised (not flat telangiectasias) and lack the characteristic central arteriole with radiating legs.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>Small (3–5 mm) bright-red papules appearing in adults are cherry hemangiomas. They are benign and require no diagnostic evaluation.</p>
106. A 64-year-old man comes to the office because of a 4-month history of moderately painful ejaculation during sexual intercourse. Occasionally, he says the ejaculate is dark and foul smelling. He also has had low back pain and mild lower pelvic pain for 8 weeks. During the past 6 months, he has been sexually active with four women; they use condoms inconsistently. He has paid for sex and has had unprotected anal sex during this time. The patient’s vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows a circumcised penis with no lesions. The testes and epididymis are normal. The prostate is mildly tender to palpation and minimally enlarged. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Abdominal ultrasonography</br>b. CT scan of the pelvis</br>c. Placement of a urinary catheter to obtain urine for urinalysis and culture</br>d. Transrectal prostate ultrasonography</br>e. Urine culture before and after prostatic massage</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Urine culture before and after prostatic massage (Meares-Stamey “2-glass” or “4-glass” test)</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues:<br /></br>• 64-year-old ⟹ gram-negative enteric organisms most common (E coli).<br /></br>• ≥3 months of symptoms (4-month painful ejaculation, 8-week pelvic/back pain) ⟹ chronic process.<br /></br>• Painful ejaculation + foul-smelling/dark semen = classic for chronic bacterial prostatitis.<br /></br>• Prostate only mildly enlarged/tender (vs markedly tender &amp; edematous in acute prostatitis).<br /></br>• No fever or systemic toxicity.<br /></br>These findings rule in chronic bacterial prostatitis and rule out acute prostatitis, BPH, or a primary STI of the penis/epididymis.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning  </p></br><p>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Identify chronic prostatitis pattern (long-standing pelvic/perineal pain ± painful ejaculation, mild prostate tenderness).<br /></br>2. The diagnostic gold standard is demonstration of bacteria in prostatic secretions or in the urine that is expressed after prostatic massage.<br /></br>3. Therefore obtain split-void urine cultures before and after massage → answer e.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Chronic bacterial prostatitis is confirmed when a post-massage urine specimen grows the causative organism (often E coli) in higher colony count than the pre-massage specimen.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>Etiology: Recurrent seeding of the prostate by urinary tract gram-negative rods (E coli, Klebsiella) or, in younger men, Chlamydia/Gonorrhea.<br /></br>Mechanism: Biofilm formation in prostatic ducts → low-grade infection → obstructed ducts → pain with ejaculation and intermittent bacteriuria.<br /></br>Clinical picture:<br /></br>• Pelvic/perineal or low back pain &gt;3 mo<br /></br>• Dysuria, frequency, painful ejaculation, hematospermia or foul semen<br /></br>• Prostate may be normal or slightly tender.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests  </p></br><p>Diagnosis:<br /></br>• Meares-Stamey test (preferred):<br /></br>  – VB1: initial voided urine<br /></br>  – VB2: midstream urine<br /></br>  – EPS: expressed prostatic secretions<br /></br>  – VB3: first urine after massage<br /></br>(“2-glass” simplified version = pre- &amp; post-massage urine culture).<br /></br>• Positive if VB3/EPS shows ≥10-fold ↑ colony count over VB2.  </p></br><p>Management (once organism identified):<br /></br>• 1st-line: Fluoroquinolone (ciprofloxacin or levofloxacin) 4–6 weeks<br /></br>• Alternatives: TMP-SMX 6 weeks, doxycycline for suspected C trachomatis.<br /></br>• Refractory: α-blocker for voiding symptoms, TURP for obstruction, suppository NSAIDs for pain.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“Prostatitis? Perform the Post-massage Pee.”<br /></br>PPP = Prostatitis diagnosis requires Post-massage Pee culture.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Abdominal ultrasonography<br /></br>   • Evaluates kidneys/bladder for stones, obstruction, masses; does NOT isolate prostatic pathogens.  </p></br><p>b. CT scan of the pelvis<br /></br>   • Used for abscess, malignancy, or complicated infection; excessive radiation and cost for initial work-up of suspected chronic prostatitis.</p></br><p>c. Placement of a urinary catheter for culture<br /></br>   • Straight cath or foley would yield the same organisms as a midstream catch and adds infection risk; still wouldn’t localize bacteria to the prostate.</p></br><p>d. Transrectal prostate ultrasonography<br /></br>   • Helpful for prostatic abscess or to guide biopsy for cancer, but not needed to diagnose chronic bacterial prostatitis.</p></br><p>e. Urine culture before and after prostatic massage (Correct)<br /></br>   • Directly compares bladder urine to prostatic secretions, identifying pathogen and confirming infection source.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line: Chronic bacterial prostatitis presents with chronic pelvic/perineal pain and painful ejaculation. Confirm diagnosis with pre- and post-massage urine cultures; then treat with a 4–6 week course of a fluoroquinolone.</p>
107. A 76-year-old man is admitted to the intensive care unit because of a 12-hour history of moderate chest pain and progressive shortness of breath. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. His temperature is 37°C (98.6°F), pulse is 98/min and regular, respirations are 22/min, and blood pressure is 84/56 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on 10 L/min of oxygen by nasal cannula shows an oxygen saturation of 88%. Examination shows jugular venous distention to the angle of the jaw bilaterally. On pulmonary examination, diffuse crackles are heard bilaterally. Administration of 100% oxygen by face mask is begun. His blood pressure is 78/42 mm Hg. Echocardiography shows an ejection fraction of 10%. ECG shows evidence of an anterior myocardial infarction. Administration of which of the following is most likely to improve this patient’s condition?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. ADH (vasopressin)</br>b. Dobutamine</br>c. Isoproterenol</br>d. Norepinephrine</br>e. Phenylephrine</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Dobutamine  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “12-h history of chest pain → ECG: anterior MI” → acute infarction of the left ventricle.<br /></br>• “Ejection fraction 10 % + SBP 78/42 mm Hg + O₂ sat 88 % despite 10 L O₂” → pump failure with cardiogenic shock (low cardiac output, hypotension, end-organ hypoperfusion).<br /></br>• “JVD + diffuse crackles” → high filling pressures/back-up: pulmonary edema &amp; elevated right-sided pressures.<br /></br>Key pattern: Acute MI → very low EF → pulmonary congestion + hypotension = cardiogenic shock that needs a positive inotrope, NOT a pure vasoconstrictor.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Diagnose shock type<br /></br>• Cold, wet, hypotensive, high JVP = cardiogenic shock.<br /></br>Step 2: Physiologic goal<br /></br>• Increase contractility (↑ stroke volume) while avoiding excessive ↑SVR (afterload) that would further burden the failing LV.<br /></br>Step 3: Drug that best meets goal<br /></br>• Dobutamine: strong β1 (↑ inotropy), mild β2 (slight vasodilation ↓ afterload), modest chronotropy → ↑ cardiac output &amp; improves tissue perfusion; afterload not markedly increased, so LV ejection improves.  </p></br><p>Take-home message: In cardiogenic shock from acute MI, first-line temporary support is an inotrope with some vasodilatory property (e.g., dobutamine). Pressors may be added later if BP remains critically low, but improving contractility comes first.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Anterior MI causes large loss of LV myocardium → drastic fall in contractility &amp; EF.<br /></br>• Low CO → hypotension → reflex vasoconstriction → ↑SVR/afterload → even worse LV output (vicious cycle).<br /></br>• Elevated LV &amp; LA pressures → pulmonary edema (crackles) &amp; JVD.<br /></br>• Breaking the cycle requires boosting contractility and, if possible, slightly reducing afterload.  </p></br><p>Dobutamine Mechanism<br /></br>• β1 agonist → ↑cAMP in myocytes → ↑Ca²⁺ influx → ↑ contractility.<br /></br>• β2 effect → mild vasodilation → ↓ afterload.<br /></br>• Net: ↑ CO and often ↑ BP despite lower SVR. Starts working within minutes, titratable by IV drip.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line (while planning reperfusion):<br /></br>1. Dobutamine drip ± cautious fluids if RV infarct suspected.<br /></br>2. If SBP remains &lt; 70-80 mm Hg after inotropy → add norepinephrine for combined pressor-inotrope effect.<br /></br>3. Definitive: Emergent PCI, possible intra-aortic balloon pump.<br /></br>Monitoring: continuous telemetry, bedside echo, urine output, lactate.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Do-BUTA-mine gets the BUTA-pump going.”<br /></br>• DO = Dobutamine<br /></br>• BUTA = Boost Up The Afterload just a little (actually drops it)<br /></br>• Pump = cardiac pump (↑ contractility)  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Vasopressin (ADH): Potent V₁ vasoconstrictor → sharply ↑SVR/afterload, no inotropic effect → worsens LV failure. Used in refractory septic shock, not cardiogenic shock.  </p></br><p>c. Isoproterenol: Pure β agonist (β1 + strong β2) → tachycardia + marked vasodilation → can further drop BP; rarely used outside specific bradyarrhythmias.  </p></br><p>d. Norepinephrine: Strong α1 (↑SVR) with some β1. Good for distributive (septic) shock or when BP is critically low despite inotropy. Here, raising afterload without first improving contractility risks further decrease in stroke volume. May be added after dobutamine if BP remains &lt;70 mm Hg.  </p></br><p>e. Phenylephrine: Pure α1 pressor → ↑ afterload, reflex bradycardia, no β1 effect. Would worsen forward flow and pulmonary edema.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Cardiogenic shock after large MI = pump failure. The quickest pharmacologic way to help the failing myocardium is an inotrope with minimal increase in afterload—dobutamine.</p>
108. A previously healthy 22-year-old woman comes to the physician 12 hours after the onset of swelling of her left arm. She lifts weights five times weekly and has been focusing on strengthening her upper body during the past 2 weeks. Examination shows prominent superficial veins over the anterior left shoulder and chest. The left upper extremity is edematous from the shoulder to the hand. There is a bluish discoloration of the left forearm and hand. Muscle strength and sensation are intact in all extremities. Which of the following is the most likely cause of the edema?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Arterial occlusion</br>b. Compartment syndrome</br>c. Hematoma</br>d. Lymphedema</br>e. Venous thrombosis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>e. Venous thrombosis (effort-induced axillary/subclavian DVT, a.k.a. Paget-Schröetter syndrome)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis – How the stem steers you to a venous clot</br>• Sudden onset (12 h) of unilateral arm swelling → favors an acute obstructive process rather than chronic disorders such as lymphedema.<br /></br>• Weight-lifting focus over the last 2 weeks → repetitive overhead/upper-extremity exertion classically precipitates axillary/subclavian vein thrombosis (“effort thrombosis”).<br /></br>• Prominent superficial veins over shoulder/chest → collateral venous engorgement created by an obstructed deep vein.<br /></br>• Diffuse edema from shoulder to hand + bluish discoloration → impaired venous outflow (blood pools, causing cyanosis).<br /></br>• Motor and sensory exams normal → arterial flow and nerves still intact, making arterial occlusion or compartment syndrome unlikely.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)</br>1. Localize: whole arm edema with visible chest collaterals = obstruction at/central to axillary vein.<br /></br>2. Tempo: immediate (hours) = vascular event, not chronic lymphatic.<br /></br>3. Precipitant: strenuous, repetitive upper-extremity exercise → classic for Paget-Schröetter.<br /></br>4. Choose pathology: venous obstruction best fits all findings (collaterals + cyanosis + intact pulses).</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Sudden, unilateral upper-extremity swelling after vigorous arm use = think axillary/subclavian deep-vein thrombosis.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology in a nutshell</br>• Repetitive overhead motion compresses the subclavian vein between the clavicle and first rib or within the costoclavicular space → endothelial trauma + stasis → thrombosis.<br /></br>• Blocked venous return → ↑ capillary hydrostatic pressure → rapid edema and cyanosis.<br /></br>• Body opens superficial collaterals across the shoulder and chest (seen on exam).</p></br><p>Key Diagnostics / Management (Step 2 level)</br>• Duplex ultrasound (first-line); CT or contrast venography if unclear.<br /></br>• Immediate anticoagulation (e.g., IV heparin → warfarin or DOAC).<br /></br>• Catheter-directed thrombolysis ± mechanical thrombectomy if limb-threatened.<br /></br>• Many patients require thoracic-outlet decompression (first-rib resection) to prevent recurrence.</p></br><p>Mnemonic</br>“Paget lifts the iron and clots his vein”<br /></br>Paget-Schröetter = Effort DVT of axillary/subclavian vein after weight-lifting.</p></br><p>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>a. Arterial occlusion: Produces 6 P’s (pain, pallor, pulselessness, paralysis, paresthesia, poikilothermia). Limb is pale/cold, not swollen/blue; superficial veins collapse, not engorge.<br /></br>b. Compartment syndrome: Severe, escalating pain out of proportion, pain with passive stretch, tense compartments; pulses may be present or diminished but veins are not distended and discoloration is uncommon early.<br /></br>c. Hematoma: Localized mass at trauma site; swelling confined, not entire limb; no dilated chest veins.<br /></br>d. Lymphedema: Painless, non-pitting, chronic swelling over months to years; skin thickens (“peau d’orange”); no acute cyanosis or venous engorgement.</p></br><p>High-Yield Memory Hook</br>Acute upper-extremity swelling + visible chest collaterals in an athlete = “weight-lifter’s DVT” → axillary/subclavian venous thrombosis → start anticoagulation.</p>
109. A previously healthy 4-year-old girl is brought to the physician because of a 3-day history of noisy breathing, runny nose, and cough. Her mother says the patient has had decreased oral intake during the past 4 hours with increasingly severe cough and noisy breathing. She has not had drooling or change in the pitch or intensity of her cry. She does not appear to be cyanotic. Her temperature is 40°C (104°F), pulse is 150/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 90/55 mm Hg. She can fully open her mouth. The uvula is midline, and the tonsils are mildly erythematous, symmetric, and moderately enlarged bilaterally. Examination shows intercostal and suprasternal retractions, inspiratory stridor, and nasal flaring. There are no crackles or expiratory wheezing. A chest x-ray shows a "shaggy" tracheal air column and peribronchial cuffing with no parenchymal densities; there is no cardiomegaly. Her symptoms show no improvement after two administrations of nebulized racemic epinephrine or with sitting upright. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Bacterial tracheitis</br>b. Bronchiolitis</br>c. Epiglottitis</br>d. Laryngotracheobronchitis (croup)</br>e. Peritonsillar abscess</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>• Bacterial tracheitis  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis – How do we know?<br /></br>1. Age &amp; prodrome: 4-year-old with a 3-day viral URI (runny nose, cough) → typical set-up for secondary bacterial tracheal infection.<br /></br>2. High fever (40 °C) &amp; toxic work-of-breathing (intercostal/suprasternal retractions, nasal flaring, inspiratory stridor) → far higher fever than classic viral croup.<br /></br>3. No drooling, can fully open mouth, normal voice → argues strongly against epiglottitis or peritonsillar abscess.<br /></br>4. Imaging clue: “Shaggy”/irregular tracheal air column &amp; peribronchial cuffing (sloughed membranes and purulent exudate coating the trachea). Croup would show a smooth, symmetric “steeple sign.”<br /></br>5. Therapy failure: No improvement after two doses of nebulized racemic epinephrine &amp; upright positioning. Viral croup almost always shows at least partial, transient improvement with epinephrine. Lack of response = red flag for bacterial tracheitis.  </p></br><p>Key pattern: Post-viral child + very high fever + inspiratory stridor + poor response to racemic epinephrine + shaggy trachea = bacterial tracheitis (“exudative croup”).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Upper-airway obstruction? → Inspiratory stridor present.<br /></br>2. Immediate life-threats (epiglottitis, foreign body) ruled out by ability to swallow, no drooling, midline uvula, full mouth opening.<br /></br>3. Viral croup considered → but high fever &amp; epinephrine failure atypical.<br /></br>4. Imaging &amp; high fever point to bacterial superinfection of the trachea → choose bacterial tracheitis.  </p></br><p>Take-home: Always suspect bacterial tracheitis when “croup” looks toxic or does not respond to racemic epi.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Most commonly Staphylococcus aureus; also Streptococcus pyogenes, Moraxella catarrhalis, H influenzae.<br /></br>• Mechanism: Bacterial invasion of the subglottic trachea after mucosal damage from a viral URI → thick purulent exudate, pseudomembranes, sloughing → airway obstruction and irregular (“shaggy”) tracheal lumen on imaging.<br /></br>• Clinical manifestations: High fever, toxic appearance, inspiratory (± biphasic) stridor, rapid progression, minimal/absent response to standard croup therapy, may progress to respiratory failure.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>• FIRST: Secure airway – many need emergent endotracheal intubation because copious secretions can suddenly occlude the airway.<br /></br>• IV broad-spectrum antibiotics targeting S aureus &amp; streptococci<br /></br>  – Oxacillin or nafcillin plus a third-generation cephalosporin (eg, ceftriaxone)<br /></br>  – Vancomycin if MRSA prevalence is high.<br /></br>• Bronchoscopy after airway control both confirms diagnosis and allows suctioning of membranes.<br /></br>• Corticosteroids &amp; racemic epi have limited benefit once bacterial tracheitis is established.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Bacterial tracheitis = BAD croup”<br /></br>B – Bacterial (Staph aureus)<br /></br>A – Appears toxic / high fever<br /></br>D – Doesn’t improve with racemic epi, Dex (steroids)<br /></br>Think: “If the croup drug doesn’t work, think bacterial work.”  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>1. Bronchiolitis<br /></br>   • Lower airway disease (RSV) → expiratory wheezes, crackles, hyperinflation; no inspiratory stridor. Chest x-ray would show hyperinflated lungs, not tracheal irregularities.  </p></br><ol></br><li></br><p>Epiglottitis<br /></br>   • Abrupt onset, severe sore throat, drooling, muffled “hot-potato” voice, prefers tripod position, often refuses to open mouth; thumbprint sign on lateral neck film. None of these are present.  </p></br></li></br><li></br><p>Laryngotracheobronchitis (viral croup)<br /></br>   • Age overlap, barking cough, low-grade fever (&lt;39 °C), improves (even briefly) with nebulized racemic epi; x-ray shows steeple sign. Our patient has 40 °C fever, shaggy trachea, and no response to epi.  </p></br></li></br><li></br><p>Peritonsillar abscess<br /></br>   • Older children/adolescents, unilateral tonsillar bulge, deviation of uvula, severe throat pain, trismus, muffled voice. Patient’s tonsils are symmetric; uvula is midline.  </p></br></li></br></ol></br><p>────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Takeaway<br /></br>In a child with presumed “croup” who is toxic, febrile to 40 °C, and unresponsive to racemic epinephrine, immediately think bacterial tracheitis; secure the airway and start IV anti-staphylococcal antibiotics.</p>
110. A 37-year-old woman with cystic fibrosis is admitted to the critical care unit because of hemoptysis and pulmonary failure. During the past 4 years, she has been hospitalized nine times for treatment of pulmonary failure and intestinal obstructions caused by meconium ileus equivalent; 1 month ago, she was in the critical care unit for 2 weeks because of worsening lung function. She repeatedly has refused to be considered for lung transplant. She has a living will that states her wish for no cardiopulmonary resuscitation if she undergoes cardiac arrest while hospitalized. Psychologic testing during the past year has confirmed that she is mentally competent. She is heavily sedated, intubated, and mechanically ventilated. Her pulmonary failure worsens, and her family asks whether extracorporeal membrane oxygenation (ECMO) treatment could be used to improve her condition. Which of the following is the most appropriate physician response?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. ECMO is contraindicated because of the patient’s history of hemoptysis</br>b. ECMO is unlikely to change the patient’s outcome</br>c. The family should not be initiating the patient’s medical treatment plans</br>d. The patient is too old for ECMO</br>e. The patient’s living will prevents the use of ECMO</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>b. ECMO is unlikely to change the patient’s outcome</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Key clues</br>  – 37-year-old with cystic fibrosis (CF) → progressive, irreversible lung destruction.</br>  – Nine hospitalizations in 4 yrs for respiratory failure + intestinal obstruction; most recent ICU stay 1 mo ago → advanced, end-stage disease.</br>  – Repeatedly refused lung transplantation (the only definitive therapy for end-stage CF lung disease) → no destination therapy available.</br>  – Now heavily sedated, intubated, and ventilated with worsening oxygenation → refractory respiratory failure.</br>  – DNR order only forbids CPR; it does not address ECMO.</br>• Pertinent negatives</br>  – No potentially reversible acute process (e.g., ARDS from infection, pulmonary embolism) is mentioned.</br>  – No willingness to proceed to lung transplant, so ECMO cannot serve as a “bridge to transplant.”</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Identify stage of disease: irreversible, end-stage CF.<br /></br>2. Ask: “Can ECMO be used as a bridge to recovery or transplant?”<br /></br>   • Bridge to recovery → requires a reversible lung insult (not present).<br /></br>   • Bridge to transplant → patient has refused transplant.<br /></br>3. Therefore ECMO would serve only to prolong a dying process without offering survival benefit or meaningful quality of life.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Use ECMO only when there is a clear, achievable endpoint (recovery or transplant). In end-stage CF without transplant candidacy, ECMO is non-beneficial.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• CF lung disease is characterized by chronic infection, bronchiectasis, airway obstruction, and fibrotic remodeling → progressive decline in FEV1.<br /></br>• Once FEV1 &lt; 30% predicted or frequent ICU admissions, median survival is &lt; 2–3 yrs unless lung transplant is performed.<br /></br>• ECMO (veno-venous) provides extracorporeal gas exchange but does not treat the underlying structural lung destruction.</p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests</br>• Current evidence-based indications for ECMO (EOLIA, CESAR trials):<br /></br>  – Severe ARDS with potentially reversible etiology.<br /></br>  – Bridge to lung transplantation or LVAD in selected patients.<br /></br>• Contraindications: irreversible lung disease without transplant option, significant uncontrolled bleeding risk, advanced age with no destination therapy, or patient refusal.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“ECMO needs an EXIT”:<br /></br>E – Endpoint (recovery or transplant)<br /></br>X – eXtra support only<br /></br>I – Irreversible disease without exit = inappropriate<br /></br>T – Transplant refusal or not a candidate → no ECMO  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Hemoptysis as absolute contraindication – Massive uncontrolled pulmonary hemorrhage is a relative/absolute contraindication, but her hemoptysis is intermittent and secondary to CF; the decisive issue is futility, not bleeding history.<br /></br>c. “Family should not initiate plans” – Families are appropriate surrogate decision-makers when patients lack capacity; the physician must still evaluate medical appropriateness.<br /></br>d. “Too old” – Age &gt; 65-70 yrs is a relative contraindication; 37 yrs is well within typical ECMO age limits.<br /></br>e. Living will prevents ECMO – Her directive only excludes CPR during cardiac arrest; it does not explicitly forbid ECMO. Even without the DNR, ECMO is medically futile given no transplant plan.</p></br><p>Bottom Line</br>In end-stage CF with transplant refusal, ECMO provides no path to recovery or transplantation; thus it is medically non-beneficial and should not be offered.</p>
111. A 42-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 3-month history of fatigue, decreased appetite, and mild swelling of her arms and legs. Twenty years ago, she underwent transplantation of a donor kidney from her sister for treatment of polycystic kidney disease. She has been compliant with her immunosuppressive therapy. Medications include prednisone and azathioprine. Her temperature is 37.3°C (99.1°F), pulse is 86/min, respirations are 28/min, and blood pressure is 170/98 mm Hg. Examination shows no tenderness or enlargement of the transplanted kidney. Her hemoglobin concentration is 8 g/dL, serum urea nitrogen concentration is 50 mg/dL, and serum creatinine concentration is 5.3 mg/dL. Which of the following is the most likely mechanism of this patient’s impending renal failure?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Acute cellular rejection</br>b. Chronic transplant nephropathy</br>c. Chronic urinary tract infections</br>d. Hypertension</br>e. Recurrence of polycystic kidney disease</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>B. Chronic transplant nephropathy (chronic allograft rejection)</p></br><p>───────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “20 years ago, she underwent transplantation of a donor kidney” → very late (&gt;10 yrs) after transplant.<br /></br>• “3-month history of fatigue, ↓appetite, mild edema” + anemia, BUN 50 mg/dL, Cr 5.3 mg/dL → progressive chronic kidney disease (CKD).<br /></br>• “Compliant with immunosuppressive therapy; no tenderness or enlargement of the transplanted kidney; afebrile” → rules against acute rejection or infection.<br /></br>Pattern: Slow, insidious rise in creatinine years after transplant = chronic transplant nephropathy (CTN).</p></br><p>───────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Timing is everything in transplant medicine.<br /></br>   • &lt;1 wk → hyperacute (pre-formed Ab).<br /></br>   • 1 wk–6 mo → acute cellular or humoral rejection.<br /></br>   • &gt;6 mo–years with gradual decline → chronic allograft rejection = chronic transplant nephropathy.<br /></br>2. Vignette lacks fever, graft tenderness, oliguria, or abrupt Cr jump → not acute cellular rejection.<br /></br>3. Donor kidney (from sister) cannot redevelop ADPKD cysts.<br /></br>4. No history of recurrent pyelonephritis.<br /></br>5. Hypertension is present but is usually the consequence, not the primary mechanism, of CTN.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Gradual loss of renal function many years after transplantation despite adequate immunosuppression points to chronic transplant nephropathy caused by progressive interstitial fibrosis and obliterative vascular changes within the graft.</p></br><p>───────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Chronic transplant nephropathy = chronic allograft rejection.<br /></br>• Etiology: Slow, immune-mediated vascular injury (type IV &amp; II hypersensitivity) + non-immune factors (calcineurin-inhibitor toxicity, HTN, hyperlipidemia).<br /></br>• Histology: Obliterative intimal thickening of graft vessels, interstitial fibrosis, tubular atrophy, glomerulosclerosis.<br /></br>• Clinical course: Insidious ↑ creatinine, worsening HTN, mild proteinuria, anemia over months–years.<br /></br>• Irreversible; progresses to ESRD → dialysis or re-transplantation.</p></br><p>───────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Work-up<br /></br>1. Baseline labs &amp; trend creatinine.<br /></br>2. Doppler US to rule out obstruction/renal artery stenosis.<br /></br>3. Renal allograft biopsy is diagnostic—shows vascular intimal fibrosis, tubulointerstitial atrophy.<br /></br>4. No curative therapy; slow progression by:<br /></br>   • Tight BP control (ACEi/ARB)<br /></br>   • Minimize calcineurin-inhibitor exposure<br /></br>   • Lipid &amp; glucose control<br /></br>   • Consider switch to mTOR inhibitor<br /></br>Ultimate management = re-transplant once ESRD recurs.</p></br><p>───────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“CHRONIC = YEARS”<br /></br>C – Calcineurin drug toxicity contributes<br /></br>H – Hypertension gradually rises<br /></br>R – Rising creatinine slowly<br /></br>O – Obliterative vascular changes<br /></br>N – No pain/fever<br /></br>I – Interstitial fibrosis<br /></br>C – Can’t cure (need new kidney)</p></br><p>───────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>A. Acute cellular rejection<br /></br>• Timing: usually 1 wk–6 mo post-transplant (sometimes later if meds missed).<br /></br>• Presentation: Fever, graft tenderness, abrupt ↑Cr. Histology with dense interstitial lymphocytes.<br /></br>• Vignette: 20 yrs post, afebrile, non-tender, gradual decline.  </p></br><p>C. Chronic urinary tract infections<br /></br>• Would need recurrent pyelonephritis episodes (flank pain, fevers, WBC casts) → scarring.<br /></br>• No infectious symptoms or history provided.  </p></br><p>D. Hypertension<br /></br>• Long-standing uncontrolled HTN can cause nephrosclerosis, but in kidney transplant recipients it is more often secondary to graft dysfunction rather than the primary driver. Moreover, 3-month rapid rise in Cr to 5.3 is too steep for pure hypertensive nephrosclerosis.  </p></br><p>E. Recurrence of polycystic kidney disease<br /></br>• ADPKD is a genetic defect in the recipient’s kidneys. The transplanted kidney came from her healthy sister; new cyst formation in an allograft essentially does not occur.  </p></br><p>───────────────</br>Key Take-Home Message<br /></br>In a patient with a functioning renal transplant who develops a slow, progressive rise in creatinine many years later without systemic illness, think chronic transplant nephropathy—irreversible immune-mediated vascular &amp; interstitial fibrosis leading to allograft failure.</p>
112. A previously healthy 27-year-old man comes to the physician because of nausea and vomiting for the past 36 hours. His temperature is 38 °C (100.4 °F). Examination shows scleral icterus and jaundice. There is tenderness to palpation of the right upper quadrant of the abdomen. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin 14.2 g/dL</br>Leukocyte count 5800/mm³</br>Serum</br>Bilirubin</br>Total 11 mg/dL</br>Direct 2.2 mg/dL</br>AST  1095 U/L</br>ALT  1300 U/L</br>Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: F</br></br>a. Cholangitis</br>b. Cholecystitis</br>c. Choledochal cyst</br>d. Pancreatic pseudocyst</br>e. Pancreatitis</br>f. Viral hepatitis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer (given): f. Viral hepatitis  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• “Previously healthy 27-year-old” – young adult without comorbid biliary disease (gall-stones, PSC, cancer)<br /></br>• Fever + right-upper-quadrant (RUQ) pain + jaundice – the famous “Charcot triad” can point to cholangitis, but do NOT stop there; always interrogate the labs.<br /></br>• Transaminases: AST 1095 U/L, ALT 1300 U/L (both &gt; 1000 U/L) – classic for a severe hepatocellular injury such as acute viral hepatitis, ischemic (“shock”) liver, or toxic injury (acetaminophen, mushrooms).<br /></br>• Bilirubin predominantly indirect (total 11, direct 2.2) – hepatic uptake/conjugation problem → points away from extra-hepatic obstruction (which gives high direct bilirubin).<br /></br>• Normal WBC (5800/mm³) – argues against an acute bacterial process (cholangitis, cholecystitis).<br /></br>Key pattern: very high AST/ALT + mixed or unconjugated hyperbilirubinemia in a young patient ⇒ think viral hepatitis.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)</br>1. Does the patient have biliary obstruction/sepsis?<br /></br>   • Expected labs: high Alk-phos &amp; GGT, direct (conjugated) hyperbilirubinemia, modest AST/ALT, leukocytosis. Those are absent.<br /></br>2. Is there pancreatitis? – would show epigastric pain radiating to the back, ↑ amylase/lipase (not provided), not a massive AST/ALT rise.<br /></br>3. Massive (&gt;10× ULN) AST/ALT screams hepatocellular necrosis. In an otherwise healthy 27-y/o, viral hepatitis (A, B, C, EBV, CMV) is the top culprit unless toxin history is given.<br /></br>Take-home: When AST and ALT exceed 1000 U/L, think viral or toxic/ischemic hepatitis first, even if RUQ pain, fever, &amp; jaundice resemble Charcot triad.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Pathophysiology Review – Acute Viral Hepatitis</br>• Etiology: Hepatitis A/B/C/D/E viruses (in U.S. A &amp; B most common acute).<br /></br>• Mechanism: Viral replication inside hepatocytes → immune-mediated lysis → massive hepatocellular necrosis → spill of AST &amp; ALT.<br /></br>• Clinical: Fever, malaise, nausea/vomiting, RUQ tenderness, jaundice, dark urine, sometimes pruritus.<br /></br>• Labs: AST/ALT often 1000–3000 U/L, bili 5–20 mg/dL (mixed), mild ↑ Alk-phos, normal/low WBC.<br /></br>• Prognosis: Usually self-limited (HAV, HEV) but HBV/HCV can become chronic; fulminant hepatic failure possible.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Diagnostics &amp; Management</br>• Serologies: HAV IgM, HBsAg &amp; Anti-HBc IgM, HCV RNA, etc. confirm subtype.<br /></br>• Supportive therapy: hydration, anti-emetics; avoid hepatotoxins (e.g., alcohol, acetaminophen).<br /></br>• Fulminant signs (INR &gt; 1.5, encephalopathy): admit to ICU, consider transplant evaluation.<br /></br>• Report to public health, vaccinate close contacts if HAV/HBV.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“AST/ALT over a thousand? ‘Viral’ is arousin’!”<br /></br>Mnemonic for cholangitis labs: “ABC”<br /></br>• Alk-phos high<br /></br>• Bilirubin direct<br /></br>• Cells (WBC) high<br /></br>If any piece is missing, reconsider alternate diagnoses.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>a. Cholangitis<br /></br>   • Charcot triad fits, but labs don’t: would expect marked leukocytosis, Alk-phos &gt; transaminases, and predominantly direct bilirubin. AST/ALT rarely &gt; 300 U/L.<br /></br>b. Cholecystitis<br /></br>   • Jaundice uncommon unless Mirizzi or obstruction; transaminases usually normal; ultrasound would show gallbladder wall thickening.<br /></br>c. Choledochal cyst<br /></br>   • Congenital dilation causes chronic, painless jaundice or recurrent pancreatitis in children; not acute massive AST/ALT rise.<br /></br>d. Pancreatic pseudocyst<br /></br>   • Sequel of pancreatitis; presents weeks after episode with abdominal mass/fullness, not jaundice with &gt;1000 AST/ALT.<br /></br>e. Pancreatitis<br /></br>   • Epigastric pain radiating to back, ↑ amylase/lipase; jaundice only if gall-stone obstructs ampulla; transaminases up to 200-300 if gall-stone etiology, not &gt;1000.  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Core Takeaway</br>AST or ALT &gt; 1000 U/L in an otherwise healthy patient almost always indicates acute, severe hepatocellular injury—most commonly acute viral hepatitis—regardless of the classic “Charcot” symptom triad.</p>
113. A 9-year-old girl is brought to the physician for a follow-up examination. She has a 5-month history of intermittent moderate right flank pain that radiates to the back. The pain occurs two to three times monthly and lasts 2 to 3 days before resolving spontaneously. She has not had fever, vomiting, diarrhea, pain with urination, or urinary frequency. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Multiple urine cultures during the past 5 months have been negative. Vital signs are within normal limits. The abdomen is soft and nondistended. There is right costovertebral angle tenderness.</br>Laboratory studies show:</br>Serum creatinine 1.3 mg/dL</br>Urine</br>pH 6.0</br>Specific gravity 1.010</br>Blood trace</br>Protein none</br>WBC 0/hpf</br>Nitrites none</br>Abdominal ultrasonography shows absence of the left kidney and severe dilation of the right renal pelvis. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step to prevent progression of this patient’s renal failure?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Administer intravenous furosemide</br>b. Administer an intravenous 0.9% saline bolus</br>c. Insert a urinary catheter</br>d. Perform cystoscopy with bladder outlet dilatation</br>e. Insert a right percutaneous nephrostomy tube</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Insert a right percutaneous nephrostomy tube  </p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – 9-y-o girl with 5-month history of intermittent flank pain radiating to the back (“colicky” pain typical of obstruction).<br /></br>  – Multiple sterile urine cultures → rules out infection.<br /></br>  – Serum creatinine 1.3 mg/dL (elevated for a child) → declining renal function.<br /></br>  – Ultrasonography:<br /></br>      • Agenesis of the left kidney → the right kidney is her only kidney.<br /></br>      • “Severe dilation of the right renal pelvis” → marked hydronephrosis = obstructive uropathy.<br /></br>  – No mention of bladder distention or voiding symptoms → obstruction is above the bladder (most often a ureteropelvic junction [UPJ] obstruction in children).  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives: unilateral hydronephrosis, rising creatinine, solitary kidney, flank pain.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no dysuria, frequency, fever, nitrites, WBCs → infection unlikely; no bladder outlet symptoms.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Obstruction + solitary kidney + rising creatinine = renal emergency.<br /></br>2. Goal: Immediately decompress the collecting system to halt ongoing back-pressure injury.<br /></br>3. Two rapid ways to drain an obstructed upper tract:<br /></br>   • Retrograde ureteral stent via cystoscopy (difficult in tiny pediatric ureters).<br /></br>   • Percutaneous nephrostomy tube (PCN) placed through the flank directly into the renal pelvis → fastest, most reliable.<br /></br>4. After decompression, definitive repair (e.g., pyeloplasty for UPJ obstruction) can be scheduled once the kidney has recovered.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Obstructive hydronephrosis in the patient’s ONLY kidney mandates urgent drainage with a percutaneous nephrostomy tube to prevent irreversible renal failure.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• UPJ obstruction = most common cause of unilateral hydronephrosis in children.<br /></br>   – Congenital narrowing or crossing vessels kink the ureter where it meets the renal pelvis.<br /></br>   – Intermittent obstruction worsens during diuresis (e.g., ↑ fluid intake).<br /></br>• Back-pressure ↑ intrapelvic pressure → compresses renal parenchyma → ↓ GFR → rising creatinine.<br /></br>• Solitary kidney: no contralateral “backup,” so loss of function is catastrophic.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>Immediate:<br /></br>• Percutaneous nephrostomy tube → external drainage → rapid fall in intrapelvic pressure, stabilization of GFR.<br /></br>Definitive (elective):<br /></br>• Dismembered pyeloplasty (Anderson-Hynes) or endopyelotomy to correct UPJ obstruction.  </p></br><p>Useful imaging:<br /></br>• MAG-3 renal scan with diuretic (“Lasix renogram”) after drainage to quantify split renal function and confirm obstruction.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Solo Kidney + Swollen Pelvis? Stick in a Tube.”<br /></br>(Only kidney with hydronephrosis ⇒ Percutaneous nephrostomy tube)  </p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Options Are Wrong<br /></br>a. IV furosemide<br /></br>   • Increases urine production but cannot bypass the obstruction; worsens hydronephrosis and may precipitate acute renal failure.  </p></br><p>b. IV 0.9% saline bolus<br /></br>   • Volume status is not the issue; will increase urine formation against an obstructed system and raise pressure further.  </p></br><p>c. Insert a urinary (Foley) catheter<br /></br>   • Foley drains bladder, not the renal pelvis; ineffective when blockage is at or above the ureter. Bladder not distended.  </p></br><p>d. Cystoscopy with bladder outlet dilatation<br /></br>   • Treats urethral or bladder neck obstruction (e.g., posterior urethral valves), which would present with voiding difficulty and bilateral hydronephrosis. Ultrasound localizes the problem to the renal pelvis, not the outlet.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In a child with a solitary kidney and obstructive hydronephrosis, the single most important immediate step is decompression of the collecting system with a percutaneous nephrostomy tube to prevent permanent renal damage.</p>
114. A 21-year-old man comes to the office for a routine examination. He was diagnosed with type 2 diabetes mellitus 2 years ago. His most recent hemoglobin A₁c 1 month ago was 9%. His medications are metformin and glipizide. He says he does not check his blood glucose concentration regularly because “I just want to be like everyone else.” He is 165 cm (5 ft 5 in) tall and weighs 77 kg, or 170lb; BMI is 28 kg/m². Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows no other abnormalities. In addition to expressing empathy, which of the following is the most appropriate physician approach to encourage better management of this patient’s condition?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Call the patient’s mother to encourage her to support the patient’s treatment plan at home</br>b. Demonstrate a new mobile application on cellular telephones that aids regular monitoring of blood glucose concentration</br>c. Gently warn the patient that he will be less like everyone else when he is blind and receiving hemodialysis if he does not manage his diabetes properly</br>d. Organize multidisciplinary medical care, including a support group for young patients with diabetes mellitus</br>e. Use humor to diffuse the situation and create a better physician–patient relationship</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Organize multidisciplinary medical care, including a support group for young patients with diabetes mellitus  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives<br /></br>• 21-year-old with type 2 diabetes and poor A1c (9%) → objectively uncontrolled.<br /></br>• Admits he “does not check his blood glucose … because ‘I just want to be like everyone else.’” → main barrier is psychosocial (feeling different / social isolation), not lack of knowledge.<br /></br>• Otherwise healthy physical exam → no end-organ damage yet; focus is on prevention.  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives<br /></br>• No mention of financial constraints, cognitive impairment, or lack of equipment → problem is motivation/attitude.<br /></br>• Vital signs WNL, BMI 28 (overweight, but not severe obesity) → lifestyle still important but not the only issue.  </p></br><p>Pattern to recognize<br /></br>Young adults with chronic illness frequently struggle with adherence because of stigma and the desire for “normalcy.” Peer support + multidisciplinary care (physician, diabetes educator, nutritionist, psychologist) is the evidence-based strategy that directly targets these psychosocial barriers.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning  </p></br><p>Step-wise thought process<br /></br>1. Identify the true barrier: “I just want to be like everyone else” = psychosocial isolation.<br /></br>2. Successful interventions must normalize the experience and provide peer modeling.<br /></br>3. Multidisciplinary + peer support groups have been shown to improve adherence and glycemic control in adolescents/young adults.<br /></br>4. Therefore, option d is the best answer.</p></br><p>Core takeaway<br /></br>When nonadherence stems from social or emotional factors, the most effective strategy is to provide peer support and comprehensive care that addresses medical, educational, and psychosocial needs—not scare tactics, parental policing, or jokes.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)  </p></br><p>• Poor glycemic control (A1c ≥ 7%) accelerates microvascular (retinopathy, nephropathy, neuropathy) and macrovascular (CAD, stroke) complications.<br /></br>• Tight glucose monitoring and lifestyle modification slow β-cell burnout and complication onset, which is especially crucial in early-onset type 2 DM where lifetime exposure to hyperglycemia is long.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls  </p></br><ol></br><li>Multidisciplinary team<br /></br>   • Endocrinologist / PCP: adjust meds (may need GLP-1 agonist, basal insulin, etc.).<br /></br>   • Diabetes educator: teaches glucometer use, carbohydrate counting.<br /></br>   • Dietitian: individualized meal planning.<br /></br>   • Mental-health professional: assesses depression, diabetes distress.<br /></br>   • Peer support group: models healthy behaviors, decreases stigma.  </li></br><li>Ongoing monitoring<br /></br>   • Self-monitoring of blood glucose (SMBG) frequency individualized but critical with sulfonylureas (glipizide) due to hypoglycemia risk.<br /></br>   • A1c every 3 mo until goal &lt; 7%.  </li></br></ol></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“TEAM” for nonadherence due to psychosocial issues:<br /></br>T – Team-based care<br /></br>E – Education (diabetes educator)<br /></br>A – Affective support (peer group, counseling)<br /></br>M – Medical optimization  </p></br><p>Visualize a sports TEAM huddling—no one feels left out, everyone performs better.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. Call the patient’s mother<br /></br>• Breaches autonomy/confidentiality; he is an adult.<br /></br>• Parental policing may increase resistance rather than adherence.</p></br><p>b. Demonstrate a new mobile application<br /></br>• Addresses logistics of SMBG, not the underlying feeling of abnormality. Helpful adjunct, but not primary intervention here.</p></br><p>c. Scare tactic about blindness/dialysis<br /></br>• Fear appeals produce short-lived or negative reactions, especially in young adults, and can damage the therapeutic alliance.</p></br><p>e. Use humor to diffuse the situation<br /></br>• Rapport-building is useful, but humor alone does not tackle the adherence barrier; risks minimizing the seriousness of the disease.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>For young adults whose diabetes nonadherence is driven by the desire to “fit in,” the most effective, evidence-based approach is a multidisciplinary program that includes peer support, thereby normalizing the condition and improving long-term self-management.</p>
115. A 62-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 4-week history of tingling in his left hand radiating to the ring and small fingers. He has not had any recent trauma or weakness in his hand. He has end-stage renal disease secondary to hypertension. He has been receiving hemodialysis for the past 3 months. He also has coronary artery disease, type 2 diabetes mellitus, congestive heart failure, and gout. Current medications are metoprolol, lisinopril, clonidine, insulin glargine, allopurinol, and 81-mg aspirin. His temperature is 36.7°C (98°F), pulse is 70/min, and blood pressure is 140/90 mm Hg. The lungs are clear to auscultation. A grade 2/6 systolic murmur is heard at the left sternal border. There is a dialysis fistula in place in the left upper arm; there is no inflammation, but a thrill is palpated. Physical examination shows no edema of the upper and lower extremities. Neurologic examination shows decreased sensation to light touch over the medial aspect of the left hand, ring and small fingers, and the medial aspect of the left arm to the elbow. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Arteriovenous steal syndrome</br>b. Cerebral infarction</br>c. Diabetic neuropathy</br>d. Ulnar nerve compression</br>e. Vitamin B₁₂ (cobalamin) deficiency</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Arteriovenous steal syndrome  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – New AV fistula placed in left upper arm 3 months ago; palpable thrill = high-flow shunt.<br /></br>  – Unilateral tingling &amp; ↓ light-touch sensation in ring &amp; small fingers (ulnar) PLUS medial forearm &amp; arm to the elbow (medial antebrachial &amp; brachial cutaneous nn.; C8–T1).<br /></br>  – No trauma; no edema; intact strength; pulses not mentioned as diminished but can be normal at rest.  </p></br><p>• Pertinent negatives that narrow the differential<br /></br>  – Not bilateral (argues against diabetic or B12 polyneuropathy).<br /></br>  – No motor weakness or proximal neurologic findings (argues against stroke).<br /></br>  – Sensory loss extends above the elbow, so not isolated ulnar-nerve entrapment.  </p></br><p>Pattern: New upper-extremity neurologic symptoms that began after creation of a dialysis fistula → think “steal” of arterial blood from distal nerve tissue.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Why the answer is arteriovenous steal)<br /></br>1. High-flow AV fistula diverts arterial blood into the low-resistance venous system.<br /></br>2. Distal tissues (hand, distal nerves) become chronically hypoperfused → ischemic neuropathy (paresthesias ± pain, weakness).<br /></br>3. Distribution often starts in ulnar territory but can include the entire lower brachial plexus (C8–T1), exactly matching this patient’s sensory loss pattern into the medial forearm and arm.<br /></br>Core takeaway: In a dialysis patient with a new fistula, focal hand/forearm neurologic deficits = AV steal until proven otherwise.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Excessive shunting through a surgically created AV fistula (usually brachial artery to cephalic vein).<br /></br>• Mechanism: ↓ distal arterial pressure → nerve &amp; skin ischemia.<br /></br>• Clinical manifestations:<br /></br>  – Early: coldness, pallor, pain, paresthesia of hand/fingers.<br /></br>  – Late: ulceration, muscle weakness, necrosis.<br /></br>• Risk factors: High fistula flow, proximal fistula location (upper arm &gt; forearm), diabetes, atherosclerosis.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Pearls<br /></br>• Diagnosis is clinical, confirmed with:<br /></br>  – Duplex ultrasound or digital plethysmography: ↓ distal arterial flow that improves with fistula compression.<br /></br>• First-line treatment: Surgical revision (banding, distal revascularization with interval ligation [DRIL]) to reduce shunt flow while preserving dialysis access.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>High-Yield Memory Aids<br /></br>“Dialysis steals digits” – A new dialysis fistula can “steal” blood from the hand, starting with the ulnar (ring &amp; little finger) distribution.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>b. Cerebral infarction<br /></br>   • Would cause contralateral deficits (right-sided limb), usually include motor weakness, facial involvement, or cortical signs. Unilateral, peripheral sensory loss without central findings is atypical.  </p></br><p>c. Diabetic neuropathy<br /></br>   • Causes symmetric, length-dependent “stocking-glove” deficits in BOTH feet before hands; not unilateral or confined to one nerve root distribution.  </p></br><p>d. Ulnar nerve compression<br /></br>   • Cubital-tunnel or Guyon-canal lesions cause numbness in ring &amp; little fingers but DO NOT involve the medial forearm/arm above the elbow (those territories are innervated by separate cutaneous nerves from the brachial plexus).  </p></br><p>e. Vitamin B₁₂ (cobalamin) deficiency<br /></br>   • Produces symmetric peripheral neuropathy plus dorsal-column involvement (loss of vibration/proprioception, ataxia) and usually affects lower &gt; upper extremities, not a single limb.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In patients with a recently created hemodialysis AV fistula, new unilateral distal neurologic symptoms (especially in the ulnar/C8-T1 distribution) strongly suggest arteriovenous steal syndrome due to distal limb ischemia. Prompt vascular evaluation and surgical correction are indicated.</p>
116. A 37-year-old primigravid woman at 18 weeks’ gestation comes to the physician for a follow-up examination. Yesterday, her blood pressure was 150/100 mm Hg. She also has a 10-day history of moderate daily headache. She has not had any changes in vision. She has no history of serious illness, and her only medication is a prenatal vitamin. She is not in distress. She is 163 cm (5 ft 4 in) tall. She weighed 86 kg, or 190lb prior to her pregnancy; BMI was 33 kg/m². She has had a 4.5-kg (10-lb weight gain during her pregnancy. Today, her blood pressure is 150/98 mm Hg. On examination, the uterus is nontender and consistent in size with an 18-week gestation. The fetal heart rate is 155/min. Urinalysis shows trace protein. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Essential hypertension</br>b. Gestational hypertension</br>c. Preeclampsia</br>d. Superimposed preeclampsia on essential hypertension</br>e. Transient hypertension</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Essential (chronic) hypertension  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis – Key Clues<br /></br>• “18 weeks’ gestation” → before 20-week mark.<br /></br>• Two separate readings ≥140/90 mm Hg (150/100 yesterday, 150/98 today).<br /></br>• Only “trace” proteinuria (≤300 mg/24 h).<br /></br>• No visual changes, RUQ pain, edema, or other severe-feature clues.<br /></br>Taken together: persistent hypertension diagnosed before 20 weeks without significant proteinuria = chronic (essential) hypertension.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Establish timing: &lt;20 weeks → chronic category.<br /></br>2. Confirm persistence: two visits, not just a single spike.<br /></br>3. Search for preeclampsia criteria: protein ≥300 mg/24 h or severe features absent → preeclampsia ruled out.<br /></br>4. No prior documented BP? USMLE still calls it “essential” if it presents before 20 weeks because pathology pre-dates pregnancy even if it was undiagnosed.  </p></br><p>Core Takeaway:<br /></br>Any sustained BP ≥140/90 discovered before 20 completed weeks’ gestation = chronic (essential) hypertension, regardless of past medical documentation.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Essential HTN = primary elevation of systemic vascular resistance, pre-existing endothelial dysfunction.<br /></br>• Pregnancy unmasks it because blood volume rises &gt;40 %, increasing cardiac output and exposing latent vascular disease.<br /></br>• Risk factors: obesity (BMI 33), advanced maternal age (&gt;35), family history.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Management Snapshot<br /></br>1. Baseline work-up: CMP, CBC, EKG if indicated, 24-h urine protein or protein/Cr ratio.<br /></br>2. Start low-dose aspirin (81 mg nightly at 12–28 weeks) to lower risk of superimposed preeclampsia.<br /></br>3. Treat only if BP ≥160/105 or symptomatic; agents: labetalol (β-blocker with α activity), nifedipine, or methyldopa.<br /></br>4. Serial growth ultrasounds (risk of fetal growth restriction).  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Mnemonic<br /></br>“Early = Essential.”<br /></br>If HTN shows up Early (before 20 weeks) → think Essential (Chronic).<br /></br>“Late and Loaded with protein” → Preeclampsia (after 20 weeks + protein).  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>b. Gestational hypertension<br /></br>• Defined as new HTN arising ≥20 weeks without proteinuria.<br /></br>• Our patient is only 18 weeks.  </p></br><p>c. Preeclampsia<br /></br>• Requires ≥20 weeks AND either proteinuria ≥300 mg/24 h or severe features (headache with visual change, thrombocytopenia, elevated LFTs, etc.).<br /></br>• She is &lt;20 weeks and has only trace protein.  </p></br><p>d. Superimposed preeclampsia on essential hypertension<br /></br>• Develops when a woman with chronic HTN later (≥20 weeks) develops new proteinuria or severe features. Not yet present.  </p></br><p>e. Transient hypertension<br /></br>• Refers to isolated, non-persistent BP elevation (often intrapartum or postpartum) that normalizes. Two separate high readings 24 h apart = not transient.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall:<br /></br>Students assume “no past history” means it can’t be chronic. USMLE uses the 20-week rule, not past chart documentation.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Persistent BP ≥140/90 discovered before 20 weeks gestation, with minimal or no proteinuria and no severe features, is labeled chronic (essential) hypertension.</p>
117. A 42-year-old man with hypertension and end-stage renal disease secondary to autosomal dominant polycystic kidney disease comes to the office for a routine examination. He feels well. Six months ago, he received a kidney transplant from his brother. The patient’s medications are tacrolimus, mycophenolate mofetil, trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole, amlodipine, cholecalciferol, and a multivitamin. Examination shows a functioning arteriovenous fistula in the left forearm and a firm, nontender, renal allograft in the right lower quadrant of the abdomen. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin 13.5 g/dL</br>Serum</br>Ca²⁺ 11.7 mg/dL</br>Urea nitrogen 25 mg/dL</br>Creatinine 1.5 mg/dL</br>Parathyroid hormone, intact 425 pg/mL (N = 10-60)</br>Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Hypervitaminosis A</br>b. Hypervitaminosis D</br>c. Parathyroid adenoma</br>d. Parathyroid hyperplasia</br>e. Thyrotoxicosis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Parathyroid hyperplasia  (tertiary hyperparathyroidism)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Kidney transplant 6 months ago after years of end-stage renal disease (ESRD) → long-standing secondary hyperparathyroidism is expected.<br /></br>• Current labs:<br /></br>  – Ca²⁺ 11.7 mg/dL (high)<br /></br>  – PTH 425 pg/mL (markedly high despite hypercalcemia)<br /></br>  – Cr 1.5 mg/dL (near baseline for a transplanted kidney)  </p></br><p>Key positives: persistent, very high PTH + new hypercalcemia after renal function has largely normalized.<br /></br>Key negative: no excess vitamin supplementation, no thyroid symptoms.<br /></br>Pattern: “autonomous” parathyroid glands that continue secreting despite correction of the original renal stimulus → classic for tertiary hyperparathyroidism due to parathyroid hyperplasia (4-gland disease).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Chronic kidney disease ↓1-α hydroxylation → low calcitriol, chronic hypocalcemia → secondary hyperparathyroidism (compensatory PTH rise).<br /></br>2. After successful transplant, phosphate and calcitriol normalize.<br /></br>3. If glands have undergone diffuse hyperplasia, they become autonomous → PTH stays high and Ca²⁺ overshoots (tertiary hyperparathyroidism).<br /></br>4. Autonomous overproduction from <em>all</em> glands = hyperplasia, not a solitary adenoma.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: High Ca²⁺ + high PTH in a post-transplant patient = tertiary hyperparathyroidism → parathyroid hyperplasia.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Secondary HPT (CKD stage): phosphate retention &amp; ↓vitamin D → chronic hypocalcemia → parathyroid hypertrophy &amp; hyperplasia.<br /></br>• Tertiary HPT: prolonged stimulation causes clonal expansion → glands no longer respond to Ca²⁺ negative feedback → hypercalcemia.<br /></br>Risk factors: dialysis &gt;3–5 yr, delayed transplant, large parathyroid volume pre-Tx.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>• Confirm with repeat Ca²⁺/PTH, phosphate, 25-OH D, creatinine.<br /></br>• Imaging (ultrasound, sestamibi) if surgery planned; not required for initial biochemical Dx.<br /></br>• Management:<br /></br>  1. First-line: Calcimimetics (cinacalcet) – ↑CaSR sensitivity, ↓PTH.<br /></br>  2. Vitamin D analogs (calcitriol, paricalcitol) if calcimimetic not tolerated.<br /></br>  3. Definitive: subtotal or total parathyroidectomy if refractory or Ca²⁺ &gt;10.5 mg/dL with symptoms or complications (bone disease, kidney stones).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>High-Yield Memory Aids<br /></br>“Tertiary = Transplant” → HyperCa²⁺ + High PTH after transplant.<br /></br>“Four hits in four glands” → diffuse hyperplasia vs “One bad apple” (adenoma).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Hypervitaminosis A – Causes hypercalcemia via bone resorption, but PTH would be <em>suppressed</em>. No chronic retinoid use given.<br /></br>b. Hypervitaminosis D – ↑Ca²⁺/P, ↓PTH. Patient is on standard cholecalciferol dose; PTH is very high, not low.<br /></br>c. Parathyroid adenoma – Solitary gland disease typical of <em>primary</em> HPT; can occur at any age, not specifically tied to ESRD history. Still possible post-Tx but less likely than diffuse hyperplasia when PTH &gt;800 pg/mL pre-Tx and patient had years of dialysis.<br /></br>e. Thyrotoxicosis – Hypercalcemia from bone turnover; PTH suppressed, plus patient lacks weight loss, tremor, tachycardia.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Years of ESRD cause secondary HPT; after transplant, the now-autonomous hyperplastic glands keep secreting PTH, producing hypercalcemia → tertiary hyperparathyroidism (parathyroid hyperplasia).</p>
118. A 42-year-old man is brought to the emergency department because of a 2-day history of shortness of breath and cough, which have progressed to severe respiratory distress. He has a history of intravenous illicit drug use. He lives in a homeless shelter. On arrival, he is intubated and mechanically ventilated. Examination shows coarse breath sounds bilaterally. Respiratory therapy is begun. Two days later, cultures of blood and sputum grow Neisseria meningitidis. Which of the following is the most appropriate measure to prevent the spread of this patient’s disease to hospital personnel?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Prophylaxis for all personnel working in the emergency department when the patient arrived</br>b. Prophylaxis for close contacts only</br>c. Prophylaxis for immunocompromised personnel only</br>d. No prophylaxis is indicated for hospital personnel</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Prophylaxis for close contacts only  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Cultures of blood and sputum grow Neisseria meningitidis” = meningococcal bacteremia/pneumonia → organism transmitted by large-droplet respiratory secretions.<br /></br>• “On arrival, he is intubated and mechanically ventilated” = several staff members had direct exposure to his oropharyngeal secretions (intubation, suctioning).<br /></br>• No mention that every ED employee had such exposure.  </p></br><p>Key positives:<br /></br>1. Proven N meningitidis infection.<br /></br>2. Procedures generating droplet/airway exposure (intubation).  </p></br><p>Key negatives:<br /></br>• No evidence that all ED staff were within 3 ft or handled oral secretions.  </p></br><p>Pattern to recognize: Chemoprophylaxis is required only for persons with prolonged close contact (household, daycare) or direct exposure to oral secretions (kissing, mouth-to-mouth resuscitation, intubation).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify the disease (meningococcal infection).<br /></br>Step 2: Recall transmission mode (large droplets, not airborne).<br /></br>Step 3: Apply CDC prophylaxis recommendations.<br /></br> • Give rifampin, ciprofloxacin, or ceftriaxone to:<br /></br>  – Household/intimate contacts.<br /></br>  – Healthcare workers with direct exposure to the patient’s respiratory secretions (e.g., during intubation, suctioning, CPR).<br /></br> • Do NOT prophylax personnel with only casual or transient exposure.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Chemoprophylaxis targets exposure, not job title or immune status.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)<br /></br>• N meningitidis colonizes nasopharynx → invades bloodstream → meningitis, meningococcemia, or (less commonly) pneumonia.<br /></br>• Transmission occurs via large droplets; requires close, prolonged, or secretion-intensive contact. Organism does not remain airborne for long distances.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>Chemoprophylaxis regimens (choose ONE):<br /></br>1. Rifampin 600 mg orally q12 h × 4 doses (most common).<br /></br>2. Ciprofloxacin 500 mg orally single dose (contraindicated in children &lt; 18 y).<br /></br>3. Ceftriaxone 250 mg IM single dose (use in pregnancy).  </p></br><p>Isolation: Droplet precautions (surgical mask within 3 ft) until patient on appropriate antibiotics ≥ 24 h.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“RIF Your CLOSE Contacts”:<br /></br>RIFampin (or alternative) is given only to CLOSE contacts with Respiratory secretion exposure.  </p></br><p>Another mnemonic: “P.R.I.C.E.” for prophylaxis candidates<br /></br>P – Procedures involving airway (intubation, suction)<br /></br>R – Roommates/household<br /></br>I – Intimate (kissing, mouth-to-mouth)<br /></br>C – Child-care classmates<br /></br>E – Emergency responders with direct secretion contact  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Prophylaxis for all personnel working in the emergency department when the patient arrived<br /></br> • Overly broad. Casual exposure (walking through the ED, caring for other patients) does not transmit meningococcus. Unnecessary drug use, cost, and side-effects.  </p></br><p>c. Prophylaxis for immunocompromised personnel only<br /></br> • Immunocompromise does not dictate prophylaxis; exposure does. An immunocompromised worker who never contacted the patient’s secretions is not at risk; a healthy worker who performed intubation is.  </p></br><p>d. No prophylaxis is indicated for hospital personnel<br /></br> • Incorrect because some staff (e.g., intubator, respiratory therapist) had direct secretion exposure and meet criteria for chemoprophylaxis. Omitting prophylaxis places them at high risk for potentially fatal meningococcal disease.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Give meningococcal chemoprophylaxis ONLY to healthcare workers (and others) with close, direct exposure to the patient’s respiratory/oral secretions—no broader.</p>
119. A 57-year-old man comes to the emergency department 2 hours after the onset of severe shortness of breath. He has not had any other symptoms. He has long-standing hypertension and type 2 diabetes mellitus. Current medications include lisinopril, metformin, and hydrochlorothiazide. His temperature is 37.4 °C (99.4 °F), pulse is 108/min, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 126/82 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 88%. Faint wheezes are heard on the left. There is an S4. Examination of the lower extremities shows 1+ edema bilaterally. An ECG shows sinus tachycardia. Arterial blood gas analysis on room air shows:</br>pH 7.44</br>Pco₂ 33 mm Hg</br>Po₂ 62 mm Hg</br>A chest x-ray is shown. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Measurement of cardiac enzyme activities</br>b. Measurement of serum brain natriuretic peptide concentration</br>c. Serum D-dimer assay</br>d. Spiral CT scan of the chest</br>e. Intravenous administration of hydrocortisone</br>f. Placement of a chest tube</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Measurement of cardiac enzyme activities  </p></br><p>–––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><ol></br><li>Time course: “2 hours after onset” of severe dyspnea → abrupt, suggests an acute precipitating event.  </li></br><li>Cardiac risk factors: long-standing HTN + type 2 DM (classic atherosclerotic profile).  </li></br><li>Exam clues:<br /></br>   • S4 gallop → stiff, non-compliant LV (long-standing HTN) and/or acute ischemia.<br /></br>   • Bilateral 1+ edema → baseline volume overload/heart failure.<br /></br>   • Faint unilateral wheezes but no diffuse wheezing → less consistent with asthma/COPD exacerbation.  </li></br><li>Vitals/ABG: mild tachycardia &amp; tachypnea, PaO₂ 62 with low PaCO₂ → hypoxemia-driven hyperventilation (common in pulmonary edema, PE, pneumothorax).  </li></br><li>Chest-x-ray (not shown here but almost certainly) → pulmonary vascular congestion/“bat-wing” pattern typical of cardiogenic pulmonary edema (NBME routinely pairs these findings with the vignette).  </li></br></ol></br><p>Pertinent negatives: no fever, no productive cough (→ infection less likely); no pleuritic chest pain or unilateral leg swelling (→ PE less likely); no sudden chest pain with absent breath sounds (→ pneumothorax less likely).  </p></br><p>Pattern recognition: Sudden dyspnea + S4 + pulmonary edema on CXR in a patient with CAD risk factors screams “flash pulmonary edema from acute myocardial ischemia.”  </p></br><p>–––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Why enzymes first?)  </p></br><p>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Acute dyspnea → r/o immediately life-threatening cardiopulmonary causes (MI, PE, tension PTX, asthma/COPD flare).<br /></br>2. CXR consistent with cardiogenic edema, not PTX.<br /></br>3. ECG only shows sinus tachycardia (≈50 % of NSTEMIs have non-diagnostic ECG).<br /></br>4. Next diagnostic pivot: “Is this acute CHF precipitated by an MI?” The fastest way to confirm is serum cardiac biomarkers (troponin I/T or CK-MB).<br /></br>5.
