Identifying MI early changes acute management (dual antiplatelet therapy, anticoagulation, emergent PCI) and directly affects mortality.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In a patient with flash pulmonary edema and strong CAD risk factors, rule out NSTEMI immediately with cardiac enzymes even when the ECG is non-diagnostic.  </p></br><p>–––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>• Acute coronary plaque rupture → ischemia of LV → abrupt loss of contractility → rapid elevation in LV end-diastolic pressure → fluid backs into pulmonary circulation (“flash” edema).<br /></br>• HTN-induced LVH → predisposes to diastolic dysfunction; an S4 often heralds acute ischemia in this setting.  </p></br><p>–––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls  </p></br><p>If enzymes return positive → treat as NSTEMI + acute decompensated HF:<br /></br>1. Oxygen, nitrates (↓ preload/afterload), loop diuretics (furosemide), ± non-invasive ventilation.<br /></br>2. Dual antiplatelet therapy (aspirin + P2Y12 inhibitor), heparin, high-dose statin, beta-blocker if no pulmonary edema contraindication.<br /></br>3. Early PCI within 24 h (sooner for STEMI).  </p></br><p>–––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“Sudden SOB with an S4? Check the troponin before anything more.”<br /></br>Think “FLASH” for flash pulmonary edema:<br /></br>F – Fast onset<br /></br>L – LV failure (s4)<br /></br>A – Atherosclerotic risk factors<br /></br>S – Saturation drops<br /></br>H – Heart enzymes STAT  </p></br><p>–––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>b. BNP level – Helpful to distinguish CHF vs COPD/PE in ambiguous cases, but the vignette (CXR + S4 + edema) already points to CHF. BNP would not identify the precipitating MI, and treatment would be delayed.  </p></br><p>c. Serum D-dimer – Used when PE probability is low/intermediate with normal CXR; here the CXR shows congestion and the patient has overt CHF findings.  </p></br><p>d. Spiral CT chest – Definitive for PE but unnecessary when clinical/imaging picture favors cardiogenic edema; involves contrast/radiation and delays critical MI therapy.  </p></br><p>e. IV hydrocortisone – Steroids for severe asthma/COPD or anaphylaxis; minimal wheezes + CXR congestion make bronchospasm unlikely.  </p></br><p>f. Chest tube – Indicated for pneumothorax/hemothorax; CXR would show collapsed lung &amp; absent breath sounds, neither described.  </p></br><p>–––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Bottom Line:<br /></br>A 57-year-old with sudden dyspnea, pulmonary edema, and CAD risk factors should be worked up for an acute MI first. Obtain cardiac enzyme levels immediately.</p>
120. An 82-year-old man is brought to the emergency department because of a 2-day history of progressive abdominal distention and lethargy. His family reports that he has not been awake or fully alert for 4 hours. The patient has hyperlipidemia, hypertension, and benign prostatic hyperplasia. His medications are atorvastatin, metoprolol, finasteride, and a daily aspirin. His temperature is 36.4°C (97.5°F), pulse is 90/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 80/55 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on 100% oxygen by nonrebreather face mask shows an oxygen saturation of 99%. The patient opens his eyes and moves his extremities in response to painful stimuli. Abdominal examination shows distention over the lower quadrants, dullness to percussion to the level of the umbilicus, and an enlarged multinodular prostate. Bladder catheterization yields 1700 mL of turbid urine. Piperacillin-tazobactam, large-volume crystalloid, and norepinephrine therapy are begun. Two hours later, the patient produces 75 mL/h of clear urine but remains lethargic. His blood pressure is 90/65 mm Hg. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin 12 g/dL</br>Hematocrit 36%</br>Leukocyte count 22,000/mm³</br>Serum</br>Na⁺ 125 mEq/L</br>K⁺ 5 mEq/L</br>Cl⁻ 93 mEq/L</br>HCO₃⁻ 22 mEq/L</br>Urea nitrogen 35 mg/dL</br>Glucose 75 mg/dL</br>Creatinine 1.5 mg/dL</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management of this patient’s persistent hypotension?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Bladder irrigation with amphotericin B solution</br>b. Immediate hemodialysis</br>c. Intravenous administration of fluconazole</br>d. Intravenous administration of hydrocortisone</br>e. Intravenous administration of metronidazole</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Intravenous administration of hydrocortisone  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis (Pertinent Clues)<br /></br>• Elderly man with obstructive uropathy → urinary retention &amp; UTI → sepsis source identified.<br /></br>• Initial resuscitation done correctly:<br /></br>  – Large-volume IV crystalloids<br /></br>  – Broad-spectrum antibiotics (piperacillin-tazobactam)<br /></br>  – Vasopressor (norepinephrine) started.<br /></br>• Despite these measures BP remains 90/65 mm Hg (MAP ≈ 73) and he is still lethargic → “vasopressor-dependent septic shock.”<br /></br>• No other imminent life-threat (K⁺ only 5 mEq/L, Cr 1.5 mg/dL, oxygenation normal).  </p></br><p>The question asks for the next step to treat persistent hypotension after appropriate fluid resuscitation and vasopressor therapy.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify type of shock → septic (infection + leukocytosis + hypotension + improved oxygen saturation).<br /></br>2. Initial bundle already completed (fluids, antibiotics, norepinephrine).<br /></br>3. Guideline-directed next option for pressor-refractory septic shock = stress-dose glucocorticoids (e.g., IV hydrocortisone 50 mg q6 h).<br /></br>   • Mechanism: Corrects relative adrenal insufficiency that accompanies prolonged severe sepsis and improves vascular responsiveness to catecholamines.<br /></br>4. None of the other choices address the pathophysiology of persistent vasodilatory shock.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In septic shock that remains hypotensive after adequate fluid resuscitation and vasopressor therapy, administer IV stress-dose steroids (hydrocortisone).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Brief Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Sepsis → systemic inflammatory response → nitric-oxide-mediated vasodilation, capillary leak, and relative adrenal insufficiency.<br /></br>• Endogenous cortisol blunts NO production and maintains vasomotor tone; deficiency or increased requirement makes vessels unresponsive to catecholamines.<br /></br>• Hydrocortisone replaces cortisol, up-regulates α-adrenergic receptors, and helps restore BP.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls (Septic Shock)<br /></br>1. Early goal-directed therapy:<br /></br>   a. Fluids (30 mL/kg isotonic crystalloid).<br /></br>   b. Broad-spectrum antibiotics within 1 h.<br /></br>   c. Vasopressors to maintain MAP ≥ 65 mm Hg (norepinephrine 1st line).<br /></br>2. If MAP still &lt; 65 on adequate pressor → give IV hydrocortisone (50 mg q6 h).<br /></br>3. Rule out/ correct other causes of hypotension (bleeding, cardiac pump failure, severe acidosis).<br /></br>4. Additional measures: source control, glucose &lt; 180, DVT &amp; stress-ulcer prophylaxis.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>High-Yield Memory Aid<br /></br>“PRESSORS are NOT enough? Think STEROIDS.”<br /></br>P = Persistent hypotension<br /></br>R = Resuscitated (fluids)<br /></br>E = Empiric antibiotics given<br /></br>S = Source identified/controlled<br /></br>S = Support (vasopressor) already initiated →<br /></br>O = “Open the hydrocortisone vial”<br /></br>R = Relative adrenal insufficiency<br /></br>S = Stabilize vascular tone  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Bladder irrigation with amphotericin B solution<br /></br>   • Used for candiduria with obstructing fungus balls. Does not raise systemic BP and is unnecessary without documented Candida infection.  </p></br><p>b. Immediate hemodialysis<br /></br>   • Indicated for refractory hyperkalemia, severe acidosis, volume overload, or uremic complications. Cr 1.5 and K⁺ 5 do not meet criteria; dialysis will not correct septic vasodilation.  </p></br><p>c. Intravenous fluconazole<br /></br>   • Antifungal for candidemia; no evidence of fungal infection, blood pressure issue unrelated.  </p></br><p>e. Intravenous metronidazole<br /></br>   • Anaerobic coverage already provided by piperacillin-tazobactam; again, will not treat vasopressor-refractory hypotension.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line: After fluids, antibiotics, and norepinephrine, persistent hypotension in septic shock warrants IV stress-dose hydrocortisone to overcome relative adrenal insufficiency and improve vascular responsiveness.</p>
121. A 22-year-old male college student comes to the clinic because of a 3-month history of fatigue, persistent cough, chronic malaise, occasional night sweats, and a 7-kg (15-lb weight loss despite no change in appetite. He says he has felt stressed at school and that his grades have been declining. He had infectious mononucleosis 5 years ago. He takes no medications. He appears tired. He is 178 cm (5 ft 10 in) tall and weighs 67 kg, or 148lb; BMI is 21 kg/m². Pulse is 98/min and regular, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 110/60 mm Hg. Cardiopulmonary examination discloses wheezing at both bases and distant heart sounds. The abdomen is soft with no tenderness; liver edge is palpated 2 cm below the right costal margin. Large, matted lymph nodes are palpated bilaterally in the supraclavicular area, axilla, and groin. Examination of a biopsy specimen of a superficial inguinal lymph node confirms the presence of Reed–Sternberg cells. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in evaluation?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Bronchoscopy</br>b. Fine-needle aspiration of additional lymph-node sites</br>c. Laparoscopy</br>d. PET scan</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. 18-fluoro-deoxy-glucose positron-emission tomography (FDG-PET) scan  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Pertinent positives<br /></br>  – 3-month history of fatigue, night sweats, weight loss → “B symptoms” typical of lymphoma.<br /></br>  – Generalized, large, matted lymphadenopathy (supraclavicular, axillary, inguinal).<br /></br>  – Excisional lymph-node biopsy → Reed–Sternberg cells → Hodgkin lymphoma already proven.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives<br /></br>  – No focal pulmonary findings to suggest a primary lung lesion (makes bronchoscopy unnecessary).<br /></br>  – No localized abdominal pain or masses to suggest a surgical abdominal exploration is needed.  </p></br><p>Key phrase: “Examination of a biopsy specimen … confirms the presence of Reed–Sternberg cells.”<br /></br>=&gt; Diagnosis has been made. The logical next step is STAGING, not more tissue sampling. Modern Hodgkin staging uses whole-body PET/CT.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (stepwise)<br /></br>1. Establish diagnosis → done with excisional node biopsy.<br /></br>2. Stage disease (Ann Arbor system) → need to know extent of nodal/extranodal involvement to select chemotherapy ± radiotherapy protocol.<br /></br>3. PET/CT is the current gold standard for initial staging and for response assessment in Hodgkin lymphoma.  </p></br><p>Take-home: After biopsy-proven Hodgkin lymphoma, perform PET/CT (± bone-marrow biopsy if PET shows stage III/IV disease) before initiating therapy.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high yield)<br /></br>• Hodgkin lymphoma: malignant transformation of B cells; characteristic Reed–Sternberg cells (CD15+, CD30+).<br /></br>• B symptoms (fever, night sweats, weight loss) signal systemic cytokine release and affect staging (suffix “B”).<br /></br>• Spread is contiguous from one nodal group to the next, which is why accurate anatomical mapping with PET/CT is critical.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Snapshot<br /></br>• Staging work-up<br /></br>  – FDG-PET/contrast CT of neck, chest, abdomen, pelvis (single study PET/CT).<br /></br>  – Bone-marrow biopsy only if PET/CT or CBC suggests marrow involvement.<br /></br>  – CBC, chemistries, ESR, HIV, hepatitis-B/C serologies, echocardiogram &amp; PFTs to prepare for chemo (doxorubicin, bleomycin, vinblastine, dacarbazine = ABVD).<br /></br>• Treatment (stage-dependent)<br /></br>  – Early stage (I–IIA): 2–4 cycles ABVD ± involved-site radiation.<br /></br>  – Advanced (IIB–IV): 6 cycles ABVD or escalated BEACOPP.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Biopsy B4 PET” (read: Biopsy before PET):<br /></br>1. Biopsy – confirms Hodgkin (Reed–Sternberg).<br /></br>2. PET – maps where it’s set (stages the spread).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Bronchoscopy<br /></br>   • Indicated if staging CT/PET shows a pulmonary mass or to evaluate hemoptysis. No such findings here. Adds nothing to Hodgkin staging.  </p></br><p>b. Fine-needle aspiration (FNA) of additional nodes<br /></br>   • FNA is inadequate for lymphoma diagnosis because it does not preserve architecture. Diagnosis already obtained; additional FNA will not change staging or management.  </p></br><p>c. Laparoscopy (or historical staging laparotomy with splenectomy)<br /></br>   • Obsolete. Modern imaging (PET/CT) has replaced invasive surgical staging. No abdominal masses requiring surgical biopsy are described.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Concept<br /></br>Once Hodgkin lymphoma is confirmed by excisional biopsy, the immediate next step is radiologic staging with FDG-PET/CT to guide therapy.</p>
122. A 67-year-old man comes to the emergency department 20 minutes after the sudden onset of substernal chest pain. He has not had shortness of breath. He has hypertension and hyperlipidemia. His medications are enalapril and simvastatin. On arrival, he appears pale and anxious. His pulse is 104/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 88/64 mm Hg. Central venous pressure is estimated to be 6 cm H₂O (N = 5–8). The point of maximal impulse is laterally displaced. The lungs are clear to auscultation. With the patient in the supine position, a grade 2/6 holosystolic murmur is heard best at the apex and radiates to the axilla. S₁ and S₂ are normal. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Serum studies show an increased troponin T concentration. An ECG shows ST-segment elevation in leads II, III, and aVF and in leads V₄R to V₆R. Which of the following is the most appropriate initial step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Intravenous administration of dobutamine</br>b. Intravenous administration of furosemide</br>c. Intravenous administration of propranolol</br>d. Intravenous administration of 0.9% saline</br>e. Pulmonary artery catheterization</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Intravenous administration of dobutamine</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis – How to read the stem like an NBME grader  </p></br><ol></br><li>Acute inferior-wall MI<br /></br>   • “ST-segment elevation in II, III, aVF” = right coronary artery territory.  </li></br><li>Concomitant right-sided lead ST elevation (V₄R-V₆R) = RCA infarct large enough to involve the posteromedial papillary muscle.  </li></br><li>New systolic murmur: grade 2/6 holosystolic at apex → classic for acute mitral regurgitation (MR).  </li></br><li>Laterally displaced PMI → LV is already volume-overloaded from severe MR.  </li></br><li>Hypotension (88/64) with a <strong>normal/low</strong> CVP (6 cm H₂O) → cardiogenic shock driven by loss of forward stroke volume, <strong>not</strong> by volume depletion or RV failure (which would show a high CVP / distended neck veins).  </li></br><li>Clear lungs → very early in the course; pulmonary edema has not yet developed.</li></br></ol></br><p>Key pattern: inferior MI + new apical holosystolic murmur + shock with normal CVP = papillary-muscle ischemia → acute severe MR → cardiogenic shock.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning – Why dobutamine first?</p></br><p>• Acute MR causes blood to gush back into the LA during systole → precipitous drop in forward cardiac output → hypotension.<br /></br>• Dobutamine (β1 &gt; β2 agonist) rapidly:</br>  1. Increases LV contractility → raises forward stroke volume.<br /></br>  2. Provides mild systemic vasodilation (β2) → lowers afterload, so a greater proportion of the LV output goes forward rather than regurgitating.<br /></br>• It is the fastest pharmacologic way to stabilize hemodynamics while the patient is prepared for definitive therapy (urgent surgical repair/revascularization ± intra-aortic balloon pump).</p></br><p>Take-home: In cardiogenic shock from acute MR after an inferior MI, give an inotrope (dobutamine) first.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology High-Yield Review</p></br><p>• RCA → supplies posteromedial papillary muscle.<br /></br>• Ischemia/rupture → abrupt loss of papillary support → flail posterior mitral leaflet.<br /></br>• Acute MR → sudden ↑ LV preload, ↓ forward SV, normal LA compliance → dramatic fall in systemic BP and rapid pulmonary congestion (often minutes-hours).<br /></br>• Without prompt inotropic support and surgery, mortality is high.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls</p></br><p>Immediate (bridge)<br /></br>• Dobutamine infusion ± norepinephrine if needed for MAP &gt; 65 mm Hg.<br /></br>• Consider vasodilator (nitroprusside) only after BP improves.<br /></br>• Intra-aortic balloon pump often added after dobutamine.</p></br><p>Definitive<br /></br>• Emergent surgical mitral valve repair/replacement + coronary artery bypass of the RCA lesion.</p></br><p>Work-up<br /></br>• TTE or TEE to confirm flail leaflet &amp; quantify MR (done once patient is stabilized).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memory Aids</p></br><p>“DObutamine for Deadly mitral regurg after an inferrior DOwn-right MI.”<br /></br>Inferior + apical holosystolic murmur + shock? → Think “papillary pops, give dobutamine.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</p></br><p>b. Intravenous furosemide<br /></br>   • Diuretic ↓ preload → drops LV filling even more → worsens hypotension; indicated only when pulmonary edema is dominant.  </p></br><p>c. Intravenous propranolol<br /></br>   • Negative inotrope; would further ↓ cardiac output and may precipitate cardiovascular collapse in acute MR.  </p></br><p>d. Intravenous 0.9% saline<br /></br>   • Helpful for right-ventricular infarction with elevated JVP, but here CVP is normal/low and the primary problem is regurgitant loss of forward flow, not under-filling. Extra volume merely increases the regurgitant fraction and risk of pulmonary edema.  </p></br><p>e. Pulmonary artery catheterization<br /></br>   • Diagnostic, not therapeutic. The patient is already in shock; stabilize first with an inotrope, then insert monitoring lines if needed.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</p></br><p>Inferior MI + new MR murmur + hypotension = papillary-muscle ischemia → acute severe MR → cardiogenic shock.<br /></br>Immediate hemodynamic stabilization requires an IV inotrope—choose dobutamine.</p>
123. A 22-year-old woman, gravida 2, para 0, aborta 1, at 9 weeks’ gestation comes to the physician because of mild vaginal bleeding for 2 days. Her first pregnancy ended in spontaneous abortion at 6 weeks’ gestation. She received Rh₀(D) immune globulin at that time. Three years ago, she underwent multiple operative repairs of injuries sustained in a motor vehicle collision and received transfusion of packed red blood cells perioperatively. She has no other history of serious illness. Her only medication is a prenatal vitamin. Examination shows a uterus consistent in size with a 9-week gestation. There is scant blood coming from the closed cervical os. Transvaginal ultrasonography shows a viable singleton intrauterine pregnancy. Routine prenatal laboratory studies are performed and Rh₀(D) immune globulin is administered. Her blood group is B, Rh-negative. She is positive for the Kell antibody with a titer of 1:16. Which of the following is the strongest predisposing risk factor for isoimmunization to the Kell erythrocyte antibody in this patient?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. History of blood transfusion</br>b. History of spontaneous abortion</br>c. Maternal ABO blood group</br>d. Previous administration of Rh₀(D) immune globulin</br>e. Threatened abortion</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. History of blood transfusion  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Pertinent positives: 22-yo Rh-negative woman; prior MVC with “multiple operative repairs” and “transfusion of packed red blood cells.” Now has anti-Kell (K) IgG titer 1 : 16.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: Only one prior (very early) pregnancy, for which she received appropriate Rh₀(D) immune globulin; fetus in current pregnancy is viable, cervix closed (threatened abortion only).<br /></br>• Key clue: “Received transfusion of packed red blood cells.” Adult donor RBCs frequently express non-D antigens (Kell, Kidd, Duffy, etc.). Unlike the Rho(D) antigen, there is no prophylactic immune-globulin for these antigens.  </p></br><p>Pattern: Maternal alloimmunization occurs when foreign RBC antigens enter maternal circulation → IgG antibodies form → antibodies cross placenta in subsequent pregnancies. Common exposures = 1) transfusion, 2) fetomaternal hemorrhage.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Identify the antibody: anti-Kell.<br /></br>2. Ask: how could a woman encounter Kell+ RBCs?<br /></br>   • Fetal blood (previous pregnancy) – possible but fetus may or may not have been K+.<br /></br>   • Packed RBC transfusion – guaranteed exposure to some K+ cells because ~9% of donors are Kell-positive.<br /></br>3. Pick the option that most reliably provides that exposure: history of blood transfusion.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Non-D RBC alloimmunization in pregnancy is most often caused by prior transfusion of antigen-positive donor blood.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Kell antigen (K) is a highly immunogenic RBC surface protein.<br /></br>• When Kell-negative individuals receive Kell-positive blood (transfusion or fetomaternal hemorrhage), they can form anti-K IgG.<br /></br>• Anti-K IgG crosses the placenta → attaches to fetal RBC precursors in the bone marrow, causing severe anemia via suppression of erythropoiesis (hydrops fetalis).<br /></br>• No prophylactic immune globulin exists for Kell; prevention = avoid unnecessary transfusions, use antigen-matched (K-negative) blood in women of child-bearing potential.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• Once alloimmunization is detected, follow serial maternal titers and monitor fetal MCA peak systolic velocity by Doppler.<br /></br>• If evidence of moderate-to-severe anemia → intrauterine transfusion of K-negative, CMV-negative, irradiated packed RBCs.<br /></br>• Delivery planning based on fetal status; RhIG has no role in Kell disease.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Kell Gets You Killed” – Kell antibodies suppress the K-id (kid) inside the marrow.<br /></br>“Transfusion = Antigen Confusion” – Any woman who has ever been transfused can make antibodies to ANY blood-group antigen.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. History of spontaneous abortion<br /></br>• Early loss at 6 wks involves minimal fetomaternal hemorrhage; fetus may not have expressed Kell (erythropoiesis just starting). Far less likely than transfusion to trigger alloimmunization.  </p></br><p>c. Maternal ABO blood group<br /></br>• ABO type does not influence formation of Kell antibodies.  </p></br><p>d. Previous administration of Rh₀(D) immune globulin<br /></br>• RhIG contains only anti-D IgG. It prevents—rather than causes—alloimmunization and has no Kell antigen.  </p></br><p>e. Threatened abortion<br /></br>• Current spotting alone does not create antibodies; significant hemorrhage would be required, and we do not know fetal Kell status.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Prior exposure to Kell-positive donor RBCs during her peri-operative transfusion is the most powerful and reliable risk factor for this patient’s anti-Kell alloimmunization.</p>
124. A 92-year-old man is admitted to the hospital after a neighbor found him lethargic in his home. The neighbor reports that the patient has no living relatives, so she visits him regularly. On admission, he is agitated, belligerent, and confused and says that he sees angels and demons. His temperature is 37°C (98.6°F), pulse is 114/min, respirations are 32/min, and blood pressure is 140/90 mm Hg. On pulmonary examination, crackles are heard over the lung bases. Serum studies show a urea nitrogen concentration of 20 mg/dL and creatinine concentration of 1.3 mg/dL. Treatment with antibiotic therapy is begun for pneumonia and a urinary tract infection. Four hours later, he pulls out his nasogastric tube and intravenous and urinary catheters. Three days later, mental status examination shows no evidence of visual hallucinations. He recalls zero of three items after 5 minutes. The most appropriate next step in discharge planning is to contact which of the following within the hospital?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Bioethics department to discuss futile medical treatment</br>b. Palliative care services to discuss end-of-life planning</br>c. Psychiatry department to address an advance directive</br>d. Social services to arrange placement in a supervised living facility</br>e. Volunteer office for canine therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>d. Social services to arrange placement in a supervised living facility</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• “92-year-old man…no living relatives…found lethargic”: extremely elderly, lives alone, limited social support.<br /></br>• “Agitated, belligerent, sees angels and demons” with fever-range vitals and infection → classic hypo-/hyperactive delirium precipitated by pneumonia &amp; UTI.<br /></br>• “Three days later…no evidence of visual hallucinations” = delirium has cleared.<br /></br>• “Recalls 0/3 items after 5 min” → persistent short-term memory deficit consistent with underlying major neurocognitive disorder (dementia).<br /></br>• No family to supervise, pulled out lines earlier (impulsive/disinhibited) → not safe to return home unattended.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1: Treat precipitating medical issues (done).<br /></br>Step 2: Reassess cognition once delirium clears. Persistent amnesia = dementia → long-term safety planning.<br /></br>Step 3: Determine who in the hospital coordinates post-acute placement, home health, custodial services = Social work. They arrange skilled nursing facility (SNF), memory-care unit, conservatorship, insurance coverage, etc.  </p></br><p>Key takeaway: After medical stabilization of an elderly patient with delirium, always evaluate baseline cognition and living situation. If deficits persist and no safe environment exists, involve social services for supervised placement.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>Delirium = acute fluctuating consciousness disturbance, reversible once etiology treated. Dementia = chronic, progressive cognitive decline (memory + ≥1 other domain) interfering with independence. Elderly with dementia living alone are high-risk for falls, medication errors, malnutrition, and recurrent delirium.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review</br>• Delirium: treat underlying cause (antibiotics, fluids, minimize restraints/catheters, orienting strategies).<br /></br>• Dementia: cognitive testing (MMSE/MoCA), basic labs (TSH, B12), imaging if focal deficits; pharmacologic (donepezil, rivastigmine, memantine), but primary need = safe environment.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“Delirium Dries, Dementia Stays”: If confusion dries up but memory loss stays, think chronic cognitive decline → arrange safety &amp; support.<br /></br>“SAFE discharge = Social worker.”</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Bioethics for futile care – Patient is improving; treatment not futile.<br /></br>b. Palliative care – Appropriate for life-limiting illness with symptom burden, but primary issue here is disposition, not goals-of-care or end-of-life management.<br /></br>c. Psychiatry to address an advance directive – Useful if behavioral issues or capacity evaluation needed, but placement remains immediate priority; social work still required.<br /></br>e. Volunteer canine therapy – Helpful for mood, not for disposition or safety needs.</p></br><p>Bottom line: Cognitively impaired elders without support must not return home alone; hospital social services coordinate supervised living arrangements.</p>
125. A 67-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 1-week history of episodes of vision loss in her left eye. She has had three episodes during this time; each episode lasted 5 minutes and resolved spontaneously. She has a 10-year history of hypertension, a 2-year history of worsening arthritis, and mitral valve prolapse. Her medications are lisinopril and ibuprofen. Her blood pressure is 140/85 mm Hg. A photograph of the left fundus is shown. On cardiac examination, a midsystolic click and a grade 2/6, late systolic murmur are heard best over the apex. The remainder of the examination, including neurologic examination, shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Carotid duplex ultrasonography</br>b. Cerebral MR angiography</br>c. Echocardiography</br>d. Measurement of intraocular pressure</br>f. MRI of the brain</br>e. Serum antinuclear antibody assay</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>a. Carotid duplex ultrasonography  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• “67-year-old woman” – atherosclerotic vascular disease is common.<br /></br>• “Episodes of vision loss in her left eye … each lasted 5 min and resolved spontaneously” – classic description of amaurosis fugax, a transient ischemic attack (TIA) of the retina.<br /></br>• Fundus photograph (not shown here for you) in the NBME exam usually demonstrates a bright, refractile, yellow-orange plaque at a retinal arterial bifurcation → Hollenhorst plaque = cholesterol embolus.<br /></br>• No focal neurologic deficits remain after the events → confirms transient ischemia rather than completed stroke.<br /></br>• Mitral valve prolapse murmur is noted but is generally not a high-risk cardioembolic source compared with carotid atherosclerosis.  </p></br><p>Key pattern: transient monocular blindness + Hollenhorst plaque ⇒ emboli from ipsilateral internal carotid artery atherosclerotic plaque.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-wise approach:</br>1. Identify the event: retinal TIA (amaurosis fugax).<br /></br>2. Localize the embolic source: &gt;70 % originate from the ipsilateral carotid bifurcation.<br /></br>3. First diagnostic test for carotid disease = non-invasive carotid duplex ultrasound (high sensitivity/specificity, no contrast, cheap, bedside).<br /></br>   • Result determines need for further imaging (CTA/MRA) or carotid endarterectomy/stenting.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Amaurosis fugax is managed like any TIA; look for a carotid plaque first.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)</br>• Atherosclerotic plaque develops at the carotid bifurcation.<br /></br>• Small cholesterol crystals (Hollenhorst plaques) shower into the retinal circulation via the ophthalmic artery.<br /></br>• Transient downstream occlusion → sudden curtain-like loss of vision that clears when embolus moves/dissolves.  </p></br><p>Risk factors: age, hypertension, hyperlipidemia, smoking, diabetes.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Pearls</br>Initial work-up:</br>1. Carotid duplex ultrasound – quantify % stenosis.<br /></br>2. If stenosis ≥70 % and surgical candidate → carotid endarterectomy (CEA) within 2 weeks.<br /></br>3. All patients: antiplatelet therapy (aspirin), statin, blood-pressure optimization, lifestyle changes.</p></br><p>Additional tests only if indicated:</br>• CTA/MRA head &amp; neck – pre-operative planning or if duplex equivocal.<br /></br>• Echocardiography – consider if carotid study normal or if history of atrial fibrillation, thrombus, mechanical valve, etc.<br /></br>• ESR/CRP for giant-cell arteritis if continuous visual loss, headache, jaw claudication (not the case here).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“AFUGAX” mnemonic for Amaurosis FUGAX work-up:<br /></br>A – Age/atherosclerosis patient<br /></br>F – Fundus = Hollenhorst plaque<br /></br>U – Ultrasound (carotid duplex) first<br /></br>G – Get risk factors controlled (HTN, lipids)<br /></br>A – Aspirin<br /></br>X – (X-cision) i.e., carotid endarterectomy if ≥70 %</p></br><p>Picture a glowing “Hollen-HORSE” galloping out of a carotid artery and briefly blocking a retinal vessel – helps you recall cholesterol embolus from carotid source.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>b. Cerebral MR angiography – Good for intracranial vasculature; more costly and not first-line. Ordered after duplex or when duplex is inconclusive.<br /></br>c. Echocardiography – Evaluates cardioembolic sources (e.g., atrial thrombosis, vegetations). MVP without regurgitation rarely sheds emboli; retinal plaque points to carotid, not heart.<br /></br>d. Measurement of intraocular pressure – Screens for glaucoma. Glaucoma causes chronic, peripheral vision loss, not transient complete monocular blindness.<br /></br>e. Serum antinuclear antibody assay – Evaluates autoimmune disease (e.g., lupus). No systemic findings; retinal cholesterol plaque is diagnostic.<br /></br>f. MRI of the brain – Would show acute infarct, but patient has no residual neurologic deficit. Does not identify the embolic source.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>Transient monocular blindness + Hollenhorst plaque = carotid embolus. First diagnostic step is carotid duplex ultrasonography to quantify stenosis and guide revascularization decisions.</p>
126. A 47-year-old man comes to the clinic because of a 3-month history of worsening dull pain “in my stomach” and generalized itching. He has had a decreased appetite resulting in an 11.3-kg (25-lb weight loss; eating does not affect the pain. He has not had fever, chills, vomiting, or diarrhea. His last visit to a physician was more than 30 years ago. He takes no medications. He has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 30 years. He does not drink alcohol or use illicit drugs. He is 178 cm (5 ft 10 in) tall and weighs 70 kg, or 154lb; BMI is 22 kg/m². Vital signs are within normal limits. Abdominal examination shows fullness and mild tenderness to palpation over the epigastric region. Rectal examination shows no abnormalities; test of the stool for occult blood is negative.</br>Serum studies show:</br>Total bilirubin 6 mg/dL</br>Alkaline phosphatase 140 U/L</br>AST 160 U/L</br>ALT 175 U/L</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Bone scan</br>b. CT scan of the abdomen with contrast</br>c. Flat and upright abdominal x-rays</br>d. HIDA scan</br>e. Ultrasonography of the abdominal right upper quadrant</br></br></br><p>Correct answer selection<br /></br>b. Contrast-enhanced CT scan of the abdomen  </p></br><p>Vignette analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – 3-month history, progressive, dull epigastric pain → suggests chronic intra-abdominal process rather than acute biliary colic<br /></br>  – 11-kg (25-lb) unintentional weight loss and anorexia → “B symptoms” that strongly suggest malignancy<br /></br>  – Generalized pruritus + total bilirubin 6 mg/dL → cholestasis/obstructive jaundice from impaired bile flow<br /></br>  – Smoking 30 pack-years → major risk factor for pancreatic adenocarcinoma<br /></br>  – Exam: epigastric fullness, no RUQ guarding, negative stool guaiac → points away from acute cholecystitis, peptic ulcer disease, or GI bleed  </p></br><p>Clinical critical reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. The combination of painless (or minimally painful) jaundice + weight loss in a middle-aged smoker is the classic pattern for a malignant obstruction at the head of the pancreas.<br /></br>2. First diagnostic objective: visualize the pancreas, define the level of biliary obstruction, and stage a potential mass.<br /></br>3. Best initial imaging modality for suspected pancreatic cancer is a multidetector contrast-enhanced CT scan of the abdomen (pancreatic protocol). It identifies &gt;90 % of pancreatic masses, detects vascular invasion, and evaluates for hepatic &amp; nodal metastases—information that simultaneously guides staging and management.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway<br /></br>Middle-aged patient with painless jaundice, weight loss, and epigastric discomfort → think pancreatic head cancer. The correct first test is a pancreatic-protocol CT scan.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Pancreatic adenocarcinoma arises from ductal epithelium; &gt;60 % occur in the head.<br /></br>• Tumor at the head obstructs the distal common bile duct → conjugated hyperbilirubinemia, pruritus, elevated alkaline phosphatase.<br /></br>• Risk factors: smoking, chronic pancreatitis, hereditary pancreatitis, Peutz-Jeghers, BRCA2.<br /></br>• Presentation: painless jaundice, weight loss, new-onset diabetes, migratory thrombophlebitis (Trousseau sign).  </p></br><p>Diagnostics &amp; treatment overview<br /></br>1. Imaging:<br /></br>   – Pancreatic-protocol CT → first.<br /></br>   – If CT is equivocal or ducts are dilated without a mass, do endoscopic ultrasound (EUS) ± FNA.<br /></br>2. Staging/management:<br /></br>   – Resectable (no major vessel invasion, no distant mets) → Whipple (pancreaticoduodenectomy).<br /></br>   – Borderline/unresectable → neoadjuvant chemo ± radiation.<br /></br>   – Metastatic → palliative chemo (FOLFIRINOX, gemcitabine/nab-paclitaxel) and biliary stent for symptom control.  </p></br><p>Mnemonic<br /></br>“Painless Jaundice in a Pack-year smoker?—CT Pancreas STAT.”  </p></br><p>Incorrect answer analysis<br /></br>a. Bone scan<br /></br>   • Used for detecting osseous metastases in prostate, breast, or lung CA. Nothing in the vignette suggests skeletal pain or elevated bone-specific ALP.  </p></br><p>c. Flat &amp; upright abdominal X-rays<br /></br>   • Detect bowel obstruction or perforation (air-fluid levels, free air). Not useful for evaluating biliary obstruction or pancreatic mass.  </p></br><p>d. HIDA scan (hepatobiliary iminodiacetic acid)<br /></br>   • Assesses cystic duct patency; ordered when acute cholecystitis is suspected after nondiagnostic RUQ US. This patient lacks acute RUQ pain, fever, leukocytosis.  </p></br><p>e. Ultrasonography of the RUQ<br /></br>   • First test for suspected gallstones or biliary colic. Sensitivity for pancreatic head tumors is low, and it cannot stage disease. In a patient whose presentation screams malignancy rather than cholelithiasis, CT is preferred because it evaluates the pancreas, assesses resectability, and looks for metastases in a single study.  </p></br><p>Common pitfall<br /></br>Choosing RUQ ultrasound reflexively for any jaundice. On exams, the presence of painless jaundice + systemic cancer “B” symptoms shifts the priority to cross-sectional imaging (CT) to look for a pancreatic or periampullary malignancy.</p>
127. A 72-year-old woman is brought to the emergency department 30 minutes after her son found her confused in her apartment. She lives alone. The outside temperature is 105.8°F, and she does not have air conditioning. En route, she was combative and oriented to person but not to place or time. On arrival, she is unresponsive and diaphoretic. Her temperature is 40.4°C (104.7°F), pulse is 90/min, respirations are 28/min, and blood pressure is 90/60 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on 10 L/min of oxygen by nasal cannula shows an oxygen saturation of 98%. Examination shows decorticate posturing. The skin is warm. The pupils are equal and reactive. No signs of injury are noted. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. CT scan of the head</br>b. Evaporative cooling</br>c. Intravenous administration of norepinephrine</br>d. Intravenous infusion of dantrolene</br>e. Lumbar puncture</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Evaporative cooling  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Environment: 105.8 °F day, apartment without air-conditioning → high external heat load.<br /></br>• Core temperature: 40.4 °C (104.7 °F) → severe hyperthermia.<br /></br>• CNS dysfunction: agitation → confusion → unresponsive with decorticate posturing = end-organ (brain) damage.<br /></br>• Skin: warm and still sweaty (“diaphoretic”) – classic for non-exertional (classic) heat stroke in the elderly.<br /></br>• Hypotension (90/60) and tachypnea common in heat stroke due to peripheral vasodilation and systemic inflammatory response.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: No trauma, equal reactive pupils, no drug history suggesting malignant hyperthermia or NMS, making primary heat stroke most likely.</p></br><p>Pattern: Elderly + hot environment + &gt;40 °C + altered mental status = “classic” (non-exertional) heat stroke. First, drop the temperature as fast as possible.</p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach<br /></br>1. Recognize heat stroke (T &gt; 40 °C + CNS dysfunction).<br /></br>2. Heat stroke is an immediately life-threatening emergency; mortality rises with every minute the temp remains elevated.<br /></br>3. Therefore, the single most important, time-critical intervention is RAPID ACTIVE COOLING.<br /></br>4. Evaporative cooling (remove clothing, spray lukewarm water, large fans) is the quickest, most widely available method in the ED and is guideline-recommended as first-line in classic heat stroke.  </p></br><p>Core Take-away: In any patient with heat stroke, ABCs are addressed simultaneously, but active cooling is the definitive, mortality-saving treatment and must be started immediately—before imaging or labs.</p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• External heat + impaired thermoregulation in the elderly → failure of heat dissipation.<br /></br>• Core temps &gt;40 °C cause enzymatic dysfunction, cytotoxicity, systemic inflammation → multiorgan failure (brain, kidneys, liver, coagulation).<br /></br>• Sweating may cease late, but presence or absence of sweat does NOT rule heat stroke in or out.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line (do NOW):<br /></br>1. Evaporative or ice-water immersion cooling (goal &lt;39 °C within 30 min).<br /></br>2. Large-bore IV access, aggressive isotonic crystalloid.<br /></br>3. Airway protection if needed, continuous temp monitoring.  </p></br><p>Second-line / adjunct:<br /></br>• Benzodiazepines for shivering.<br /></br>• If hypotension persists after fluids → vasopressor (e.g., norepinephrine).<br /></br>• Labs: CMP, CK, coagulation, ABG, urinalysis for rhabdo; ECG.<br /></br>• Imaging (CT head) only after temperature control or if focal deficit/trauma suspected.</p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Mnemonic<br /></br>“Heat STROKE = Sprinklers &amp; Fans first”<br /></br>S – Strip clothing<br /></br>T – Tepid water spray<br /></br>R – Rapid airflow (fans)<br /></br>O – Oxygen, IV fluids<br /></br>K – Keep checking core temp<br /></br>E – End (stop) cooling at 38–39 °C  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. CT scan of the head<br /></br>• Would evaluate for intracranial bleed or stroke, but does NOT treat the immediately fatal problem (hyperthermia). Imaging can wait until cooling has begun or temp &lt;39 °C.  </p></br><p>c. Intravenous norepinephrine<br /></br>• Vasopressor for refractory hypotension. In heat stroke, hypotension usually responds to volume resuscitation once peripheral vasodilation resolves with cooling. Give only after fluids &amp; cooling if BP still low.  </p></br><p>d. Intravenous dantrolene<br /></br>• Dantrolene treats malignant hyperthermia (succinycholine/volatile anesthetics) and neuroleptic malignant syndrome (antipsychotics). It has no proven benefit in classic or exertional heat stroke.  </p></br><p>e. Lumbar puncture<br /></br>• Evaluates meningitis/encephalitis, but high temp (&gt;40 °C) with environmental exposure + diaphoresis points to heat stroke; LP delays lifesaving therapy and risks herniation in an unstable patient.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Classic heat stroke is a “cool first, ask questions later” emergency. Immediate evaporative (or ice-water) cooling is the most appropriate next step; all other diagnostics and therapies are secondary once rapid cooling is underway.</p>
128. A 15-year-old girl is brought to the emergency department 2 hours after ingesting a handful of nonprescription diet pills. She has a severe pounding headache and chest pain. Her temperature is 37 °C (98.6 °F), pulse is 55/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 220/125 mm Hg. Examination shows generalized hyperreflexia. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Intravenous calcium therapy</br>b. Intravenous furosemide therapy</br>c. Intravenous phenytoin therapy</br>d. Intravenous physostigmine therapy</br>e. Intravenous sodium nitroprusside therapy</br>f. Intravenous verapamil therapy</br>g. Endotracheal intubation</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Intravenous sodium nitroprusside therapy  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Handful of non-prescription diet pills” → classically contain sympathomimetics (eg, ephedrine, phenyl-propanolamine, phentermine) that massively increase catecholamine release.<br /></br>• “Severe pounding headache, chest pain” → signs of acute end-organ stress from extreme hypertension.<br /></br>• Blood pressure 220/125 mm Hg → Hypertensive emergency (&gt;180/120 + symptoms).<br /></br>• Generalized hyperreflexia → another hyper-adrenergic clue.<br /></br>• Pulse 55/min → reflex (baroreceptor-mediated) bradycardia can accompany malignant hypertension.  </p></br><p>Taken together the vignette is screaming “catecholamine surge → malignant / hypertensive emergency.” The single most urgent step is to bring the blood pressure down quickly with a rapid-onset, titratable IV agent.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify a hypertensive emergency (symptoms + &gt;180/120).<br /></br>2. Decide on IV drug with:<br /></br>   • Rapid onset (seconds-minutes)<br /></br>   • Easy titration<br /></br>   • Works even with high catecholamine state.<br /></br>3. Sodium nitroprusside = IV vasodilator (arterial + venous) with onset &lt;30 sec and half-life &lt;2 min → ideal.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Sympathomimetic diet pills → ↑ NE &amp; dopamine release → α-1 vasoconstriction → marked ↑ SVR → acute severe hypertension. Prolonged extreme pressure injures arterioles, causing headache (cerebral hyper-perfusion), chest pain (↑ afterload, possible aortic dissection, myocardial ischemia), and neurological hyper-excitability. Immediate BP reduction prevents stroke, MI, aortic catastrophe.  </p></br><p>Sodium nitroprusside releases nitric oxide → ↑ cGMP → smooth-muscle relaxation → balanced venous &amp; arterial dilation → ↓ preload AND ↓ afterload → rapid BP control.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• Goal: ↓ MAP by ≈ 10–20 % within first hour; avoid precipitous drops that compromise cerebral/renal perfusion.<br /></br>• Preferred agents in hypertensive emergencies: nitroprusside, IV nicardipine, clevidipine, labetalol, fenoldopam.<br /></br>• Monitor for cyanide toxicity with prolonged/high-dose nitroprusside (rare in ED setting).  </p></br><p>Memorization Tip<br /></br>“Needle-PRESS side” → Nitro-PRUSS(ide) for PRES sure emergencies. Think red emergency needle pumping out NO.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. IV calcium therapy – Given for hyperkalemia, calcium-channel blocker overdose, or hypocalcemia tetany, not for hypertension.<br /></br>b. IV furosemide – Helpful for pulmonary edema; onset too slow and unpredictable for acute pressure control.<br /></br>c. IV phenytoin – Anticonvulsant; no role unless patient is seizing.<br /></br>d. IV physostigmine – Acetylcholinesterase inhibitor for severe anticholinergic toxicity (eg, atropine overdose). Diet pills cause sympathetic excess, not anticholinergic crisis.<br /></br>f. IV verapamil – Slower onset, risk of further bradycardia/asystole, and primarily decreases HR/contractility rather than providing potent, titratable vasodilation in emergencies.<br /></br>g. Endotracheal intubation – Airway seems intact (normal RR, no altered mental status). Address life-threatening BP first; intubation has its own sympathetic surge risk.  </p></br><p>Key Takeaway<br /></br>Hypertensive emergency from sympathomimetic ingestion requires an immediate, rapidly titratable IV vasodilator—sodium nitroprusside is the classic board-favorite agent.</p>
129. A 22-year-old woman comes to the physician for a routine health maintenance examination. She has had a cardiac murmur since early childhood. She drinks alcohol socially once weekly and smokes cigarettes only when she drinks. She is sexually active and uses an oral contraceptive; she has had one lifetime sexual partner. Her temperature is 36.7 °C (98 °F), pulse is 72/min, respirations are 12/min, and blood pressure is 100/70 mm Hg. An S₂ is widely split. A grade 3/6 midsystolic murmur is heard best at the second left intercostal space. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Over the next 10 years, this patient is at greatest risk for which of the following conditions?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Cholestasis</br>b. Hepatic vein thrombosis</br>c. Hepatitis</br>d. Pulmonary hypertension</br>e. Renovascular hypertension</br>f. Systemic hypertension</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Pulmonary hypertension  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “S₂ is widely split” that does not vary with respiration = fixed, wide splitting → classic for an atrial septal defect (ASD).<br /></br>• “Grade 3/6 midsystolic murmur … 2nd left intercostal space” = flow murmur across the pulmonic valve caused by ↑ flow from left-to-right shunt.<br /></br>• Young, otherwise asymptomatic adult with a lifelong “cardiac murmur” → congenital heart lesion that has not yet produced symptoms.</p></br><p>Key Positives: fixed wide split S2, pulmonic flow murmur, normal vitals, no cyanosis.<br /></br>Key Negatives: no diastolic rumble (would suggest VSD), no continuous machinery murmur (PDA), no systemic HTN or cyanosis yet.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify lesion → ASD (typically secundum type).<br /></br>Step 2: Recall natural history → chronic left-to-right shunt → progressive ↑ pulmonary blood flow → endothelial injury &amp; medial hypertrophy of pulmonary arterioles → pulmonary vascular resistance rises.<br /></br>Step 3: Major long-term complication → pulmonary hypertension (may progress to Eisenmenger syndrome with reversal of shunt and late cyanosis).</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Untreated moderate-to-large ASDs predispose to pulmonary vascular remodeling and pulmonary hypertension within the 3rd-4th decade.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• ASD allows oxygenated blood from LA → RA.<br /></br>• Consequences: volume overload of RA/RV → dilation, ↑ flow through pulmonary circulation.<br /></br>• Pulmonary artery walls respond by muscular hypertrophy &amp; intimal proliferation → progressively ↑ pulmonary artery pressure.<br /></br>• Once pulmonary artery pressure ≈ systemic pressure, shunt reverses (Eisenmenger), causing cyanosis &amp; clubbing.</p></br><p>Risk factors for faster progression: large defect, Down syndrome, concomitant pulmonary disease. Median time to clinically significant pulmonary HTN ≈ 20–30 yrs.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics (for context)<br /></br>• Diagnosis: Echo with color Doppler; transesophageal echo best for secundum ASD.<br /></br>• Indications to close: evidence of RV volume overload, significant shunt (Qp:Qs ≥1.5), prior paradoxical embolus.<br /></br>• Closure options: percutaneous device (secundum) or surgical patch (primum/sinus venosus). Early closure prevents pulmonary hypertension.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Fixed Split = ASDefect”<br /></br>Picture a rigid (“fixed”) diving board over a “split” swimming pool labeled “ASD → ↑ Pulm blood flow → Pulm HTN.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Cholestasis – Presents with pruritus, jaundice, ↑ ALP; associated with pregnancy, OCPs, PBC. No link to ASD.<br /></br>b. Hepatic vein thrombosis (Budd-Chiari) – Triad of abdominal pain, ascites, hepatomegaly; risk factors = polycythemia vera, OCPs, pregnancy, malignancy, NOT congenital heart shunts.<br /></br>c. Hepatitis – Would require viral exposure, drug toxicity, or autoimmune triggers; murmur findings irrelevant.<br /></br>e. Renovascular hypertension – Renal artery stenosis causes refractory systemic HTN, abdominal bruit; patient is normotensive.<br /></br>f. Systemic hypertension – Prevalence rises with age, obesity, family history; ASD neither predisposes to nor protects from systemic HTN. The vignette specifically points toward a pulmonary, not systemic, vascular complication.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Fixed wide S₂ + pulmonic flow murmur = ASD. The dominant long-term danger is pulmonary hypertension, which can be prevented by timely defect closure.</p>
130. A 14-year-old girl is brought to the physician because of a 1-week history of a malodorous vaginal discharge. The discharge is grayish white and causes mild itching. She has not had burning with urination, urinary frequency or urgency, or abdominal pain. Menarche was at the age of 12 years; her last menstrual period was 2 weeks ago and lasted 5 days. Two weeks ago, she completed a 10-day course of antibiotics for streptococcal pharyngitis. On questioning, she states that she has sexual she takes showers but occasionally takes bubble baths. When interviewed alone, she reports that she had sexual intercourse once 2 weeks ago, and her partner used a condom. Pelvic examination shows a small amount of grayish white discharge in the vagina. The cervix appears normal. There are no rashes or excoriations on the perineum. Pubic hair development is sexual maturity rating stage 5. A wet mount preparation of the vaginal discharge shows few leukocytes, numerous epithelial cells with a granular appearance and irregular margins, and many coccobacilli. Which of the following is the most likely explanation for this patient’s symptoms?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Bacterial infection</br>b. Chemical irritation</br>c. Latex allergy</br>d. Physiologic secretions</br>e. Protozoan infestation</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer: a. Bacterial infection  → Bacterial vaginosis (Gardnerella vaginalis–predominant polymicrobial overgrowth)</p></br><p>─────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• Key descriptors of the discharge<br /></br>  – “Grayish-white,” “malodorous,” “mild itching” → classic buzzwords for bacterial vaginosis (BV).<br /></br>  – Absence of dysuria, pelvic pain, or cervical motion tenderness rules out urethritis, cystitis, or pelvic inflammatory disease.</p></br><p>• Wet-mount findings<br /></br>  – “Few leukocytes” → minimal inflammation (typical for BV).<br /></br>  – “Numerous epithelial cells with a granular appearance and irregular margins” → CLUE CELLS (vaginal epithelial cells coated with gram-variable coccobacilli).<br /></br>  – “Many coccobacilli” → heavy colonization with anaerobic bacteria (Gardnerella, Mobiluncus, etc.).</p></br><p>• Recent 10-day antibiotic course → altered vaginal flora → promotes anaerobic overgrowth → BV.</p></br><p>─────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>1. Discharge description + fishy odor (implied by “malodorous”) → raises suspicion for BV.<br /></br>2. Wet mount showing clue cells is essentially diagnostic.<br /></br>3. Lack of inflammation (few WBCs) distinguishes BV from candidiasis or Trichomonas.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Gray malodorous discharge + clue cells + few WBCs = bacterial vaginosis.</p></br><p>─────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Etiology: Loss of normal lactobacilli → ↑ vaginal pH (&gt;4.5) → overgrowth of Gardnerella vaginalis and other anaerobes.<br /></br>• Risk factors: Recent antibiotics, new sexual partner, douching, smoking. (Note: BV is not strictly an STI but sexual activity increases risk.)<br /></br>• Manifestations: Thin gray discharge, fishy odor (amine whiff when KOH added), mild/no pruritus, few leukocytes on microscopy.</p></br><p>─────────────────────</br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Pearls</br>Diagnostics (if wet mount not already diagnostic):<br /></br>• pH &gt; 4.5, positive KOH “whiff test,” ≥20% clue cells on saline microscopy.</p></br><p>First-line therapy:<br /></br>• Metronidazole 500 mg PO twice daily for 7 days  (alt: metronidazole 0.75% gel intravaginally; clindamycin cream).<br /></br>• Sexual partners do NOT need treatment (contrast with Trichomonas).<br /></br>• Counsel on avoiding douching; consider probiotics after antibiotics.</p></br><p>─────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>CLUE that you’re in the GARDEN (Gardnerella):<br /></br>C – Clue cells<br /></br>L – Lack of Leukocytes<br /></br>U – Unpleasant fishy odor<br /></br>E – Elevated pH (&gt;4.5)</p></br><p>─────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>b. Chemical irritation (bubble bath)<br /></br>• Would cause vulvar erythema/itching without clue cells; discharge usually minimal and not fishy.</p></br><p>c. Latex allergy<br /></br>• Presents immediately after condom use with intense pruritus, swelling, urticaria; no clue cells; temporal link to intercourse episodes.</p></br><p>d. Physiologic secretions<br /></br>• Clear/white, odorless, cyclic with hormones; microscopy shows abundant squamous cells but NO adherent bacteria (no clue cells), normal pH (3.8-4.5).</p></br><p>e. Protozoan infestation (Trichomonas)<br /></br>• Green-yellow frothy discharge, profuse inflammation (many WBCs), motile trichomonads on wet mount, “strawberry cervix.” Not present here.</p></br><p>─────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>Clue cells + gray, fishy discharge after antibiotics = bacterial vaginosis → treat with oral or intravaginal metronidazole.</p>
131. A 14-year-old boy is brought to the physician because of a 1-month history of painful swelling beneath his left nipple. He has had no drainage from the nipple. He has no history of recent injury. He has major depressive disorder treated with a selective serotonin reuptake inhibitor (SSRI) for the past 3 months. He takes no other medications. He has smoked three to four cigarettes daily for 1 year and smoked marijuana once. He is at the 75th percentile for height and weight. Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows a 1-cm, tender, firm, mobile mass beneath the left areola. Pubic hair and genital development are sexual maturity rating stage 3. There is no axillary hair. Which of the following is the most likely explanation for the swelling in this patient’s breast?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Adverse effect of SSRI</br>b. Breast cancer</br>c. Marijuana use</br>d. Puberty</br>e. Seminiferous tubule dysgenesis (Klinefelter syndrome)</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Puberty (physiologic pubertal gynecomastia)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age 14 y → squarely within the typical window (10-16 y) for physiologic pubertal gynecomastia.<br /></br>• Sexual-maturity (Tanner) stage 3 genitalia &amp; pubic hair, no axillary hair → mid-puberty, when the transient estrogen/androgen imbalance peaks.<br /></br>• 1-cm, mobile, rubbery, TENDER subareolar mass → classic description (benign, often unilateral early on).<br /></br>• No galactorrhea, nipple discharge, systemic illness, or testicular findings → rules out prolactin-mediated processes, malignancy, or Klinefelter syndrome.<br /></br>• No drugs classically linked to gynecomastia (spironolactone, cimetidine, ketoconazole, anabolic steroids, marijuana use only once). SSRI use is possible but uncommon and the timing (started 3 mo ago) is less convincing than physiologic puberty.</p></br><p>Key Positives: adolescent male, Tanner stage 3, tender subareolar nodule.<br /></br>Key Negatives: unilateral only 1 mo, no chronic/recreational drug use, no systemic findings.</p></br><p>Pattern: “B” cup &amp; “B”rief pain in Boys = Benign pubertal breast bud.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Ask: Is this a classic age and Tanner stage for physiologic gynecomastia? → Yes.<br /></br>2. Is there a common medication or systemic condition more likely? → No; SSRI-gynecomastia is rare.<br /></br>3. Malignancy is extraordinarily uncommon at 14 y and usually painless, hard, fixed ± lymphadenopathy → absent here.<br /></br>Conclusion: transient pubertal gynecomastia.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In mid-puberty boys, a small tender subareolar mass is almost always physiologic and needs only reassurance.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Puberty triggers a transient rise in testicular estrogen production relative to testosterone.<br /></br>• Breast tissue is exquisitely sensitive to estrogen → proliferation results in a palpable “breast bud.”<br /></br>• Peaks at Tanner stage 2-3, regresses within 6–18 months in &gt;90 % of boys.<br /></br>• Unilateral onset is common; eventual bilateral or complete resolution is the norm.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Management = Reassurance and routine follow-up.<br /></br>• No labs or imaging needed unless: rapid growth, &gt;4 cm, hard/fixed mass, abnormal testicular exam, galactorrhea, or systemic signs (then obtain serum β-hCG, estradiol, testosterone, LH/FSH, prolactin, ± testicular ultrasound).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Teen TENDER Tit” mnemonic:<br /></br>T – Tanner 2-3<br /></br>E – Estrogen transiently high<br /></br>N – Node under nipple (1 cm)<br /></br>D – Disappears in a year<br /></br>E – Examine but no tests<br /></br>R – REASSURE</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Adverse effect of SSRI<br /></br>• SSRIs can very rarely raise prolactin → gynecomastia, but far more common with antipsychotics or TCAs. Would expect bilateral, subacute onset and possibly galactorrhea. Pubertal timing fits better.</p></br><p>b. Breast cancer<br /></br>• Pediatric male breast CA is exceedingly rare; mass would be hard, non-tender, fixed, possibly with skin/nipple retraction or axillary nodes—none seen here.</p></br><p>c. Marijuana use<br /></br>• Chronic heavy cannabis can lower testosterone and cause gynecomastia, but patient used marijuana only once (insufficient exposure). Age/Tanner stage make physiologic cause far more likely.</p></br><p>e. Seminiferous tubule dysgenesis (Klinefelter syndrome, 47,XXY)<br /></br>• Presents with tall stature, small firm testes, delayed puberty, and often bilateral gynecomastia. This patient’s testicular development is normal for Tanner 3, and no other stigmata (long limbs, learning difficulties) are noted.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Take-Home Message<br /></br>Tender subareolar nodules in mid-pubertal boys = physiologic gynecomastia. Reassure; no work-up unless atypical features present.</p>
132. A 47-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 6-month history of increasingly severe low back pain. She reports that she has had episodes of low back pain during the past 2 years but that the episodes have recently become more severe. The pain is worst when she sits for long periods of time, rises from a sitting-to-standing position, or bends toward the floor. She has not had leg pain or any prior treatment. Nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drugs provide minimal relief, and she asks if she could benefit from operative treatment. Examination shows moderate tenderness to palpation of the midline to the level of L4-5. Straight-leg raise testing is negative. Peripheral pulses are normal. Neurologic examination shows no focal findings. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Bone scan</br>b. CT scan–guided myelography</br>c. MRI of the lumbar spine</br>d. X-ray series of the lumbar spine</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. X-ray series of the lumbar spine  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 47-year-old with 6 months of slowly progressive low back pain.<br /></br>• Pain pattern is mechanical: worsens with prolonged sitting, bending, position change.<br /></br>• No radicular leg pain, no bowel/bladder symptoms, normal straight-leg raise, normal neuro exam → argues against disc herniation or spinal stenosis.<br /></br>• No “red-flag” features (fever, weight loss, trauma, cancer history, IV-drug use, nighttime pain, age &gt;50).<br /></br>• She is contemplating surgery, so baseline imaging is reasonable, but the condition still appears benign and localized.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach to chronic (&gt;6 wk) mechanical low-back pain WITHOUT red flags:<br /></br>1. Trial of conservative therapy (NSAIDs, activity modification, PT) → already attempted NSAIDs.<br /></br>2. Baseline imaging, starting with the least invasive and lowest radiation/cost test: plain lumbar spine radiographs (AP, lateral, ± oblique views).<br /></br>3. Reserve advanced imaging (MRI, CT, bone scan) for:<br /></br>   • Focal neurologic deficits<br /></br>   • Concern for infection, malignancy, fracture<br /></br>   • Pre-operative planning after abnormal X-ray or failed conservative measures with neuro findings.<br /></br>Because she lacks worrisome features, the guideline-directed next step is plain X-rays.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Chronic, non-radicular low-back pain without red flags → start with plain radiographs; escalate only if findings or symptoms warrant.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (brief)<br /></br>Most chronic low-back pain in middle-aged adults is mechanical (degenerative facet or disc disease, lumbar strain). Pathology is confined to bones/soft tissue without nerve root compression or systemic disease, so simple X-rays often suffice to detect osteoarthritis, spondylolisthesis, scoliosis, etc.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Test Review<br /></br>• X-ray (AP/lateral/oblique): evaluates alignment, vertebral body integrity, degenerative changes, spondylolysis/listhesis.<br /></br>• If X-ray is normal yet symptoms persist or neuro deficits appear → MRI lumbar spine (best for discs, nerves, infection, tumor).<br /></br>• Bone scan: screens entire skeleton for metastatic disease or occult osteomyelitis.<br /></br>• CT myelography: invasive, now mainly used when MRI is contraindicated or equivocal for nerve root compression.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Back pain ladder” mnemonic – RULE<br /></br>R – Red flags? yes → MRI<br /></br>U – Under 6 weeks &amp; mild → no imaging<br /></br>L – Long-term (&gt;6 wk) without red flags → Lumbosacral X-ray<br /></br>E – Escalate (MRI/CT) if X-ray abnormal or neuro deficits emerge  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Bone scan – Indicated when you suspect metastatic disease, occult fracture, or infection; none of those clues (no systemic Sx, normal neuro, age &lt;50 without cancer history).<br /></br>b. CT-guided myelography – Invasive, reserved for detailed nerve-root/CSF-space evaluation when MRI contraindicated; negative straight-leg raise and no neuro deficits make this unnecessary.<br /></br>c. MRI lumbar spine – First-line for radiculopathy, cord compression, infection, tumor; over-kill for uncomplicated mechanical pain and contrary to cost-effective guidelines.<br /></br>d. X-ray series – Correct; least invasive, low cost, guideline-supported initial imaging for chronic, non-red-flag low-back pain.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>For a patient with chronic, purely mechanical low-back pain and no red flags, obtain plain lumbar spine radiographs before pursuing advanced imaging or operative intervention.</p>
133. A 32-year-old man comes for a follow-up examination. He has membranous glomerulonephritis that is in remission. Examination shows no abnormalities. His serum creatinine concentration is 1.2 mg/dL. Urinalysis shows 2+ protein. Which of the following is most likely to delay progression of this patient’s renal disease?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. High-potassium diet</br>b. High-protein diet</br>c. β-Adrenergic blocking agent therapy</br>d. Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitor therapy</br>e. Diuretic therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitor therapy  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Membranous glomerulonephritis” → prototypic nephrotic‐range disease in adults.<br /></br>• “2+ protein” despite clinical remission → ongoing proteinuria, the single strongest modifiable risk factor for CKD progression.<br /></br>• Normal creatinine (1.2 mg/dL) → kidney function still preserved; goal is to keep it that way.  </p></br><p>Key pattern: Any patient with persistent proteinuria from a chronic glomerular process should be on an ACE inhibitor (or ARB) unless contraindicated.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Protein leaking through the glomerulus accelerates tubulo-interstitial damage and scarring.<br /></br>2. Lowering intraglomerular pressure → ↓ protein filtration → slows scarring.<br /></br>3. ACE inhibitors dilate the efferent arteriole (block angiotensin II) → fall in glomerular capillary pressure → less proteinuria + proven renoprotection that is independent of systemic BP reduction.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: ACE inhibitors (or ARBs) are first-line renoprotective agents in ANY proteinuric chronic kidney disease.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Membranous GN: immune complex deposition on the subepithelial side of the GBM → thickened capillary walls, nephrotic syndrome.<br /></br>• Proteinuria itself is nephrotoxic: filtered proteins trigger cytokine release, tubular inflammation, fibrosis.<br /></br>• Angiotensin II constricts the efferent arteriole → ↑ glomerular pressure → ↑ protein loss. Blocking this step slows the vicious cycle.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Monitoring<br /></br>1. Start ACEI (e.g., lisinopril) → titrate to maximal tolerated dose.<br /></br>2. Monitor serum creatinine &amp; potassium within 1–2 weeks; expect up to 30 % creatinine rise (acceptable) and watch for hyperkalemia.<br /></br>3. Adjuncts: statin, salt restriction, BP goal ≤130/80 mm Hg, consider ARB if cough/angioedema.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“CAP your protein leak”<br /></br>C – Converting enzyme (ACE)<br /></br>A – ARB (same idea)<br /></br>P – Proteinuria ↓, Progression slowed  </p></br><p>Think Efferent = Exit → ACE inhibitors dilate the Exit vessel. Lower pressure, less leak.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. High-potassium diet<br /></br>• Offers no renoprotection and risks hyperkalemia as GFR declines.  </p></br><p>b. High-protein diet<br /></br>• High protein load → ↑ GFR (“hyperfiltration”) → ↑ intraglomerular pressure → accelerates damage. Opposite of what we want.  </p></br><p>c. β-Adrenergic blocker therapy<br /></br>• Lowers systemic BP but does not preferentially reduce intraglomerular pressure or proteinuria like ACEI/ARB. Inferior renoprotection.  </p></br><p>e. Diuretic therapy<br /></br>• Useful for edema/volume control; no evidence it slows CKD progression.  </p></br><p>Common pitfall: Equating general BP control (β-blocker, diuretic) with specific anti-proteinuric, renoprotective benefit of ACEI/ARB. Always choose ACEI/ARB first when proteinuria is present.</p>
134. An 81-year-old woman comes to the physician for a follow-up examination. She has osteoporosis treated with calcium and vitamin D supplementation. Previous trials of two medications were not well tolerated. Her mother and sister had a history of hip fractures. The patient says she would like to try another medication for prevention of hip fracture. The physician finds a recently published study evaluating the efficacy of a new drug for osteoporosis. A total of 19,916 women between the ages of 60 and 80 years were enrolled in the study; all participants had osteoporosis treated with calcium and vitamin D supplementation, and were observed for 3 years. Participants were randomized to receive either the new drug or placebo. Results showed:</br>Total Number of Patients  |  Fracture (Number of Patients)  |  Fracture Rate  |  Discontinued Therapy (Number of Patients)</br>Study drug  9951  |  401  |  0.042 (p=0.047)  |  1548</br>Placebo  9965  |  442  |  0.044  |  1195</br>Which of the following conclusions is the most appropriate to draw from these data?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. The results are likely caused by chance</br>b. The results may be explained by the placebo effect</br>c. The results may be statistically significant but not clinically significant</br>d. The results of this study do not apply to this patient</br>e. The study has inadequate power to draw a conclusion about the benefits of the drug</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>c. The results may be statistically significant but not clinically significant  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Population studied: 19,916 post-menopausal women (age 60-80) with documented osteoporosis already on Ca2+/vit D—very similar to the patient in the stem.<br /></br>• Randomization: study-drug (n = 9,951) vs placebo (n = 9,965).<br /></br>• Outcome of interest: hip fracture over 3 yrs.<br /></br>• Results given:<br /></br> – Fracture rate study-drug = 0.042 (4.2 %) → 401 events<br /></br> – Fracture rate placebo       = 0.044 (4.4 %) → 442 events<br /></br> – p = 0.047  </p></br><p>Key clue: The p-value is &lt; 0.05 (statistically significant), BUT the absolute risk difference is only 0.002 (0.2 %)—extremely small.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1. Determine statistical significance.<br /></br> • p = 0.047 → reject the null; there is evidence of a difference.  </p></br><p>Step 2. Quantify the size of the difference.<br /></br> • Absolute risk reduction (ARR) = 0.044 – 0.042 = 0.002 (0.2 %).<br /></br> • Relative risk reduction (RRR) = 0.002 / 0.044 ≈ 4.5 %.<br /></br> • Number-needed-to-treat (NNT) = 1 / ARR = 1 / 0.002 ≈ 500.  </p></br><p>Step 3. Judge clinical importance.<br /></br> • An NNT of ~500 over 3 years to prevent ONE hip fracture is unlikely to be worth cost, adverse effects, or the high discontinuation rate (15.6 % vs 12.0 %).<br /></br>Therefore, while statistically significant, the benefit is minimal—hence “statistically significant but not clinically significant.”  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Always pair the p-value with effect size. A very small p-value does not guarantee a meaningful clinical benefit.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology &amp; Treatment Context (concise)<br /></br>• Osteoporosis = low bone mass → ↑ fragility fracture risk.<br /></br>• Hip fractures cause high morbidity and mortality, so therapies aim to produce a sizable absolute risk reduction.<br /></br>• Effective agents (e.g., bisphosphonates, denosumab, teriparatide) typically lower hip fracture risk by ~1–2 % absolute over 3 yrs (NNT 50–100). This new drug falls far short.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“P-value tells you IF there’s a difference; ARR tells you IF you care.”<br /></br>NNT Quick Check:<br /></br> NNT ≈ 100 / (% ARR). Here, %ARR = 0.2 %, so NNT ≈ 500—easy red flag for trivial benefit.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Results are likely caused by chance<br /></br> • p = 0.047 (&lt; 0.05) argues against chance as the explanation; chance would be suspected with p &gt; 0.05.  </p></br><p>b. May be explained by the placebo effect<br /></br> • Both groups received identical placebo control/randomization, so placebo effect is balanced; cannot explain the between-group difference.  </p></br><p>d. Results do not apply to this patient<br /></br> • Study population (post-menopausal women on Ca/vit D) matches the patient well—external validity is good.  </p></br><p>e. Inadequate power<br /></br> • Nearly 20,000 participants yield high power—statistical significance achieved.
