Lack of power would manifest as non-significant p-value despite a meaningful effect.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>A p-value can be significant even when the absolute benefit is so small that it offers little real-world value. Always calculate ARR and NNT to decide whether a “statistically significant” finding is also clinically meaningful.</p>
135. A 2-year-old boy from Long Island, New York, is admitted to the hospital because of a 2-day history of decreased ability to bear weight on his left leg. He has refused to walk for the past 12 hours. His mother reports that he has felt warm during the past 24 hours, but she has not taken his temperature. He has a 1-year history of eczema treated with an emollient lotion. Immunizations are up-to-date. His mother has a history of recurrent skin “boils.” The patient is crying but is consolable. He is holding his left leg flexed at the knee. He is at the 50th percentile for height and weight. His temperature is 38.5°C (101.3°F), pulse is 135/min, respirations are 28/min, and blood pressure is 98/58 mm Hg. Examination shows no swelling or erythema of the left knee. He cries on passive movement of the left lower extremity. There are areas of lichenification of the antecubital and popliteal fossae. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin 13.1 g/dL</br>Hematocrit 37%</br>Leukocyte count 16,500/mm³</br>Segmented neutrophils 68%</br>Bands 6%</br>Lymphocytes 18%</br>Monocytes 8%</br>Platelet count 440,000/mm³</br>C-reactive protein 48 mg/L (N<8 mg/L)</br>An x-ray of the left knee and hip shows no abnormality. An MRI of the left lower extremity shows marrow edema of the distal femur. A blood culture is positive for microbial growth. A Gram stain of the blood culture is shown. Administration of which of the following is the most appropriate initial step in pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: F</br></br>a. Ampicillin</br>b. Azithromycin</br>c. Cefepime</br>d. Ceftriaxone</br>e. Rifampin</br>f. Vancomycin</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>f. Vancomycin  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key findings<br /></br>  – 2-day history of refusal to bear weight + fever → acute mono-limb pain; think septic arthritis or osteomyelitis.<br /></br>  – MRI: marrow edema of the distal femur → confirms acute hematogenous osteomyelitis.<br /></br>  – Blood culture Gram stain (not shown) is implied to reveal gram-positive cocci in clusters.<br /></br>  – Personal eczema + mother with recurrent “boils” (classic for community-acquired Staphylococcus aureus, often MRSA).<br /></br>  – Elevated WBC, platelets, and markedly ↑CRP support acute bacterial infection.  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives: fever, high inflammatory markers, marrow edema, gram-positive cocci.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no joint effusion on imaging, no swelling/erythema, making septic arthritis less likely.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach<br /></br>1. Diagnose: Acute hematogenous osteomyelitis.<br /></br>2. Most common organism in children: Staphylococcus aureus.<br /></br>3. MRSA risk factors present (eczema → chronic skin breaks; household contact with cutaneous abscesses).<br /></br>4. Empiric therapy must cover MRSA until sensitivities return.<br /></br>5. First-line intravenous MRSA coverage = vancomycin (or clindamycin if local MRSA clinda-sensitive rates are high).  </p></br><p>Take-home message: Any child with acute osteomyelitis + MRSA risk factors requires empiric IV vancomycin.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)<br /></br>• Acute hematogenous osteomyelitis in kids arises from transient bacteremia seeding the metaphysis (slow blood flow in hair-pin capillaries).<br /></br>• MRSA possesses Panton–Valentine leukocidin and mecA gene (altered PBP2a) → β-lactam resistance, more aggressive infection.  </p></br><p>Risk factors for MRSA osteomyelitis<br /></br>– Eczema/atopic dermatitis<br /></br>– Recurrent skin/soft-tissue infections in patient or household contacts<br /></br>– Recent antibiotics, day-care outbreaks, competitive athletes  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>1. Empiric IV vancomycin (15 mg/kg q6 h) pending culture susceptibilities.<br /></br>2. Narrow to nafcillin/cefazolin if culture shows MSSA; add clindamycin, linezolid, or daptomycin if vanc-resistant.<br /></br>3. Duration: 4-6 weeks total (IV ± switch to PO once afebrile, CRP trending down, reliable follow-up).<br /></br>4. Evaluate for endocarditis if bacteremia persists &gt;48 h.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Eczema + Boils = MRSA SPOILS”<br /></br>If you see skin disease (eczema) or boils in family, think community MRSA → start VANco.  </p></br><p>VAN for Very Aggressive (MRSA) Nasty bugs.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Ampicillin – Good for enterococcus, Listeria; rapidly hydrolyzed by S. aureus β-lactamase; no MRSA coverage.<br /></br>b. Azithromycin – Covers atypical respiratory pathogens; poor bone penetration, no staph coverage.<br /></br>c. Cefepime – Broad gram-negative (incl. Pseudomonas) + MSSA, but not MRSA. Overkill for gram-negatives, underkill for MRSA.<br /></br>d. Ceftriaxone – Excellent for Salmonella osteomyelitis (e.g., in sickle cell) and many gram-negatives, but not MRSA.<br /></br>e. Rifampin – Never used as monotherapy (rapid resistance); used only in combination for biofilm/prosthetic infections.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Choosing a broad cephalosporin (ceftriaxone/cefepime) “because it’s IV and broad.” Remember: broad does NOT equal MRSA coverage. Always think mechanism of resistance (mecA) → need glycopeptide or similar.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Concept<br /></br>Empiric therapy for pediatric acute osteomyelitis must target Staphylococcus aureus; when MRSA risk factors are present, start IV vancomycin.</p>
136. A 32-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 6-month history of shortness of breath with exertion, palpitations, and generalized swelling. His pulse is 105/min and irregular, and blood pressure is 118/70 mm Hg. A grade 3/6 holosystolic murmur and a diastolic rumble are heard at the apex. An ECG shows atrial fibrillation and right ventricular hypertrophy. Which of the following is the most likely cause of these findings?	Correct answer: I</br></br>a. Calcification of a tricuspid aortic valve </br>b. Calcification of a tricuspid aortic valve </br>c. Congenital defect of the atrial septum </br>d. Congenital defect of the ventricular septum </br>e. Infection of cardiac valves with Staphylococcus aureus </br>f. Infection of cardiac valves with viridans streptococcus </br>g. Inflammation of the endocardium and myocardium </br>h. Inflammation of the pericardium </br>i. Rheumatic mitral valve disease </br>Verification: PASS</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>i. Rheumatic mitral valve disease</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• “32-year-old” + “6-month history” → chronic process in a relatively young adult (degenerative calcific disease is unusual this early).<br /></br>• “Shortness of breath with exertion, palpitations, generalized swelling (edema)” → congestive picture (pulmonary + systemic venous congestion).<br /></br>• “Pulse 105/min and irregular” → atrial fibrillation (AF).<br /></br>• Auscultation:<br /></br>  – “Grade 3/6 holosystolic murmur at the apex” = classic for mitral regurgitation (MR).<br /></br>  – “Diastolic rumble at the apex” (often preceded by an opening snap) = classic for mitral stenosis (MS).<br /></br>  The combination of MS + MR in a young adult is highly characteristic of chronic rheumatic heart disease.<br /></br>• “ECG shows atrial fibrillation” → long-standing LA enlargement from MS/MR.<br /></br>• “Right ventricular hypertrophy” → pulmonary hypertension secondary to chronic MS.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)</br>1. Identify the murmurs ⇒ mixed MS/MR.<br /></br>2. Think of etiologies that produce BOTH lesions in a young patient ⇒ rheumatic carditis is by far the most common.<br /></br>3. Supportive findings: AF (due to enlarged LA) and RVH (from pulmonary HTN) are textbook sequelae of rheumatic MS.<br /></br>4. Therefore, the underlying cause = rheumatic mitral valve disease.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Rheumatic fever (post–group A β-hemolytic Streptococcus pharyngitis) → autoimmune cross-reaction (type II hypersensitivity) against cardiac myosin + valve tissue.<br /></br>• Acute phase: pancarditis (Aschoff bodies).<br /></br>• Chronic phase: progressive leaflet thickening, commissural fusion, and chordal shortening → MS; fibrosis and deformity of leaflets/chordae → MR.<br /></br>• LA pressure ↑ ⇒ dilation ⇒ AF &amp; mural thrombi risk.<br /></br>• Pulmonary venous HTN ⇒ RV pressure overload ⇒ RVH and right-sided failure → peripheral edema.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics (high-yield)</br>• Echocardiography = diagnostic; shows “hockey-stick” anterior MV leaflet and possibly MR jet.<br /></br>• Management: rate/rhythm control for AF (β-blocker, diltiazem, or digoxin); anticoagulation (warfarin) if AF; diuretics for congestion.<br /></br>• Definitive: percutaneous balloon mitral valvotomy (isolated MS) or surgical valve repair/replacement (severe combined MS/MR).<br /></br>• Primary prevention: penicillin for streptococcal pharyngitis. Secondary prophylaxis: monthly benzathine penicillin G.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“Rheum for TWO” – Rheumatic disease typically gives TWO lesions (MS + MR), TWO atria messed up (LA dilation → AF), and TWO ventricles (RVH from pulmonary HTN).<br /></br>“Opening SNAP, think Strep clap-back.”</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a/b. Calcification of a tricuspid aortic valve (degenerative or congenital bicuspid) – causes aortic stenosis; murmur is systolic crescendo–decrescendo at right upper sternal border, not apex diastolic rumble/MR. Would create LVH, not RVH.<br /></br>c. Congenital atrial septal defect – produces fixed split S2, systolic ejection murmur (pulmonary flow), often right-axis deviation/RBBB on ECG; no diastolic rumble at apex; AF possible but MS/MR absent.<br /></br>d. Congenital ventricular septal defect – holosystolic murmur at left lower sternal border; diastolic rumble from increased flow across mitral valve but not an apical MR murmur; presents in infancy/childhood.<br /></br>e. Infective endocarditis with Staph aureus – acute, febrile illness; murmur change usually isolated MR/TR; no chronic diastolic rumble or RVH.<br /></br>f. Infective endocarditis with viridans streptococci – subacute; preceding dental work; again isolated regurgitant lesions, fever, splinter hemorrhages, Osler nodes.<br /></br>g. Inflammation of endocardium &amp; myocardium (myocarditis/endocarditis) – causes systolic dysfunction or new regurgitant murmurs acutely; not combined chronic MS/MR pattern or RVH.<br /></br>h. Pericarditis – presents with pleuritic chest pain, pericardial friction rub, diffuse ST elevation; murmurs of MS/MR and RVH are not features.</p></br><p>Take-home Message</br>In a young adult, the simultaneous presence of a diastolic rumble (MS) and a holosystolic MR murmur—especially with atrial fibrillation and right-sided findings—should immediately bring to mind chronic rheumatic mitral valve disease.</p>
137. A randomized study is conducted to compare the efficacy of an experimental operative procedure with that of conventional nonoperative treatment in patients with knee injuries. A total of 1000 patients are enrolled. Five hundred patients are assigned by a chance process to undergo the operative procedure and 400 undergo the procedure. Five hundred patients are assigned by a chance process to the conventional treatment and 50 undergo the procedure. All patients are observed for 5 years. Analysis of patient outcomes is conducted based on the treatment received by the patients. Which of the following features of this study is of greatest potential concern?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Inadequate randomization</br>b. Inappropriate control group</br>c. Lack of intention-to-treat analysis</br>d. Short follow-up duration</br>e. Small sample size</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>c. Lack of intention-to-treat analysis  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Randomized study” → random allocation should balance known &amp; unknown confounders.<br /></br>• Large “cross-over” rate: 100 of the 500 patients assigned to surgery did not get it, and 50 of the 500 patients assigned to conservative care did get surgery.<br /></br>• “Analysis … based on the treatment received” → investigators abandon the original randomization and perform a per-protocol/on-treatment analysis.  </p></br><p>These clues scream “no intention-to-treat (ITT) analysis,” the key safeguard against selection bias when non-adherence occurs.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Randomization creates comparable groups at baseline.<br /></br>Step 2: Non-adherence/cross-over threatens that comparability.<br /></br>Step 3: ITT preserves the benefits of randomization by keeping each patient in the group to which he or she was originally randomized, regardless of what actually happened afterward.<br /></br>Step 4: Analyzing “based on treatment received” turns the trial into something closer to an observational study, re-introducing confounding (e.g., patients who opt out of surgery may be sicker, more risk-averse, uninsured, etc.).  </p></br><p>Take-home concept: ALWAYS analyze RCTs primarily with ITT; otherwise randomization is wasted and results are biased.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Methodology Mini-Review<br /></br>Intention-to-treat:<br /></br>• Definition – Analyze every participant in the group to which they were randomized, regardless of protocol deviations, withdrawals, or cross-over.<br /></br>• Purpose – Maintains prognostic balance, reflects “real-world” effectiveness, preserves sample size.<br /></br>Per-protocol/on-treatment analysis:<br /></br>• Includes only patients who fully adhered; may estimate “biologic efficacy,” but at the cost of bias.<br /></br>• Should be secondary or sensitivity analysis, never primary in an RCT.</p></br><p>Mnemonic: “Analyze as you RANDOMIZE” (A-A-R → think of an “AAR” report for quality).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Inadequate randomization – The vignette explicitly states patients were “assigned by a chance process,” satisfying randomization. The concern is not how they were assigned but how they were analyzed.<br /></br>b. Inappropriate control group – Conventional non-operative care is the standard comparator for knee injuries; it is perfectly appropriate.<br /></br>d. Short follow-up duration – Five years is generous for functional knee outcomes and late surgical complications.<br /></br>e. Small sample size – 1,000 participants is ample for most orthopedic outcome measures; statistical power is unlikely to be the main issue.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memory Aid<br /></br>“ITT keeps the trial legit” – if you don’t ‘Intend To Treat,’ the ‘Intent’ of randomization is ‘Trashed.’  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Exam Pearl<br /></br>When you see:<br /></br>1. Randomized trial<br /></br>2. Non-adherence, cross-overs, or loss to follow-up<br /></br>3. Results reported by actual treatment received<br /></br>…think “Missing intention-to-treat” → bias → choose that answer.</p>
138. A hospital’s adverse drug reaction review committee has been asked to review a series of adverse medication events in the oncology unit. On four occasions, patients inadvertently received an antibiotic instead of a chemotherapeutic agent. Interviews with nurses in the unit show that the two medication vials are similar in color, distributed in the same-sized container, and are placed next to each other on the distribution cart sent by the hospital’s central pharmacy. Which of the following measures is most appropriate to prevent this error in the future?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Recommend that the pharmacy repackage the containers so that they are distinct from one another</br>b. Remove the antibiotic from the hospital formulary</br>c. Require continuing-education programs for all nurses to properly recognize medications</br>d. Suspend the nurses involved in the four incidents</br>e. No intervention is likely to decrease the incidence of such error</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Recommend that the pharmacy repackage the containers so that they are distinct from one another  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Repeated error: 4 identical mix-ups → points to a SYSTEM flaw, not 1 person’s lapse.<br /></br>• Contributing clues: 1) Vials “similar in color,” 2) “same-sized container,” 3) “placed next to each other” on cart. All three are classic “look-alike/sound-alike” (LASA) or “look-alike, package-alike” hazards.<br /></br>• Pattern: When the same error happens multiple times, the root cause is usually latent design issues (Swiss-cheese model) rather than knowledge or intent of staff.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify error type → Medication errors due to packaging/label similarity.<br /></br>Step 2: Apply hierarchy of safety interventions.<br /></br>   • Strong (force-function, eliminate, automation)<br /></br>   • Intermediate (standardize, simplify, differentiate packaging)<br /></br>   • Weak (education, warnings, policies)<br /></br>Step 3: Choose the strongest feasible fix: physically differentiate the vials so the wrong drug cannot be selected inadvertently. Repackaging, color-coding, or “tall-man lettering” are textbook solutions.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In patient-safety questions, favor system-level, engineering controls over blaming or retraining individuals.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>(Not applicable; this is a patient-safety/patient-care systems question. The “disease” is the system defect.)  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Safety “treatment”:<br /></br>• Pharmacy repackaging → different size, shape, label color, tall-man lettering (e.g., cefTAZidime vs. cefTAZIDIME)<br /></br>• Physical separation in storage bins<br /></br>• Use of barcode medication administration (BCMA) scanners  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“LASA error? Make the LASA look ASA-part.”<br /></br>L – Look-alike<br /></br>A – Avoid identical packaging<br /></br>S – Separate storage<br /></br>A – Alter label (tall-man, color, warnings)  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Remove the antibiotic from the hospital formulary<br /></br>   • Unrealistic; drug may be essential for other patients. Eliminating needed meds is not a feasible or proportional response.<br /></br>c. Require continuing-education programs for all nurses<br /></br>   • Education is a weak control; errors already occurred despite presumably competent nurses. Does not address root cause (vial similarity).<br /></br>d. Suspend the nurses involved in the four incidents<br /></br>   • Blame-and-punish culture discourages reporting, fails to correct latent system flaws, and does not prevent recurrence.<br /></br>e. No intervention is likely to decrease the incidence of such error<br /></br>   • Contradicts human-factors evidence: packaging changes, barcoding, and separation do reduce LASA errors.  </p></br><p>Key Difference: Choices b–e target people or remove drugs; only choice a targets the DESIGN flaw that triggered the repeated error.</p>
139. A 67-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 1-month history of cough productive of blood-tinged sputum. During this time, she has had one episode of coughing up blood and a decreased appetite resulting in a 2.3-kg (5-lb weight loss. She has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 50 years. Five years ago, she was treated for breast cancer with no metastasis to the lymph nodes. Examination shows no abnormalities. Her serum calcium concentration is 12 mg/dL. A chest x-ray shows a 3-cm left upper lobe mass. A CT scan of the liver and a bone scan show no abnormalities. A biopsy specimen of the lung mass is most likely to show which of the following?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Adenocarcinoma</br>b. Large cell carcinoma</br>c. Melanoma</br>d. Small cell carcinoma</br>e. Squamous cell carcinoma</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e.  Squamous cell carcinoma  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 67-year-old lifelong smoker (50 pack-years) – heavy tobacco use strongly favors smoking-related bronchogenic cancers (squamous, small-cell, large-cell).<br /></br>•  Hemoptysis with a 3-cm lung mass – classic presenting features of centrally located non-small cell carcinomas.<br /></br>• Hypercalcemia (serum Ca²⁺ 12 mg/dL) – a key paraneoplastic clue. In lung cancer, the most tested cause of humoral hypercalcemia is secretion of parathyroid hormone–related peptide (PTHrP) by squamous cell carcinoma.<br /></br>•  No metastatic disease on liver CT or bone scan – supports a paraneoplastic (humoral) source of hypercalcemia rather than osteolytic metastases.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Recognize heavy smoking → favors squamous or small-cell carcinoma.<br /></br>2. Identify paraneoplastic hypercalcemia → points almost uniquely to PTHrP-producing squamous cell carcinoma.<br /></br>3. Integrate with imaging (central/upper-lobe mass) → squamous cell carcinoma commonly arises centrally and cavitates.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Hypercalcemia + smoking + lung mass = think squamous cell carcinoma (PTHrP).</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Squamous cell carcinoma arises from bronchial epithelium, usually central/hilar.<br /></br>• Tumor produces PTHrP → ↑ osteoclastic bone resorption → hypercalcemia, ↓ PTH.<br /></br>• Histology: keratin pearls &amp; intercellular bridges.<br /></br>Risk factors: tobacco smoke, asbestos, radon.</p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• Confirm diagnosis with bronchoscopic or percutaneous biopsy.<br /></br>• Work-up: Serum PTH (low), PTHrP (high), CXR/CT.<br /></br>• Management depends on stage: surgical resection (early), chemoradiation (locally advanced), immunotherapy/targeted therapy if unresectable.<br /></br>• Hypercalcemia: IV fluids + bisphosphonate (e.g., zoledronic acid) acutely.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Squamous = Stones”:<br /></br>S – Smoking<br /></br>S – Central (Sentral)<br /></br>S – Secretes PTHrP → Stones (↑Ca²⁺)<br /></br>S – Keratin “Swirls” (pearls) on histology.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Adenocarcinoma<br /></br>• Most common in women &amp; nonsmokers; usually peripheral, associated with clubbing, not PTHrP hypercalcemia.  </p></br><p>b. Large cell carcinoma<br /></br>• Peripheral or central; may secrete β-hCG (gynecomastia), rarely PTHrP; histology shows pleomorphic giant cells. Less classically linked to hypercalcemia in exams.  </p></br><p>c. Melanoma (metastatic)<br /></br>• Could cause hemoptysis, but patient lacks skin lesion history; hypercalcemia would more likely be from bony mets, which are absent on scan.  </p></br><p>d. Small cell carcinoma<br /></br>• Strong smoking link but paraneoplastic syndromes are SIADH (hyponatremia), ACTH/Cushing, Lambert-Eaton. Hypercalcemia is uncommon. Central mass often with bulky mediastinal adenopathy.  </p></br><p>Key difference: Only squamous cell carcinoma classically produces PTHrP-mediated hypercalcemia in lung cancer questions.</p>
140. A previously healthy 62-year-old woman comes to the emergency department because of a 2-day history of nausea and increasingly severe abdominal pain. She vomited once 6 hours ago but has not had constipation or diarrhea. She had a partial gastrectomy for peptic ulcer disease 20 years ago. Current medications are a multivitamin, calcium and iron supplements, and occasional intramuscular injections of vitamin B₁₂ (cyanocobalamin). Physical examination shows abdominal distention and diffuse tenderness. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin 14.1 g/dL</br>Hematocrit 43%</br>Serum Na⁺ 135 mEq/L</br>K⁺ 3.6 mEq/L</br>Cl⁻ 99 mEq/L</br>HCO₃⁻ 24 mEq/L</br>A CT scan of the abdomen is shown. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Bacterial sequestration</br>b. Intestinal volvulus</br>c. Intussusception</br>d. Jejunal enteritis</br>e. Small-bowel adhesions</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>e. Small-bowel adhesions</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis (key clues)</br>• 62-year-old woman with previous abdominal surgery: “partial gastrectomy for peptic ulcer disease 20 years ago.”<br /></br>   – Prior intra-abdominal operations are the single most common risk factor for adhesive disease.<br /></br>• 48-h history of colicky abdominal pain, nausea, vomiting, distention → classic small-bowel obstruction (SBO) picture.<br /></br>• CT (image not shown to you) – USMLE loves to pair “dilated, air-fluid–filled small-bowel loops with abrupt transition point” with adhesions.<br /></br>• No mention of hernias, inflammatory bowel disease, or mass-like lesion → steers away from other SBO etiologies.</p></br><p>Pertinent negatives</br>• “No constipation or diarrhea” yet → early SBO before complete obstipation.<br /></br>• Normal HCO₃⁻ (24) and Cl⁻ (99) → not the massive hypochloremic metabolic alkalosis seen with large-volume gastric outlet obstruction; electrolytes still relatively preserved = short duration.<br /></br>• No GI bleeding, no systemic infection signs (fever, leukocytosis) → argues against enteritis or strangulated/ischemic bowel at this moment.</p></br><p>Pattern to recognize</br>Older patient + any prior laparotomy/laparoscopy + SBO symptoms = think adhesions first.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)</br>1. Identify SBO: diffuse crampy pain, nausea/vomiting, abdominal distention.<br /></br>2. Establish etiology: postoperative adhesions account for &gt;60% of SBO. Her only major risk factor = prior gastrectomy.<br /></br>3. Rule out alternatives:<br /></br>   • Hernia – none seen/mentioned.<br /></br>   • Tumor/intussusception – rare, classically with “target sign” and often some bleeding.<br /></br>   • Volvulus – more common sigmoid/cecal in elderly; CT usually described with “whirl sign.”<br /></br>   • Inflammatory / infectious enteritis – gives diarrhea, fever, leukocytosis, not obstruction.</p></br><p>Take-home “one-liner”</br>In a patient with a history of abdominal surgery, new small-bowel obstruction is adhesive disease until proven otherwise.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Adhesions = fibrous bands formed after peritoneal inflammation (surgery, infection, trauma).<br /></br>• They tether bowel loops; peristalsis wraps the bowel around the band → obstruction (and possible closed-loop strangulation if both ends trapped).<br /></br>• Time from surgery can be days to decades—scar tissue persists for life.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostics &amp; Management</br>Initial work-up: upright abdominal x-ray or CT with oral/IV contrast.<br /></br>Typical CT:<br /></br>• Dilated proximal small bowel (&gt;2.5 cm diameter).<br /></br>• Air-fluid levels.<br /></br>• Abrupt transition to collapsed distal bowel at adhesive band.  </p></br><p>Treatment (non-strangulated):<br /></br>1. NPO, NG tube decompression.<br /></br>2. IV fluids + electrolyte repletion (K⁺, Cl⁻).<br /></br>3. Serial abdominal exams/labs.<br /></br>4. If signs of ischemia, peritonitis, or failure to improve in 24–48 h → laparoscopic lysis of adhesions.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memory Tips</br>“ABC of SBO causes”<br /></br>A – Adhesions (most common)<br /></br>B – Bulge (hernias)<br /></br>C – Cancer (tumors)  </p></br><p>If previous “Abdomen was Cut,” think Adhesions.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>a. Bacterial sequestration (small-intestinal bacterial overgrowth)<br /></br>   • Presents with bloating, watery diarrhea, malabsorption (B12, fat-soluble vitamin deficiency), not acute obstructive pain/distention. Imaging usually normal.</p></br><p>b. Intestinal volvulus<br /></br>   • Twisting causes closed-loop obstruction often in sigmoid or cecum; risk factors megacolon, chronic constipation. CT: “whirl sign,” markedly dilated sigmoid with coffee-bean on x-ray. Prior gastrectomy does not predispose.</p></br><p>c. Intussusception<br /></br>   • Telescoping bowel leads to episodic colicky pain + “currant-jelly” stools in children; in adults usually subacute and associated with a lead-point tumor. CT shows “target/bull’s-eye” mass. No bleeding mentioned, and adhesions fit better.</p></br><p>d. Jejunal enteritis<br /></br>   • Infectious or inflammatory. Expect fever, leukocytosis, diarrhea. Does not cause mechanical obstruction or dilated air-fluid levels.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>A prior intra-abdominal operation is the dominant risk factor for adhesive bands, which are by far the most common cause of small-bowel obstruction.</p>
141. A 5-year-old girl is brought to the emergency department 20 minutes after the onset of difficulty breathing that began while she was eating a granola bar. Her pulse is 120/min, respirations are 28/min, and blood pressure is 70/50 mm Hg. Examination shows stridor. There are erythematous wheals over the top half of her body. Which of the following is the most appropriate initial step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Admission to the hospital for observation</br>b. Administration of an antihistamine</br>c. Administration of epinephrine</br>d. Intravenous administration of fluids</br>e. Endotracheal intubation</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Administration of epinephrine (IM 0.01 mg/kg of 1:1,000 solution, max 0.5 mg)  </p></br><p>— Note: The answer key you supplied lists “Admission for observation” as correct. That is not consistent with current emergency-management guidelines or NBME/USMLE standards. The first, lifesaving step in suspected anaphylaxis is immediate intramuscular epinephrine. All other interventions (airway support, IV fluids, antihistamines, observation) are secondary. —</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Sudden onset while eating: suggests allergic trigger (peanut/tree-nut in granola).<br /></br>• Airway/respiratory compromise: stridor, RR 28.<br /></br>• Skin involvement: erythematous wheals.<br /></br>• Hemodynamic instability: BP 70/50 (profound hypotension), HR 120.<br /></br>Pattern = multi-system involvement (skin + respiratory + cardiovascular) → textbook anaphylaxis.</p></br><p>Pertinent negatives: no mention of focal airway obstruction (e.g., foreign body); no fever/infection signs; timing (20 min) fits IgE-mediated reaction.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Recognize anaphylaxis: acute onset PLUS (skin/mucosa) AND (respiratory compromise OR hypotension).<br /></br>2. Immediate priority is to halt progression and reverse bronchoconstriction &amp; vasodilation.<br /></br>3. IM epinephrine is the only intervention that rapidly addresses ALL pathophysiologic components—α1 vasoconstriction (↑BP, ↓angioedema), β1 (↑CO), β2 (bronchodilation, ↓mediator release).<br /></br>Take-home: “Epi first, epi fast, epi IM.” Delay is the chief cause of fatal outcomes.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• IgE antibodies on mast cells/basophils cross-link with allergen → massive release of histamine, tryptase, leukotrienes, prostaglandins.<br /></br>• Results: capillary leakage → hypotension; bronchial smooth-muscle constriction → stridor/wheeze; dermal edema → urticaria.<br /></br>• Epinephrine directly counteracts these effects and stabilizes mast cells.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Initial (within seconds):<br /></br>1. IM epinephrine (anterolateral thigh). Re-dose every 5–15 min PRN.<br /></br>2. Place supine, legs elevated; high-flow O₂; cardiac/SpO₂ monitoring.<br /></br>3. Large-bore IV crystalloids if hypotension persists.<br /></br>Adjuncts (after epi): H1 blocker (diphenhydramine), H2 blocker (ranitidine), corticosteroid (methylprednisolone) to prevent biphasic reaction.<br /></br>Observation 4–6 h (mild) or ≥24 h (severe/refractory) AFTER stabilization.</p></br><p>No diagnostic test is required acutely; serum tryptase can confirm later.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“EPI” mnemonic for order of therapy:<br /></br>E – Epinephrine IM first<br /></br>P – Position &amp; Pressors/IV fluids<br /></br>I – Inhaled oxygen &amp; adjuncts (antihistamines, steroids)</p></br><p>Visual: Picture a bright red “Epi-Pen” as the fire extinguisher you grab before anything else when a patient “catches fire” with anaphylaxis.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Admission for observation<br /></br>• Appropriate only AFTER patient is stabilized. Delaying epinephrine increases mortality.  </p></br><p>b. Antihistamine<br /></br>• Relieves cutaneous symptoms; does NOT reverse airway edema or hypotension. Adjunct, not first-line.  </p></br><p>d. IV fluids<br /></br>• Helpful for distributive shock but does not treat bronchospasm or prevent further mediator release. Should follow epinephrine if hypotension persists.  </p></br><p>e. Endotracheal intubation<br /></br>• Consider if airway obstruction worsens or no response to epi, but intubation without stabilizing hemodynamics can precipitate arrest. Epi may obviate need for intubation.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Assuming “secure the airway” or “give fluids” comes first in every shock scenario. In anaphylaxis, epinephrine addresses BOTH airway and circulation simultaneously and should precede other steps.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Key Takeaway<br /></br>Anaphylaxis = multisystem allergic reaction → Treat immediately with intramuscular epinephrine. Every second of delay raises the risk of fatal airway obstruction or cardiovascular collapse.</p>
142. A 24-year-old woman comes to the emergency department because of a 1-week history of weakness and occasional palpitations. She admits that she uses laxatives daily to purge herself after binge eating baked goods. During the last month, she has had to increase the dose of laxative to achieve the same effect. There is no history of vomiting. She appears well hydrated. She is 160 cm (5 ft 3 in) tall and weighs 54 kg, or 120lb; BMI is 21 kg/m². While supine, her pulse is 80/min, and blood pressure is 120/80 mm Hg. While standing, her pulse is 90/min, and blood pressure is 80/55 mm Hg; she reports light-headedness when she first stands up. Examination shows no other abnormalities. Which of the following sets of laboratory findings is most likely in this patient?</br>Serum   Arterial Blood Gas Analysis on Room Air</br>K⁺ (mEq/L) pH Pco₂ (mm Hg) Po₂ (mm Hg) HCO₃⁻ (mEq/L)	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. 6.5  7.3  25     92    12</br>b. 2.7  7.5  46     86    34</br>c. 3   7.3  30     90    14</br>d. 4   7.4  40     90    26</br>e. 3.7  7.5  29     88    24</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Serum K⁺ 3 mEq/L | pH 7.3 | Pco₂ 30 mm Hg | Po₂ 90 mm Hg | HCO₃⁻ 14 mEq/L  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Daily laxative use” → chronic secretory/osmotic diarrhea.<br /></br>• “Increase the dose to achieve same effect” → ongoing, high-volume stool losses.<br /></br>• Orthostatic hypotension + light-headedness → intravascular volume depletion.<br /></br>• NO vomiting → rules out gastric H⁺/Cl⁻ loss (metabolic alkalosis).<br /></br>Key pattern: Chronic diarrhea → loss of HCO₃⁻ and K⁺ in stool → normal-anion-gap (hyperchloremic) metabolic acidosis with hypokalemia.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify purging mechanism.<br /></br>   • Vomiting → metabolic alkalosis.<br /></br>   • Diuretics → metabolic alkalosis.<br /></br>   • Laxatives/diarrhea → metabolic acidosis.<br /></br>2. Decide acid-base disorder: metabolic acidosis (low pH, low HCO₃⁻).<br /></br>3. Predict compensation: Winter’s formula (Pco₂ ≈ 1.5[HCO₃⁻] + 8 ± 2).<br /></br>   • For HCO₃⁻ ≈ 14 → expected Pco₂ ≈ 29–33 mm Hg (matches 30 mm Hg).<br /></br>4. Anticipate electrolytes: GI K⁺ loss + secondary hyperaldosteronism → low serum K⁺ (~3 mEq/L).<br /></br>Answer choice “c” fits all.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Persistent diarrhea = normal-gap metabolic acidosis + hypokalemia; use Winter’s formula to check respiratory compensation.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Colon secretes K⁺ and HCO₃⁻; high-volume stool losses remove both.<br /></br>• Volume depletion → RAAS activation → ↑aldosterone → renal K⁺ wasting, amplifying hypokalemia.<br /></br>• No gastric/renal acid loss, so anion gap stays normal (Cl⁻ rises as HCO₃⁻ falls).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics (board-relevant)<br /></br>1. Stop laxative.<br /></br>2. Volume resuscitation with isotonic saline.<br /></br>3. Replace potassium.<br /></br>4. Address underlying eating disorder (bulimia nervosa, purging-type) with CBT ± SSRI (fluoxetine).<br /></br>No additional imaging is required acutely; basic metabolic panel and venous/arterial blood gas confirm diagnosis.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Diarrhea DUMPS base and K⁺”:<br /></br>• D = Diarrhea<br /></br>• U = Understand it causes<br /></br>• M = Metabolic<br /></br>• P = acidosis (pH↓) with<br /></br>• S = Small (normal) anion gap<br /></br>Remember: Vomiting = Alkalosis, Diarrhea = Acidosis.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. K⁺ 6.5 / pH 7.3 / HCO₃⁻ 12 (metabolic acidosis + hyperkalemia). Hyperkalemia suggests renal failure or cell lysis, not pure diarrheal loss.<br /></br>b. K⁺ 2.7 / pH 7.5 / HCO₃⁻ 34 (metabolic alkalosis). Fits vomiting or loop/thiazide diuretics, not laxative-induced diarrhea.<br /></br>d. Normal values—doesn’t explain orthostatic symptoms or chronic purging.<br /></br>e. pH 7.5 with normal HCO₃⁻ (respiratory alkalosis) plus near-normal K⁺—would require hyperventilation scenario, not present here.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Chronic laxative use → stool HCO₃⁻ &amp; K⁺ loss → normal-gap metabolic acidosis with hypokalemia and appropriate respiratory compensation: choice “c”.</p>
143. A 67-year-old woman is admitted to the hospital because of fever, weakness, light-headedness, and right lower back pain 3 days after beginning nitrofurantoin for a urinary tract infection.</br>She has a 10-year history of recurrent urinary tract infections with enterococci and several gram-negative organisms that have been resistant to ampicillin and cephalosporins.</br>She has no other history of serious illness. She appears acutely ill.</br>Her temperature is 39.5°C (103.1°F), pulse is 120/min, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 98/68 mm Hg.</br>On examination, there is right costovertebral angle tenderness.</br>Urinalysis shows clumps of WBCs too numerous to count.</br>Treatment with piperacillin-tazobactam and gentamicin is begun.</br>In addition to administration of intravenous fluids, which of the following is the most appropriate initial management to decrease this patient's risk for acute renal failure?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Measurement of central venous pressure</br>b. Measurement of plasma aminoglycoside trough concentrations</br>c. Discontinuation of piperacillin-tazobactam</br>d. Administration of bicarbonate</br>e. Low-dose dopamine therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Measurement of central venous pressure (CVP)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Septic picture: fever 103 °F, tachycardia 120/min, tachypnea 24/min, BP 98/68 → systemic inflammatory response + hypotension.<br /></br>• Source: right-sided pyelonephritis (CVA tenderness, innumerable WBCs in urine).<br /></br>• Highest near-term threat to the kidneys = sepsis-induced hypoperfusion (prerenal injury) that can progress to acute tubular necrosis.<br /></br>• She is already receiving IV fluids; the next step is to be sure they are being given in adequate—but not excessive—amounts.  </p></br><p>Key positives → septic shock physiology, aminoglycoside on board (adds nephrotoxic risk).<br /></br>Key negatives → no mention of volume status monitoring yet.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach in early goal-directed therapy for sepsis:<br /></br>1. Rapid broad-spectrum antibiotics.<br /></br>2. Aggressive volume resuscitation with crystalloids.<br /></br>3. Hemodynamic monitoring to make sure the patient is actually getting enough preload.  </p></br><p>Central venous pressure measurement (target 8–12 mm Hg) is the quickest, bedside way to guide ongoing fluid resuscitation and protect renal perfusion. If CVP is low, give more fluids; if adequate, add vasopressors. This directly reduces the risk of prerenal azotemia → acute renal failure.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In septic patients the most immediate, modifiable renal risk is under-resuscitation; monitor CVP to titrate fluids.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Sepsis → peripheral vasodilation + capillary leak → ↓ effective arterial blood volume → ↓ renal perfusion → prerenal azotemia → ischemic ATN.<br /></br>• Aminoglycosides cause renal cortex accumulation and free-radical injury, but this requires several doses and is potentiated by hypoperfusion.<br /></br>• Restoring/maintaining intravascular volume (CVP 8–12 mm Hg) preserves GFR and oxygen delivery.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Pearls<br /></br>• Early goal-directed therapy targets: CVP 8–12 mm Hg, MAP ≥65 mm Hg, urine output ≥0.5 mL/kg/h, ScvO₂ ≥70 %.<br /></br>• If CVP goal reached yet MAP &lt;65 → start norepinephrine.<br /></br>• Check gentamicin troughs after 2nd–3rd dose; keep trough &lt;2 µg/mL to minimize nephrotoxicity.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tip<br /></br>“Septic ATN PREVENTION = ‘C-MAP’ ”<br /></br>C – CVP 8–12 mm Hg<br /></br>M – MAP ≥65 mm Hg<br /></br>A – Antibiotics ASAP<br /></br>P – Plenty of fluids (guided by CVP)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Measurement of plasma aminoglycoside trough concentrations<br /></br>• Important, but nephrotoxicity develops over days; does not address the immediate prerenal threat. Initial priority is perfusion monitoring.</p></br><p>c. Discontinuation of piperacillin-tazobactam<br /></br>• Piperacillin is not classically nephrotoxic and provides crucial gram-negative + Enterococcus coverage. Stopping it would jeopardize source control.</p></br><p>d. Administration of bicarbonate<br /></br>• Urinary alkalinization prevents myoglobin/hemoglobin precipitation, not septic or aminoglycoside nephrotoxicity. No metabolic acidosis given.</p></br><p>e. Low-dose dopamine therapy<br /></br>• “Renal-dose” dopamine has not been shown to prevent or treat acute kidney injury and may cause tachyarrhythmias. No longer recommended.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In a septic patient already receiving IV fluids, the FIRST maneuver to reduce the risk of acute renal failure is to guide and titrate those fluids with an objective preload measure—central venous pressure monitoring.</p>
144. A 47-year-old woman comes to the physician for a routine health maintenance examination. She has no history of serious illness or abnormal Pap smears. Menses occur at irregular 45-day intervals, last 5 days, and are not painful; menstrual flow is normal. She has no intermenstrual bleeding. Her last visit to a physician was 2 years ago; at that time, a Pap smear showed no abnormalities. She has not been sexually active for 5 years. Her father was diagnosed with colon cancer, cardiovascular disease, hypertension, and type 2 diabetes mellitus in his 60s. The patient is 157 cm (5 ft 2 in) tall and weighs 82 kg, or 180lb; BMI is 33 kg/m². Her blood pressure is 125/75 mm Hg. Physical examination, including pelvic examination, shows no other abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate recommendation?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Annual Pap smear</br>b. DEXA scan</br>c. ECG now</br>d. Mammography every 3 years</br>e. Measurement of fasting serum glucose concentration now</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Measurement of fasting serum glucose concentration now  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age 47 y, BMI 33 kg/m² → patient is middle-aged AND obese (BMI ≥30).<br /></br>• No acute complaints; question is purely about preventive care.<br /></br>• Last Pap smear was normal 2 y ago → currently up-to-date.<br /></br>• Father developed type 2 diabetes and multiple cardiometabolic conditions → family history raises her baseline risk further.<br /></br>The stem is therefore probing which USPSTF/ACP adult-screening recommendation is due right now in an asymptomatic, obese, 47-y-old woman.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Identify guideline that links age + obesity to a screening test.<br /></br>2. USPSTF Grade B: Screen for type 2 DM (fasting plasma glucose, HbA1c, or 2-h OGTT) in adults 35-70 y who are overweight (BMI ≥25) or obese (BMI ≥30); repeat every 3 y if normal.<br /></br>3. She falls squarely into 35-70 y + BMI 33 → screening is indicated now.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Obesity triggers diabetes screening beginning at age 35 under current U.S. guidelines.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Obesity → increased visceral adiposity → insulin resistance → pancreatic β-cell overwork → progressive failure → overt hyperglycemia. Early identification via fasting plasma glucose (FPG ≥126 mg/dL ×2, or HbA1c ≥6.5%) allows lifestyle and/or metformin intervention, preventing micro- and macro-vascular complications.  </p></br><p>Treatments / Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• First-line screen: Fasting plasma glucose (≥8-h fast).<br /></br>  - Normal: &lt;100 mg/dL<br /></br>  - Prediabetes: 100-125 mg/dL → counsel on diet/exercise, consider metformin if high-risk.<br /></br>  - Diabetes: ≥126 mg/dL on two occasions → confirm, then initiate comprehensive DM management (lifestyle + metformin, ASCVD risk control, eye/foot exams, etc.).<br /></br>• Alternative approved tests: HbA1c or 2-h 75-g OGTT; choose based on practicality.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“35-70 WITH A BELLY = CHECK THE JELLY (blood sugar).”<br /></br>OR<br /></br>“Overweight over thirty-five? Screen to keep pancreas alive.”  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Annual Pap smear<br /></br>  • Cervical cancer screening for 21-65 y women with cytology alone is every 3 years (not yearly). She is current (last test 2 y ago).<br /></br>  • Co-testing (Pap + HPV) q5 y is another option at ≥30 y, also not due yet.  </p></br><p>b. DEXA scan<br /></br>  • Routine osteoporosis screening begins at 65 y in average-risk women. Earlier only with major risk factors (e.g., long-term steroids, premature menopause). Obesity is actually protective for BMD.  </p></br><p>c. ECG now<br /></br>  • No society recommends routine resting ECG in asymptomatic, low-risk adults. Only indicated if cardiovascular symptoms or known CAD equivalents.  </p></br><p>d. Mammography every 3 years<br /></br>  • USPSTF: Biennial screening mammography for women 50-74 y (Grade B). Initiation at 40-49 y is individualized but frequency is still every 1-2 y, not every 3 y. She is 47 y; if chosen, it would be biennial, not triennial.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall Highlight<br /></br>Students often default to Pap or mammogram for any middle-aged female vignette. Remember to check timing intervals and guideline age cut-offs before choosing.  </p></br><p>Key Take-Home Message<br /></br>In adults 35-70 y with BMI ≥ 25 (overweight/obese), screening for type 2 diabetes with fasting plasma glucose (or HbA1c/OGTT) is recommended every 3 years.</p>
145. A 47-year-old woman comes to the clinic because of a 3-year history of morning fatigue and an uncomfortable sensation in her legs. The sensation is relieved by walking or by tapping her feet if she is sitting. Her husband sleeps in another bed because she frequently shifts and disturbs his sleep. She has a 3-year history of anemia treated with iron supplementation. She has no other history of serious illness and takes no other medications. Her father has major depressive disorder, her mother has type 2 diabetes mellitus and osteoarthritis, and her brother has type 2 diabetes mellitus. On examination of the patient, muscle strength is 5/5 and symmetric in the lower extremities. Sensation to light touch is intact. Straight-leg raise test is negative bilaterally. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Doppler ultrasonography of the lower extremities</br>b. EEG</br>c. Measurement of fasting serum glucose concentration</br>d. Measurement of serum ferritin concentration</br>e. No further testing is indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Measurement of serum ferritin concentration</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis – Why this is Restless Legs Syndrome (RLS)</br>• Key phrases: “uncomfortable sensation in her legs,” “relieved by walking or tapping,” “worse at night (disturbs husband’s sleep),” “morning fatigue.”<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: Normal strength, intact sensation, straight-leg raise negative → rules out radiculopathy, neuropathy, spinal disease.<br /></br>• Chronic iron-deficiency anemia in history—classic secondary cause of RLS.</p></br><p>Classic RLS diagnostic tetrad (memorize “URGE”):</br>U – Urge to move the legs<br /></br>R – Rest induces symptoms<br /></br>G – Getting up/movement relieves symptoms<br /></br>E – Evening or night predominance  </p></br><p>All four are satisfied.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning &amp; Core Take-away<br /></br>Step 1: Recognize clinical picture = RLS.<br /></br>Step 2: RLS is often idiopathic but iron deficiency is the most common treatable secondary cause, even when Hb is normal.<br /></br>Step 3: Guidelines (AASM, UpToDate) ⇒ check serum ferritin; if &lt;75 ng/mL give iron.<br /></br>Therefore the next diagnostic step = serum ferritin level.</p></br><p>Bottom line: Any patient with RLS symptoms needs a ferritin level because iron replacement can cure or markedly improve symptoms.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology High-Yield Review</br>• Low iron in CNS → ↓ iron-dependent tyrosine hydroxylase activity → ↓ dopamine synthesis in substantia nigra → sensory urge &amp; motor restlessness.<br /></br>• Secondary causes: Iron deficiency, ESRD, pregnancy, Parkinson disease, neuropathy, multiple sclerosis, certain drugs (e.g., SSRIs, metoclopramide).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics Snapshot</br>1. Diagnostic work-up<br /></br>   – Serum ferritin (goal &gt;75 ng/mL) ± serum iron/TIBC.<br /></br>2. If ferritin low → oral or IV iron (ferrous sulfate 325 mg TID with vitamin C).<br /></br>3. Persistent symptoms despite normal ferritin → dopamine agonists (pramipexole, ropinirole) or α2-δ Ca-channel ligands (gabapentin, pregabalin).<br /></br>4. Avoid exacerbating drugs: antihistamines, SSRIs, dopamine antagonists.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“RLS patients have an IRON URGE”<br /></br>IRON → always check Iron (ferritin);<br /></br>URGE → Urge, Rest, Gets better, Evening.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>a. Doppler ultrasonography of the lower extremities<br /></br>   • Used to evaluate DVT or PAD. DVT pain is constant, not relieved by movement; PAD pain is exercise-induced claudication, not nocturnal restlessness.</p></br><p>b. EEG<br /></br>   • Detects seizures or sleep disorders like nocturnal epilepsy; RLS diagnosis is clinical plus ferritin.</p></br><p>c. Measurement of fasting serum glucose concentration<br /></br>   • Diabetic peripheral neuropathy causes numbness/tingling, stocking-glove distribution, sensory loss—absent here.</p></br><p>e. No further testing is indicated<br /></br>   • Would miss a readily treatable secondary cause. Current guidelines recommend ferritin measurement in all patients with suspected RLS.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Take-Home Message</br>When you see nocturnal leg discomfort relieved by movement, think RLS and always order a serum ferritin; iron repletion is first-line therapy if ferritin &lt;75 ng/mL.</p>
146. An 87-year-old woman is brought to the emergency department by her daughter because of pain in her hand and wrist after tripping and catching herself with her outstretched right hand. The daughter says the patient has 'frail bones' and is taking calcium and vitamin D supplements. Medical history is remarkable for hypertension and degenerative joint disease. Her only other medication is hydrochlorothiazide. She had a tetanus shot 4 years ago. Vital signs are temperature 37.3°C (99.1°F), pulse 86/min, respirations 13/min, and blood pressure 118/65 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 97%. Physical examination shows an ecchymosis and an abrasion of the right palm. There is swelling and diffuse tenderness of the right wrist. X-rays of the right hand and wrist show a scaphoid fracture. Which of the following is the most likely complication of this patient's fracture?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Distal fat embolus</br>b. Nonunion</br>c. Osteomyelitis</br>d. Poor angulation</br>e. Wrist fusion</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Nonunion (secondary to avascular necrosis of the proximal scaphoid fragment)</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Mechanism: “Tripping and catching herself with her outstretched hand” → classic FOOSH (fall on out-stretched hand).<br /></br>• Location: Diffuse wrist tenderness; radiograph confirms a scaphoid fracture.<br /></br>• At-risk population: Elderly woman with presumed osteoporosis (“frail bones”).<br /></br>• Key pattern to recognize: Scaphoid = most frequently fractured carpal bone; blood enters distally and travels proximally (retrograde flow). Any break through the waist can cut off the blood supply to the proximal pole.</p></br><p>Pertinent positives: FOOSH + wrist pain/swelling + radiographic scaphoid break.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: No mention of femur/pelvis fracture (fat embolus), penetrating wound (osteomyelitis), or distal radius deformity (poor angulation).</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify injury → scaphoid fracture.<br /></br>2. Recall high-yield complication → avascular necrosis (AVN) of the proximal pole → impaired healing → NONUNION.<br /></br>3. Choose answer that best describes that complication → Nonunion.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Retrograde radial-artery flow makes the proximal scaphoid uniquely prone to AVN and failure to unite.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Blood supply: Dorsal carpal branch of the radial artery enters distally → travels proximally.<br /></br>• Waist fractures (most common site) sever inflow, leaving proximal bone ischemic.<br /></br>• Ischemia → necrosis → bone resorption and collapse → persistent gap = nonunion.<br /></br>Risk factors: Delayed diagnosis, displacement &gt;1 mm, smoking, advanced age.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Management Pearls<br /></br>• Initial treatment: Thumb-spica cast even if initial x-ray is negative (occult fractures common).<br /></br>• Imaging: Repeat x-ray, CT, or MRI at 7-10 days if suspicion remains.<br /></br>• ORIF (open reduction &amp; internal fixation) if displaced or high-risk for nonunion.<br /></br>• Monitor: Serial x-rays for union; if nonunion develops → bone grafting ± vascularized graft.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Quick Mnemonic<br /></br>“Scaphoid = SCARe”:<br /></br>S – Snuffbox pain<br /></br>C – Cast even if x-ray Clear<br /></br>A – AVN<br /></br>R – Retrograde blood supply → leads to Nonunion</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Distal fat embolus<br /></br>• Seen after long-bone (femur), pelvic, or multiple fractures—not isolated carpal injuries. Presents with hypoxemia, petechiae, AMS 24-72 h later. No respiratory or neuro clues here.</p></br><p>c. Osteomyelitis<br /></br>• Requires open fracture, penetrating wound, systemic infection, or immune compromise. Closed scaphoid fractures rarely seed infection.</p></br><p>d. Poor angulation<br /></br>• Complication of distal radius (Colles/Smith) fractures; manifests as “dinner-fork” deformity, altered carrying angle—not typical for scaphoid.</p></br><p>e. Wrist fusion (radiocarpal/partial carpal arthrodesis)<br /></br>• Salvage procedure for severe degenerative arthritis or chronic instability—far downstream, not the most likely early complication.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────<br /></br>High-Yield Memory Hook<br /></br>“Break the boat (scaphoid) and you may not get it to float together again.” → think AVN → nonunion.</p>
147. A 23-year-old man who is a landscaper comes to the office because of a 3-month history of lethargy, diffuse muscle aches, and knee pain. He also had a rash on his left knee 3 months ago similar to the one shown in the photograph. One month ago, he had an episode of right knee pain that resolved after 5 days. He now has left knee pain. Temperature is 37.6°C (99.7°F), pulse is 100/min, and respirations are 16/min. Physical examination shows swelling of the left knee; there is no rash. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Blood culture</br>b. CT scan of the head</br>c. Joint aspiration</br>d. Serum testing for antibodies to Borrelia burgdorferi</br>e. Skin biopsy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>d. Serum testing for antibodies to Borrelia burgdorferi  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Landscaper” → frequent exposure to Ixodes (deer) ticks.<br /></br>• “Rash … similar to the photograph” 3 mo ago → classic erythema migrans (bull’s-eye lesion) = early localized Lyme disease.<br /></br>• “Intermittent knee pain, now involving the opposite knee” → migratory, large-joint oligoarthritis typical of late Lyme disease (Lyme arthritis).<br /></br>• Low-grade fever, constitutional symptoms, no current rash → fits late (months-later) manifestation rather than acute septic arthritis.<br /></br>• Key negatives: no penetrating trauma, no acute toxic appearance, no neurologic findings; rash not present now (so you no longer treat empirically without testing).  </p></br><p>Pattern: Outdoor exposure + prior EM rash + months-later mono/oligoarthritis = Lyme arthritis → next diagnostic step = 2-tier serology (ELISA followed by Western blot).  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Identify likely etiology → Lyme borreliosis (Borrelia burgdorferi).<br /></br>2. Recognize current stage → late-stage arthritis after early localized infection.<br /></br>3. Decide on best diagnostic confirmation → Because the pathognomonic rash is no longer present, CDC/IDSA guidelines recommend serologic testing (ELISA ± Western blot) before treatment.<br /></br>Core takeaway: In a patient with possible late Lyme manifestations (arthritis, neuroborreliosis) but WITHOUT the characteristic erythema migrans lesion at presentation, the next step is Lyme serology.  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Etiology: Borrelia burgdorferi spirochete transmitted by Ixodes scapularis.<br /></br>• Early localized (days-wks): erythema migrans ± flu-like symptoms.<br /></br>• Early disseminated (wks-mo): multiple EM lesions, carditis (AV block), cranial neuropathies (CN VII palsy), meningitis.<br /></br>• Late (mo-yrs): mono- or oligoarthritis of large joints (knee most common), peripheral neuropathy, encephalopathy.<br /></br>• Lyme arthritis pathogenesis: immune complex–mediated synovitis; spirochetes may be present in synovial tissue.  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Management &amp; Diagnostics (succinct)<br /></br>1. Diagnosis<br /></br>   • Two-step testing: ELISA (or IFA) → if positive/equivocal, confirm with IgM/IgG Western blot.<br /></br>   • Synovial fluid PCR is second-line if serology inconclusive.<br /></br>2. Treatment (once confirmed)<br /></br>   • Oral doxycycline 100 mg BID × 28 days (first-line).<br /></br>   • Alternatives: amoxicillin or cefuroxime if pregnant, &lt;8 yr, or doxycycline-intolerant.<br /></br>   • IV ceftriaxone 2 g/day × 28 days for severe/refractory arthritis or neurologic disease.  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“MEAN LATE” for Lyme disease chronology:<br /></br>M – Migratory rash (EM) → Early<br /></br>E – Early symptoms (flu-like)<br /></br>A – AV block (carditis)<br /></br>N – Nerve palsy (Bell’s)<br /></br>L – Large-joint arthritis → Late<br /></br>A – Autoantibodies (positive serology)<br /></br>T – Test with ELISA → Western blot<br /></br>E – Extended doxycycline (28 days)  </p></br><p>Visual mnemonic: Think of a DEER (outdoor exposure) kicking first one knee, then the other months later. Before treating the kicks (arthritis), you need “to ELIS-ten” (ELISA) for Borrelia music.  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Blood culture<br /></br>   • Useful for hematogenous septic arthritis (e.g., S. aureus) with high fevers/toxic appearance. Lyme arthritis rarely yields positive blood cultures.<br /></br>b. CT scan of the head<br /></br>   • Reserved for neurologic complications (e.g., encephalitis, abscess). No CNS signs here.<br /></br>c. Joint aspiration<br /></br>   • Arthrocentesis is indicated when septic arthritis, crystalline arthritis, or hemarthrosis is strongly suspected. This patient’s subacute, migratory pattern with low-grade fever and prior EM rash points to Lyme, not an acute septic joint. Synovial fluid in Lyme is inflammatory but nonspecific; diagnosis still hinges on serology.<br /></br>e. Skin biopsy<br /></br>   • EM is diagnosed clinically; biopsy is unnecessary and would be unhelpful months after the rash resolved.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Jumping to arthrocentesis for any swollen knee. Remember: in classic Lyme setting without toxic features, confirm with serology first; treat empirically only if EM is present at the time of visit.</p>
148. A 16-year-old girl comes to the office alone for a physical examination required for participation in athletics. The patient immediately states that results of a home urine pregnancy test 2 days ago were positive. Her last menstrual period was 2 months ago. She has had mild nausea but no vomiting or fever. Medical history is noncontributory, and she takes no medications. Family history is remarkable for a congenital heart defect, craniosynostosis, and severe cognitive impairment in her brother caused by an unbalanced chromosomal rearrangement; her father is known to be a carrier of a balanced chromosomal translocation and is asymptomatic. The patient lives with her stepmother and biological father, who has full custody. She is at the 50th percentile for height, weight, and BMI. Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. Serum β-hCG testing confirms the pregnancy. The patient reports that she does not want to have a child with the same condition as her brother and requests genetic testing for herself. It is most appropriate to obtain informed consent for the requested genetic testing in this patient from which of the following individuals?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. A court-appointed advocate</br>b. The patient</br>c. The patient's biological mother</br>d. The patient's father</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. The patient  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 16-year-old = legal minor.<br /></br>• Pregnant: positive home and serum β-hCG, 2-month amenorrhea.<br /></br>• Requests genetic testing on herself because of family history of a balanced translocation that could affect her fetus.<br /></br>Key clue: Pregnancy places the encounter in the realm of “pregnancy-related medical care.” Most U.S. jurisdictions grant minors the right to consent for their own obstetric/prenatal services, including diagnostic testing that affects the pregnancy. No evidence of mental incapacity, abuse, or need for a surrogate decision-maker.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process<br /></br>1. Determine if the patient has decision-making capacity → Yes (alert, understands situation, expresses a reasoned choice).<br /></br>2. Ask whether a minor can legally consent for this type of care → In nearly every state, pregnancy creates an exception (“medical emancipation”) that allows minors to consent to prenatal/obstetric care. Prenatal genetic testing falls under that umbrella because the results influence pregnancy management.<br /></br>3. Therefore, informed consent should be obtained directly from the patient.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Pregnant minors are generally authorized to make independent medical decisions about their own obstetric and prenatal care, including genetic, laboratory, and imaging studies.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology / Counseling Pearls (brief)<br /></br>• Balanced translocation carriers are phenotypically normal but can produce gametes with unbalanced chromosomal content → risk of miscarriage, congenital anomalies, or intellectual disability in offspring.<br /></br>• Prenatal options once carrier status is known: chorionic villus sampling, amniocentesis, or pre-implantation genetic testing if future IVF is chosen.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>First step for this patient: karyotype or chromosomal microarray to detect any balanced translocation. If positive, offer partner testing and discuss prenatal diagnostic options.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“PAST” mnemonic for minor self-consent:<br /></br>• P – Pregnancy/obstetric care<br /></br>• A – Addiction treatment<br /></br>• S – Sexual health (STIs, contraception)<br /></br>• T – mental health Therapy<br /></br>If the visit falls into PAST, the minor signs the consent.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Court-appointed advocate<br /></br>• Needed only if the patient lacks decision-making capacity or is a ward of the state with no guardian. Neither applies here.  </p></br><p>c. The patient’s biological mother<br /></br>• Has no legal custody; even if she did, pregnancy exemption permits the patient to consent herself.  </p></br><p>d. The patient’s father<br /></br>• He has legal custody but his consent is not required for pregnancy-related medical decisions. Seeking it without the patient’s permission would violate confidentiality.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Believing that minors always require parental permission. Remember statutory “medical emancipation” categories (pregnancy, STIs, substance abuse, mental health).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>High-Yield Take-Home Message<br /></br>Pregnant minors with intact decision-making capacity can independently consent to prenatal and obstetric evaluations, including genetic testing.</p>
149. A 37-year-old man is brought to the emergency department 45 minutes after the sudden onset of severe, sharp pain in his back and chest while he was playing basketball. He rates his pain as an 8 on a 10-point scale. After playing for 2 hours, he had the onset of pain and then had loss of consciousness for 45 seconds. He has Marfan syndrome and long-standing gastroesophageal reflux disease treated with a proton pump inhibitor. On arrival, he is alert and oriented to person, place, and time. He is 198 cm (6 ft 6 in) tall and weighs 77 kg, or 170lb; BMI is 20 kg/m2. His temperature is 37.8 °C (100 °F), pulse is 120/min, respirations are 26/min, and blood pressure is 95/65 mm Hg. Examination shows dry mucous membranes. Breath sounds are distant at the left lung base. There is dullness to percussion on the left. Fluid resuscitation is begun. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Measurement of cardiac enzyme activities</br>b. CT scan of the chest</br>c. Ventilation-perfusion lung scans</br>d. Oral rehydration therapy</br>e. Esophageal manometry</br>f. Placement of a chest tube on the left</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. CT scan of the chest (with IV contrast)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis (Pertinent Positives &amp; Negatives)</p></br><ol></br><li>Marfan syndrome → major risk factor for cystic medial degeneration of the aorta → predisposes to thoracic aortic aneurysm/dissection.</li></br><li>Sudden, severe “sharp” chest + back pain with brief syncope → classic presentation of an acute aortic catastrophe.</li></br><li>Hypotension (95/65) &amp; tachycardia (120/min) → suggests significant blood loss or obstructive process.</li></br><li>Left-sided dullness to percussion + decreased breath sounds → pleural effusion, almost certainly hemothorax if the aorta has ruptured into the pleural space (classically the left pleura).</li></br><li>No trauma, no pleuritic symptoms, no calf tenderness → argues against pneumothorax, pulmonary embolism, or pneumonia.</li></br></ol></br><p>Putting the clues together: “Marfan + tearing chest/back pain + left hemothorax” = ruptured descending thoracic aortic dissection.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning / Next Step</p></br><p>• Management of suspected aortic dissection hinges on RAPID CONFIRMATORY IMAGING.</br>  – Hemodynamically stable → CT angiography of the chest, abdomen, pelvis.</br>  – Hemodynamically unstable or renal insufficiency → transesophageal echocardiogram (TEE) in the trauma bay/OR.</p></br><p>The patient, although hypotensive, is still awake and has already received IV fluids; CT angiography is the fastest, most definitive study in most EDs. Confirming the diagnosis guides the two lifesaving steps that follow: (1) emergent vascular surgery / cardiothoracic surgery and (2) blood pressure control (β-blocker ± vasodilator).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology High-Yield Review</p></br><p>• Marfan → fibrillin-1 defect → cystic medial necrosis → ascending &amp; descending aortic aneurysms/dissections.</br>• Dissection may rupture into:</br>  – Pericardial sac → tamponade (most common cause of death in ascending dissections).</br>  – Pleural cavity (usually left) → massive hemothorax, as in this vignette.</br>• Classic CXR finding: widened mediastinum; but CT angiography is diagnostic gold standard.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearls</p></br><ol></br><li>Immediate blood pressure and heart rate control → IV β-blocker (esmolol) ± nitroprusside after imaging if time allows.</li></br><li>Type A (ascending) → emergent surgery; Type B (descending) → medical therapy unless complications (rupture, malperfusion) → then endovascular repair.</li></br><li>Chest tube for hemothorax is performed only AFTER the aortic source is identified and the surgical team is ready; blindly draining the pleural cavity can remove the “tamponade” effect and precipitate exsanguination.</li></br></ol></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</p></br><p>“Marfan’s MaJoR CAT”<br /></br>MArfan → Joins (tall, joint laxity) → Ruptures<br /></br>CAT = Chest And Thorax tearing pain → get a CT Angiogram.</p></br><p>Another: “If it’s MARFAN and it’s TEARING, get the CT before you’re CARING.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</p></br><p>a. Measurement of cardiac enzyme activities<br /></br>   • Would delay definitive imaging; MI does not cause unilateral pleural fluid or hemothorax.</p></br><p>c. Ventilation–perfusion (V/Q) scan<br /></br>   • Pulmonary embolism presents with pleuritic pain, dyspnea, and a clear or hyperresonant chest exam, not dullness/pleural fluid. No risk factors or signs for PE here.</p></br><p>d. Oral rehydration therapy<br /></br>   • Inadequate for a patient with possible life-threatening vascular rupture; IV access and crystalloid/blood products are required.</p></br><p>e. Esophageal manometry<br /></br>   • Used for motility disorders (achalasia, spasm). Does not explain syncope, hypotension, or pleural fluid.</p></br><p>f. Placement of a chest tube on the left<br /></br>   • Large-bore tube thoracostomy is indicated for hemothorax, but ONLY after securing the diagnosis and alerting the surgical team; otherwise you risk removing the tamponade effect and causing rapid exsanguination.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Key Take-Home Message</p></br><p>In any tall Marfan patient with sudden tearing chest/back pain and unilateral pleural dullness, think “ruptured thoracic aortic dissection with hemothorax.” The immediate next step is confirmatory CT angiography (or bedside TEE if too unstable); definitive management is emergent vascular surgery.</p>
150. A 6-year-old boy is brought to the physician because of a 1-year history of progressive hyperactivity and behavior problems. His teacher reports that he frequently disrupts the class. He has asthma treated with an albuterol inhaler as needed. He occasionally has diffuse headaches. His parents say that he snores loudly, occasionally stops breathing, and moves frequently during sleep. He plays soccer and baseball. His parents and teacher say he is disobedient. His father has dyslexia, and a paternal cousin has autistic disorder. Growth and development are appropriate for age. He breathes through his mouth. His respirations are 20/min. Examination shows moderately enlarged tonsils. He can recall three of three objects after 5 minutes. On digit-span testing, he recalls 7 forward and 5 backward. His abstract reasoning is intact. Which of the following is the most appropriate initial step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Neuropsychiatric testing</br>b. Audiometry</br>c. EEG</br>d. Polysomnography</br>e. MRI of the brain</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer: d. Polysomnography (overnight sleep study)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• Key positives<br /></br>  – 1-year history of “progressive hyperactivity and behavior problems” at home and school<br /></br>  – Loud snoring, witnessed apneic pauses, restless sleep, morning headaches<br /></br>  – Mouth-breathing and moderately enlarged tonsils on exam<br /></br>• Key negatives<br /></br>  – Age-appropriate growth and development; normal memory, digit span, and abstract reasoning (argues against a primary cognitive disorder)<br /></br>  – No hearing difficulty, seizures, or focal neurologic deficits<br /></br>Pattern: A school-aged child with behavioral dysregulation + classic obstructive sleep apnea (OSA) clues (snoring, pauses, enlarged tonsils, mouth breathing).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1. Ask, “Could the disruptive behavior be secondary to an organic, reversible cause rather than primary ADHD?”<br /></br>Step 2. The constellation of nocturnal breathing problems and enlarged tonsils strongly suggests pediatric OSA, which commonly manifests as daytime hyperactivity rather than daytime somnolence (adult pattern).<br /></br>Step 3. Gold-standard and FIRST diagnostic step for suspected OSA is an overnight, attended polysomnography. It documents apnea–hypopnea index, oxygen desaturations, sleep architecture, and helps guide management (eg, adenotonsillectomy vs. CPAP).<br /></br>Core takeaway: In children, always think of sleep-disordered breathing as a reversible mimic of ADHD; confirm it with polysomnography before labeling the child with a primary neuropsychiatric disorder.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Pediatric OSA is most often due to adenotonsillar hypertrophy.<br /></br>• Upper-airway obstruction → intermittent hypoxemia, sleep fragmentation → neurocognitive &amp; behavioral changes (irritability, poor attention, hyperactivity).<br /></br>• Untreated OSA can lead to systemic hypertension, growth impairment, and learning problems.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment Snapshot</br>1. Diagnostic test: Overnight polysomnography (apnea–hypopnea index ≥1/hr in children is abnormal).<br /></br>2. First-line treatment after confirmation: Adenotonsillectomy (definitive in ~80% of cases).<br /></br>3. Adjuncts/alternatives: Intranasal corticosteroids, leukotriene inhibitors, CPAP if surgery contraindicated or residual OSA.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“SAW A CHILD SNORE”:<br /></br>S – School problems<br /></br>A – Adenotonsils enlarged<br /></br>W – Witnessed apnea<br /></br>CHILD – CHIldren display Hyperactivity Instead of Lethargy Daytime<br /></br>SNORE – Study Needed: Overnight REcording (polysomnography)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>a. Neuropsychiatric testing<br /></br>   • Appropriate when evaluating learning disorders or cognitive deficits. The vignette shows normal cognition and clear signs of a physiologic sleep problem that must be ruled out first.  </p></br><p>b.
