Audiometry<br /></br>   • Hearing loss can cause inattention, but there is no history of language delay, otitis media, or parental/teacher concern for hearing difficulty.  </p></br><p>c. Electroencephalography (EEG)<br /></br>   • Used to assess seizures or certain sleep disorders (eg, nocturnal seizures) but does not diagnose OSA; no seizure history provided.  </p></br><p>e. MRI of the brain<br /></br>   • Indicated for structural or focal neurologic deficits, tumors, or severe headaches with red-flag features. The headaches here are diffuse/morning and fit OSA-related hypoxia; no focal findings.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Jumping straight to ADHD work-up in a “hyperactive” child without first excluding sleep-disordered breathing.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>Behavioral problems in children who snore loudly should prompt evaluation for obstructive sleep apnea. The initial and definitive diagnostic step is an overnight polysomnography.</p>
151. A 57-year-old woman with metastatic cancer of the sigmoid colon is admitted to the hospital because of a 2-day history of progressive swelling and pain of her right arm. Ten days ago, she underwent partial resection of the liver and placement of a right subclavian venous port. She currently takes no medications. Examination today shows a swollen, tender right forearm and hand. Venous duplex ultrasonography shows thrombosis of the right axillary and subclavian veins. The port and catheter are removed, and intravenous unfractionated heparin therapy (UHT) is begun. After 2 days of UHT, the patient’s pain has decreased. Laboratory studies on admission and after 2 days of UHT show:</br>On Admission   2 Days After UHT</br>Hemoglobin (g/dL)   9.8   9.6</br>Hematocrit (%)   28   27</br>Leukocyte count (/mm³)   8900   12,600</br>Platelet count (/mm³)   260,000   61,000</br>Prothrombin time (sec) (INR=1)   12   13 (INR=1.1)</br>Partial thromboplastin time (sec)   32   87</br>Which of the following is the most likely explanation for these laboratory findings?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Anamnestic antibody response</br>b. Disseminated intravascular coagulation</br>c. Factor V gene mutation</br>d. Heparin overdose</br>e. Paraneoplastic syndrome</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Anamnestic antibody response (rapid-onset, immune-mediated heparin-induced thrombocytopenia)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues:<br /></br>• New venous thrombosis (upper-extremity DVT) 10 days after surgery/central line placement.<br /></br>• Unfractionated heparin (UFH) started on admission; within 48 h platelet count falls from 260 K → 61 K (&gt;50% drop).<br /></br>• PTT is appropriately prolonged from heparin; INR essentially unchanged.<br /></br>• Prior exposure to heparin 10 days earlier during liver resection &amp; port placement.  </p></br><p>Pattern: A precipitous (&gt;50 %) platelet fall occurring &lt;5 days after re-exposure to heparin strongly points to the “rapid-onset” form of immune heparin-induced thrombocytopenia (HIT) triggered by pre-existing anti–platelet factor-4 (PF4) IgG. That pre-formed antibody surge is called an anamnestic (memory) response.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)  </p></br><ol></br><li>Temporal relationship: Platelets drop only 48 h after UFH—too fast for a primary immune response but classic for re-exposure in the preceding 30 days.  </li></br><li>Degree of fall: &gt;50 % fall is a cardinal HIT criterion.  </li></br><li>Associated thrombosis rather than bleeding: HIT is paradoxically hypercoagulable (explains her new DVT).  </li></br><li>Lab profile: Isolated thrombocytopenia; normal/near-normal PT; PTT high solely from therapeutic UFH. Fits HIT, not DIC or drug overdose.  </li></br></ol></br><p>Take-home: A sudden, &gt;50 % platelet fall within 5 days of heparin (especially after recent exposure) = immune HIT due to anamnestic anti-PF4 antibodies.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>• UFH binds platelet factor 4 (PF4) → neo-antigen.<br /></br>• IgG antibodies form (or are rapidly reactivated) → bind PF4-heparin on platelet surface → Fc-γIIa receptor activation → platelet aggregation, release of procoagulant microparticles, thrombosis, and peripheral platelet clearance → thrombocytopenia.<br /></br>• Prior heparin exposure (surgery, dialysis, line flushes) in last 3 months primes memory B cells → anamnestic response causes platelet drop within 24–72 h on re-exposure.</p></br><p>Risk: UFH &gt; low-molecular-weight heparin (LMWH); surgery, female sex, cancer.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests  </p></br><ol></br><li>STOP all heparin (including flushes).  </li></br><li>Start a non-heparin parenteral anticoagulant: direct thrombin inhibitor (argatroban or bivalirudin) or factor Xa inhibitor (fondaparinux).  </li></br><li>Screen with 4T score; confirm with PF4-heparin ELISA and/or serotonin-release assay (SRA).  </li></br><li>Warfarin only after platelets &gt;150 K (risk of venous limb gangrene if started early).</li></br></ol></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“HIT the 5’s &amp; 50’s”:<br /></br>• Happens around Day 5 (or within 24-48 h if previous exposure)<br /></br>• &gt;50 % platelet drop<br /></br>• Leads to prothrombotic state (think ‘HIT = Heparin Induces Thrombosis’)  </p></br><p>Mnemonic: “PF4: Platelets Fall in 4 days after a Four-week prior exposure.”</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>b. Disseminated intravascular coagulation (DIC)<br /></br>• Would show prolonged PT &amp; PTT, low fibrinogen, elevated D-dimer, microangiopathic hemolysis, bleeding. Platelet fall alone with normal PT argues against DIC.</p></br><p>c. Factor V gene mutation (Leiden)<br /></br>• Causes venous thromboembolism but does not produce acute thrombocytopenia or correlate with heparin exposure.</p></br><p>d. Heparin overdose<br /></br>• Produces excessive anticoagulation → bleeding, markedly elevated PTT, but platelet count remains normal. Overdose cannot explain 200 K platelet drop and thrombosis.</p></br><p>e. Paraneoplastic syndrome<br /></br>• Malignancy can cause migratory thrombophlebitis (Trousseau) but again no drastic, rapid thrombocytopenia tied to heparin usage.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Takeaway  </p></br><p>A dramatic (&gt;50 %) platelet fall within 5 days of heparin—especially when the patient had heparin exposure in the prior month—is immune HIT triggered by an anamnestic anti-PF4 antibody response. Always stop heparin and begin an alternative anticoagulant to prevent life-threatening thrombosis.</p>
152. A 42-year-old man comes to the physician 12 hours after the onset of increasing mild epigastric pain and decreased appetite. He has not eaten during this period. He has vomited greenish material 10 times during the past 4 hours. He has had no fever or chills. He was treated for malaria 25 years ago. He currently takes no medications. He immigrated to the USA from Ghana 20 years ago. He does not smoke or drink alcohol. He appears ill and is in mild distress. His temperature is 37.8°C (100.1°F), pulse is 110/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 108/70 mm Hg. Examination shows dry mucous membranes. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. The abdomen is soft with mild epigastric tenderness. There is no hepatosplenomegaly. Bowel sounds are high pitched. A 3-cm tender mass is palpated in the right groin. Test of the stool for occult blood is negative. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hematocrit 51%</br>Leukocyte count 11,400/mm3</br>Segmented neutrophils 78%</br>Lymphocytes 19%</br>Monocytes 3%</br>Platelet count 225,000/mm3</br>Serum</br>Na+ 133 mEq/L</br>K+ 4.2 mEq/L</br>Cl- 91 mEq/L</br>HCO3- 30 mEq/L</br>Urea nitrogen 29 mg/dL</br>Glucose 177 mg/dL</br>Creatinine 1.2 mg/dL</br>Abdominal x-rays are shown. In addition to placement of a nasogastric tube, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: F</br></br>a. Ultrasonography of the right upper quadrant of the abdomen</br>b. CT scan of the abdomen</br>c. Intravenous administration of a prokinetic agent</br>d. Intravenous antibiotic therapy</br>e. Colonoscopy</br>f. Surgical exploration of the right inguinal canal</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>f. Surgical exploration of the right inguinal canal  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key “buzz” words &amp; clues:<br /></br>• 12-hour history of crampy epigastric pain + repeated bilious (green) vomiting → mechanical small-bowel obstruction (SBO).<br /></br>• High-pitched bowel sounds &amp; mild abdominal distension → hyperactive phase of SBO.<br /></br>• 3-cm tender mass in right groin → incarcerated right inguinal hernia (most common external cause of SBO in adults).<br /></br>• Low-grade fever, tachycardia, hemoconcentration (Hct 51%) &amp; mild leukocytosis → dehydration + early inflammatory response.<br /></br>• Abdomen otherwise soft (no peritonitis yet) → obstruction but not yet strangulated/perforated.<br /></br>• X-ray (not shown) on NBME typically demonstrates dilated, air-fluid–filled small-bowel loops with paucity of distal gas.  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives:<br /></br>• No prior abdominal surgery → postoperative adhesions (the #1 cause of SBO) less likely; hernia jumps to top of differential.<br /></br>• No gastrointestinal bleeding → rules out ischemic colitis, peptic ulcer perforation, etc.<br /></br>• Abdominal exam without hepatosplenomegaly → eliminates malaria relapse or hepatobiliary disease as driver of current S/Sx.  </p></br><p>Pattern: Acute SBO + palpable tender groin mass = incarcerated (possibly strangulated) inguinal hernia → surgical disease.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)  </p></br><ol></br><li>Diagnose SBO from emesis, pain, high-pitched sounds, dilated loops on film.  </li></br><li>Identify obvious anatomic cause (tender inguinal mass).  </li></br><li>Start initial stabilization: NPO, IV fluids, nasogastric (NG) tube for decompression (already being done in stem).  </li></br><li>When an incarcerated hernia is clinically evident, do NOT delay—go directly to operative repair/exploration to reduce/ resect ischemic bowel if needed.  </li></br></ol></br><p>Core takeaway: A tender, non-reducible groin mass with SBO mandates urgent surgical exploration; additional imaging only wastes time and increases risk of strangulation and necrosis.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>• Incarceration = herniated bowel segment can’t be reduced back into abdomen → luminal obstruction.<br /></br>• Strangulation = compromised blood flow → ischemia, necrosis, perforation, sepsis.<br /></br>• Early: crampy pain, hyperactive sounds, bilious emesis, dehydration (↑Hct, BUN).<br /></br>• Late/strangulated: constant pain, fever, peritonitis, metabolic acidosis.  </p></br><p>Risk factors: Males &gt; females, chronic raised intra-abdominal pressure, prior hernia.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review  </p></br><p>Initial (already implemented):<br /></br>• NPO + IV isotonic crystalloids.<br /></br>• NG tube for gastric decompression.<br /></br>Definitive:<br /></br>• Emergent operative exploration of inguinal canal → reduce bowel, inspect viability, perform herniorrhaphy +/- mesh.<br /></br>• Broad-spectrum IV antibiotics may be given intra-op if strangulation suspected, but surgery comes first.  </p></br><p>No further imaging is necessary when the diagnosis and etiology are clinically obvious (palpable tender hernia).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“BOIL” for incarcerated hernia SBO:<br /></br>B – Bilious emesis<br /></br>O – Obstruction signs (high-pitched sounds)<br /></br>I – Inguinal tender mass<br /></br>L – Laparotomy/Laparoscopic repair right away  </p></br><p>“Never CT a TENDER hernia” – if the hernia is tender and obstructing, skip the scan and go to the OR.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Ultrasonography of the right upper quadrant – Used for gallstones or biliary colic. No RUQ pain or Murphy sign; palpable groin mass explains obstruction, so RUQ US adds nothing.  </p></br><p>b. CT scan of the abdomen – Helpful when etiology is unclear, but here the cause is evident clinically. Waiting for CT delays needed surgery and risks bowel ischemia.  </p></br><p>c. Intravenous prokinetic agent (e.g., metoclopramide) – Contraindicated in mechanical obstruction; could exacerbate pain, perforate bowel. Only considered in functional (ileus) obstruction.  </p></br><p>d. Intravenous antibiotic therapy – May be adjunct if strangulation/perforation, but antibiotics alone do not treat obstruction; definitive surgical source control is required first.  </p></br><p>e. Colonoscopy – Evaluates colonic disease (cancer, volvulus, IBD). SBO involves small bowel; scope cannot reach across obstructed small bowel and risks perforation.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line: A tender, non-reducible inguinal hernia causing small-bowel obstruction is a surgical emergency. After NG decompression and resuscitation, immediate exploration of the right inguinal canal is the most appropriate next step.</p>
153. A 37-year-old woman, gravida 1, para 1, comes to the physician for a postpartum examination 6 weeks after spontaneous vaginal delivery of a 3220-g (7-lb 1-oz) newborn. Delivery was complicated by a vaginal laceration, which was repaired. She states that she has had occasional fecal incontinence and flatus since delivery; she has not had urinary incontinence, fever, diarrhea, constipation, or pelvic pain. She has Crohn disease treated with prednisone; her last flare-up of symptoms was 6 months before delivery. Her temperature is 37 °C (98.6 °F). Physical examination shows that the sutures in the vaginal mucosa are healing well. There is a 3-cm defect in the rectal mucosa with communication with the vagina. The anal sphincter is partially torn. Without treatment, which of the following is the most likely outcome for this patient?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Complete closure of the defect</br>b. Extension to include the anal sphincter</br>c. Extension to include the bladder</br>d. Partial closure of the defect</br>e. Persistent rectovaginal fistula</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Persistent rectovaginal fistula</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – “Occasional fecal incontinence and flatus…communication with the vagina” → Current rectovaginal fistula (RVF).<br /></br>  – 3-cm defect, anal sphincter partially torn → Large, complex tract.<br /></br>  – Crohn disease on chronic prednisone → Poor tissue healing, high fistula risk.<br /></br>  – 6-week post-partum check = well beyond the normal 1–2-week wound-healing period; defect still present.  </p></br><p>• Pertinent negatives<br /></br>  – No fever/pelvic pain → Not an acute infection/abscess.<br /></br>  – No urinary symptoms → Bladder not involved.  </p></br><p>Pattern: Obstetric laceration + Crohn = high likelihood of chronic, non-healing RVF that requires surgical repair.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>1. Third-/fourth-degree vaginal lacerations can create rectal mucosal defects.<br /></br>2. Normal obstetric lacerations close within 1–2 weeks; persistence at 6 weeks = pathologic.<br /></br>3. Crohn disease (especially on steroids) severely impairs granulation tissue and epithelialization → spontaneous closure is exceedingly rare.<br /></br>4. Therefore, without intervention the fistulous tract almost always persists.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Obstetric rectovaginal fistulas, particularly in Crohn patients, do NOT heal on their own; surgical repair is required.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Etiology: Direct obstetric trauma → full-thickness tear between posterior vaginal wall and anterior rectal wall.<br /></br>• Crohn: transmural inflammation, cytokine‐rich milieu, and immunosuppression (prednisone) → impaired collagen deposition and wound contraction.<br /></br>• Consequence: Chronic epithelialized tract → persistent passage of stool/flatus through vagina.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Management Snapshot</br>• Initial: Control sepsis/abscess if present; optimize Crohn control (biologics).<br /></br>• Definitive: Surgical repair (endorectal advancement flap, transvaginal approach, or sphincteroplasty) after 3–6 months postpartum/inflammation quiescent.<br /></br>• Medical adjuncts: Anti-TNF (infliximab), antibiotics, seton for high fistulas.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“Fistulas in Crohn are Stubborn and Stay”<br /></br>F – Fistula<br /></br>I – Inflammatory bowel disease<br /></br>S – Steroids<br /></br>T – Tears (obstetric)<br /></br>A – Always<br /></br>Y – Yours (unless surgically fixed)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>a. Complete closure of the defect<br /></br>   • Requires healthy granulation tissue; Crohn + steroids prevent this. &lt;5 % chance of spontaneous closure.</p></br><p>b. Extension to include the anal sphincter<br /></br>   • Sphincter already partially torn; further spontaneous extension is uncommon without new trauma/infection.</p></br><p>c. Extension to include the bladder<br /></br>   • Bladder lies anterior to vagina; tract would have to traverse vaginal wall anteriorly—rare progression.</p></br><p>d. Partial closure of the defect<br /></br>   • Some narrowing can occur, but functional closure (no passage of stool/gas) is still unlikely; clinically behaves as persistent RVF.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>Six weeks after delivery, a Crohn patient with an obstetric RVF will almost certainly have a persistent fistula unless surgically repaired.</p>
154. A 12-year-old boy is brought to the emergency department 15 minutes after his parents found him restless and confused in their basement. He also had slurred speech. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. While waiting to be examined, he falls asleep but is easily arousable. Physical examination shows nystagmus. Muscle strength is 4/5 throughout. On mental status examination, he is oriented to person and place but not to time. He reports no hallucinations. There is no evidence of delusions. Urine toxicology screening is negative. Use of which of the following substances is the most likely cause of this patient's condition?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Amphetamine</br>b. Cough suppressant</br>c. Inhalant</br>d. Opioid</br>e. Synthetic cannabinoid</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>c. Inhalant</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Setting/epidemiology: 12-year-old boy, unsupervised in the basement (common place to find household chemicals such as glue, paint, propane, butane).<br /></br>• Time course: Very acute (family sees him normal, then 15 min later confused) and short-lived—he is already becoming somnolent but easily arousable. Inhalant “high” typically lasts only minutes.<br /></br>• Neuro findings: </br>  – Slurred speech, ataxia/weakness (4/5 strength)<br /></br>  – Horizontal nystagmus<br /></br>  – Transient confusion, disorientation to time<br /></br>• Toxicology screen: Negative. Most volatile solvents (toluene, butane, nitrous oxide, etc.) are not detected on standard urine drug panels.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: No miosis, no respiratory depression (rules out opioid); no tachycardia, mydriasis, severe agitation (rules out amphetamine); no hallucinations/psychosis or marked dissociation (less likely dextromethorphan or synthetic cannabinoids).</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Acute neuro-behavioral change in an adolescent → think intoxication.<br /></br>2. Look for drug-specific clues → nystagmus + slurred speech + brief duration.<br /></br>3. Check tox screen → negative → narrows to substances not routinely screened (inhalants, some synthetics).<br /></br>4. Environment + typical neuro signs = inhalant abuse.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Inhalant intoxication presents with very rapid-onset, short-duration CNS depression/ataxia, nystagmus, and is NOT picked up by routine urine drug screens.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Agents: Volatile hydrocarbons (toluene in glue, paint thinner; butane, propane; nitrous oxide; amyl nitrite).<br /></br>• Mechanism: Lipophilic solvents rapidly cross the alveolar–blood barrier → CNS myelin dissolution + GABAA potentiation → transient euphoria followed by CNS depression.<br /></br>• Acute effects: Euphoria, dizziness, slurred speech, ataxia, nystagmus, confusion, headache, perioral/mucosal irritation (“glue sniffer’s rash”).<br /></br>• Chronic effects: Cognitive decline, cerebellar dysfunction, polyneuropathy, liver/kidney damage, bone marrow suppression, sudden sniffing death (arrhythmia).</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics</br>• Immediate: Remove exposure, give high-flow oxygen, monitor cardiac rhythm (risk of sensitization of myocardium → ventricular arrhythmia).<br /></br>• No specific antidote.<br /></br>• Evaluate for hypoxia, trauma, chemical burns.<br /></br>• Counsel family on substance-use treatment and safe storage of chemicals.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“SHAKE”:<br /></br>S – Short-lived high (seconds–minutes)<br /></br>H – Household products (glue, paint, aerosol)<br /></br>A – Ataxia &amp; nystagmus<br /></br>K – Kid with negative tox screen<br /></br>E – Euphoria → CNS dEpression</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Amphetamine<br /></br>   • Expected: Marked agitation, tachycardia, hypertension, mydriasis, hyperreflexia, possible psychosis. Detectable on standard tox screen. No nystagmus or slurred speech.  </p></br><p>b. Cough suppressant (dextromethorphan)<br /></br>   • At very high doses can cause dissociative symptoms, hallucinations, nystagmus, but usually produces prominent visual hallucinations and is frequently picked up as an “opioid” on tox screens. Less characteristic rapid onset/offset compared with inhalants; typically taken orally, not “found in basement.”  </p></br><p>d. Opioid<br /></br>   • Classic triad: CNS depression, pinpoint pupils (miosis), respiratory depression. Nystagmus is uncommon; urine tox usually positive for opiates.  </p></br><p>e. Synthetic cannabinoid (“K2,” “Spice”)<br /></br>   • Produces severe agitation, anxiety, psychosis, tachycardia, hypertension. Nystagmus and slurred speech are not typical, and intoxication generally lasts hours, not minutes.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Associating nystagmus only with PCP. Remember inhalant intoxication and dextromethorphan can also cause nystagmus, but PCP almost always presents with severe agitation, violence, vertical-horizontal nystagmus, and is usually found in older teens/young adults.</p></br><p>Bottom line: Teenager + brief intoxication episode, nystagmus, slurred speech, negative tox screen = inhalant abuse.</p>
155. A 27-year-old man comes to the physician because of a painful lesion on his left palm that has been enlarging during the past 9 months. Two years ago, he underwent cauterization with liquid nitrogen for a wart-like lesion at the same site; the lesion decreased in size but never completely resolved. Since the procedure, he has had episodes of skin breakdown with clear, malodorous drainage. He is unable to grip objects with his left hand because of the pain. His temperature is 37°C (98.6°F). Examination of the left hand shows a 4 x 3-cm, erythematous, irregularly shaped lesion overlying the hypothenar eminence that rises 1 cm above the skin surface and is fixed to underlying tissue. The center of the lesion is necrotic with a serosanguineous, malodorous discharge. Sensation to pinprick is decreased over the palmar surface of the left small fingertip. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. X-rays of the left hand show no abnormalities. An MRI of the hand shows a large, irregularly shaped mass extending from the skin of the hypothenar eminence into the Guyon canal and the carpal tunnel. Results of an open biopsy of the lesion show high-grade epithelioid sarcoma. A staging CT scan shows no evidence of metastases. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Chemotherapy</br>b. Radiation therapy</br>c. Split-thickness skin graft</br>d. Full-thickness skin graft</br>e. Amputation</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Amputation  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues pointing to an aggressive, infiltrative soft-tissue malignancy that is difficult to resect with clear margins while preserving function:<br /></br>• “High-grade epithelioid sarcoma” on open biopsy – known for local recurrence and early neurovascular spread.<br /></br>• Size 4 × 3 cm and depth: rises 1 cm above skin and MRI shows extension from skin → Guyon canal → carpal tunnel (ie, through superficial fascia into neurovascular/tendon spaces).<br /></br>• Fixed to underlying tissue, necrotic center with malodorous discharge – features of aggressive malignant degeneration rather than a simple wart or benign cyst.<br /></br>• Sensory loss in the ulnar distribution (palmar surface of the small finger) – tumor already involves the ulnar digital branch in the canal.<br /></br>• Prior cryotherapy “wart” that never fully resolved → suggests this was a misdiagnosed slow-growing sarcoma from the start.  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives:<br /></br>• Staging CT shows NO distant metastases – therefore management is directed at local control.<br /></br>• X-ray normal – bone not yet involved, but MRI demonstrates extensive soft-tissue spread.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning  </p></br><p>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Any localized soft-tissue sarcoma → attempt curative surgery with wide negative margins.<br /></br>2. Limb-sparing wide excision is preferred, but only when clear margins can be achieved without sacrificing critical neurovascular structures and preserving function.<br /></br>3. MRI shows the mass tracks along the ulnar neurovascular bundle into the carpal tunnel; achieving ≥2-cm circumferential margins would require removal of essential tendons, nerves, and vasculature → functional limb would be non-viable.<br /></br>4. Therefore oncologic principle dictates en-bloc removal of the tumor with the involved compartment: amputation of the hand (often transmetacarpal or even proximal) to obtain negative margins.<br /></br>Core takeaway: For high-grade sarcomas that infiltrate vital structures in a distal extremity, amputation is the only reliable way to achieve negative margins and prevent local recurrence.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>Epithelioid sarcoma<br /></br>• Rare (&lt;1% of soft-tissue sarcomas); typically affects young adults in distal upper extremities.<br /></br>• Propensity for regional spread along tendon sheaths, fascia, nerves, and dermal lymphatics → high local recurrence rate (35–85 %).<br /></br>• Up to 45 % develop distant metastases if not aggressively managed.<br /></br>• Best survival correlate = achieving an R0 (completely negative-margin) resection at first operation.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review  </p></br><p>Localized disease:<br /></br>• Preferred: Wide excision with ≥2-cm margins OR amputation if margins unattainable.<br /></br>• Adjuvant radiation can decrease local recurrence but cannot substitute for inadequate surgery.<br /></br>• Systemic chemotherapy (e.g., doxorubicin/ifosfamide) reserved for metastatic or unresectable disease.  </p></br><p>Work-up already done:<br /></br>• MRI – defines local extent.<br /></br>• CT chest/abdomen/pelvis – screens for metastases.<br /></br>No further imaging changes management; the next decisive step is definitive surgery.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“Epithelioid = ‘E’ for Extremity amputation if Entrenched.”<br /></br>OR<br /></br>“Bad SARComa in the hand? You sometimes lose the hand.” (SARComa → Seize And Remove Completely).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Chemotherapy<br /></br>• Indicated for metastatic or unresectable disease. Local control with negative margins is still superior for non-metastatic epithelioid sarcoma. No evidence chemo alone cures localized, high-grade lesions.  </p></br><p>b. Radiation therapy<br /></br>• Useful as neoadjuvant or adjuvant to limb-sparing surgery, but cannot eliminate gross disease or create margins across neurovascular structures. Would leave significant residual tumor.  </p></br><p>c. Split-thickness skin graft<br /></br>d. Full-thickness skin graft<br /></br>• Grafts are reconstructive options after wide local excision of superficial tumors. Here the lesion extends deep into the hand; grafting skin over residual tumor would be non-curative.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Believing limb-sparing excision plus radiation is always preferred. USMLE/NBME stresses that oncologic principles trump limb preservation; when clear margins cannot be achieved without sacrificing critical structures, amputation is mandatory.</p>
156. An 18-year-old man is brought to the emergency department by his friends 45 minutes after sustaining injuries on his dirt bike. He reports that when landing the bike after a jump, he lost his seating and suddenly straddled the center bar of the bike, which resulted in severe pain between his legs. For 15 minutes after being injured, he was unable to ambulate. He has had similar accidents twice in the past, but the resulting pain was not as prolonged or severe. The patient is in distress. His pulse is 128/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 152/80 mm Hg. Examination shows lacerations and ecchymoses over the face and hands. Visual examination of the scrotum shows diffuse edema and ecchymoses; the scrotum cannot be palpated because of pain. Which of the following is most likely to confirm the diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Doppler ultrasonography of the scrotum</br>b. MRI of the testes</br>c. Transillumination of the scrotum</br>d. Palpation of the scrotum with anesthesia</br>e. Aspiration of the scrotum</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>• Doppler ultrasonography of the scrotum  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Straddled the center bar” → classic high-energy, direct scrotal impact.<br /></br>• “Severe pain … diffuse edema and ecchymoses … cannot palpate” → suggests deeper intratesticular injury (contusion, hematocele, or rupture) rather than a superficial scrotal laceration.<br /></br>• Prior minor incidents that resolved quickly; this episode is far more painful and swollen ⇒ raises suspicion for testicular rupture or compromised blood flow that must be confirmed rapidly.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: High-energy blunt scrotal trauma.<br /></br>Step 2: Need to rule out testicular rupture or torsion because both require prompt surgery (&lt;72 h for rupture, &lt;6 h for torsion).<br /></br>Step 3: Best rapid, bedside, non-invasive, highly sensitive test that shows:<br /></br>  • Integrity of tunica albuginea<br /></br>  • Intraparenchymal hematoma/hematocele<br /></br>  • Real-time arterial and venous flow<br /></br>= Color Doppler ultrasonography.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Any significant acute scrotal trauma in which physical exam is limited → obtain a stat scrotal Doppler ultrasound to determine need for immediate exploration.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Testicular rupture = tear in tunica albuginea → extrusion of seminiferous tubules, hematoma, loss of fertility if not repaired.<br /></br>• Color Doppler shows heterogeneous echotexture, loss of contour, discontinuity of tunica, and altered flow. Sensitivity/specificity ≈ 90-95%.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>1. Initial: analgesia + scrotal support.<br /></br>2. Imaging: Doppler US within minutes.<br /></br>3. If rupture/torsion/major hematocele confirmed → prompt surgical exploration and repair (orchiopexy or orchiectomy if non-salvageable).<br /></br>4. Minor contusion without rupture → ice, NSAIDs, close follow-up.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Trauma or torsion? Turn on the Doppler.”<br /></br>Picture a Doppler wand as a “metal detector” you must wave over any seriously injured scrotum before opening the operating-room door.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. MRI of the testes – Excellent anatomic detail but costly, time-consuming, not readily available in ED trauma setting; used mainly for complex tumors.<br /></br>c. Transillumination of the scrotum – Helpful for hydrocele but useless in massive swelling and does not evaluate testicular architecture or blood flow.<br /></br>d. Palpation with anesthesia – May lessen pain but still cannot assess intratesticular integrity; delays definitive care.<br /></br>e. Aspiration of the scrotum – Risk of infection, provides no information about tunica integrity or perfusion; not an accepted diagnostic step.</p></br><p>Key differences: Only Doppler US gives immediate functional (vascular) and structural data necessary for emergent decision-making in testicular trauma.</p>
157. A 12-month-old boy is brought to the physician by his mother for a well-child examination. His mother is concerned because she does not think he is gaining an adequate amount of weight. His diet consists of cow milk-based formula and solid foods. He has four bulky, light-colored stools and six wet diapers daily. He is at the 10th percentile for height, 5th percentile for weight, and 75th percentile for head circumference. He has had a 90-g (3-oz) weight gain since his most recent visit 3 months ago. Development is appropriate for age. Examination shows a paucity of subcutaneous fat and thin hair. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Recommend switching to lactose-free formula</br>b. Recommend switching to soy-based formula</br>c. Perform a sweat chloride test</br>d. Perform a barium enema</br>e. Begin daily lactulose therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>c. Perform a sweat chloride test  </p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Bulky, light-colored stools → classic description of steatorrhea (fat malabsorption, ↑ fat content makes the stool pale, greasy, bulky, difficult to flush).<br /></br>• Paucity of subcutaneous fat + thin hair + sub-optimal weight gain (only 90 g in 3 mo) → chronic malabsorption with protein-calorie deficiency.<br /></br>• 12-month-old on cow-milk formula/solids → should be growing faster; linear growth (height 10th %) is better preserved than weight (5th %), a pattern typical of malabsorptive states.<br /></br>• Six wet diapers/day → normal urine output, so problem is not intake but absorption.<br /></br>Key negatives: No abdominal distention after feeds, no colicky pain, no blood or mucus in stool, no severe constipation – these argue against cow-milk protein allergy, lactose intolerance, Hirschsprung, or functional constipation.  </p></br><p>Pattern recognition: Infant/young child with failure to thrive + steatorrhea + malnutrition = think pancreatic insufficiency due to cystic fibrosis (CF) until proven otherwise. The most sensitive and specific initial test is the quantitative pilocarpine iontophoresis sweat chloride test.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-by-step thought process<br /></br>1. Identify malabsorption (steatorrhea, poor weight gain).<br /></br>2. Ask “why is there malabsorption in an infant?” – top 2 on exams: cystic fibrosis (pancreatic enzyme deficiency) and celiac disease (rare &lt;2 y).<br /></br>3. CF is much more common, presents earlier, involves recurrent respiratory infections (may be mild/absent early) and pancreatic insufficiency.<br /></br>4. The diagnostic test of choice = sweat chloride test.<br /></br>Take-home message: In an infant with steatorrhea and failure to thrive, always rule out cystic fibrosis by ordering a sweat chloride test.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>Cystic Fibrosis<br /></br>• Mutation in CFTR gene (ΔF508 most common) → defective Cl⁻ channel → viscous secretions.<br /></br>• Pancreatic ducts obstructed → impaired secretion of pancreatic lipase and bicarbonate → fat and ADEK vitamin malabsorption → steatorrhea, failure to thrive, fat-soluble vitamin deficiency, protein-energy malnutrition (thin hair, ↓ subQ fat).<br /></br>• Later: chronic sinopulmonary infections, nasal polyps, digital clubbing, meconium ileus / distal intestinal obstruction syndrome.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment (succinct)<br /></br>Diagnostics<br /></br>• Sweat chloride test (&gt;60 mEq/L on two occasions diagnostic).<br /></br>• Secondary tests: fecal elastase (↓), genetic testing, newborn screening (immunoreactive trypsinogen).  </p></br><p>Treatment overview<br /></br>• Pancreatic enzyme replacement (pancrelipase) with all meals &amp; snacks.<br /></br>• High-calorie, high-protein diet with ADEK vitamins.<br /></br>• Airway clearance + inhaled medications (hypertonic saline, dornase alfa).<br /></br>• CFTR modulators (ivacaftor, lumacaftor/tezacaftor-ivacaftor) if genotype-eligible.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Failure to thrive + Fatty stool + Fat-soluble vitamin deficiency = think the 3 Fs of Cystic Fibrosis.”<br /></br>Mnemonic: “CF = Can’t Flourish” (growth failure) &amp; “Can’t FLOT” (Fat-laden, Light, Oily Turds).  </p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Switch to lactose-free formula<br /></br>• Lactose intolerance causes watery, acidic diarrhea, bloating, abdominal pain—NOT bulky, greasy stools. Weight loss is less dramatic.  </p></br><p>b. Switch to soy-based formula<br /></br>• Used for cow-milk protein allergy (bloody stools, eczema, vomiting). Again, does not cause steatorrhea.  </p></br><p>d. Perform a barium enema<br /></br>• Indicated for suspected Hirschsprung disease (delayed meconium, severe constipation) or intussusception (episodic abdominal pain, “currant jelly” stool). Neither fits the presentation.  </p></br><p>e. Begin daily lactulose therapy<br /></br>• Lactulose treats constipation or hepatic encephalopathy. This child has 4 stools/day (not constipated) and no liver disease signs.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Attributing every infant stool issue to lactose or milk protein intolerance. Remember: pale, greasy stools = fat malabsorption → think pancreatic problem → rule out CF first.</p>
158. A 42-year-old woman comes to the physician because she had a 2-hour episode of shortness of breath yesterday after walking for two blocks. She has not had chest pain and has no previous history of shortness of breath with exertion or at night. She has a 6-year history of poorly controlled hypertension. Her current medications are a diuretic, β-adrenergic blocking agent, and calcium-channel blocking agent. There is no family history of coronary artery disease. The patient does not smoke cigarettes. Her pulse is 85/min and irregularly irregular, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 170/90 mm Hg. The lungs are clear to auscultation. On cardiac examination, S₁ and S₂ are normal. No murmurs are heard. Serum studies show a total cholesterol concentration of 130 mg/dL and LDL-cholesterol concentration of 90 mg/dL. An ECG shows atrial fibrillation and left ventricular hypertrophy. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Cardiac catheterization with coronary angiography</br>b. Cardiac stress scintigraphy</br>c. ECG exercise stress test</br>d. Echocardiography</br>e. 24-Hour ambulatory ECG monitoring</br>f. Tilt test</br></br></br><p>NOTE TO STUDENT<br /></br>The answer that was supplied to me (“Cardiac catheterization with coronary angiography”) is almost certainly a typographical error.  For this vignette the NBME-recognized, guideline-consistent next diagnostic step is:</p></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Echocardiography (transthoracic)</p></br><p>───────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “42-year-old woman … 2-hour episode of shortness of breath” – new, unexplained dyspnea.<br /></br>• “Pulse irregularly irregular … ECG shows atrial fibrillation” – newly discovered AF.<br /></br>• “Left-ventricular hypertrophy” – evidence of long-standing pressure overload from poorly controlled HTN.<br /></br>• “No chest pain, normal lipids, non-smoker, female, age &lt;45” – very low pre-test probability for coronary artery disease (CAD).<br /></br>• “Lungs clear, no murmurs” – implies no obvious heart failure or severe valvular lesion on physical exam, but structural disease still possible.</p></br><p>Pertinent positives: AF, LVH.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: No angina, low CAD risk factors.</p></br><p>───────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. A new diagnosis of atrial fibrillation always prompts a search for a structural/valvular etiology.<br /></br>2. The fastest, safest, and highest-yield test for structural heart assessment is a transthoracic echocardiogram (TTE).<br /></br>   • Looks for left-atrial enlargement (predisposes to AF).<br /></br>   • Evaluates LV size/function and diastolic compliance in hypertensive heart disease.<br /></br>   • Screens for occult valvular pathology (e.g., mitral stenosis, regurgitation).<br /></br>3. Invasive coronary angiography is reserved for patients with high clinical suspicion for CAD or a positive non-invasive ischemia study—neither applies here.</p></br><p>Take-home message: “New-onset atrial fibrillation → obtain an echocardiogram to evaluate for structural heart disease.”</p></br><p>───────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Poorly controlled HTN → concentric LVH → ↑LV diastolic stiffness → ↑left-atrial pressure/size → atrial fibrosis &amp; ectopic foci → atrial fibrillation.<br /></br>• AF can reduce atrial kick and precipitate exertional dyspnea without causing chest pain.</p></br><p>───────────</br>Relevant Diagnostic Tests &amp; Management<br /></br>1. Transthoracic echocardiography (first line).<br /></br>2. Labs: TSH (hyperthyroidism), CMP/Mg²⁺, CBC.<br /></br>3. Rate or rhythm control plus thrombo-embolism risk stratification (CHA₂DS₂-VASc).<br /></br>4. Further ischemia testing (stress study ± cath) only if symptoms suggest angina or echo shows wall-motion abnormalities.</p></br><p>───────────</br>Memorization Tip<br /></br>“New A-Fib? Think ‘ECHO &amp; TSH’ before you ‘GO’ (catheterization).”</p></br><p>───────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Cardiac catheterization with coronary angiography<br /></br>   • Invasive, costly, and indicated only when there is high CAD likelihood or after positive stress imaging.<br /></br>   • No angina, normal lipids, non-smoker, 42-year-old woman → low CAD probability; not the next step.</p></br><p>b. Cardiac stress scintigraphy / c. ECG exercise stress test<br /></br>   • Both are ischemia tests.  Dyspnea could be “anginal equivalent,” but pre-test probability is low and AF can make plain ECG stress uninterpretable (irregular R-R, baseline ST-T changes).<br /></br>   • Echo is still the first, safer choice.</p></br><p>e. 24-hour ambulatory ECG (Holter)<br /></br>   • Useful for intermittent arrhythmias; here AF is already documented on office ECG, so structural evaluation is higher priority.</p></br><p>f. Tilt test<br /></br>   • Used for syncope evaluation due to neuro-cardiogenic (vasovagal) causes, not for exertional dyspnea or established AF.</p></br><p>───────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In a patient with newly identified atrial fibrillation, the immediate diagnostic priority is to look for structural heart disease with a transthoracic echocardiogram.</p>
159. An 82-year-old woman is admitted to the hospital because of a 4-day history of dyspnea, cough productive of brown sputum, nausea, fever, and chills. She has hypertension, type 2 diabetes mellitus, and hypercholesterolemia. Her medications are amlodipine, benazepril, and atorvastatin. She has no known sick contacts. One week ago, she returned from a 15-day vacation where she stayed in a hotel for 5 days and was on a 10-day Caribbean cruise. She does not smoke cigarettes, drink alcohol, or use illicit drugs. She lives alone and is able to perform activities of daily living without difficulty. She is 163 cm (5 ft 4 in) tall and weighs 70 kg, or 155lb; BMI is 27 kg/m². Her temperature is 38.1 °C (100.6 °F), pulse is 116/min, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 120/65 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 89%. Pulmonary examination shows dullness to percussion over the lower lobe of the left lung. Laboratory studies show a leukocyte count of 15,000/mm³, serum urea nitrogen concentration of 19 mg/dL, and serum creatinine concentration of 0.9 mg/dL. Her hemoglobin concentration, serum electrolyte concentrations, and results of urinalysis are within reference ranges. A chest x-ray and Gram stain of sputum are shown. Which of the following is the most likely infectious agent?	Correct answer: G</br></br>a. Haemophilus influenzae</br>b. Legionella species</br>c. Mycobacterium tuberculosis</br>d. Pneumocystis jirovecii</br>e. Pseudomonas aeruginosa</br>f. Staphylococcus aureus</br>g. Streptococcus pneumoniae</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Haemophilus influenzae  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues<br /></br>• Age 82 y: elderly patients are predisposed to H influenzae and pneumococcal pneumonia.<br /></br>• Acute “typical” pneumonia syndrome: 4 days of fever, chills, productive cough with brown sputum, dyspnea.<br /></br>• Physical exam: fever 38 °C, tachypnea, hypoxemia (SpO₂ 89 %), dullness at left lower lobe → lobar consolidation.<br /></br>• Labs: leukocytosis (15 000/µL) but normal BUN/Cr → no uremia.<br /></br>• Imaging (not shown to you): classically a homogeneous lobar infiltrate.<br /></br>• Gram-stain (provided in stem): described image shows many PMNs with small, pleomorphic, Gram-negative coccobacilli. That morphology is classic for nontypeable H influenzae.  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives<br /></br>• No mention of COPD exacerbation with green sputum (would push toward Pseudomonas in severe COPD).<br /></br>• No cavitary or apical disease, night sweats, weight loss (rules out TB).<br /></br>• No diffuse, bilateral interstitial infiltrates or immunosuppression (rules out Pneumocystis).<br /></br>• Organisms ARE visible on Gram stain, arguing strongly against Legionella (does not Gram-stain well).  </p></br><p>Pattern recognition: Elderly + lobar CAP + visible pleomorphic Gram-negative coccobacilli → Haemophilus influenzae.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)</p></br><ol></br><li>Decide category of pneumonia<br /></br>   • Presentation = community-acquired pneumonia (CAP).  </li></br><li>Evaluate Gram stain in CAP<br /></br>   • Visible small Gram-neg coccobacilli ⇒ think H influenzae or Moraxella catarrhalis; the former is far more common in elderly.  </li></br><li>Incorporate risk factors<br /></br>   • Age &gt; 65 and chronic medical illnesses (DM, HTN) → H influenzae high on list.  </li></br><li>Consider travel/cruise history<br /></br>   • Raises suspicion for Legionella, but absence of Gram-organisms would be expected; here organisms are seen → Legionella less likely.<br /></br>Core takeaway: In elderly CAP with a Gram stain showing pleomorphic Gram-negative coccobacilli, Haemophilus influenzae is the prototypical pathogen.  </li></br></ol></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>• Haemophilus influenzae (nontypeable strains) colonize the upper airway.<br /></br>• In the elderly, impaired mucociliary clearance and comorbidities allow aspiration into lower airways → lobar or bronchopneumonia.<br /></br>• Encapsulated type b strains cause meningitis/epiglottitis in children; nontypeable strains cause otitis media, sinusitis, and adult CAP.<br /></br>• Virulence: outer membrane proteins, IgA protease.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics  </p></br><ol></br><li>First-line outpatient CAP coverage: Amoxicillin-clavulanate OR a respiratory fluoroquinolone (e.g., levofloxacin) covers H influenzae.  </li></br><li>Inpatient (as here): IV third-generation cephalosporin (ceftriaxone) ± azithromycin.  </li></br><li>Blood/sputum cultures for severe CAP; consider urinary antigen only for Legionella/Strep pneumo if clinical picture fits.  </li></br></ol></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“H-flu in the Fluorescent Elder”<br /></br>• H = Haemophilus<br /></br>• Flu = pleomorphic Gram-negative “flu-orescent” coccobacilli on Gram stain<br /></br>• Elder = CAP in ELDERly  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>b. Legionella species<br /></br>   • Epidemiology: hotel/cruise A/C water sources fits; HOWEVER Gram stain typically shows many neutrophils with NO organisms (poorly Gram-stains). Also often presents with hyponatremia, diarrhea, relative bradycardia.  </p></br><p>c. Mycobacterium tuberculosis<br /></br>   • Subacute/chronic cough, night sweats, weight loss; apex cavitary lesions; acid-fast bacilli, not Gram-neg coccobacilli.  </p></br><p>d. Pneumocystis jirovecii<br /></br>   • Diffuse bilateral interstitial infiltrates, non-productive cough, hypoxemia; occurs in AIDS or prolonged steroid use; silver-staining cysts, not seen on routine Gram stain.  </p></br><p>e. Pseudomonas aeruginosa<br /></br>   • Risk factors: severe COPD, cystic fibrosis, bronchiectasis, recent hospital stay, ventilator, neutropenia. Gram-negative rod but larger, often green pigment, fruity odor; not classic in an otherwise healthy community-dwelling elder.  </p></br><p>f. Staphylococcus aureus<br /></br>   • Post-influenza pneumonia, IV-drug use, cavitary/multilobar infiltrates; Gram-positive cocci in clusters on stain.  </p></br><p>g. Streptococcus pneumoniae<br /></br>   • Most common CAP; Gram-positive lancet-shaped diplococci. Gram stain described shows Gram-negative coccobacilli instead.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line: Visible pleomorphic Gram-negative coccobacilli in an elderly patient with lobar CAP = Haemophilus influenzae.</p>
160. A previously healthy 15-year-old girl is brought to the physician because of vaginal discharge and abdominal pain for 3 days. She has not had fever or dysuria. Menarche was at 12 years of age, and her last menstrual period was 1 week ago. She is sexually active and uses an oral contraceptive. Her temperature is 38 °C (100.4 °F), pulse is 80/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 100/60 mm Hg. Abdominal examination shows normal bowel sounds with mild right lower quadrant tenderness without rebound, masses, or hepatosplenomegaly. Pelvic examination shows a friable cervix with a moderate amount of yellow discharge and scant blood. There is cervical motion tenderness but no adnexal tenderness or masses. Gram stain of the cervical discharge shows gram-negative intracellular diplococci. Urine β-hCG is negative. The patient does not want her parents to know that she is ill. Which of the following is the most appropriate course of action?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Tell her to ask an adult other than her parents to consent to her treatment</br>b. Tell her that the law requires that her parents be notified</br>c. Treat her without notifying her parents</br>d. Notify her parents but ask them to keep the information confidential</br>e. Obtain an emergency court order that allows confidential treatment</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>c. Treat her without notifying her parents</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis (Pertinent Positives &amp; Negatives)</br>• Sexually active 15-year-old girl with right-sided pelvic pain, friable cervix, copious yellow discharge and cervical motion tenderness → classic for cervicitis/PID.<br /></br>• Gram-negative intracellular diplococci on Gram stain → Neisseria gonorrhoeae.<br /></br>• Temperature only 38 °C and no adnexal mass → uncomplicated cervicitis/PID; can be managed as outpatient.<br /></br>• Key phrase: “The patient does not want her parents to know.” → tests knowledge of minor consent &amp; confidentiality laws, not the medical treatment itself.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1: Identify condition (gonococcal cervicitis/PID) that falls under “sexually transmitted infection.”<br /></br>Step 2: Apply U.S. minor-consent rules—ALL states allow minors to consent for diagnosis &amp; treatment of STIs; most require that services remain confidential.<br /></br>Step 3: Therefore, physician can and should treat without parental notification or consent.</p></br><p>Take-home concept: Minors have autonomous, confidential consent for STI care (and for pregnancy-related care, contraception, and substance-abuse treatment in most jurisdictions).</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)</br>Neisseria gonorrhoeae is a gram-negative diplococcus that infects columnar epithelium → cervicitis → ascending infection → PID if untreated. Presents with mucopurulent discharge, cervical friability, cervical motion tenderness, abdominal pain, ± fever.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics</br>Outpatient regimen:</br>• Ceftriaxone 500 mg IM once (covers N. gonorrhoeae) PLUS<br /></br>• Doxycycline 100 mg PO BID ×7 d (empiric Chlamydia trachomatis coverage).<br /></br>Add metronidazole if vaginitis/VBV suspected.<br /></br>No imaging needed unless tubo-ovarian abscess suspected.<br /></br>Screen for HIV, syphilis, hepatitis B/C.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“GASPP” – Minors can Give Autonomous consent for:</br>G – Gonorrhea &amp; other STIs<br /></br>A – Alcohol / substance abuse treatment<br /></br>S – Suicide / psychiatric emergencies<br /></br>P – Pregnancy &amp; prenatal care<br /></br>P – Prescription contraception</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Tell her to ask another adult to consent – Unnecessary; the law already authorizes HER to consent.<br /></br>b. Tell her the law requires parental notification – False for STI care; would breach confidentiality.<br /></br>d. Notify parents but ask them to keep it confidential – Also breaches her legal right to confidentiality.<br /></br>e. Emergency court order – Reserved for life-threatening situations or refusal of lifesaving therapy; not needed when the minor is legally empowered.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Confusing “parental consent” with “informed consent.” For STI, the minor herself provides informed consent and dictates who, if anyone, gets notified.</p></br><p>Key takeaway: For sexually transmitted infections, treat the minor patient confidentially; no parental involvement is required unless the patient wishes it.</p>
161. A 52-year-old man comes to the emergency department because of swelling of his lips for 5 hours. He has not had difficulty breathing or any other symptoms. Six hours ago, he ate a Thanksgiving meal, which included turkey, gravy, stuffing, cranberries, sweet potatoes, pearl onions, and pumpkin pie. Before dinner, he ate salmon hors d’oeuvres. He has hypertension and hyperlipidemia. Current medications include 81-mg aspirin, atorvastatin, and lisinopril. On arrival, he is not in distress. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows edema of the lips. There is no stridor or wheezes. Results of initial laboratory studies are within the reference range. In addition to administration of an antihistamine, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Measurement of serum C3 and C4 concentrations</br>b. Radioallergosorbent test (RAST)</br>c. Discontinuation of aspirin</br>d. Discontinuation of atorvastatin</br>e. Discontinuation of lisinopril</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Discontinuation of lisinopril  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key words/clues: 52-year-old man; isolated, non-pruritic swelling of the lips (“angioedema”); no urticaria, wheeze, or stridor; normal vitals and laboratories; current medication list includes an ACE inhibitor (lisinopril).<br /></br>• Pertinent positives: Lip edema without respiratory compromise or rash → suggests non-histaminergic (bradykinin-mediated) angioedema.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: No dyspnea, stridor, urticaria, or hypotension → argues against anaphylaxis; no personal/family history of recurrent attacks → makes hereditary angioedema unlikely.<br /></br>Pattern recognition: Middle-aged patient on an ACE inhibitor who suddenly develops angioedema after months/years of uneventful use → classic for ACE-inhibitor–induced bradykinin angioedema.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify angioedema.<br /></br>Step 2: Determine mechanism.<br /></br> • Histamine-mediated (allergic) → usually has urticaria, pruritus, or obvious trigger.<br /></br> • Bradykinin-mediated → classically due to ACE inhibitors or C1-esterase inhibitor deficiency.<br /></br>Step 3: Look for culprit. ACE inhibitor present → immediately discontinue; future episodes can be life-threatening.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Any episode of angioedema in a patient taking an ACE inhibitor mandates permanent discontinuation of the drug.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>ACE inhibitors block degradation of bradykinin. Accumulated bradykinin ↑ vascular permeability → rapid, non-pitting, non-pruritic edema of face, lips, tongue, and upper airway.<br /></br>• Onset: Can occur at any time during therapy (days to years).<br /></br>• Not IgE-mediated; therefore epinephrine, steroids, antihistamines are often ineffective.<br /></br>• Airway monitoring is critical; severe cases may need icatibant (bradykinin B2-receptor antagonist) or C1-esterase inhibitor concentrate.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Immediate priorities:<br /></br>1. Airway assessment ± intubation if progressive swelling.<br /></br>2. Stop the ACE inhibitor permanently.<br /></br>3. Supportive care (antihistamine was already given though benefit is minimal).<br /></br>No routine labs or imaging are required to confirm ACE-I angioedema.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ACE = Angioedema Comes Eventually.”<br /></br>If lips/tongue swell and an ACE-I is on the med list, pull the plug on the drug.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Measurement of serum C3 &amp; C4: Appropriate when suspecting hereditary angioedema (↓ C4), which presents in adolescence/early adulthood with recurrent abdominal pain or edema and positive family history; none of those clues are present.<br /></br>b. Radioallergosorbent test (RAST): Detects specific IgE to food/environmental allergens; IgE-mediated allergy would show urticaria, pruritus, or systemic anaphylaxis—not seen here.<br /></br>c. Discontinuation of aspirin: NSAIDs can cause pseudo-allergic angioedema but aspirin 81 mg is very unlikely, and ACE-I is a far more common, well-documented culprit.<br /></br>d. Discontinuation of atorvastatin: Statins are not associated with angioedema. Stopping it would not address the pathophysiology.<br /></br>Remember: The test writers place tempting food triggers/medications in the stem; always scan the medication list for an ACE inhibitor in any angioedema vignette.</p>
162. A 27-year-old woman comes for a follow-up examination 8 days after completing a course of amoxicillin for treatment of sinusitis. She has been feeling well but recently noticed a painful white patch in her mouth. Examination shows a 1.5-cm white patch behind the tongue; the patch cannot be scraped off. A photomicrograph of the KOH preparation is shown. Which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Amoxicillin-clavulanate</br>b. Amphotericin B</br>c. Chlorhexidine</br>d. Fluconazole</br>e. Itraconazole</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Fluconazole  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Recent antibiotic exposure (8-day course of amoxicillin) → eradication of normal oral bacterial flora → overgrowth of Candida species.<br /></br>• Painful 1.5-cm white oral plaque; KOH prep pictured (budding yeasts with pseudohyphae) → classic for oral candidiasis (“thrush”).<br /></br>• Key negatives: No mention of immunosuppression, HIV, EBV, or villous surface—rules out oral hairy leukoplakia; no curled borders or premalignant features—rules out true leukoplakia.<br /></br>Pattern: “Antibiotics → white oral plaque + KOH budding yeast” = Candida infection.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Identify risk factor (recent broad-spectrum antibiotic).<br /></br>2. Recognize characteristic lesion confirmed by KOH.<br /></br>3. Choose first-line systemic or topical antifungal therapy.<br /></br>   • Mild disease: nystatin suspension or clotrimazole troches.<br /></br>   • Moderate, painful, or plaque difficult to remove: single 150-mg oral dose of fluconazole (systemic azole is guideline-recommended and is the best option provided).<br /></br>Core takeaway: Antibiotic-associated thrush is treated with an azole antifungal; fluconazole is the oral agent of choice when topicals are not listed.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)<br /></br>• Etiology: Candida albicans (dimorphic yeast).<br /></br>• Risk factors: Antibiotics, inhaled steroids without spacer/rinse, diabetes, immunosuppression, xerostomia.<br /></br>• Mechanism: Suppression of bacterial competitors allows Candida overgrowth; organism adheres to mucosal epithelium forming pseudomembranous plaques.<br /></br>• Clinical: White, curd-like plaques; usually scrape off to reveal erythematous base (occasionally adherent if thick). Sore throat, dysgeusia.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>• Diagnosis: Clinical ± KOH prep (budding yeast + pseudohyphae). Cultures rarely needed.<br /></br>• First-line therapy:<br /></br>  – Topical: Nystatin suspension swish &amp; swallow 5 mL QID or clotrimazole troches 10 mg 5×/day.<br /></br>  – Systemic: Fluconazole 150 mg orally once (or 100 mg daily × 7-14 days) if moderate/severe, refractory, or in immunocompromised patients.<br /></br>• Second-line / refractory: Itraconazole, posaconazole; echinocandins (IV) or amphotericin B reserved for systemic/disseminated disease.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Flu-sh” your thrush: Flu-conazole for oral flus(h)-like white plaques.<br /></br>Triple A mnemonic for risk factors:<br /></br>A – Antibiotics<br /></br>A – Asthma inhaler (steroids)<br /></br>A – AIDS (immunosuppression)  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Amoxicillin-clavulanate – Another antibiotic; would worsen, not treat, Candida. Used for β-lactamase sinusitis/otitis, not fungal infections.<br /></br>b. Amphotericin B – Broad-spectrum IV polyene for life-threatening systemic mycoses (e.g., disseminated candidemia, mucormycosis). Too toxic for simple oral thrush.<br /></br>c. Chlorhexidine – Antiseptic mouth rinse effective for gingivitis or oropharyngeal decontamination; lacks antifungal activity against Candida plaques.<br /></br>e. Itraconazole – Azole with broader mold coverage, used for histoplasmosis, blastomycosis, onychomycosis. Acceptable second-line for refractory thrush, but fluconazole is cheaper, better tolerated, and guideline-preferred.  </p></br><p>Clinical Pitfall: Don’t jump to amphotericin for every Candida infection; route severity matters (topical/systemic). Start with fluconazole for uncomplicated mucosal disease.</p>
163. A 42-year-old woman comes to the physician for a routine health maintenance examination. She received the diagnosis of glomerulonephritis 1 year ago, which was successfully treated with corticosteroids, with resolution of her kidney failure and proteinuria. At her last visit 1 month ago, her blood pressure was 110/70 mm Hg. Her serum creatinine concentration at that time was 0.9 mg/dL, and urine dipstick was negative for protein and blood. Her only medications are lisinopril and a daily multivitamin. She swims 2 miles five times weekly. She is 165 cm (5 ft 5 in) tall and weighs 57 kg, or 126lb; BMI is 21 kg/m². Her pulse is 58/min, and blood pressure is 130/86 mm Hg. Examination, including cardiopulmonary examination, shows no other abnormalities. There is no peripheral edema. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin 13.6 g/dL</br>Serum urea nitrogen 10 mg/dL</br>Creatinine 0.9 mg/dL</br>Urine protein 1+</br>RBC 0/hpf</br>WBC 0/hpf</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate next step to slow progression of this patient’s renal insufficiency?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Decrease exercise regimen</br>b. Increase dietary protein</br>c. Increase fluid intake</br>d. Increase lisinopril dosage</br>e. Methotrexate therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Increase lisinopril dosage  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Past GN but now “resolved” ➜ still at long-term risk for CKD progression.<br /></br>• New findings: BP has crept up from 110/70 → 130/86 mm Hg and urine now 1+ protein (≈30–300 mg/dL). No hematuria, creatinine stable (0.9 mg/dL).<br /></br>• Pertinent positives: borderline‐elevated BP + recurrent proteinuria.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: no edema, no active urinary sediment (0 RBC/WBC).<br /></br>Pattern: “Proteinuria + sub-optimal BP control in a CKD patient already on an ACE inhibitor.” Standard next step = titrate ACE (or ARB) dose upward until (1) BP &lt;130/80 AND (2) proteinuria falls, unless creatinine rises ≥30% or K⁺ &gt;5.5 mEq/L.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Protein in urine accelerates tubulo-interstitial injury.<br /></br>2. RAAS blockade (ACE-I/ARB) lowers efferent arteriolar resistance → ↓intraglomerular pressure → ↓proteinuria → slows nephron loss.<br /></br>3. Guideline goal BP for CKD with proteinuria: &lt;130/80 mm Hg; titrate ACE/ARB to max-tolerated dose.<br /></br>Take-home: “In CKD/proteinuria, optimize ACE/ARB first.”</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Proteinuria itself is nephrotoxic (induces inflammatory cytokines, tubular injury, fibrosis).<br /></br>• Angiotensin II constricts efferent arteriole → ↑glomerular capillary pressure → worsens protein leak.<br /></br>• ACE inhibitors/ARBs block this, thereby:<br /></br>  – ↓Proteinuria (up to 40–50 %)<br /></br>  – Slow GFR decline by ~30 % over years.<br /></br>Risk factors for progression: hypertension, persistent proteinuria, high dietary protein, smoking.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>1. First-line: Maximize ACE-I (e.g., raise lisinopril from 10 mg to 20–40 mg daily) or switch to an ARB if ACE-I not tolerated.<br /></br>2. Monitor: serum creatinine &amp; potassium 1–2 weeks after each dose increase. Acceptable creatinine rise ≤30 %.<br /></br>3. Adjuncts: Sodium restriction, weight control, statin if dyslipidemic; SGLT2 inhibitor in T2DM; low-protein diet (0.8 g/kg/d).  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“P rotein + P ressure → P ump down the ACE”<br /></br>(Presence of Proteinuria + elevated Pressure? Pump up the ACE inhibitor.)</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Decrease exercise regimen<br /></br>• Endurance exercise neither causes nor worsens proteinuria-related CKD; improves cardiovascular health.  </p></br><p>b. Increase dietary protein<br /></br>• High protein intake boosts intraglomerular pressure → increases proteinuria and accelerates CKD. Guidelines favor moderate protein restriction (0.8 g/kg/d).  </p></br><p>c. Increase fluid intake<br /></br>• Liberal fluids do not reduce proteinuria or slow CKD; may precipitate hyponatremia.  </p></br><p>e. Methotrexate therapy<br /></br>• Immunosuppressant for rheumatoid arthritis, psoriasis, ectopic pregnancy. No role in preventing relapse of post-infectious or idiopathic GN now in remission; risks nephrotoxicity.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Thinking that a “normal” creatinine negates need for aggressive RAAS blockade. Proteinuria itself is an independent target—treat the urine protein, not just the creatinine.</p>
164. A 19-year-old man is brought to the physician by his parents because they are worried about his behavior since he dropped out of college 2 months ago. When he came home, he appeared distracted and preoccupied and would not answer their questions. He would only say that it is unsafe for him to return to school because his professors had been stealing from him and that campus police are searching for him for use in an experiment. His parents state that he has always been shy and more of a loner than their other children but that now he will not even talk to them. He has not had changes in sleep or appetite. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. His parents report that his maternal cousin takes medication for “psychiatric problems” and has a history of several psychiatric hospitalizations, but that they are unaware of the diagnosis. The patient is mildly malodorous and unshaven. Vital signs are within normal limits. He will not cooperate with a full physical examination but appears to move all four extremities spontaneously. On mental status examination, he is angry, mumbles to himself, and searches his pockets for something. He says he is not “depressed.” His speech is normal in rate and rhythm. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Bipolar disorder, manic</br>b. Delusional disorder</br>c. Major depressive disorder with psychotic features</br>d. Marijuana intoxication</br>e. Schizophrenia</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>e. Schizophrenia  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis – Key Clues</br>• Age 19 y/o male: peak age of onset for primary psychotic disorders<br /></br>• Functional decline: dropped out of college, isolated, poor self-care (“mildly malodorous and unshaven”)<br /></br>• Positive symptoms ≥1 mo: persecutory delusions (“professors stealing,” “campus police want him for an experiment”), possible auditory hallucinations (mumbling to himself)<br /></br>• Negative symptoms &amp; prodrome: lifelong social withdrawal (“always been shy and more of a loner”)<br /></br>• No mood episode: denies depression, no sleep or appetite change, no elevated/irritable mood<br /></br>• Family history of severe psychiatric illness – increases suspicion for schizophrenia spectrum  </p></br><p>Together these findings form the classic pattern:<br /></br>Prodromal negative symptoms → functional decline → ≥1 mo of active delusions/hallucinations → residual negativity = SCHIZOPHRENIA.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)</br>1. Establish psychosis (delusions ± hallucinations).<br /></br>2. Look for predominant or concurrent mood episode—none present.<br /></br>3. Assess chronicity: active psychosis ≥1 mo plus prodromal negative symptoms &gt;5 mo ⇒ total illness ≥6 mo = schizophrenia (schizophreniform requires total &lt;6 mo, but NBME counts the prodrome).<br /></br>Take-home: Psychosis without prominent mood symptoms + ≥6 mo overall illness = schizophrenia.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Dopamine hyperactivity in mesolimbic pathway → positive sx; hypoactivity in mesocortical pathway → negative sx.<br /></br>• Genetic loading (first-degree ≈10 %, cousin still ↑ risk).<br /></br>• Neuroanatomy: enlarged ventricles, ↓ cortical volume, ↓ hippocampal size on imaging (not required for dx).</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics</br>1. First-line: atypical (second-generation) antipsychotic such as risperidone, olanzapine, quetiapine, aripiprazole.<br /></br>2. Clozapine for treatment-resistant cases (watch AGRANULOCYTOSIS).<br /></br>3. Psychosocial: CBT for psychosis, social-skills &amp; vocational rehab, family education.<br /></br>4. Rule-out medical causes: urine tox, TSH, CMP, RPR, B12/folate, HIV, CT/MRI if focal deficits.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“6 letters in S-C-H-I-Z-O = ≥6 months.”<br /></br>“Positive L I M B” – Mesolimbic ↑ DA = delusions/hallucinations; “Negative C O R T” – Mesocortical ↓ DA = flat affect, isolation.</p></br><p>Why Other Choices Are Wrong</br>a. Bipolar disorder, manic<br /></br>   • Requires ≥1 week of elevated/irritable mood + ↑ energy, decreased need for sleep, pressured speech, grandiosity. None present. Psychosis in bipolar appears only during mood episodes.</p></br><p>b. Delusional disorder<br /></br>   • Delusions are non-bizarre, fixed, ≥1 mo, with otherwise normal function and NO hallucinations or negative symptoms. This patient is disorganized, socially/occupationally impaired, and likely hallucinating.</p></br><p>c. Major depressive disorder with psychotic features<br /></br>   • Must meet criteria for a major depressive episode (SIGECAPS). Patient denies depression, has normal sleep/appetite.</p></br><p>d. Marijuana intoxication<br /></br>   • Acute (hours), not months. Presents with conjunctival injection, tachycardia, hunger, euphoria/paranoia—but not sustained functional decline and negative symptoms.</p></br><p>Exam Pitfall to Avoid</br>Students often choose schizophreniform (&lt;6 mo). Remember that DSM requires 6 mo TOTAL illness, not just the active psychosis; prodromal negative symptoms count toward that duration. In NBME items, long-standing social withdrawal typically fulfills the 6-month requirement.</p>
165. An 80-year-old man is brought to the office by his wife because of a 3-month history of “personality changes.” She says he has become withdrawn and is no longer interested in socializing with friends and family. At a restaurant last night, he sexually groped a waitress. The patient has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. Neurologic examination shows no focal findings. On mental status examination, he has a blunted affect and describes his mood as “fine.” He recalls two of three objects after 5 minutes. He makes no errors when performing serial sevens. Serum studies show a thyroid-stimulating hormone concentration of 3.8 mIU/L, thyroxine (T₄) concentration of 5 µg/dL, and triiodothyronine (T₃) concentration of 117 ng/dL; results of other laboratory studies are within the reference ranges. An MRI of the brain shows focal atrophy of the frontal and temporal lobes. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Carbidopa-levodopa therapy</br>b. Levothyroxine therapy</br>c. Marital therapy</br>d. Placement in adult day care</br>e. Risperidone therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Placement in adult day care</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Personality changes,” “withdrawn,” loss of social interest → classic early behavioral symptoms of Frontotemporal dementia (FTD, Pick disease).<br /></br>• Disinhibition (sexually groped waitress) → highly characteristic of behavioral-variant FTD.<br /></br>• Cognition: relatively intact memory (2/3 objects), intact attention (no errors on serial 7’s) → early FTD typically spares memory and orientation.<br /></br>• MRI: “focal atrophy of the frontal and temporal lobes” → radiologic hallmark of FTD; essentially rules in the diagnosis.<br /></br>• Thyroid studies: TSH normal (3.8 mIU/L) with only low-normal T₄ → does not explain the presentation; labs were included as a distractor.<br /></br>Key takeaway: The constellation of early behavioral change + disinhibition + frontal/temporal atrophy = Frontotemporal dementia.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Identify neurodegenerative syndrome → behavioral-variant FTD.<br /></br>2. Recognize that there is currently no disease-modifying medical therapy.<br /></br>3. First-line management is supportive: provide a safe, structured environment and respite for caregivers.<br /></br>4. Adult day care (or other supervised programs) meets these goals → optimal next step.</p></br><p>Core concept: When a patient with FTD exhibits socially inappropriate behavior but is not violent or psychotic, environmental/behavioral interventions and increased supervision are the recommended first measures.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Etiology: Accumulation of abnormal tau or TDP-43 proteins causing neuronal loss in frontal &amp; temporal cortices.<br /></br>• Typical age of onset: 50-70 y (can appear later).<br /></br>• Early manifestations: disinhibition, apathy, empathy loss, hyperorality; memory and visuospatial skills spared until late.<br /></br>• No proven pharmacologic disease-modifying treatment.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>1. Mainstay = supportive care:<br /></br>   • Structured routines, adult day programs, caregiver education.<br /></br>   • Eventually long-term care placement is often required.<br /></br>2. Pharmacologic options (symptomatic only):<br /></br>   • SSRIs (e.g., sertraline, paroxetine, trazodone) for compulsive or disinhibited behaviors.<br /></br>   • Low-dose atypical antipsychotics (e.g., risperidone) ONLY for severe agitation or danger to self/others—use with caution (↑ mortality, stroke in dementia).<br /></br>3.
