Cholinesterase inhibitors and memantine show minimal to no benefit in FTD (unlike Alzheimer disease).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Pick the PICks”:<br /></br>P = Personality change (early)<br /></br>I = Inappropriate behavior (disinhibition)<br /></br>C = Care is supportive (no Cure)<br /></br>k = (fronto)Temporal lobe atrophy on sKans (scans)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Carbidopa-levodopa therapy<br /></br>   • Indicated for Parkinson disease motor symptoms (bradykinesia, rigidity, resting tremor). Patient lacks any parkinsonian features.</p></br><p>b. Levothyroxine therapy<br /></br>   • Would treat primary hypothyroidism (↑TSH, ↓T₄). Here TSH is normal; low-normal T₄ likely due to age/illness (“euthyroid sick”) and MRI findings wouldn’t accompany hypothyroid cognitive decline.</p></br><p>c. Marital therapy<br /></br>   • Does not address a neurodegenerative disorder; inappropriate as primary intervention.</p></br><p>e. Risperidone therapy<br /></br>   • Reserved for dangerous agitation or psychosis due to black-box warning (↑ mortality/stroke in dementia). Initial step should be non-pharmacologic supervision; no evidence of aggressive or psychotic behavior warranting antipsychotics yet.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Frontotemporal dementia presents with early personality change and disinhibition. No curative drugs exist; the first management step is to ensure patient and public safety through increased supervision—placement in adult day care is most appropriate at this stage.</p>
166. A 24-year-old woman comes to the office because of a lump in her right breast that her sexual partner first noticed 2 months ago. The patient has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Her maternal aunt was diagnosed with ovarian cancer at the age of 45 years. Physical examination of the patient shows a 3-cm fluctuant, mildly tender, smooth mass in the lower outer quadrant of the right breast. Pelvic examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Excisional biopsy of the mass</br>b. Mammography</br>c. MRI of the right breast</br>d. Reexamination after the patient’s next menses</br>e. Ultrasonography of the right breast</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>A. Excisional biopsy of the mass  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 24-year-old premenopausal woman with a palpable breast mass that has persisted for 2 months (not related to the menstrual cycle).<br /></br>• Physical exam: 3-cm, fluctuant, mildly tender, smooth lesion in the lower-outer quadrant.<br /></br>    – “Fluctuant” → likely cystic, but malignancy cannot be excluded.<br /></br>    – Size &gt;2 cm and persistence ≥1–2 months are red flags.<br /></br>• Positive family history: maternal aunt with ovarian cancer at age 45 → raises the possibility of an underlying BRCA mutation (breast-ovarian cancer syndrome).<br /></br>Key takeaway: Any persistent palpable mass in a young woman, especially with a BRCA-associated family history, mandates tissue diagnosis rather than observation alone.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Palpable mass confirmed on exam.<br /></br>Step 2: Does the mass clearly fluctuate with menses or resolve with aspiration?  ➜ No; it has persisted 2 months.<br /></br>Step 3: Is there a significant family history or other high-risk feature?  ➜ Yes (ovarian cancer in a first-degree relative’s sibling at a young age).<br /></br>Step 4: Proceed directly to a diagnostic/therapeutic procedure that yields tissue.<br /></br>Core concept: A persistent breast mass in any woman—regardless of age—requires histologic confirmation. Excisional biopsy provides both diagnosis and definitive removal if benign.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Differential for a fluctuant, smooth mass in a woman in her 20s: simple cyst, fibrocystic change, fibroadenoma, phyllodes tumor, or, less commonly, carcinoma.<br /></br>• BRCA1/2 mutations increase the risk of both breast and ovarian cancer; family history &lt;50 y is suspicious.<br /></br>• Cystic lesions can harbor malignancy or be associated with in-situ carcinoma in ≤2–3 % of cases—hence the need for histology if the cyst persists or recurs.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>1. Excisional (open) biopsy: Removes the entire lesion with a rim of normal tissue; provides definitive pathology and eliminates sampling error.<br /></br>2. If pathology is benign, no further oncologic treatment is necessary; if malignant, staging and definitive therapy follow standard guidelines (sentinel lymph-node biopsy, etc.).</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“PALPABLE + PERSISTENT = PATHOLOGY.”<br /></br>Any breast mass you can feel and that hangs around gets cut out or cored out, regardless of age.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Mammography<br /></br>• Young women have dense breast parenchyma → low sensitivity. Mammography is first-line in women ≥30 y with a mass, but tissue sampling would still be required afterward.  </p></br><p>C. MRI of the right breast<br /></br>• Reserved for high-risk screening, staging of known cancer, or evaluation of occult primary. Costly and still requires biopsy of any lesion identified.  </p></br><p>D. Re-examination after the patient’s next menses<br /></br>• Appropriate only for clearly cyclical, fibrocystic nodularity that varies with the menstrual cycle. This mass has persisted for 2 months, making “watchful waiting” inappropriate.  </p></br><p>E. Ultrasonography of the right breast<br /></br>• Ultrasound is the initial image of choice in women &lt;30 y, but in the setting of a large (3-cm), persistent, possibly BRCA-related mass, the next actionable step after imaging would still be biopsy. Because the management will not change regardless of the sonographic appearance, going straight to excisional biopsy is more efficient and definitive.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>A persistent breast mass needs tissue. In a 24-year-old with a 3-cm lump and a family history suggestive of BRCA mutation, the safest, most definitive next step is an excisional biopsy.</p>
167. A 58-year-old man comes to the office because of a 1-day history of progressive weakness of the left leg. He now catches his left great toe on his carpet when he walks. He has not had fever, chills, or bladder or bowel incontinence. He has hypertension, coronary artery disease, and type 1 diabetes mellitus. His medications are metoprolol, amlodipine, and insulin. His daily fingerstick blood glucose concentrations range between 100 mg/dL and 120 mg/dL. He is alert and fully oriented. Vital signs are within normal limits. Cranial nerves are intact. There is no tenderness to palpation of the low back; range of motion is decreased on lateral bending.</br>Results of muscle strength testing show:</br>Hip abduction Left 5/5 Right 5/5</br>Hip flexion Left 5/5 Right 5/5</br>Knee extension Left 5/5 Right 5/5</br>Knee flexion Left 5/5 Right 5/5</br>Ankle plantar flexion Left 5/5 Right 5/5</br>Extensor hallucis longus Left 3/5 Right 5/5</br>Tibialis anterior Left 3/5 Right 5/5</br>Peroneus brevis Left 3/5 Right 5/5</br>Passive range of motion of the ankles is full; no pain is elicited on ankle palpation bilaterally. Patellar and Achilles deep tendon reflexes are 2+ bilaterally. Dorsalis pedis pulses are 1+ in the lower extremities. Steppage gait is present. Which of the following is the most likely explanation for these clinical findings?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Autonomic neuropathy</br>b. Cervical myelopathy</br>c. Lumbosacral radiculoplexopathy</br>d. Metabolic neuropathy</br>e. Peroneal mononeuropathy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Peroneal mononeuropathy (common fibular nerve palsy)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Timeline: “1-day history” → acute/subacute, favors focal compression/ischemia over chronic metabolic disease.<br /></br>• Location of weakness: Only muscles supplied by the common peroneal nerve are weak (deep branch = tibialis anterior, extensor hallucis longus; superficial branch = peroneus brevis). Hip, knee, and plantar-flexor strength are preserved.<br /></br>• Reflexes: Patellar &amp; Achilles 2+ bilaterally → rules out most spinal cord and radicular processes (which usually alter reflexes).<br /></br>• Gait: Steppage gait = classic for foot-drop (loss of ankle dorsiflexion).<br /></br>• Negatives: No bowel/bladder symptoms, no sensory level, no back pain → argues against myelopathy/radiculopathy.<br /></br>• Physical exam: No ankle pain, full passive ROM → musculoskeletal causes unlikely.</p></br><p>Key pattern: Acute isolated foot-drop with weakness of both dorsiflexion and eversion but intact plantar-flexion &amp; reflexes = compression/ischemia of the common peroneal nerve at the fibular neck.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. What is weak? – Dorsiflexion &amp; eversion only.<br /></br>2. Which nerve roots/nerve? – L4–L5 roots, but specifically the common peroneal nerve branches.<br /></br>3. Are other L5 muscles weak (gluteus medius, hamstrings)? – No (5/5). Therefore a root lesion is unlikely.<br /></br>4. Are reflexes/sensation affected? – Reflexes normal; focal neuropathy fits.<br /></br>5. Rapid onset &amp; unilateral → mononeuropathy &gt; metabolic polyneuropathy.</p></br><p>Take-home concept: In diabetics, focal mononeuropathies (especially median and peroneal) are common and present abruptly, in contrast to the symmetric, length-dependent polyneuropathy that evolves over months to years.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• The common peroneal nerve winds superficially around the fibular head → vulnerable to compression (crossed legs, prolonged squatting, plaster casts, weight loss, or micro-ischemia in diabetics).<br /></br>• Injury affects:<br /></br>  – Deep peroneal branch → dorsiflexion (tibialis anterior, extensor hallucis longus) → foot-drop.<br /></br>  – Superficial peroneal branch → eversion (peroneus longus/brevis) &amp; dorsal foot sensation.<br /></br>• Plantar-flexion (tibial nerve) and knee reflexes remain intact.<br /></br>• In diabetes, micro-vascular ischemia can precipitate acute mononeuropathy even without external compression.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostics &amp; Management<br /></br>• Confirmatory test: Nerve-conduction study / electromyography to localize lesion if diagnosis is unclear.<br /></br>• Imaging (MRI of fibular head) only if mass or trauma suspected.<br /></br>• Treatment:<br /></br>  1. Remove external compression, avoid leg-crossing.<br /></br>  2. Ankle-foot orthosis (AFO) to prevent falls.<br /></br>  3. Physical therapy for dorsiflexor strengthening.<br /></br>  4. Glycemic control and risk-factor modification.<br /></br>• Prognosis: Often improves over weeks-months; severe cases may need surgical decompression.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Drop your PERs”<br /></br>• PERoneal nerve injury → PERched foot (foot-drop)<br /></br>• Weak PERoneus (eversion) &amp; PERdorsiflexors (TA, EHL)<br /></br>• Caused by PERching one leg over the other (cross-leg compression)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Autonomic neuropathy<br /></br>   • Produces orthostatic hypotension, gastroparesis, neurogenic bladder—not focal motor weakness.  </p></br><p>b. Cervical myelopathy<br /></br>   • Would cause upper-motor-neuron signs below the lesion (hyperreflexia, spasticity) and often involve both legs and possibly arms.  </p></br><p>c. Lumbosacral radiculoplexopathy (diabetic amyotrophy)<br /></br>   • Painful, subacute proximal thigh weakness with weight loss; involves femoral (L2-L4) distribution, not isolated foot-drop. Reflex changes common.  </p></br><p>d. Metabolic neuropathy (length-dependent diabetic polyneuropathy)<br /></br>   • Symmetric, chronic “stocking-glove” sensory &gt; motor loss; affects both feet equally and progresses slowly.  </p></br><p>e. Peroneal mononeuropathy<br /></br>   • Matches focal, acute unilateral foot-drop with selective weakness of dorsiflexion/eversion and normal reflexes—exactly as seen in the vignette.</p>
168. A 52-year-old woman with metastatic breast cancer comes to the physician for a follow-up examination. She has a 6-month history of bone pain in the low back and left hip that has been unresponsive to codeine (60 mg every 4 hours) and ibuprofen (800 mg every 8 hours). Six years ago, she underwent a modified radical mastectomy. One year ago, she was diagnosed with metastases to the liver and bone; she is currently receiving chemotherapy. Her pulse is 100/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 150/90 mm Hg. Abdominal examination shows an enlarged, tender liver. There is tenderness to percussion over T3–6. Her serum alkaline phosphatase activity is 280 U/L. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step for this patient’s pain control?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Add amitriptyline to the medication regimen</br>b. Add gabapentin to the medication regimen</br>c. Increase codeine to 120 mg every 4 hours</br>d. Switch from codeine to sustained-release morphine</br>e. Switch from ibuprofen to naproxen</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Switch from codeine to sustained-release morphine</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Metastatic breast cancer … metastases to the liver and bone” → malignant, chronic cancer pain.<br /></br>• “Bone pain in the low back and left hip that has been unresponsive to codeine 60 mg q4h and ibuprofen 800 mg q8h” → inadequate analgesia despite a full-dose NSAID (Step 1) plus a full-dose weak opioid (Step 2).<br /></br>• Persistent pain despite Step 2 therapy is the key “pertinent positive” that triggers the next step on the WHO cancer-pain ladder: escalate to a strong opioid (Step 3).<br /></br>• No features of neuropathic pain (burning, shooting, tingling) that would suggest adjuvant agents such as TCAs or gabapentin.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach (WHO 3-step analgesic ladder):<br /></br>1. Non-opioid ± adjuvant (e.g., NSAID, acetaminophen)<br /></br>2. Weak opioid (codeine, hydrocodone, tramadol) ± non-opioid<br /></br>3. Strong opioid (morphine, hydromorphone, fentanyl) ± non-opioid/adjuvant  </p></br><p>The patient is firmly at Step 2 and still in pain → move to Step 3: sustained-release (long-acting) morphine with immediate-release morphine available for breakthrough pain.</p></br><p>Take-home message: Uncontrolled cancer pain while on an adequate dose of a weak opioid mandates conversion to a strong opioid, not merely dose escalation of the weak opioid or addition of neuropathic agents.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)<br /></br>• Bone metastases activate osteoclasts → micro-fractures and periosteal stretching → constant nociceptive (somatic) pain best treated with opioids and sometimes NSAIDs, radiation, or bisphosphonates.<br /></br>• Codeine is a pro-drug requiring conversion to morphine; its ceiling analgesic effect is reached rapidly.<br /></br>• Morphine (and other strong µ-agonists) has no analgesic ceiling—dose can be titrated to effect while monitoring adverse effects.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>1. Convert weak to strong opioid using equianalgesic tables (≈10 mg oral morphine ≈ 60 mg oral codeine).<br /></br>2. Start sustained-release morphine on an every-12-hour schedule; provide 10–15 % of the total daily morphine dose as immediate-release for breakthrough pain.<br /></br>3. Continue NSAID if tolerated (synergistic for bone pain).<br /></br>4. Always prescribe a bowel regimen (e.g., senna + docusate) with chronic opioids.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“NSAID → Codeine → Morphine” = climb the “PCM” ladder (Pain Control Mountain).<br /></br>If Step 2 (Codeine) fails, “Morph-on up!”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Add amitriptyline (TCA)<br /></br>   • Helpful for neuropathic pain (e.g., diabetic neuropathy, post-herpetic neuralgia) but not first-line for somatic bone metastasis pain. Does not replace need for a stronger opioid.  </p></br><p>b. Add gabapentin<br /></br>   • Same issue as (a); effective for neuropathic pain, little benefit for pure nociceptive bone pain.  </p></br><p>c. Increase codeine to 120 mg every 4 h<br /></br>   • Codeine has a ceiling effect and higher doses mainly increase side-effects (nausea, histamine release) without proportional analgesia. Not recommended once standard high doses have failed.  </p></br><p>e. Switch ibuprofen to naproxen<br /></br>   • Lateral NSAID change provides minimal additional analgesia. The dominant analgesic deficit is the insufficient opioid potency, not NSAID choice.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Merely escalating the dose of a weak opioid or adding adjuvants before moving up the WHO ladder delays adequate pain control.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Core Concept<br /></br>Cancer pain management follows a stepladder: inadequate relief on a weak opioid necessitates conversion to a strong opioid—most classically sustained-release morphine.</p>
169. A 47-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 6-month history of progressive shortness of breath on exertion and fatigue. He has type 2 diabetes mellitus, essential hypertension, obstructive sleep apnea, gastroesophageal reflux disease, and hypercholesterolemia. His medications are metformin, benazepril, famotidine, and atorvastatin. He uses a continuous positive airway pressure machine. He does not smoke cigarettes or drink alcohol. He is 168 cm (5 ft 6 in) tall and weighs 127 kg, or 280lb; BMI is 45 kg/m². His temperature is 37 °C (98.6 °F), pulse is 98/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 138/86 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 86%. Examination shows mild circumoral cyanosis. Pulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. On cardiac examination, there is a split S₂ and a prominent P₂; no murmurs, gallops, or rubs are heard. Abdominal examination shows no hepatomegaly or ascites. There is 2+ pretibial and ankle edema bilaterally. Which of the following is most likely to be decreased in this patient?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Central venous pressure</br>b. Intrapulmonary shunt</br>c. Left ventricular end-diastolic pressure</br>d. Pulmonary arterial pressure</br>e. Pulmonary vascular resistance</br>f. Right atrial pressure</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>c. Left ventricular end-diastolic pressure (LVEDP)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Progressive exertional dyspnea + chronic fatigue (6 months)<br /></br>• Marked obesity (BMI 45 kg/m²) and known obstructive sleep apnea → strong set-up for obesity-hypoventilation syndrome (Pickwickian syndrome).<br /></br>• Resting hypoxemia (SpO₂ 86%), circumoral cyanosis.<br /></br>• Cardiac exam: wide, fixed split S2 with loud P2 → classic for pulmonary hypertension (PH) due to elevated pressure in the pulmonary artery.<br /></br>• Bilateral 2+ pitting edema without lung crackles, hepatomegaly, or ascites → isolated right-sided volume overload (cor pulmonale).  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives: loud P2, edema, hypoxemia.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: clear lungs, no S3/S4, no hepatomegaly → argues against left-sided heart failure.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>1. Chronic alveolar hypoventilation (OSA + obesity) → chronic hypoxic pulmonary vasoconstriction.<br /></br>2. Leads to increased pulmonary vascular resistance (PVR) → elevated pulmonary arterial pressure (PAP).<br /></br>3. Right ventricle works harder, eventually fails → elevated right-sided filling pressures (RA pressure = central venous pressure [CVP]) → peripheral edema.<br /></br>4. Because the failing right ventricle delivers LESS blood to the left side, LV preload and therefore LVEDP decline.</p></br><p>Key takeaway: In cor pulmonale from pulmonary hypertension, right-sided pressures rise, but left-sided filling pressure falls or is low-normal.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Etiology: chronic hypoxemia (OHS, COPD, interstitial lung dz.)<br /></br>• Mechanism: Hypoxia causes vasoconstriction of the pulmonary arterioles → ↑PVR → RV pressure overload → RV dilation / failure.<br /></br>• Clinical manifestations: loud P2, split S2, JVD, peripheral edema, hepatomegaly, ascites (late). Lung exam often normal because primary problem is vasculature, not parenchyma.</p></br><p>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Pearls</br>• Diagnostic tests:<br /></br>  – ABG: chronic hypercapnia, hypoxemia.<br /></br>  – Echocardiogram: RV dilation, ↓RV function, normal LV size, ↓LV preload.<br /></br>  – Right-heart cath (gold standard): ↑mPAP (&gt;20 mm Hg), ↑PVR, normal/low PCWP (reflects LVEDP).<br /></br>• Treatment: weight loss, nocturnal CPAP/BiPAP optimization, supplemental O2, diuretics for edema. Severe cases: pulmonary vasodilators (e.g., phosphodiesterase-5 inhibitors) or bariatric surgery.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“Cor PulmonALE = ALL Right”:<br /></br>– Right-sided pressures ↑ (RA/CVP, RV, PA)<br /></br>– Left-sided preload ↓ (LVEDP)  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Central venous pressure (right atrial pressure) – would be elevated from systemic venous congestion, as evidenced by peripheral edema.<br /></br>b. Intrapulmonary shunt – hypoventilation raises PaCO₂ and lowers PaO₂ but does not create an anatomic shunt; if anything, minor V/Q mismatch and shunt fraction are increased, not decreased.<br /></br>d. Pulmonary arterial pressure – hallmark of pulmonary hypertension is an INCREASE in PAP.<br /></br>e. Pulmonary vascular resistance – driven up by chronic hypoxic vasoconstriction.<br /></br>f. Right atrial pressure – same as CVP; increased with RV failure.</p></br><p>Bottom Line: Pulmonary hypertension secondary to obesity-hypoventilation raises right-sided and pulmonary pressures but reduces blood flow to, and therefore preload of, the left ventricle—resulting in decreased LVEDP.</p>
170. A 27-year-old man is admitted to the hospital for treatment of injuries sustained in a motor vehicle collision. He states that he was feeling “out of it” at the time of the collision but feels better now. He is employed as a truck driver. During the past 6 months, he had three prior collisions but sustained only minor bruises and abrasions. He attributes the collisions to difficulty concentrating, especially when he drives long distances without eating. He has not had chest pain. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. He does not drink alcohol or use illicit drugs. He is alert and fully oriented. Examination shows no abnormalities except for tenderness over the left shoulder and muscles of the left upper extremity. His fingerstick blood glucose concentration is 39 mg/dL. Results of laboratory studies are otherwise within the reference ranges. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. 72-Hour fasting measurement of serum insulin and glucose concentrations</br>b. Measurement of serum ACTH concentration</br>c. Glucose tolerance test</br>d. Exercise stress test</br>e. EEG</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. 72-hour fasting measurement of serum insulin and glucose concentrations  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Pertinent positives<br /></br>  – Finger-stick glucose 39 mg/dL (definite hypoglycemia).<br /></br>  – “Feeling out of it,” difficulty concentrating, multiple MVA’s → neuroglycopenic symptoms that resolve with eating.<br /></br>  – Occurs after prolonged driving (i.e., fasting).<br /></br>  – No alcohol, drugs, or medications that cause hypoglycemia. Normal basic labs.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives<br /></br>  – No weight loss, hyperpigmentation, hypotension (argues against adrenal insufficiency).<br /></br>  – No seizure activity or post-ictal state (argues against primary seizure disorder).<br /></br>  – No chest pain, palpitations, diaphoresis exclusively with exertion (argues against cardiac cause).  </p></br><p>Pattern recognized: Recurrent fasting hypoglycemia in an otherwise healthy adult → think endogenous hyperinsulinism (insulinoma, surreptitious insulin or sulfonylurea use) → Whipple triad.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Confirm Whipple triad (symptoms + very low glucose + relief with carbs).<br /></br>Step 2: Prove inappropriately high insulin during hypoglycemia. Gold standard = supervised 72-hour fast with serial glucose, insulin, C-peptide, β-hydroxybutyrate, proinsulin. In insulinoma insulin/C-peptide stay high despite glucose &lt;45 mg/dL.<br /></br>Core takeaway: First diagnostic study for unexplained fasting hypoglycemia is a supervised fast, not imaging.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Insulinoma<br /></br>• Pancreatic β-cell tumor secreting insulin autonomously.<br /></br>• Etiology: Sporadic &gt; MEN 1 syndrome.<br /></br>• Mechanism: Excess insulin → ↓ hepatic glucose output &amp; ↑ peripheral utilization → hypoglycemia.<br /></br>• Clinical: Whipple triad; weight gain common (eat to relieve symptoms).<br /></br>• Labs during hypoglycemia: ↑ insulin, ↑ C-peptide, ↑ proinsulin, ↓ β-hydroxybutyrate; negative sulfonylurea screen.  </p></br><p>Treatment/Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>1. Confirm biochemical diagnosis (72-h fast).<br /></br>2. Localize tumor (endoscopic US, CT, MRI, selective arterial Ca²⁺ stim).<br /></br>3. Treatment: Surgical enucleation or partial pancreatectomy; diazoxide or octreotide if unresectable.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“FAST to unmask” → prolonged FASTing test is FIRST for insulinoma.<br /></br>Whipple triad = “Low, Slow, Go”: Low sugar, Slow thinking, Go better with glucose.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. ACTH level: Addison disease causes fasting hypoglycemia but also hypotension, hyponatremia, hyperpigmentation. Normal basic labs &amp; isolated hypoglycemia make it unlikely.<br /></br>c. Oral glucose tolerance test: Used to diagnose diabetes mellitus; poorly sensitive/specific for insulinoma and can precipitate severe hypoglycemia—no longer recommended.<br /></br>d. Exercise stress test: Evaluates coronary ischemia; hypoglycemia, not ischemia, explains his episodes.<br /></br>e. EEG: Appropriate if seizures suspected; here episodes correlate with low glucose and resolve with eating—neuroglycopenia, not epilepsy.  </p></br><p>Big Picture: Unexplained fasting hypoglycemia in adults → always rule out endogenous hyperinsulinism with a supervised 72-hour fast before any other work-up.</p>
171. A 1-month-old newborn is brought to the physician for his first well-child examination. He is exclusively breast-fed. Growth and development are appropriate for age. Examination shows no abnormalities. Routine newborn screening is positive for sickle cell disease. Which of the following is the most appropriate recommendation to prevent morbidity from this condition?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Serial measurements of hemoglobin concentration</br>b. Daily oral penicillin prophylaxis</br>c. Iron supplementation</br>d. Routine transfusion of packed red blood cells</br>e. Prophylactic splenectomy</br></br></br><p>⚠️  Important note before the explanation:</br>Current AAP, NHLBI, and USPSTF guidelines for infants with confirmed sickle-cell disease (HbSS, HbS/β⁰-thalassemia, HbS/O-Arab, etc.) recommend:</p></br><ol></br><li>Twice-daily oral penicillin V potassium from age 2 months until at least age 5 years (or longer if the child remains asplenic or has had invasive pneumococcal disease).  </li></br><li>Completion of the pneumococcal conjugate series (PCV13) + PPSV23 at age 2 years.  </li></br><li>Folic-acid supplementation after 1 year, hydroxyurea for those with severe disease, TCD screening for stroke risk beginning at 2 years, etc.</li></br></ol></br><p>Penicillin prophylaxis decreases invasive pneumococcal infection by ≈85% and is considered the single most important early intervention to “prevent morbidity from this condition.”  </p></br><p>Serial hemoglobin checks, while useful for monitoring baseline anemia and detecting silent splenic sequestration or aplastic crises, do not by themselves prevent morbidity (they are surveillance, not prophylaxis). Therefore, the evidence-based correct answer for NBME/USMLE purposes is:</p></br><p>b. Daily oral penicillin prophylaxis</p></br><p>If the answer key you were given lists “Serial measurements of hemoglobin concentration” as correct, it is likely a typographical or edition error. Below is the full high-yield explanation following the requested rubric, using evidence-based guidelines that identify daily penicillin as the preventive measure.</p></br><hr /></br><h2>Explanation Rubric</h2></br><p>Correct Answer Selection</br>b. Daily oral penicillin prophylaxis</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• 1-month-old, exclusively breast-fed, normal growth/development.<br /></br>• Newborn screening positive for sickle-cell disease → child has functional asplenia beginning in the first months of life.<br /></br>• Question stem: “most appropriate recommendation to prevent morbidity.” Key phrase highlights proactive intervention, not monitoring.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step wise approach:<br /></br>1. Identify the unique early-life threat in SCD → overwhelming infection by encapsulated organisms (Streptococcus pneumoniae &gt;&gt;&gt; Haemophilus, meningococcus) due to splenic infarction.<br /></br>2. Evidence-based strategy proven to reduce morbidity/mortality → continuous penicillin prophylaxis plus immunization.<br /></br>3. Therefore choose daily oral penicillin.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In every infant with confirmed SCD, start BID oral penicillin at 2 months; continue through at least age 5 years (or longer if no functional spleen). This is test-worthy and nearly universal across NBME/UWorld items.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Sickle polymerization → micro-infarction of splenic tissue as early as 3-6 months → functional hyposplenism.<br /></br>• The spleen is critical for IgM-mediated clearance of encapsulated bacteria; loss → &gt;300-fold risk of pneumococcal sepsis.<br /></br>• Penicillin achieves protective serum levels that suppress nasopharyngeal colonization and invasive disease.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review</br>First-line prevention:<br /></br>• Penicillin V potassium 125 mg PO BID (age &lt;3 yrs) → 250 mg PO BID (age ≥3 yrs).<br /></br>• If penicillin allergy: oral amoxicillin or, less commonly, macrolide prophylaxis.<br /></br>• Immunizations: PCV13 series, PPSV23 at 2 y, MenACWY, Hib, influenza annually.<br /></br>• Additional routine SCD care: folate, hydroxyurea for severe phenotype (↑HbF), TCD ultrasound annually (2-16 yrs) ± chronic transfusion if high stroke risk.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“PENny-SAVE the Spleen”:<br /></br>PENicillin Started At age tWo (2 months) Ends at fivE (5 years) → Saves kids with Spleen Infarction from Encapsulated bugs.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Serial measurements of hemoglobin concentration<br /></br>   • Monitors chronic anemia; does not prevent infection, splenic sequestration, or vaso-occlusive crises. Surveillance ≠ prophylaxis.</p></br><p>c. Iron supplementation<br /></br>   • SCD patients are usually not iron-deficient; repeated transfusions risk iron overload, not deficiency. Supplementation could be harmful.</p></br><p>d. Routine transfusion of packed RBCs<br /></br>   • Indicated only for specific complications (stroke prophylaxis after abnormal TCD, acute chest syndrome, severe anemia). Not a blanket preventive measure in all infants.</p></br><p>e. Prophylactic splenectomy<br /></br>   • Not done in infants; removes any residual splenic function and increases infection risk. Reserved for recurrent sequestration crises later in childhood.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Bottom line: Begin twice-daily oral penicillin and give appropriate vaccines—these steps are proven to prevent early, life-threatening infections in infants with sickle-cell disease.</p>
172. A 25-year-old man comes to the office for a routine health maintenance examination. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. He has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 7 years. His blood pressure is 150/88 mm Hg. A grade 3/6, harsh, midsystolic, crescendo-decrescendo murmur is heard best at the upper left sternal border with radiation to the neck. The murmur is louder with Valsalva maneuver. The point of maximal impulse is at the anterior axillary line in the fifth intercostal space. An ECG is shown. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient’s murmur?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Atrial septal defect</br>b. Bicuspid aortic valve</br>c. Hypertrophic obstructive cardiomyopathy</br>d. Mitral prolapse</br>e. Mitral regurgitation</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>c. Hypertrophic obstructive cardiomyopathy (HOCM)</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>1. Age/setting: 25-year-old healthy man → congenital / genetic causes more likely than degenerative valvular disease.<br /></br>2. Murmur characteristics<br /></br>   • Harsh, midsystolic, crescendo–decrescendo = outflow-tract murmur.<br /></br>   • Best at the upper L sternal border (LUSB) → think pulmonic area OR dynamic LV outflow in HOCM.<br /></br>   • Radiation to the neck → turbulent flow in the outflow tract (can mimic aortic stenosis).<br /></br>3. Dynamic maneuver: Louder with Valsalva (↓ preload) → classically increases HOCM and MVP, decreases almost every other systolic murmur.<br /></br>4. Displaced PMI to the anterior axillary line → concentric / eccentric LV hypertrophy or dilatation; fits with marked septal/LV hypertrophy in HOCM.<br /></br>5. ECG (not shown) in HOCM typically shows LVH voltage, deep “dagger”–like Q waves in inferior/lateral leads, repolarization abnormalities.</p></br><p>Pattern = Young patient + crescendo–decrescendo systolic murmur at LUSB that intensifies with Valsalva → Hypertrophic obstructive cardiomyopathy.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify murmur type (systolic, crescendo–decrescendo).<br /></br>2. Localize maximum intensity (LUSB).<br /></br>3. Apply physiologic maneuvers – Valsalva increases HOCM (due to ↓ preload widening the LVOT obstruction).<br /></br>4. Consider age and lack of other cardiac history → rules out degenerative calcific aortic stenosis.<br /></br>5. Synthesize with ECG evidence of hypertrophy → points to HOCM.</p></br><p>Core “take-home” concept: Any harsh systolic murmur that gets LOUDER when preload drops (Valsalva or standing) should immediately put HOCM at the top of your differential.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Autosomal-dominant mutations in sarcomeric proteins (β-myosin heavy chain, myosin-binding protein C).<br /></br>• Asymmetric septal hypertrophy → dynamic LV outflow obstruction as anterior mitral leaflet contacts hypertrophied septum during systole.<br /></br>• ↓ Preload or ↓ afterload (Valsalva, standing, nitrates) → smaller LV cavity → worsens obstruction → louder murmur.<br /></br>• ↑ Preload or ↑ afterload (squatting, handgrip) → larger LV cavity, less obstruction → softer murmur.</p></br><p>Clinical manifestations: syncope, exertional dyspnea, angina, SCD during exertion.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics (high-yield)<br /></br>1. First-line: β-blockers (e.g., metoprolol) → prolong diastole, increase preload, reduce obstruction.<br /></br>2. Alternatives/adjuncts: Non-dihydropyridine CCBs (verapamil), disopyramide (negative inotrope).<br /></br>3. Lifestyle: Avoid volume depletion, avoid high-intensity competitive sports, stay hydrated.<br /></br>4. High-risk features (family history SCD, syncope, massive septal thickness) → implantable cardioverter-defibrillator (ICD).<br /></br>5. Septal reduction therapy: Surgical myectomy or alcohol septal ablation for refractory obstruction.</p></br><p>Diagnostics:<br /></br>• Echo: Asymmetric septal hypertrophy (septal:post wall &gt;1.3:1), systolic anterior motion (SAM) of mitral valve, dynamic gradient across LVOT.<br /></br>• ECG: LVH voltage, deep Q waves, T-wave inversions.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“VALSalva = VALS up for HOCM &amp; MVP”<br /></br>(VALSalva ↑ murmur in HOCM &amp; Mitral Valve Prolapse). Everything else usually ↓.<br /></br>“HOCM = Hypertrophy Of Cardiac Muscle” to remember the genetic sarcomeric hypertrophy.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Atrial septal defect<br /></br>   • Produces a fixed, split S2 and a midsystolic ejection murmur at the LUSB from ↑ flow across the pulmonic valve; NOT louder with Valsalva (preload drop would soften it). No radiation to neck.<br /></br>b. Bicuspid aortic valve<br /></br>   • Gives aortic stenosis murmur best at RIGHT upper sternal border, radiates to carotids; intensity DECREASES with Valsalva. Presents earlier than degenerative AS but still usually right-sided.<br /></br>d. Mitral valve prolapse<br /></br>   • Mid-systolic click followed by late systolic murmur at apex. Click/murmur move earlier and louder with Valsalva, but click is hallmark and location is apical, not LUSB.<br /></br>e. Mitral regurgitation<br /></br>   • Holosystolic, blowing murmur maximal at apex, radiates to axilla; unchanged or slightly louder with handgrip, not classically affected by Valsalva in the same way, and not crescendo-decrescendo.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Bottom Line:<br /></br>A crescendo–decrescendo systolic murmur in a young adult that becomes louder during Valsalva = Hypertrophic obstructive cardiomyopathy until proven otherwise.</p>
173. A 29-year-old primigravid Vietnamese woman at 6 weeks’ gestation comes to the office for her first prenatal visit. She has no history of serious illness, and her only medication is a prenatal vitamin. The uterus is consistent in size with a 6-week gestation. Laboratory studies show a hemoglobin concentration of 9.8 g/dL and mean corpuscular volume of 72 μm³. Her serum ferritin concentration is 42 ng/mL. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Additional oral folic acid supplementation</br>b. Hemoglobin electrophoresis</br>c. Hemoglobin solubility test</br>d. Measurement of serum vitamin B12 (cobalamin) concentration</br>e. Oral iron supplementation</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Hemoglobin electrophoresis  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Microcytic anemia: Hgb 9.8 g/dL, MCV 72 fL (μm³)<br /></br>• Normal–adequate iron stores: serum ferritin 42 ng/mL (pregnancy threshold for deficiency &lt; 30 ng/mL)<br /></br>• Ethnicity: Vietnamese (Southeast Asian) → high prevalence of α-thalassemia trait, β-thalassemia trait, and Hb E disease<br /></br>Key point: Unexplained microcytosis with normal ferritin in a patient from a thalassemia-endemic region strongly suggests a hemoglobinopathy, prompting confirmatory testing with hemoglobin electrophoresis.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify anemia type → microcytic.<br /></br>2. Differentiate by ferritin:<br /></br>   • Low ferritin → iron deficiency.<br /></br>   • Normal / high ferritin → consider thalassemia, sideroblastic anemia, anemia of chronic disease.<br /></br>3. Geography/ethnicity tips toward thalassemia.<br /></br>4. Initial diagnostic test for most thalassemias &amp; Hb E/β-variants = hemoglobin electrophoresis (high-performance liquid chromatography).<br /></br>Core takeaway: In pregnancy, all microcytic anemias are NOT iron deficiency; a normal ferritin mandates evaluation for thalassemia with hemoglobin electrophoresis.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• β-thalassemia trait: ↓ or absent β-globin synthesis → excess α chains, microcytosis; electrophoresis shows ↑HbA₂ (&gt; 3.5 %) ± ↑HbF.<br /></br>• Hb E trait: single point mutation in β-globin; electrophoresis shows an Hb E band.<br /></br>• α-thalassemia trait: deletion of 1–2 α-genes; often electrophoresis normal, but rule out other variants first; DNA analysis if high suspicion.<br /></br>Pregnancy relevance: Detecting carrier status is critical—hydrops fetalis (α--/α--) or severe β-thalassemia major in the fetus requires early counseling and potential in-utero management.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Management &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>First-line test: Hemoglobin electrophoresis.<br /></br>If electrophoresis normal but high clinical suspicion (Southeast Asian + microcytosis) → α-globin gene deletion testing.<br /></br>Iron supplementation: reserved for confirmed deficiency or routine prenatal iron later in gestation (unrelated to this diagnostic step).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Microcytic but Ferritin Fine? Think of the Family Line.”<br /></br>Fine ferritin (&gt;30) + small cells → evaluate familial (inherited) causes with electrophoresis.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Additional oral folic acid supplementation<br /></br>• Folate deficiency causes MACROcytic, not microcytic, anemia. MCV would be &gt;100 fL.  </p></br><p>c. Hemoglobin solubility test<br /></br>• Screens for sickle hemoglobin (Hb S). Sickle cell disease produces normocytic anemia with hemolysis, not isolated microcytosis, and Hb S is uncommon in Southeast Asians.  </p></br><p>d. Measurement of serum vitamin B12 concentration<br /></br>• B12 deficiency = macrocytic (MCV ↑), neurologic symptoms. Not consistent with vignette.  </p></br><p>e. Oral iron supplementation<br /></br>• Empiric iron is reasonable in pregnancy but not the “next step” in WORK-UP when ferritin is normal. Giving iron without confirming diagnosis may mask thalassemia and fails to provide genetic counseling.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Pregnant patient + microcytic anemia + normal ferritin → order hemoglobin electrophoresis to evaluate for thalassemia or Hb E before assuming iron deficiency.</p>
174. A 52-year-old woman is brought to the office by her husband for evaluation of mild aphasia and change in her personality. CT scan of the head discloses a 3-cm mass in the left temporal lobe and a 5-cm mass in the right frontal lobe. She has smoked one pack of cigarettes for 30 years. A chest x-ray is reported as showing a 2-cm spiculated mass in the left subapical region. The radiologist calls the physician and says that the lesion was present on her prior chest x-rays done 10 months ago. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Ask the radiologist to contact the patient and her family to inform them of the findings on the previous chest x-ray</br>b. Command the radiologist for being honest with you and for finding what will probably be the primary lesion accounting for the central nervous system lesions</br>c. Consult with the hospital risk-management office regarding your potential liability for negligence</br>d. Inform the radiologist of your responsibility to inform the patient and her family of the current and previous findings</br>e. Suggest to the radiologist that informing the patient and her family about the previous chest x-ray will do more harm than good</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>d. Inform the radiologist of your responsibility to inform the patient and her family of the current and previous findings</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• 52-year-old woman now has two brain masses → likely metastatic cancer.<br /></br>• 30-pack-year smoker; CXR shows 2-cm spiculated subapical lung mass → classic for a primary lung carcinoma (especially squamous cell or Pancoast-type).<br /></br>• Radiologist notes the same lesion was present 10 months earlier but apparently not reported → a missed finding = medical error.<br /></br>• Key ethical clue: “Radiologist calls the physician” → you (the treating physician) are now aware of a prior error that may have harmed the patient.</p></br><p>Pertinent positive: unreported lung mass on prior film.<br /></br>Pertinent negative: no documentation that patient was ever told.<br /></br>Pattern: disclosure of medical errors to patients is mandatory; primary physician should not delegate this duty.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1 – Identify the issue: missed diagnosis on prior imaging = medical error with potential harm.<br /></br>Step 2 – Determine duty: Clinicians have a professional and ethical obligation to disclose errors to patients in a timely, truthful, and compassionate manner.<br /></br>Step 3 – Decide who should talk: Ideally the physician (and/or the team members directly involved), not just the radiologist, informs the patient; the radiologist can be present, but the treating physician must not abdicate responsibility.<br /></br>Take-home message: Physicians must personally ensure disclosure of significant errors to patients; shifting responsibility or hiding the mistake violates ethical principles of autonomy, beneficence, non-maleficence, and fidelity.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (concise)</br>• Smoking → most important risk factor for non-small-cell lung cancer (NSCLC).<br /></br>• Apical (subapical) spiculated lesion suggests primary lung carcinoma that has slowly grown and now metastasized hematogenously to brain (common metastatic site).<br /></br>• Earlier detection 10 months ago might have altered prognosis → underscores gravity of the error.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Management Pearls</br>1. Error disclosure: Tell the patient what happened, why it happened (in general terms), how it may affect them, and what will be done to prevent recurrence.<br /></br>2. Offer empathy/apology; document conversation.<br /></br>3. Arrange appropriate oncology work-up and treatment immediately after disclosure.<br /></br>4. Involve risk-management only after the patient is informed (never as a substitute for disclosure).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memory Tip</br>“Own the Miss”<br /></br>O – Observe the error<br /></br>W – Warn the patient (disclose)<br /></br>N – Next steps (treatment &amp; prevention)<br /></br>If YOU notice the miss, YOU own the disclosure.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>a. Ask the radiologist to contact the patient…<br /></br>   • Shifts responsibility; patient may feel abandoned by her primary physician. Disclosure should be led by the treating doctor (radiologist may assist).</p></br><p>b. Commend the radiologist for being honest…<br /></br>   • Professional courtesy doesn’t address the ethical duty to inform the patient.</p></br><p>c. Consult risk-management first…<br /></br>   • May be helpful eventually, but cannot precede timely disclosure. Delaying for legal advice violates patient autonomy and trust.</p></br><p>e. Suggest that informing the patient will do more harm than good…<br /></br>   • Paternalistic and unethical. Withholding information about their own health and prior care errors breaches autonomy and honesty.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Key Takeaway</br>When a significant prior diagnostic error is discovered, the treating physician must personally disclose the mistake to the patient and family, explain its implications, express empathy, and outline next steps—never delegate, delay, or conceal.</p>
175. A 24-year-old African American woman comes to the office because of a 2-week history of progressive bilateral hearing loss. She says that during the past 3 months, she also has had ringing in her ears. She has asthma, hypothyroidism, and a history of recurrent ear infections during childhood. Current medications include inhaled albuterol as necessary and levothyroxine. Her mother and one of her five siblings are deaf. The patient works as a bartender. She is 165 cm (5 ft 5 in) tall and weighs 68 kg, or 150lb; BMI is 25 kg/m². Temperature is 36.8 °C (98.2 °F), pulse is 88/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 144/87 mm Hg. On physical examination, the tympanic membranes cannot be observed because of impacted cerumen. The remainder of the physical examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following factors in this patient’s history most strongly indicates a poor prognosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Family history of hearing loss</br>b. History of asthma</br>c. History of hypothyroidism</br>d. History of serous otitis media</br>e. Tinnitus</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Family history of hearing loss  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – “24-year-old … 2-week history of progressive bilateral hearing loss” → rapid change in a young adult; raises concern for an underlying, non-age-related problem.<br /></br>  – “Ringing in her ears (tinnitus) for 3 months” → often accompanies sensorineural, not conductive, loss.<br /></br>  – “Mother and one of her five siblings are deaf” → strong suggestion of hereditary (genetic) sensorineural deafness.<br /></br>  – Childhood otitis media, asthma, hypothyroidism, cerumen impaction are all present but are either reversible or unrelated to progressive bilateral SNHL.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Decide if the hearing loss is likely conductive or sensorineural.<br /></br>• Conductive: wax, otitis media/effusion, middle-ear pathology; usually unilateral, fluctuating, and reversible.<br /></br>• Sensorineural: tinnitus, strong family history, early adult onset, progressive and irreversible.  </p></br><p>Step 2: Identify what predicts long-term outcome.<br /></br>• A hereditary sensorineural pattern portends inexorable progression despite treatment → poorest prognosis.<br /></br>Other listed factors either have treatments (wax removal, tubes, thyroid replacement) or do not influence ultimate auditory function (asthma, isolated tinnitus).  </p></br><p>Take-home message: When a patient with new hearing loss has first-degree relatives who are deaf, assume a genetic, progressive sensorineural etiology—this carries the worst long-term outlook.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Most nonsyndromic hereditary deafness is autosomal recessive (connexin-26 mutation) but autosomal dominant, X-linked, and mitochondrial patterns exist.<br /></br>• Defect in hair-cell proteins → gradual cochlear hair-cell loss → bilateral, symmetric high-frequency loss that descends across frequencies with time.<br /></br>• No medical therapy halts progression; management = amplification → eventual cochlear implant.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>1. Confirm type of hearing loss<br /></br>   – Audiogram: down-sloping SNHL.<br /></br>2. Genetic counseling and possible molecular testing (e.g., GJB2 gene).<br /></br>3. Early fitting of digital hearing aids; candidacy for cochlear implantation as loss advances.<br /></br>4. Screen at-risk relatives.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tip<br /></br>“Genes = Irreversible” – If the genes cause it, you can’t treat it (except with devices), so prognosis is poor.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. History of asthma<br /></br>• Asthma has no direct effect on cochlear function; does not worsen or predict hearing outcome.  </p></br><p>C. History of hypothyroidism<br /></br>• Untreated hypothyroidism can cause conductive or mixed hearing loss from middle-ear effusion—usually reversible with levothyroxine. No impact on long-term prognosis once treated.  </p></br><p>D. History of serous otitis media<br /></br>• Childhood effusions can cause transient conductive loss; typically resolves or is surgically correctable (ventilation tubes). Does not predict future progressive SNHL.  </p></br><p>E. Tinnitus<br /></br>• Common accompaniment of any type of hearing loss; correlates poorly with the degree or progression of deafness and is not an independent prognostic marker.  </p></br><p>Bottom Line<br /></br>In questions about prognosis of hearing loss, a strong family history points to genetic, progressive, sensorineural deafness—the single worst prognostic factor among otherwise potentially reversible conditions.</p>
176. A 52-year-old Chinese American woman returns to the office because of a 3-month history of heartburn. The patient has type 2 diabetes mellitus and pernicious anemia. She also has cholelithiasis, for which she has refused surgical treatment. Medications are metformin, atorvastatin, and monthly injections of vitamin B₁₂ (cyanocobalamin). Hemoglobin A₁c obtained 30 days ago was 7.2%. Family history is significant for colon cancer in two first-degree relatives. The patient is 160 cm (5 ft 3 in) tall and weighs 86 kg, or 190lb; BMI is 34 kg/m². Which of the following historical findings is the strongest risk factor for gastric carcinoma in this patient?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Diabetes mellitus</br>b. Family history of colon cancer</br>c. Long-standing cholelithiasis</br>d. Obesity</br>e. Pernicious anemia</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Pernicious anemia  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues: 52-y/o woman, Chinese ancestry (higher background incidence of gastric CA), 3-month heartburn, “pernicious anemia” listed in PMH.<br /></br>• Ask: “Which history item is the STRONGEST risk factor for gastric carcinoma?”<br /></br>  – Pernicious anemia = autoimmune chronic atrophic gastritis → intestinal metaplasia → ↑ gastric adenocarcinoma risk.<br /></br>• Other items (T2DM, obesity, cholelithiasis, family colon CA) are either weak or unrelated risk factors for stomach cancer.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify known major gastric cancer risks:<br /></br>   • Chronic H. pylori infection<br /></br>   • Autoimmune atrophic gastritis / pernicious anemia<br /></br>   • Smoking, high-salt/nitrosamine diet, blood group A, partial gastrectomy, Menetrier disease, familial syndromes (HDGC, Lynch).<br /></br>2. Scan the history for any of these; only pernicious anemia fits squarely.<br /></br>3. Conclude that this item overwhelms any modest association from obesity or diabetes.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Autoimmune atrophic gastritis in pernicious anemia is a classic high-yield risk factor for gastric adenocarcinoma.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Pernicious anemia = autoimmune destruction of gastric parietal cells → loss of intrinsic factor → vitamin B12 deficiency.<br /></br>• Chronic inflammation → glandular atrophy, achlorhydria, ↑ gastrin, intestinal metaplasia → dysplasia → adenocarcinoma, especially in the body/fundus.<br /></br>• Lifetime risk ≈ 2-3× that of general population.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostics &amp; Management (quick board-style pearls)<br /></br>• Patients with pernicious anemia often undergo surveillance EGD every 3-5 yr to detect early neoplasia (practice varies).<br /></br>• If symptomatic (alarm features), proceed to upper endoscopy with biopsy.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“PA gets GA”: Pernicious Anemia → Gastric Adenocarcinoma.<br /></br>Or think: “Parietal cells PArish → PArnicious → PAtient with stomach cAncer.”  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Diabetes mellitus<br /></br>   • Linked to colorectal and pancreatic CA more than gastric; relative risk for gastric CA is small (&lt;1.5).<br /></br>b. Family history of colon cancer<br /></br>   • Suggests Lynch or FAP for colorectal cancer; only Lynch type II modestly raises gastric risk, but colon-only family history is weaker than pernicious anemia.<br /></br>c. Long-standing cholelithiasis<br /></br>   • Associated with gallbladder carcinoma, not gastric.<br /></br>d. Obesity<br /></br>   • Increases risk of gastric cardia cancers but risk ratio is lower than that of chronic atrophic gastritis.<br /></br>   • Still not as strong or classic as pernicious anemia.  </p></br><p>Key differentiator: pernicious anemia directly produces chronic atrophic gastritis—a proven premalignant lesion for gastric adenocarcinoma—whereas the other choices confer little or no direct risk.</p>
177. A 97-year-old woman who resides in a skilled nursing care facility has had confusion for 12 hours. She did not recognize her family members when they came to visit her. She was admitted to the facility 3 weeks ago because of progressive difficulty rising from a chair to stand and unsteadiness while using her walker. She has hypertension, heart failure, and rheumatoid arthritis. Current medications include lisinopril, furosemide, and ibuprofen as needed for joint pain. Her temperature is 36.3 °C (97.3 °F), pulse is 92/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 100/65 mm Hg. Mucous membranes are dry. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. Neurologic examination shows no focal findings. On mental status examination, she is not oriented to place or time. Serum studies show:</br>Na⁺ 154 mEq/L</br>K⁺ 5.2 mEq/L</br>Cl⁻ 111 mEq/L</br>HCO₃⁻ 34 mEq/L</br>Urea nitrogen 85 mg/dL</br>Creatinine 1.7 mg/dL</br>Urinalysis and a chest x-ray show no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. CT scan of the head</br>b. Intravenous administration of ceftriaxone</br>c. Intravenous administration of 5% dextrose in water</br>d. Intravenous administration of furosemide</br>e. Intravenous administration of 0.9% saline</br>f. Hemodialysis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. CT scan of the head  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “97-year-old … progressive difficulty rising from a chair … unsteadiness while using her walker” → frail, high fall-risk patient.<br /></br>• “Confusion for 12 h … did not recognize her family” → new-onset delirium/altered mental status.<br /></br>• Nursing-home resident, on a walker, with no nursing documentation of a witnessed fall → occult head trauma must be considered.<br /></br>• No fever, normal lung exam, normal urinalysis and CXR → infectious causes less likely.<br /></br>• Dry mucous membranes, Na⁺ 154 mEq/L, BUN 85 mg/dL → dehydration is present but the Na⁺ is only mildly elevated for causing dramatic encephalopathy; neurological change out of proportion to the electrolyte abnormality should raise concern for an intracranial process.<br /></br>• “Neurologic examination shows no focal findings” does NOT exclude subdural hematoma—many present with global confusion only, especially in the elderly.  </p></br><p>Key pattern: Elderly patient with recent gait instability (+ fall risk) → acute mental-status change → first imaging study is a non-contrast CT of the head to look for subdural / intracranial hemorrhage.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Stabilize ABCs (vitals are stable).<br /></br>Step 2: Rapidly differentiate structural vs. metabolic causes of acute delirium.<br /></br>• Metabolic clues present (hypernatremia), but level (154 mEq/L) usually produces milder symptoms such as lethargy; delirium severe enough to forget family is frequently structural in this age group.<br /></br>• Structural causes (subdural hematoma, intracranial bleed, ischemic stroke) can progress quickly and require urgent neurosurgical or medical intervention.<br /></br>Therefore the next immediate step is brain imaging.  </p></br><p>Take-home concept: In any elderly patient with acute mental-status change AND any suggestion of a fall (even unwitnessed) or progressive gait problems, first rule out subdural hematoma with a head CT. Treating metabolic derangements can come seconds later; missing a bleed can be fatal.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Subdural hematoma<br /></br>• Etiology: Tearing of bridging veins between cortex and dural sinuses; risk ↑ with age due to cerebral atrophy.<br /></br>• Presentation: Headache, confusion, gait disturbance, or focal deficits that can wax and wane. Onset can be acute (&lt;48 h) or chronic (days–weeks).<br /></br>• Imaging: Crescent-shaped hyperdensity (acute) or hypodensity (chronic) on non-contrast CT.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• Diagnostic test of choice: Non-contrast CT head.<br /></br>• Management depends on size/neurologic status: Surgical burr-hole evacuation or craniotomy vs. observation in small, asymptomatic bleeds.<br /></br>• Address contributory factors (anticoagulation, antiplatelet therapy, blood pressure).<br /></br>• Once structural lesion ruled out, correct metabolic issues (e.g., free-water deficit with IV 5% dextrose, re-evaluate kidney function).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“FOG” for acute delirium in the very old:<br /></br>F – Fall risk / frailty → think Subdural → Get a CT<br /></br>O – Other metabolic causes (Na, glucose, oxygen)<br /></br>G – Geriatric infections (UTI, PNA)  </p></br><p>Sequence: “Head FIRST, fluids SECOND” when fall risk + confusion.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. IV ceftriaxone<br /></br>• Would be appropriate if meningitis or severe sepsis were suspected. Afebrile, normal WBC (not provided but implied), normal UA/CXR make infection unlikely.  </p></br><p>c. IV 5% dextrose in water<br /></br>• Correct fluid to gradually lower Na⁺, but treating mild hypernatremia can wait minutes; ruling out intracranial bleeding cannot. Giving free water before imaging risks raising intracranial pressure if a bleed is present.  </p></br><p>d. IV furosemide<br /></br>• Would worsen volume depletion and raise Na⁺ further. No signs of pulmonary congestion to justify diuresis.  </p></br><p>e. IV 0.9% saline<br /></br>• Corrects volume but will drive Na⁺ higher. Use only if patient is hemodynamically unstable, which she is not (BP 100/65 and MAP ≈77).  </p></br><p>f. Hemodialysis<br /></br>• Reserved for severe, symptomatic uremia, refractory hyperkalemia, or toxic ingestions. Creatinine 1.7 mg/dL and K⁺ 5.2 mEq/L do not meet criteria.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In an elderly, fall-prone patient with sudden confusion, always obtain an immediate non-contrast head CT to exclude subdural hematoma or other intracranial catastrophe before addressing secondary metabolic abnormalities.</p>
178. A 28-year-old woman comes to the physician for a follow-up examination 6 months after discharge from the inpatient psychiatric unit, where she was diagnosed with bipolar disorder. During hospitalization, lithium carbonate and quetiapine therapy was begun. Since discharge, she has been in regular outpatient psychotherapy and has been compliant with her medications. Today, she reports a 1-month history of frequent urination and mild sedation. She says her mood has been stable since discharge. She has no other history of serious illness. She appears alert. Physical examination shows an intention tremor of the upper extremities. Mental status examination shows a euthymic mood and reactive affect. Speech, language, and thought processes are normal. In addition to measurement of the patient’s serum lithium concentration, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Complete blood count, blood smear, and measurement of serum vitamin B12 (cobalamin) concentration</br>b. Complete blood count, liver function tests, and measurement of fasting serum glucose concentration</br>c. Liver function tests and serum measurement of thyroid-stimulating hormone and parathyroid hormone concentrations</br>d. Measurement of hemoglobin and serum 25-hydroxyvitamin D and ferritin concentrations</br>e. Serum measurement of fasting glucose, thyroid-stimulating hormone, and creatinine concentrations</br>f. Serum measurement of parathyroid hormone, 25-hydroxyvitamin D, and vitamin B12 (cobalamin) concentrations</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>e. Serum measurement of fasting glucose, thyroid-stimulating hormone, and creatinine concentrations</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis (Key Clues)</br>• Bipolar disorder treated with lithium + quetiapine<br /></br>• New symptoms: polyuria &amp; intention tremor → classic lithium side effects (nephrogenic diabetes insipidus, tremor)<br /></br>• Mild sedation → common with quetiapine (H1 blockade)<br /></br>• Otherwise mood is stable; compliant with meds → question is about routine safety monitoring, not a diagnostic work-up for other disease</p></br><p>These clues immediately focus attention on two drugs and their long-term toxicities:</br>1. Lithium → renal &amp; thyroid dysfunction</br>2. Atypical antipsychotic (quetiapine) → metabolic syndrome (weight gain, dyslipidemia, insulin resistance)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-wise thought process:</br>1. Lithium requires:</br>   • Baseline &amp; q6-12 mo BUN/creatinine (renal clearance, risk of nephrogenic DI)<br /></br>   • Baseline &amp; q6-12 mo TSH (risk of lithium-induced hypothyroidism)<br /></br>2. Quetiapine (and all SGAs) requires:</br>   • Baseline &amp; periodic BMI, fasting glucose, lipids (risk of metabolic syndrome/diabetes)</p></br><p>Therefore the single option that captures BOTH lithium (TSH + creatinine) and quetiapine (fasting glucose) monitoring is answer choice e.</p></br><p>Core Takeaway</br>Every visit with a patient on lithium + an atypical antipsychotic should trigger the “TLC-G” checklist:</br>T – TSH<br /></br>L – Lithium level<br /></br>C – Creatinine (renal)<br /></br>G – Glucose (metabolic effects of SGA)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>Lithium</br>• Filtered &amp; almost entirely reabsorbed in the proximal tubule with Na⁺. Any renal impairment → ↑ lithium levels.<br /></br>• Interferes with cAMP in the collecting duct → nephrogenic DI → polyuria, polydipsia.<br /></br>• Inhibits thyroid hormone release → goiter, hypothyroidism (fatigue, weight gain, cold intolerance).<br /></br>• Causes postural/intention tremor via action on Purkinje cells.  </p></br><p>Quetiapine (second-generation antipsychotic)</br>• 5-HT2A &amp; D2 antagonism; strong H1 antagonism → sedation, weight gain.<br /></br>• Blocks hypothalamic 5-HT2C &amp; H1 → ↑ appetite → dyslipidemia, insulin resistance, type 2 DM.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostic &amp; Management Pearls</br>1. Serum lithium level: check 5–7 days after a dose change then q3–6 mo. Therapeutic range 0.6-1.2 mEq/L.<br /></br>2. TSH &amp; creatinine: baseline, 3 mo, then q6-12 mo.<br /></br>3. Fasting glucose &amp; lipids: baseline, 3 mo, then annually (or sooner if weight ↑).<br /></br>4. Encourage hydration and avoid NSAIDs, ACE-Is, thiazides—these raise lithium levels.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>High-Yield Memory Aids</br>• “LiTHIUM”<br /></br>  L – Levels (0.6–1.2)<br /></br>  T – Thyroid<br /></br>  H – Hydration / Kidney (creatinine)<br /></br>  I – Increases tremor<br /></br>  U – Urination (DI)<br /></br>  M – Metabolism (monitor glucose if on SGA)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>a. CBC, blood smear, B12<br /></br>   – Appropriate for clozapine (agranulocytosis) or macrocytic anemia, not for lithium/quetiapine.</p></br><p>b. CBC, LFTs, fasting glucose<br /></br>   – LFTs needed for valproate, carbamazepine; quetiapine rarely causes hepatotoxicity. Omits creatinine &amp; TSH.</p></br><p>c. LFTs, TSH, PTH<br /></br>   – PTH/Calcium may elevate with chronic lithium, but renal &amp; metabolic monitoring is higher priority and glucose is missing.</p></br><p>d. Hgb, vitamin D, ferritin<br /></br>   – Iron or vitamin D deficiency unrelated to either drug.</p></br><p>f. PTH, vitamin D, B12<br /></br>   – Same issues: omits kidney, thyroid, and metabolic monitoring.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>A patient on lithium plus quetiapine needs routine surveillance of:</br>• Serum lithium level<br /></br>• Renal function (creatinine)<br /></br>• Thyroid function (TSH)<br /></br>• Metabolic status (fasting glucose ± lipids)</p></br><p>Answer choice e is the only option that captures these essential safety labs.</p>
179. A 62-year-old woman comes to the physician for a follow-up examination. She was discharged from the hospital 7 days ago after treatment for left iliofemoral venous thrombosis. Pain and swelling of the left lower extremity were successfully controlled with heparin and warfarin; the heparin was discontinued on discharge. Her only medication is warfarin. Examination of the left breast shows a 3 x 4-cm erythematous area with patches of purple-to-black skin. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Switch to low-molecular-weight heparin</br>b. Hyperbaric oxygen therapy</br>c. Intravenous administration of antibiotics</br>d. Intravenous administration of corticosteroids</br>e. Local excision of the area of erythema</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>a. Switch to low-molecular-weight heparin (LMWH) and stop warfarin.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• Timeline: Necrotic skin changes 7 days after starting warfarin.</br>• Distribution: Painful, erythematous → purple/black plaque on fatty area of breast (classic site).</br>• Medication history: Only warfarin (heparin stopped).</br>Key pattern = “painful skin necrosis within the first week of warfarin therapy,” especially on breasts, buttocks, thighs, or abdomen.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1: Early warfarin effect → depletes vitamin-K–dependent anticoagulant Protein C faster than pro-coagulant factors II, IX, X.<br /></br>Step 2: Transient hypercoagulable state → dermal vessel thrombosis → skin necrosis.<br /></br>Step 3: Immediate action → stop warfarin, reverse with vitamin K/FFP if needed, and give a rapidly acting anticoagulant (UFH or LMWH) until INR therapeutic again.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Sudden painful purpuric plaques in the first week of warfarin = warfarin skin necrosis. Treat by discontinuing warfarin and bridging with heparin ± protein C.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Etiology: Rapid fall of Protein C (t½ ≈ 8 h) while procoagulant factors decline more slowly.<br /></br>• Risk factors: Inherited protein C or S deficiency, large loading doses, obesity (more fatty tissue).<br /></br>• Mechanism: Microvascular thrombosis → ischemia → full-thickness necrosis.<br /></br>• Clinical course: Begins as painful erythema → petechiae → hemorrhagic bullae → black eschar in 3-5 days.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls</br>Immediate</br>1. STOP warfarin.</br>2. Start parenteral anticoagulant (UFH or LMWH).</br>3. Give vitamin K IV.<br /></br>Adjunct</br>• Protein C concentrate or fresh frozen plasma (FFP) in severe cases.<br /></br>Later</br>• Surgical debridement or grafting only after clear demarcation of necrotic tissue.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“Warfarin Wears off the C’s First”<br /></br>W = Warfarin<br /></br>C = Protein C depletion → Cutaneous Complications (necrosis).<br /></br>Picture a “war-farmer” painting black patches on a pink cow (breast/buttock).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>b. Hyperbaric oxygen therapy – Used for gas gangrene, carbon-monoxide poisoning, chronic non-healing wounds. Does not treat thrombotic skin necrosis.</p></br><p>c. Intravenous antibiotics – Appropriate for cellulitis or necrotizing fasciitis (fever, spreading erythema, leukocytosis). Here the lesion is thrombotic, not infectious, and patient is afebrile.</p></br><p>d. Intravenous corticosteroids – Employed for immune vasculitides (e.g., leukocytoclastic vasculitis) or allergic reactions, not for warfarin-induced thrombosis.</p></br><p>e. Local excision – Surgical management is delayed until tissue demarcation; immediate excision does nothing to halt ongoing thrombosis and would worsen the defect.</p></br><p>Common pitfall: Mistaking the lesion for cellulitis or vasculitis. Remember the timing (first week of warfarin) and the purpuric → necrotic evolution in fatty areas.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>High-Yield Take-Home Message</br>Painful purple-black plaques in fatty skin within days of starting warfarin = warfarin skin necrosis. Stop warfarin, reverse with vitamin K/FFP, and anticoagulate with heparin (LMWH or UFH) while planning adjunct protein C replacement if severe.</p>
180. A previously healthy 22-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 3-week history of intermittent episodes of stabbing pain in her right lower abdomen. Each episode lasts up to 2 hours; the most recent episode awakened her from sleep. She is currently in no pain. Menses occur at regular 28-day intervals. Her last menstrual period was 2 weeks ago and lasted 5 days. She is sexually active with one male partner and uses condoms consistently. Her temperature is 37.2°C (99°F), pulse is 68/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 110/70 mm Hg. Examination of the abdomen shows no tenderness or distention. There is a 6-cm, mobile, right adnexal mass anterior to the uterus. Test of the stool for occult blood is negative. Her hemoglobin concentration is 11.2 g/dL, leukocyte count is 7000/mm³, and platelet count is 298,000/mm³. A urine pregnancy test is negative. Pelvic ultrasonography shows a partially cystic mass in the right ovary with a single echogenic nodule and decreased blood flow. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Measurement of serum CA-125 concentration</br>b. Oral contraceptive therapy</br>c. Ultrasound-guided biopsy of the mass</br>d. Ovarian cystectomy</br>e. Right salpingo-oophorectomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Ovarian cystectomy  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues that point to a mature cystic teratoma (dermoid cyst):<br /></br>• Young woman (peak incidence ≤30 yr)<br /></br>• 6-cm, mobile, partially cystic ovarian mass<br /></br>• Single echogenic nodule on ultrasound = “Rokitansky protuberance” (dermoid plug)<br /></br>• Intermittent, stabbing pain that can awaken patient → recurrent partial torsion (dermoids are the most common ovarian tumor to torse because of their fat content and size 5–10 cm)<br /></br>• Normal labs, afebrile, negative pregnancy test = rules out ectopic pregnancy, tubo-ovarian abscess, corpus luteum hemorrhage, etc.<br /></br>• Decreased blood flow on Doppler = torsion risk, not malignancy per se  </p></br><p>The pattern of “young + 5-10 cm cystic mass with echogenic nodule” is classic for mature cystic teratoma.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Why cystectomy?)  </p></br><p>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Identify mass as dermoid → benign, low malignant potential.<br /></br>2. Patient is symptomatic, and Doppler shows compromised flow → elective removal is indicated both to relieve symptoms and prevent inevitable complete torsion or rupture.<br /></br>3. Fertility-preserving surgery is preferred in women of reproductive age → laparoscopic ovarian cystectomy (enucleation of the cyst with preservation of normal ovarian tissue).  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Symptomatic mature cystic teratomas ≥5 cm in pre-menopausal women require surgical excision, and the fertility-sparing procedure is cystectomy.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>• Mature cystic teratoma = germ-cell tumor containing well-differentiated derivatives of all three germ layers (skin, hair, sebaceous material, bone/teeth).<br /></br>• Benign; 1–2% undergo malignant transformation (usually to squamous cell carcinoma).<br /></br>• Complications: ovarian torsion (most common), rupture → chemical peritonitis, infection.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls  </p></br><p>First-line: Laparoscopic ovarian cystectomy.<br /></br>– Preserve ovarian tissue &amp; fertility.<br /></br>– Perform oophorectomy only if ovarian tissue not salvageable or suspicion for cancer (post-menopause, solid components, ascites, elevated tumor markers, or Doppler neovascular flow).  </p></br><p>No additional pre-op tumor markers are required when imaging is classic and patient is low-risk for malignancy.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“Teratoma Triad” for Step 2:<br /></br>1. Woman &lt;30 yr<br /></br>2. 5–10 cm partially cystic ovarian mass<br /></br>3. Echogenic nodule (Rokitansky protuberance)<br /></br>Management mantra: “Dermoid = DO (DermOid) a cystectomy before it Torses.”  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Measurement of serum CA-125 concentration<br /></br>• CA-125 screens for epithelial ovarian cancer primarily in post-menopausal women or when malignancy is suspected. Benign dermoids do not elevate CA-125; ordering it would not change management.  </p></br><p>b.
