Oral contraceptive therapy<br /></br>• Combined OCPs shrink functional (follicular/luteal) cysts and prevent new ones, but have no effect on dermoid cysts that contain mature tissue.  </p></br><p>c. Ultrasound-guided biopsy of the mass<br /></br>• Trans-abdominal or trans-vaginal biopsy of an ovarian mass is avoided due to risk of spilling contents (chemical peritonitis with dermoids) and tumor seeding if malignant.  </p></br><p>e. Right salpingo-oophorectomy<br /></br>• Removes entire ovary &amp; tube—too aggressive for a benign lesion in a young woman desiring fertility; reserved for suspected malignancy or non-salvageable torsed ovary.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line  </p></br><p>A symptomatic, 6-cm dermoid cyst in a 22-year-old requires fertility-sparing surgical removal—ovarian cystectomy is the appropriate next step.</p>
181. A 37-year-old woman, gravida 2, para 2, comes to the physician for a routine health maintenance examination. Menses occur at regular 28-day intervals. Routine Pap smears have shown no abnormalities. She has no history of serious illness. Her mother had breast cancer at the age of 63 years, her maternal aunt had endometrial cancer at the age of 54 years, and her paternal grandmother had colon cancer at the age of 72 years. The patient is sexually active and monogamous with her husband and has been using an oral contraceptive for 8 years. She does not smoke cigarettes or drink alcohol. Her children are 9 and 11 years of age. She works as a nurse. Physical examination, including breast and pelvic examinations, shows no abnormalities. She is concerned about the history of cancer in her family and asks about a screening test for cancer. Which of the following is the most appropriate recommendation at this visit?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Endometrial biopsy</br>b. Mammography</br>c. Measurement of serum CA-125 concentration</br>d. Testing for BRCA mutations</br>e. No screening is indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. No screening is indicated</p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age 37: below the usual starting age for population-based screening of breast (≥40–50 yr), colorectal (≥45–50 yr), or cervical cancer (already up-to-date with Pap tests).<br /></br>• Family history:<br /></br>  – Mother: breast cancer at 63 yr (post-menopausal, not “early onset”).<br /></br>  – Maternal aunt: endometrial cancer at 54 yr (single second-degree relative).<br /></br>  – Paternal grandmother: colon cancer at 72 yr.<br /></br>  None of these meet “high-risk” criteria (multiple first-degree relatives, cancer &lt;50 yr, male breast CA, ovarian CA, colon CA &lt;50 yr, etc.).<br /></br>• Protective factor: 8 yr of combined oral contraceptives (↓ ovarian &amp; endometrial CA risk).<br /></br>• No abnormal symptoms or exam findings; Pap smears normal.</p></br><p>Key takeaway: In an asymptomatic, average-risk 37-year-old woman, no additional cancer screening beyond routine age-appropriate care is recommended at this visit.</p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Determine baseline risk: only one first-degree relative with post-menopausal breast cancer → average risk.<br /></br>2. Compare patient’s age with guideline thresholds: below screening ages for breast (40–50), colon (45–50), endometrial (no routine screen), ovarian (no routine screen).<br /></br>3. Check for hereditary cancer syndromes criteria: BRCA and Lynch criteria not met.<br /></br>4. Conclude: reassure; continue routine health maintenance (Pap q3 yr or HPV testing per guidelines).</p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield bullets)<br /></br>• Most breast, endometrial, and colon cancers are sporadic; hereditary syndromes account for &lt;10%.<br /></br>• BRCA1/2: autosomal dominant, ↑ breast &amp; ovarian CA; red flags include breast CA &lt;50 yr, bilateral, male breast CA, multiple relatives.<br /></br>• Lynch syndrome (HNPCC): DNA mismatch repair defect → early colon &amp; endometrial CA; criteria involve ≥3 relatives, ≥2 generations, one dx &lt;50 yr.<br /></br>• CA-125: glycoprotein marker that rises with many benign and malignant conditions—poor PPV in screening.</p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Not applicable—no screening or treatment indicated today. Continue:<br /></br>• Cervical cancer screening per USPSTF/ACOG (Pap ± HPV).<br /></br>• Begin mammography at 40 yr (ACOG) or 50 yr (USPSTF) if average risk.<br /></br>• Colonoscopy at 45–50 yr if average risk.<br /></br>• Counsel on lifestyle (smoking cessation, weight control, etc.).</p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ABCs of average-risk screening”<br /></br>A – Age matters (start Breast 40/50, Colon 45/50, Cervix 21)<br /></br>B – BRCA test only with ‘Bad’ family clusters (early, bilateral, male)<br /></br>C – CA-125 Can’t be a screen<br /></br>D – Don’t Biopsy Endometrium unless bleeding or Lynch flags</p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Endometrial biopsy<br /></br>   • Indicated for abnormal uterine bleeding or Lynch syndrome/high-risk women—not for a single second-degree relative.<br /></br>b. Mammography<br /></br>   • Start at 40–50 yr for average risk. Earlier only if first-degree relative diagnosed &lt;40 yr or known BRCA mutation.<br /></br>c. Serum CA-125 measurement<br /></br>   • Not recommended for ovarian CA screening due to very low specificity and PPV in asymptomatic women.<br /></br>d. BRCA mutation testing<br /></br>   • Testing criteria not met: single first-degree relative with breast CA after 60 yr is insufficient.<br /></br>e. No screening is indicated<br /></br>   • Matches guidelines—patient is average risk and below the age thresholds.</p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In a 37-year-old woman with no high-risk features, reassure and follow standard age-appropriate screening intervals; do not add premature or low-value cancer tests.</p>
182. A 58-year-old man returns to the clinic for follow-up 2 weeks after an initial acute episode of severe pain in his right great toe that prevented him from bearing weight on the toe or wearing shoes. He had had similar pain prior to that episode. At his initial visit 2 weeks ago, naproxen therapy was initiated. Today, he reports resolution of his symptoms. Medical history is significant for hypertension and hypertriglyceridemia. Medications are lisinopril, lovastatin, and aspirin. The patient works as a fisherman harvesting shrimp, and he frequently eats some of the shrimp he has caught. He drinks two to three 12-oz beers nightly. He does not smoke cigarettes. He is 175 cm (5 ft 9 in) tall and weighs 102 kg, or 225lb; BMI is 33 kg/m². Vital signs are temperature 37.0 °C (98.6 °F), pulse 95/min, respirations 18/min, and blood pressure 150/80 mm Hg. Physical examination shows slight erythema of the right great toe with no swelling or pain. Results of serum laboratory studies obtained 3 months ago during a routine health maintenance examination are shown:</br>Creatinine 1.1 mg/dL</br>Glucose 104 mg/dL</br>Cholesterol, total 180 mg/dL</br>HDL 45 mg/dL</br>LDL 71 mg/dL</br>Triglycerides 400 mg/dL</br>In addition to recommending decreased alcohol consumption, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Add long-term colchicine therapy</br>b. Begin daily allopurinol</br>c. Recommend a low-purine diet</br>d. Replace lisinopril with hydrocholorthiazide</br>e. No additional step is necessary</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Add long-term colchicine therapy</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis – pulling out the clues</br>• Age / sex: 58-y-o man<br /></br>• Classic podagra: “severe pain in his right great toe … could not bear weight or wear shoes.”<br /></br>• Prior similar episode → this was at least his second gout flare.<br /></br>• Acute attack treated with an NSAID (naproxen) 2 weeks ago → symptoms now resolved.<br /></br>• Purine-rich diet &amp; alcohol: fisherman who “frequently eats shrimp” + 2–3 beers nightly.<br /></br>• Obesity (BMI 33) and hypertriglyceridemia (TG 400 mg/dL) are additional gout risk factors.<br /></br>• Normal creatinine (1.1 mg/dL) and no mention of tophi, renal stones, or chronic kidney disease.</p></br><p>Key takeaway: patient has recurrent—but still relatively infrequent—attacks of gout with no tophi or renal manifestations and normal renal function.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:</p></br><ol></br><li>Acute attack management? Already done (naproxen).  </li></br><li>Now at an asymptomatic follow-up visit: decide on secondary prophylaxis.  </li></br><li></br><p>Options for chronic prophylaxis<br /></br>   • Lifestyle: ↓ alcohol, weight loss, low-purine diet<br /></br>   • Pharmacologic<br /></br>        – Low-dose colchicine or NSAID = attack prophylaxis<br /></br>        – Urate-lowering therapy (ULT): xanthine-oxidase inhibitors (allopurinol, febuxostat) or uricosurics (probenecid)</p></br></li></br><li></br><p>Indications to START ULT (e.g., allopurinol) on NBME/USMLE:<br /></br>   • ≥2 attacks PER YEAR, OR<br /></br>   • Tophaceous gout, CKD stage ≥2, or uric-acid nephrolithiasis.  </p></br></li></br></ol></br><p>The vignette documents only one attack in the past 12 months (the previous episode could have been years ago). He has none of the “automatic” ULT indications. Therefore, the recommended next step is low-dose chronic colchicine (0.6 mg once or twice daily) to reduce flare frequency.</p></br><p>Core concept: In patients with recurrent but not yet frequent/severe gout, low-dose colchicine is the first pharmacologic prophylaxis; allopurinol is reserved for ≥2 attacks/year or complications of hyperuricemia.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Gout = monosodium urate crystal deposition due to hyperuricemia.<br /></br>• Alcohol (especially beer) + seafood (shrimp) ↑ purine load and ↓ renal uric-acid excretion.<br /></br>• Obesity and hypertriglyceridemia often coexist with hyperuricemia (metabolic syndrome).<br /></br>• Colchicine binds tubulin, inhibiting leukocyte chemotaxis and microtubule polymerization → prevents neutrophil-mediated inflammation that triggers attacks.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• Low-dose colchicine for prophylaxis: 0.6 mg PO daily–BID; start after acute flare completely resolves.<br /></br>• Counsel on:<br /></br>  – Alcohol reduction (esp. beer)<br /></br>  – Weight loss, exercise, low-purine diet (organ meats, shellfish)<br /></br>• Monitor for colchicine toxicity (GI upset, myopathy—potentiated by statins; check CK if symptoms).<br /></br>• If patient later develops ≥2 attacks/year, tophi, CKD, or stones → add a xanthine-oxidase inhibitor (e.g., allopurinol) PLUS continue colchicine for the first 3–6 mo to prevent mobilization flares.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tip<br /></br>“Colchicine Keeps Crystals Calm”<br /></br>Use COLCHICINE for Chronic prophylaxis when the case is mild (≤1 attack per year) and Calm; jump to Allopurinol only After Attacks become <em>A lot</em> (≥2/yr) or complications Appear.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>b. Begin daily allopurinol – Indicated only if ≥2 attacks/yr, tophi, CKD, or uric-acid stones. Starting it now is premature and could precipitate another flare without clear indication.<br /></br>c. Recommend a low-purine diet – Good adjunct but lifestyle advice alone isn’t adequate pharmacologic prophylaxis after recurrent gout; NBME asks for “most appropriate next step.”<br /></br>d. Replace lisinopril with hydrochlorothiazide – Thiazides INCREASE serum uric acid and worsen gout; ACE inhibitors are gout-neutral.<br /></br>e. No additional step is necessary – Recurrent gout warrants preventive therapy; doing nothing risks future flares.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>After a second gout attack in a patient without severe disease or indications for urate-lowering therapy, the best next step is long-term low-dose colchicine prophylaxis.</p>
183. A 62-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 6-month history of midback pain, numbness and swelling of his legs, and fatigue. He has a 2-week history of swelling of his face and hands. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination of the lower thoracic spine shows tenderness to percussion. There is 3+ edema of the face, hands, and lower extremities. Sensation to light touch and pinprick is decreased over the lower extremities. His serum creatinine concentration is 3.5 mg/dL. Serum protein electrophoresis shows monoclonal gammopathy. X-rays of the thoracic spine show lytic lesions in three vertebrae. Urinalysis is most likely to show which of the following sets of findings?</br>Blood Protein RBC (/hpf) WBC (/hpf) Casts Other Microscopic Findings	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. None none none none hyaline none</br>b. None 4+ none none none oval fat bodies</br>c. Trace 1+ 0–5 >50 none WBC clumps</br>d. 4+ 4+ 0–5 0–5 pigmented-granular renal tubular epithelial cells</br>e. 1+ 1+ 5–10 10–20 none eosinophils</br>f. 3+ 4+ >50 none RBC casts none</br>g. 4+ 4+ none none pigmented-granular squamous epithelial cells</br>h. 4+ 4+ 40–20 dysmorphic none none none</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Blood – None Protein – None RBC – None WBC – None Casts – Hyaline Other – None  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Mid-back pain,” “lytic lesions,” “monoclonal gammopathy” → classic triad for multiple myeloma (MM).<br /></br>• Renal insufficiency (Cr 3.5 mg/dL) + edema → myeloma kidney (light-chain cast nephropathy).<br /></br>• No fever or dysuria; neurologic deficits are sensory, not infectious → rules out pyelonephritis or GN.<br /></br>Key pattern: CRAB (hyperCalcemia, Renal failure, Anemia, Bone pain/lytic lesions) → think MM.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify the disease (MM).<br /></br>Step 2: Ask, “What does the urine dipstick show in light-chain nephropathy?”<br /></br> • Standard dipsticks detect albumin (an anionic protein) via a color-change indicator.<br /></br> • Free κ or λ light chains (Bence-Jones proteins) are cationic, so the dipstick reads “no protein” even when grams of light chains are present.<br /></br>Step 3: Decide what other elements are (not) expected.<br /></br> • No hematuria (no glomerular inflammation).<br /></br> • No pyuria (no infection).<br /></br> • Casts: can be occasional bland hyaline casts; diagnostic “myeloma casts” are seen on light microscopy but are simply reported as “casts” or “hyaline” on a routine UA.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Light-chain proteinuria is often missed by routine dipstick → UA appears bland in MM despite renal failure.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Plasma-cell dyscrasia → overproduction of monoclonal light chains.<br /></br>• Light chains filtered by glomerulus, combine with Tamm-Horsfall protein in distal tubule → obstructive, inflammatory “myeloma casts” → acute/chronic kidney injury.<br /></br>• Dipstick negative because it is specific for albumin, not light chains.<br /></br>• Sulfosalicylic acid or urine protein electrophoresis would reveal heavy protein load.  </p></br><p>Treatment/Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>1. Confirmatory tests:<br /></br> • 24-hr urine protein electrophoresis → light-chain (Bence-Jones) spike.<br /></br> • Serum free light-chain assay.<br /></br>2. Renal protection: IV fluids, avoid NSAIDs/IV contrast.<br /></br>3. Disease-directed therapy: bortezomib + dexamethasone ± lenalidomide; consider autologous stem-cell transplant.<br /></br>4. Hypercalcemia management: bisphosphonates (zoledronic acid).  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Dip the albumin, miss the Jones”:<br /></br> Dipstick detects Albumin → Bence-Jones proteins slip through.<br /></br>CRAB = Calcium, Renal, Anemia, Bone pain → Multiple Myeloma.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. 4+ protein + oval fat bodies = massive albuminuria → nephrotic syndrome (e.g., minimal change, FSGS). Dipstick would be strongly positive.<br /></br>c. Trace blood/protein, &gt;50 WBC, WBC clumps = acute pyelonephritis/UTI. Our patient has no fever or dysuria.<br /></br>d. 4+ blood &amp; protein, pigmented “muddy brown” casts, renal tubular epithelial cells = acute tubular necrosis (ischemia/toxin). Dipstick blood positive from free Hb/myoglobin, not RBCs.<br /></br>e. 1+ blood &amp; protein, eosinophils = drug-induced acute interstitial nephritis (e.g., NSAIDs, β-lactams).<br /></br>f. Protein 4+, hematuria with RBC casts = nephritic glomerulonephritis (post-strep, IgA, etc.).<br /></br>g. Blood 4+, protein 4+, pigmented granular casts but no RBCs = myoglobinuria/hemoglobinuria (rhabdomyolysis, intravascular hemolysis).<br /></br>h. Heavy blood with dysmorphic RBCs = proliferative GN (e.g., IgA, Alport).  </p></br><p>All of the above contain findings (albuminuria, hematuria, pyuria) not expected in light-chain cast nephropathy, making option a the only compatible urinalysis.</p>
184. A clinical trial is conducted of a new drug-eluting coronary artery stent that does not require antiplatelet therapy for patency in animal models. Twenty patients who had non–ST-elevation acute myocardial infarction are recruited to participate in this study; all patients have absolute contraindications for chronic anticoagulation and are not surgical candidates. This study design is most appropriate for evaluating which of the following?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Comparability</br>b. Effectiveness</br>c. Efficacy</br>d. Rare adverse effects</br>e. Safety</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Safety  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Clinical trial … 20 patients” → very small sample size, typical of an early-phase (Phase I) study.<br /></br>• Goal is to place a completely new device in humans for the first time (“new drug-eluting stent that does not require antiplatelet therapy … shown only in animal models”).<br /></br>• Patients are high-risk (cannot take chronic anticoagulation) → investigators must first be sure the device will not harm them (thrombosis, bleeding, etc.).<br /></br>These clues point to a study whose PRIMARY purpose is to determine whether the intervention is safe enough to proceed to larger trials.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. What type of study is described? Small, first-in-human clinical trial.<br /></br>2. What question do Phase I studies answer? “Is it SAFE?” (toxicity, tolerability, pharmacokinetics/dynamics).<br /></br>3. Can 20 patients establish effectiveness, efficacy, or rare adverse effects? No—sample size is far too small.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Very small early trials are designed to establish safety, not efficacy/effectiveness.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (concise)<br /></br>• Drug-eluting stents normally require dual antiplatelet therapy to prevent in-stent thrombosis.<br /></br>• A stent that purports to avoid this need poses two major safety risks:<br /></br>  – Acute/subacute thrombosis if the coating fails.<br /></br>  – Bleeding if unforeseen systemic drug release occurs.<br /></br>Hence safety must be confirmed before larger efficacy trials.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Test Notes<br /></br>Phase I device trials usually include:<br /></br>• Serial angiography or intravascular ultrasound to look for early thrombosis/restenosis.<br /></br>• Lab monitoring (cardiac enzymes, platelet counts) to detect toxicity.<br /></br>• Strict stopping rules for any major adverse cardiac event.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Phase I = Is it Safe?<br /></br>Phase II = Does it Work? (Efficacy)<br /></br>Phase III = Is it better than what we have? (Effectiveness/Comparative)<br /></br>Phase IV = Can we pick up rare problems? (Rare adverse effects)”  </p></br><p>Mnemonic:<br /></br>• “SWEet R” – Safety, Works, Enhancement, Rare (Phases I-IV).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Comparability<br /></br>   • Refers to the degree to which baseline characteristics of two randomized groups are similar. This study has no control arm; with only 20 subjects, it is not designed to test comparability.  </p></br><p>b. Effectiveness<br /></br>   • Assessed in Phase III trials (hundreds-thousands of patients) under real-world conditions, often comparing to standard therapy. Sample here is far too small.  </p></br><p>c. Efficacy<br /></br>   • Tested in Phase II trials (≈100-200 selected patients) to see if the intervention works under ideal circumstances. Again, 20 subjects is insufficient.  </p></br><p>d. Rare adverse effects<br /></br>   • Requires very large Phase IV post-marketing surveillance or big registries; a sample of 20 cannot detect rare events.  </p></br><p>e. Safety (Correct)<br /></br>   • Primary endpoint of Phase I trials; small numbers are enough to identify common or severe toxicities that preclude further study.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Key Takeaway<br /></br>When you see a brand-new intervention tested in a tiny cohort (dozens of patients or fewer), think Phase I: the investigators want to know, first and foremost, “Is it safe?”</p>
185. A 32-year-old woman who works as a nurse comes to the office because of a 4-month history of increasing pain and swelling of her ankles. Her ankles are stiff in the morning and become progressively painful and swollen by the end of her work shifts. She runs on a treadmill and lifts weights three times weekly, but her symptoms have prevented her from running during the past month. There is no history of trauma, and she has no other joint pain. She has hypothyroidism treated with levothyroxine. Examination of the ankles shows warmth and moderate effusions. There is no erythema. Active and passive range of motion of the ankles elicits mild pain. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. X-rays of the ankles show no fractures or degenerative changes. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Anti-cyclic citrullinated peptide (CCP) antibody test</br>b. Aspiration of the ankles with Gram stain and culture</br>c. MRI of the ankles</br>d. Triple-phase technetium 99m bone scan</br>e. Venous duplex ultrasonography of the lower extremities</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Anti–cyclic citrullinated peptide (anti-CCP) antibody test  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Time course: 4 months (chronic, not acute)<br /></br>• Distribution: Bilateral ankles; symmetric involvement is typical of systemic inflammatory arthritis.<br /></br>• Inflammatory clues: Morning stiffness and swelling that worsen with use; warmth &amp; effusion on exam.<br /></br>• Imaging: Plain films are normal → consistent with early rheumatoid arthritis (RA) in which erosions have not yet developed.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: No erythema or fever (argues against septic arthritis or cellulitis); no other joints so far, but early RA may present in only a few joints.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify inflammatory arthritis (morning stiffness &gt;30 min, synovitis, multi-month course).<br /></br>Step 2: In a young woman, the highest-yield diagnosis to confirm is RA.<br /></br>Step 3: First-line diagnostic test = serology (anti-CCP ± rheumatoid factor). Anti-CCP is &gt;95 % specific and predicts erosive disease, so ordering it early changes management (early DMARDs).  </p></br><p>Core Take-Home: In a chronic symmetric inflammatory arthritis with non-diagnostic x-ray, screen with anti-CCP before proceeding to invasive tests or costly imaging.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• RA is an autoimmune, RF- and anti-CCP-mediated attack on synovium → pannus formation → cartilage destruction and bone erosions.<br /></br>• Anti-CCP antibodies appear years before symptoms; specificity is higher than RF (95 % vs 80 %).<br /></br>• Risk factors: Female, HLA-DR4, smoking.<br /></br>• Typical early joints: MCP, PIP, wrists, MTP—but ankles, elbows, or knees can be first.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests (overview)<br /></br>Once RA is confirmed:<br /></br>1. Initiate a conventional DMARD (methotrexate is first-line).<br /></br>2. Add NSAIDs/glucocorticoids only for short-term symptom control.<br /></br>3. Monitor disease with ESR/CRP and serial x-rays.  </p></br><p>No further imaging is necessary before serologic confirmation unless atypical features arise.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“CCP = Confirm Chronic Polyarthritis.”<br /></br>Think “Morning CCP coffee for RA stiffness.”<br /></br>Remember: Anti-CCP is the “RA sniper” (highly specific).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>b. Joint aspiration with Gram stain &amp; culture<br /></br>• Indicated in acute, single, red, exquisitely painful joint to rule out septic arthritis or crystals.<br /></br>• This patient has chronic bilateral involvement, minimal pain, no erythema, and no systemic signs—low pre-test probability.  </p></br><p>c. MRI of the ankles<br /></br>• Detects early erosions but is expensive and not first-line. Serology is cheaper, less invasive, and guides therapy.  </p></br><p>d. Triple-phase technetium-99m bone scan<br /></br>• Used for occult fractures, osteomyelitis, or reflex sympathetic dystrophy. Does not distinguish inflammatory arthritides.  </p></br><p>e. Venous duplex ultrasonography<br /></br>• Evaluates DVT. DVT causes unilateral swelling, not bilateral morning stiffness or synovitis.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Jumping to aspiration or imaging for any swollen joint. Always consider disease chronicity, symmetry, and inflammatory vs non-inflammatory features first. Early serologic testing for RA speeds DMARD initiation and prevents irreversible joint damage.</p>
186. A 57-year-old woman comes to the physician for a routine gynecologic examination. Two years ago, results of a complete blood count were within the reference ranges, and her total cholesterol concentration was 180 mg/dL. She feels well. She has had a 4.5-kg (10-lb weight gain during the past 6 months. She takes no medications. Her last menstrual period was 4 years ago. She does not smoke cigarettes. She drinks two glasses of wine daily. Her temperature is 37.2 °C (99 °F), pulse is 68/min, respirations are 12/min, and blood pressure is 130/85 mm Hg. She is 168 cm (5 ft 6 in) tall and weighs 77 kg, or 170lb; BMI is 27 kg/m². Pelvic examination shows mild atrophy of the vaginal mucosa. Serum studies show:</br>Cholesterol, total 240 mg/dL</br>HDL‐cholesterol 45 mg/dL</br>LDL‐cholesterol 165 mg/dL</br>Triglycerides 200 mg/dL</br>Total bilirubin 0.9 mg/dL</br>Alkaline phosphatase 60 U/L</br>AST 20 U/L</br>ALT 28 U/L</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate next step to determine the cause of this patient’s increased cholesterol concentration?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Measurement of serum cortisol concentration</br>b. Measurement of serum C-reactive protein concentration</br>c. Measurement of serum follicle-stimulating hormone concentration</br>d. Measurement of serum homocysteine concentration</br>e. Measurement of serum thyroid-stimulating hormone concentration</br>f. Serum mitochondrial antibody assay</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Measurement of serum thyroid-stimulating hormone (TSH) concentration  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• Postmenopausal woman (57 y; LMP 4 y ago) with new hyperlipidemia compared with values 2 y earlier<br /></br>  – Total cholesterol ↑ from 180 → 240 mg/dL<br /></br>  – LDL 165 mg/dL, TG 200 mg/dL<br /></br>• Mild weight gain, no lipid-raising medications, normal LFTs (rules out cholestasis, nephrotic features not mentioned).<br /></br>• Key clue: Evaluate “secondary” (reversible) causes of dyslipidemia before labeling her with primary hypercholesterolemia.</p></br><p>Pertinent positives → weight gain, mild fatigue-suggestive temperature, hyperlipids.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives → Normal alkaline phosphatase &amp; bilirubin (argues against cholestatic liver dz/PBC), no exogenous steroids, no cushingoid appearance, no acute inflammation, no nephrotic proteinuria reported.</p></br><p>Pattern recognition: Hypothyroidism is a classic, common, and screen-worthy secondary cause of elevated LDL and triglycerides in middle-aged women.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-wise thought process:</br>1. Identify new hyperlipidemia.<br /></br>2. Ask: “Could this be secondary?”<br /></br>   • Most common reversible causes: hypothyroidism, diabetes/insulin resistance, nephrotic syndrome, liver disease, certain drugs (thiazides, beta-blockers, OCPs), pregnancy.<br /></br>3. Hypothyroidism is the single most frequently screened cause because:<br /></br>   – Prevalence high in women &gt;50 y.<br /></br>   – Untreated hypothyroidism not only raises LDL/TG but also increases cardiovascular risk.<br /></br>   – Simple, inexpensive test (TSH) with high sensitivity.<br /></br>4. Therefore obtain a TSH before initiating lipid-lowering therapy.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Always exclude hypothyroidism with a TSH in any adult who presents with newly discovered hypercholesterolemia.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Thyroid hormones up-regulate LDL receptors &amp; hepatic LDL clearance. ↓T4/T3 → ↓receptor density → LDL accumulation.<br /></br>• Hypothyroidism also decreases lipoprotein lipase activity → ↑ triglycerides.<br /></br>Risk factors: female sex, age &gt;50, autoimmune hx (Hashimoto).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests</br>• Screen: Serum TSH (if high, reflex free T4).<br /></br>• If overt/subclinical hypothyroidism confirmed → start levothyroxine, then re-check lipid panel in 6–8 wk; levels often normalize or improve, possibly obviating statin therapy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>Mnemonic “THINK” for secondary lipids:</br>T – Thyroid (hypothyroidism)<br /></br>H – Hyperglycemia (DM)<br /></br>I – Iatrogenic (drugs) / Increased cortisol (Cushing)<br /></br>N – Nephrotic syndrome<br /></br>K – (K)hole-stasis: liver cholestasis/PBC  </p></br><p>Always order TSH first—most common &amp; most fixable.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Serum cortisol: Cushing syndrome can raise LDL/TG, but far less common; patient lacks truncal obesity, striae, HTN &gt;140, proximal weakness. TSH screening is standard before considering Cushing testing.<br /></br>b. C-reactive protein: Marker of inflammation/cardiac risk, not a cause of lipid elevation. Would not explain LDL 165 mg/dL.<br /></br>c. Follicle-stimulating hormone: Confirms menopause (already clear from 4 y amenorrhea); menopause can alter lipids but FSH does not identify a correctable pathological cause.<br /></br>d. Homocysteine: Elevated levels increase CV risk but do not cause hyperlipidemia; measurement would not explain or treat lipid abnormality.<br /></br>f. Mitochondrial (anti-mitochondrial) antibody: Screens for primary biliary cholangitis, which causes markedly ↑alkaline phosphatase and cholesterol; her ALP and bilirubin are normal, making PBC unlikely.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>New hyperlipidemia → always rule out hypothyroidism first with a serum TSH.</p>
187. A 2-year-old boy who is in the hospital has apnea 12 hours after undergoing surgical drainage of the left hip because of sepsis. His medications are intravenous vancomycin, cefotaxime, and morphine. After application of bag-valve-mask ventilation and administration of naloxone, the patient’s apnea resolves. Review of the hospital database shows that the patient’s morphine dose was too high. Which of the following is most likely to prevent recurrence of this type of error?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Perform a root cause analysis</br>b. Report the nurse who administered the morphine dose</br>c. Verify the correct dose with the hospital pharmacist</br>d. Verify the patient’s weight</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>A. Perform a root cause analysis (RCA)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Apnea … after morphine” + “naloxone reversed it” → classic opioid‐induced respiratory depression.<br /></br>• “Database shows the dose was too high” → a medication administration error.<br /></br>• Question stem asks for an action “most likely to prevent recurrence.” This signals a SYSTEM-level, not person-level, solution.</p></br><p>Key testing clue: Safety/quality improvement questions on NBME/Step exams usually want the broad, process-based fix (RCA, standardized protocols, checklists) rather than blaming an individual.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thinking:<br /></br>1. Harm occurred (adverse event).<br /></br>2. Goal = keep it from happening again.<br /></br>3. Best practice after an adverse event is a multidisciplinary RCA:<br /></br>   • Reconstruct timeline.<br /></br>   • Identify latent system flaws (order entry, dosing calculations, double-checks, pump programming, etc.).<br /></br>   • Implement process changes (weight-based protocols, electronic alerts, nurse–pharmacist double verification, etc.).<br /></br>Take-home: RCA is retrospective and aims at SYSTEM redesign, not individual punishment.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Morphine → μ-opioid receptor agonist → ↓ brainstem respiratory center sensitivity to CO₂ → apnea.<br /></br>• Naloxone = competitive μ antagonist with rapid reversal.<br /></br>• Young children are especially dose-sensitive because of immature hepatic clearance.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>Acute management (already done): airway support + naloxone.<br /></br>Prevention: weight-based dosing, smart pumps, electronic order sets—but these specific fixes are identified and prioritized only AFTER completing an RCA.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“R”oot cause analysis happens After the event (Retrospective).<br /></br>“F”ailure-mode &amp; effect analysis (FMEA) happens Forward (Prospective) before harm occurs.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Report the nurse who administered the morphine dose<br /></br>• Person-focused blame creates a punitive culture; does not uncover underlying system gaps (e.g., confusing order set, unreadable concentration labels). Punishing individuals seldom prevents repeat errors.</p></br><p>C. Verify the correct dose with the hospital pharmacist<br /></br>• Helpful single-step check, but narrow. Without first doing an RCA, you may miss other contributors (e.g., wrong concentration in automated dispensing cabinet, faulty weight entry, look-alike/vial-size issues).</p></br><p>D. Verify the patient’s weight<br /></br>• Important for pediatric dosing, yet only one potential root; error could recur from other sources (decimal placement, pump programming). Again, it addresses a symptom, not the system.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Concept<br /></br>After any significant adverse event, the highest-yield action for preventing recurrence is to perform a multidisciplinary root cause analysis to identify and fix system failures rather than punishing individuals or instituting piecemeal checks.</p>
188. A 57-year-old man comes to the physician because he has coughed up blood once weekly for the past month. He has had an intermittent mild cough for 10 years. He has a history of hypertension well controlled with lisinopril and hyperlipidemia well controlled with pravastatin. He was treated for pneumonia six times during his 20s and 30s. He says he had “all of the usual childhood diseases.” There is no family history of serious illness. He does not smoke. He works as a teacher’s aide. His temperature is 37.3°C (99.1°F), pulse is 80/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 138/84 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 99%. A few crackles are heard at the lung bases. A grade 1/6 systolic murmur is heard best at the upper right sternal border. A CT scan of the chest is shown. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Bronchial carcinoid</br>b. Bronchiectasis</br>c. Bronchogenic carcinoma</br>d. Mitral stenosis</br>e. Sarcoidosis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Bronchiectasis  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – “Coughed up blood once weekly for the past month” → recurrent, mild‐volume hemoptysis.<br /></br>  – “Intermittent mild cough for 10 years” + “treated for pneumonia six times during his 20s and 30s” → long history of recurrent lower-respiratory infections.<br /></br>  – CT image (not shown here) on NBME almost always displays dilated, thick-walled bronchi, tram-track opacities, or signet-ring sign in bronchiectasis vignettes.<br /></br>  – No smoking history and normal SpO₂ → makes a primary lung malignancy or COPD less likely.<br /></br>  – Physical exam: scattered crackles at the bases (common in bronchiectasis), otherwise benign.  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives<br /></br>• No fever, weight loss, or large hilar mass → against cancer or sarcoidosis.<br /></br>• Murmur is a soft systolic murmur at the upper RSB (likely benign aortic sclerosis) NOT a diastolic, low-pitched, rumbling apex murmur of mitral stenosis.<br /></br>• Absence of flushing, wheezing, diarrhea → against carcinoid syndrome.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Recurrent infections + chronic productive cough → think of structural lung disease.<br /></br>2. Permanent damage to conducting airways from repeated infections classically produces bronchiectasis.<br /></br>3. Bronchiectasis frequently causes hemoptysis when fragile, inflamed bronchial vessels rupture.<br /></br>Core takeaway: A middle-aged adult with decades of recurrent/persistent cough and infections who now has hemoptysis = bronchiectasis until proven otherwise; CT chest &gt; CXR is the imaging study of choice and will show dilated airways.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: repeated infections, impaired mucociliary clearance (cystic fibrosis, primary ciliary dyskinesia), obstruction, severe childhood pneumonias, immune deficiency.<br /></br>• Mechanism: Chronic inflammation → neutrophil elastase + oxidants destroy elastic &amp; muscular airway tissue → irreversible bronchial dilation, mucus pooling, bacterial colonization → infection-inflammation cycle perpetuates.<br /></br>• Manifestations: chronic productive cough, crackles, wheezing, clubbing (late), recurrent pneumonias, hemoptysis.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics (High Yield)<br /></br>1. Confirm with high-resolution CT (HRCT): dilated bronchi &gt; adjacent vessel (signet-ring), lack of airway tapering, cystic changes.<br /></br>2. Management<br /></br>   – Daily airway clearance: chest physiotherapy, oscillatory PEP devices.<br /></br>   – Treat acute exacerbations: culture-guided antibiotics; empiric (e.g., levofloxacin, azithromycin).<br /></br>   – Chronic macrolide therapy for frequent flare‐ups.<br /></br>   – Bronchodilators for concomitant obstructive physiology.<br /></br>   – Embolization or surgery for massive/refractory hemoptysis.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tip<br /></br>“Three B’s”: Bronchiectasis = Bronchi dilated (CT), Bloody sputum, Bacterial infections (recurrent). Visualize a “ring” (CT signet-ring) made of sticky mucus.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Bronchial carcinoid<br /></br>   • Central, well‐circumscribed endobronchial mass; hemoptysis common, but typically a solitary lesion in a younger nonsmoker with recurrent pneumonias localized to one lobe. The NBME CT usually shows a round enhancing mass, not diffuse airway dilatation. Carcinoid syndrome (flushing, diarrhea) is rare unless liver mets.<br /></br>c. Bronchogenic carcinoma<br /></br>   • Requires significant smoking risk in &gt;90% of cases (except adenocarcinoma in nonsmokers). Would show a discrete pulmonary nodule/mass, lymphadenopathy, or pleural effusion on CT—not diffuse dilated bronchi.<br /></br>d. Mitral stenosis<br /></br>   • Causes pulmonary congestion &amp; hemoptysis (rupture of bronchial veins) but exam would show an opening snap + diastolic rumble at the apex, LAE on CXR, +/- A-fib. This patient has a soft systolic ejection murmur at the right upper sternal border (likely innocent aortic sclerosis).<br /></br>e. Sarcoidosis<br /></br>   • Non-caseating granulomas → bilateral hilar adenopathy, interstitial reticulonodular infiltrates, restrictive PFTs. Hemoptysis is uncommon; more often presents with dry cough, dyspnea, erythema nodosum, uveitis.  </p></br><p>Bottom Line<br /></br>Decades-long history of recurrent pneumonias + chronic cough + hemoptysis + characteristic HRCT findings = bronchiectasis.</p>
189. A previously healthy 8-year-old boy is brought to the emergency department because of a 1-day history of increasingly severe abdominal pain. He has no history of serious illness and receives no medications. He appears well developed and well nourished. His temperature is 38.1 °C (100.5 °F), pulse is 100/min, and blood pressure is 90/70 mm Hg. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. Abdominal examination shows localized tenderness over the right lower quadrant; there is guarding at the McBurney point. Bowel sounds are decreased. Laboratory studies show a hemoglobin concentration of 12.5 g/dL (N = 11.5–15.5) and leukocyte count of 14,500/mm³ (N = 4500–13,500). Urinalysis shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. CT scan of the abdomen</br>b. Measurement of serum C-reactive protein concentration</br>c. Operative intervention</br>d. Ultrasonography of the abdomen and pelvis</br>e. Admission to the hospital for observation only</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>A. CT scan of the abdomen  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key positives: 1-day history of progressively worsening RLQ (right-lower-quadrant) pain, focal tenderness/guarding at McBurney point, low-grade fever (38.1 °C), leukocytosis (14,500/mm³), and decreased bowel sounds.<br /></br>• Key negatives: no urinary findings (rules out ureterolithiasis/pyelonephritis) and no preceding GI or systemic illness (makes mesenteric adenitis, gastroenteritis, IBD less likely).<br /></br>• Pattern: “Fever + leukocytosis + RLQ pain/guarding in a school-aged child” → classic for acute appendicitis.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1. Suspect appendicitis (highest on differential).<br /></br>Step 2. Decide whether to operate immediately or obtain confirmatory imaging.<br /></br>     – In adults with a rock-solid exam you can go straight to the OR, but in children negative appendectomy rates are high (∼15-30%) and lifetime radiation risk slightly lower than the morbidity of an unnecessary laparotomy.<br /></br>     – Therefore most U.S. algorithms recommend diagnostic imaging first, unless peritonitis or hemodynamic instability is present (neither is true here).<br /></br>Step 3. Pick the best imaging modality.<br /></br>     – CT abdomen/pelvis with IV ± oral contrast = gold standard (sens/spec &gt;95%).<br /></br>     – Ultrasonography is radiation-free but operator-dependent and much less sensitive if appendix is retrocecal or child is obese; negative/indeterminate scans → CT anyway.<br /></br>Because the child is stable and has a classic but not absolutely diagnostic presentation, the next best step is CT imaging to confirm appendicitis and plan surgery.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In a stable pediatric patient with suspected appendicitis but no diffuse peritonitis, confirm with imaging—most sensitive test is CT abdomen.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Appendicitis = luminal obstruction (fecalith, lymphoid hyperplasia) → venous congestion → ischemia → bacterial overgrowth → localized inflammation progressing to perforation.<br /></br>• Presents as periumbilical pain migrating to RLQ, fever, vomiting, anorexia, leukocytosis. Perforation risk rises sharply after 24–48 h.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Pearls<br /></br>1. Imaging hierarchy (peds):<br /></br>   – Ultrasound first if readily available and experienced sonographers.<br /></br>   – CT abdomen/pelvis if U/S equivocal or unavailable, or if highest sensitivity required.<br /></br>2. Labs: Leukocytosis and elevated CRP support inflammation but are neither sensitive nor specific; they cannot rule in/out appendicitis.<br /></br>3. Definitive therapy = appendectomy (laparoscopic preferred). Pre-op single-dose broad-spectrum antibiotics (e.g., piperacillin-tazobactam).<br /></br>4. If perforation/abscess: IR-guided drainage + IV antibiotics prior to interval appendectomy.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“C-T for ‘Confirm Then take out’ the appendix” – For a stable child, CT confirms, THEN surgery.<br /></br>“U-S if Uncertain Staff” – Use ultrasound first only if skilled sonographers are available.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Serum C-reactive protein – Elevated in any inflammation; poor specificity. Does not change management once clinical suspicion is already high.<br /></br>C. Operative intervention now – Reasonable in adults with classic presentation, but pediatric protocols favor imaging to reduce negative appendectomies and radiation-adjusted risk/benefit. No signs of perforation or peritonitis that would mandate emergent surgery.<br /></br>D. Ultrasonography of abdomen/pelvis – Acceptable first test in many centers, but sensitivity ~70-85 %. The exam stem asks for “most appropriate” next step; CT provides definitive diagnosis and obviates limitations of U/S (body habitus, retrocecal appendix).<br /></br>E. Observation only – Risks delayed perforation; not appropriate given focal peritoneal signs and leukocytosis.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Stable pediatric patient + suspected appendicitis → obtain definitive imaging. CT abdomen/pelvis is the most sensitive test and the appropriate next step before proceeding to appendectomy.</p>
190. A previously healthy 47-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 5-day history of an itchy rash. The rash initially began on her trunk but now has spread to her face, arms, and legs. She has not had fever, shortness of breath, fatigue, loss of appetite, or weight loss. She returned from a trip to Jamaica 2 days ago. She takes no medications and has no known drug allergies. She has had a peanut allergy since childhood and typically has shortness of breath and swelling of the lips when she eats peanuts. She does not recall ingesting peanuts on her trip but says that she ate food at the hotel. Her temperature is 36.9°C (98.4°F), pulse is 78/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 110/80 mm Hg. Examination shows no abnormalities except for 1- to 2-cm, circular, blanched, raised urticarial lesions over the face, trunk, and upper and lower extremities; the rash is most prominent over the face, hands, and feet. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Skin test for reaction to peanuts</br>b. Complete blood count with leukocyte differential and measurement of erythrocyte sedimentation rate</br>c. Measurement of serum C3, C4, and total hemolytic complement concentrations and serum antinuclear antibody assay</br>d. Danazol therapy</br>e. Diphenhydramine therapy</br>f. Hydroxychloroquine therapy</br>g. Prednisone therapy</br>h. Skin biopsy of a lesion with immunofluorescence</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Diphenhydramine therapy (first-generation H1-receptor antagonist)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key findings: 5-day history, intensely pruritic, well-circumscribed, blanching, raised lesions that wax and wane (“wheals”) → classic acute urticaria.<br /></br>• Distribution: face, hands, feet are especially involved—typical for urticaria; no mucosal edema, no dyspnea, no hypotension → NOT anaphylaxis or angioedema emergency.<br /></br>• Timing: &lt;6 weeks = acute; triggers often unidentified (≈40–60 %). Recent travel and hotel meals could be the inciting allergen, but identifying the exact food immediately is rarely possible or necessary for acute management.<br /></br>• Vitals: normal → no systemic involvement.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach to pruritic wheals:<br /></br>1. Establish that lesions are urticaria, not vasculitis or infection → blanching, transient, intensely itchy.<br /></br>2. Determine severity: isolated skin findings only → outpatient care.<br /></br>3. Evidence-based first-line therapy for acute urticaria = nonsedating or sedating oral antihistamine.<br /></br>4. Reserve corticosteroids for severe or refractory cases; extensive work-up only after 6 weeks (chronic urticaria) or red-flag features.</p></br><p>Take-home: The test is checking recognition that uncomplicated, acute urticaria is treated symptomatically with an H1 blocker; no labs or skin testing are needed initially.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Type I (IgE-mediated) or non-IgE mast-cell degranulation → histamine, leukotrienes → dermal edema in the superficial dermis → transient wheals.<br /></br>• Triggers: foods, medications, infections, insect bites, temperature, pressure, idiopathic.<br /></br>• In acute urticaria most patients never learn the exact trigger; the eruption usually resolves within days to a few weeks.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>1. First-line: Oral H1 antihistamine<br /></br>   • Nonsedating (cetirizine, loratadine, fexofenadine) preferred for daytime; diphenhydramine or hydroxyzine at night if itching disrupts sleep.<br /></br>2. Second-line/refractory: Short prednisone taper (≤10 days).<br /></br>3. Chronic (&gt;6 weeks) or recurrent episodes: consider H2 blockers, leukotriene receptor antagonists, omalizumab, or referral for allergen testing.<br /></br>4. Diagnostic work-up (CBC, ESR, ANA, complement) indicated only when urticaria is chronic, accompanied by systemic symptoms, or suggestive of vasculitis.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“FAST” for acute wheals:<br /></br>• Fewer than 6 weeks<br /></br>• Alone on skin (no systemic signs)<br /></br>• Symptomatic relief with<br /></br>• anTihistamine  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Skin test for reaction to peanuts – Appropriate only after chronic or severe anaphylactic history; does not change acute management.<br /></br>b. CBC &amp; ESR – Useful for urticarial vasculitis or systemic disease; no red-flag features here.<br /></br>c. Complement levels &amp; ANA – Work-up for urticarial vasculitis, lupus, hereditary angioedema; not indicated in isolated, acute wheals.<br /></br>d. Danazol – Attenuated androgen used for hereditary angioedema (C1-esterase deficiency), which presents with NON-pruritic, deeper angioedema—not urticaria.<br /></br>f. Hydroxychloroquine – Disease-modifying agent for connective-tissue disorders and lupus; no role in acute urticaria.<br /></br>g. Prednisone – Can be used for severe or refractory urticaria, but antihistamines are first-line; unnecessary at initial presentation.<br /></br>h. Skin biopsy with immunofluorescence – Needed when vasculitis or bullous diseases are suspected; classic transient blanching wheals do not require biopsy.</p></br><p>Core Concept<br /></br>Isolated acute urticaria = treat symptomatically with an oral antihistamine; extensive testing or systemic steroids are excessive at this stage.</p>
191. A 7-year-old boy is brought to the office by his mother because she is concerned about his behavior at school since he began second grade 3 months ago. His teacher says he often fidgets and talks to his classmates when he has been told to be quiet. He misbehaves in language arts and spelling class but is well behaved during math and science classes. He has been sent to the principal’s office three times during the past month because of disruptive behavior. He says recess is his favorite part of school; he dislikes reading. His mother has no concerns about her son’s behavior at home. She says he has many friends in the neighborhood and enjoys sports activities. He has no history of serious illness and receives no medications. His paternal cousin has attention-deficit/hyperactivity disorder. The patient’s father dropped out of high school in 10th grade because of difficulty reading. During the examination, the patient is talkative and tells the physician about his new bicycle. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows a mild eye-blink motor tic. His mother says the tic has occurred intermittently during the past 2 weeks. No other abnormalities are noted. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Attention-deficit/hyperactivity disorder</br>b. Autism spectrum disorder</br>c. Dyslexia</br>d. Intellectual developmental disorder</br>e. Oppositional defiant disorder</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Attention-deficit/hyperactivity disorder (ADHD)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age 7 yr – peak age when ADHD first becomes functionally impairing at school.<br /></br>• Teacher report: “often fidgets and talks … disruptive behavior … sent to principal 3× in 1 mo.” → core hyperactive/impulsive symptoms.<br /></br>• Behavior worst during language-arts/reading (tasks he dislikes, require sustained attention), but acceptable during math/science (tasks he enjoys). ADHD symptoms typically fluctuate with interest level—not truly “context-specific.”<br /></br>• Mother sees him as “fine” at home (common; less demand for prolonged attention) but physician directly observes talkativeness and a hard-to-sit-still quality—providing the second setting required by DSM-5.<br /></br>• Symptoms present ≥3 mo and causing impairment (principal’s office).<br /></br>• Family history of ADHD (paternal cousin) supports genetic loading.<br /></br>• Isolated transient motor tic is common in ADHD (≈20%) and often worsens under stress.  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives: no global social/communication deficits, normal peer relationships, normal academic performance in non-reading subjects, normal developmental history → argues against autism spectrum disorder (ASD), intellectual developmental disorder (IDD), or a primary reading-intellectual deficit.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify core ADHD behaviors: hyperactivity (fidgets, excessive talking) and impulsivity (interrupts, disruptive).<br /></br>2. Verify onset &lt;12 yr and duration ≥6 mo (teacher notes x3 mo → we presume similar pattern since start of school year).<br /></br>3. Confirm impairment (disciplinary actions) and presence in ≥2 settings (school; physician exam).<br /></br>4. Exclude learning disorder as the primary cause – performs well in math/science, difficulties not confined only to reading comprehension but manifest as global behavioral disinhibition.  </p></br><p>Take-home message: ADHD symptoms intensify during boring, low-reward tasks and may be minimal when the child is interested; fluctuation by task interest still satisfies the “multiple settings” criterion.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Dopamine &amp; norepinephrine dysregulation in frontostriatal circuits → impaired executive function, inhibitory control, and sustained attention.<br /></br>• Highly heritable (≈70-80%); first-degree relatives at ↑ risk.<br /></br>• Comorbidities: tic disorders, ODD, specific learning disorders, anxiety.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearl<br /></br>1. First-line pharmacologic: stimulant (e.g., methylphenidate, amphetamine salts).<br /></br>2. Behavioral classroom modifications, parent-management training.<br /></br>3. Evaluate for co-occurring learning disorders; a reading evaluation is still appropriate but does not replace ADHD treatment.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>High-Yield Memory Aid<br /></br>“FIDGET” for ADHD<br /></br>F – Fidgets or squirms<br /></br>I – Interrupts / Impatient<br /></br>D – Difficulty staying seated or focused<br /></br>G – Gets distracted easily<br /></br>E – Excessive talking / on the go<br /></br>T – Trouble awaiting turn  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>b. Autism spectrum disorder<br /></br>• Would show persistent social-communication deficits (poor eye contact, difficulty with friends) and restricted interests; this child is socially appropriate with “many friends” and good reciprocal conversation.  </p></br><p>c. Dyslexia (specific learning disorder – reading)<br /></br>• Isolated academic difficulty in reading with normal behavior otherwise. Here, the disruptive behavior is global (sent to principal, fidgets in class, talkative in clinic). Also performs well in math/science, but dyslexia alone would not explain hyperactivity across multiple settings.  </p></br><p>d. Intellectual developmental disorder<br /></br>• Requires global cognitive impairment (IQ &lt;70) and adaptive-function deficits; the child performs adequately in math/science and social settings, arguing against global delay.  </p></br><p>e. Oppositional defiant disorder<br /></br>• Characterized by angry/irritable mood, vindictiveness, deliberate defiance toward authority in multiple settings. This boy is not argumentative or intentionally defiant; behaviors are more distractible than hostile.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Key Board-Level Pitfall<br /></br>Don’t reject ADHD simply because symptoms appear worst in only one subject or because the parent sees fewer problems at home. Task demand and reinforcement strongly influence ADHD manifestations; observations from teacher + clinician satisfy the “two settings” requirement.</p>
192. A 42-year-old woman, gravida 2, para 2, comes to the office because of a 1-day history of loss of clear, yellow vaginal fluid while walking; she has soaked two absorbent pads during this time. Two weeks ago, she underwent laparoscopic-assisted total vaginal hysterectomy for menorrhagia, dysmenorrhea, and adenomyosis. Intraoperatively, dissection of the bladder from the cervix was complicated by adhesions. She has mild asthma treated with an albuterol inhaler three to four times monthly. Both her pregnancies ended in low-transverse cesarean deliveries because of breech presentations. She has been taking ibuprofen four times daily and reports good pain control. Temperature is 37.8 °C (100.0 °F), pulse is 92/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 128/78 mm Hg. On examination, the abdomen is nondistended and nontender. There is no blood over the perineum. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient’s current condition?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Bladder injury</br>b. Cystitis</br>c. Detrusor instability</br>d. Overflow incontinence</br>e. Stress incontinence</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>A. Bladder injury → vesicovaginal fistula formation</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Timing: 2-weeks after laparoscopic-assisted total vaginal hysterectomy (TVH)<br /></br>• Intra-op complication: “dissection of the bladder from the cervix was complicated by adhesions” → red flag for iatrogenic bladder injury<br /></br>• Symptoms: Continuous loss of clear, yellow fluid; soaked two full pads in 24 h; occurs while walking, not just with coughing/sneezing → large-volume, painless, continuous leakage typical of a vesicovaginal fistula (VVF) rather than episodic incontinence<br /></br>• No associated urgency, dysuria, suprapubic pain, abdominal tenderness, or neurologic symptoms<br /></br>Together these cues strongly suggest a bladder leak into the vagina secondary to surgical injury.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)</br>1. Recent pelvic surgery with difficult bladder dissection → high pre-test probability of VVF.<br /></br>2. Continuous leakage of clear fluid → think urinary tract fistula rather than stress/urge.<br /></br>3. Large volume (soaked pads) with no position or activity dependence → bladder emptying directly into vagina.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Post-hysterectomy patients who present with painless, continuous watery vaginal discharge likely have a vesicovaginal fistula from inadvertent bladder injury.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Etiology: Iatrogenic (hysterectomy &gt; pelvic radiation &gt; obstructed labor).<br /></br>• Mechanism: Ischemia or direct surgical laceration creates an epithelial tract between bladder and anterior vaginal wall.<br /></br>• Clinical: 1–2 wks post-op once necrosis sloughs; continuous watery discharge, often alkaline (↑pH, +nitrazine).<br /></br>• Complications: Recurrent UTIs, skin maceration, social distress.</p></br><p>Diagnostics</br>• Simple bedside: Tampon dye test (fill bladder with methylene blue; blue fluid on vaginal gauze = VVF).<br /></br>• Confirm: Cystoscopy + retrograde cystogram or CT urography to map fistula before repair.</p></br><p>Treatment (FYI)</br>• Small, early fistulas may close with prolonged Foley drainage.<br /></br>• Most require surgical repair (e.g., transvaginal layered closure) after tissue inflammation subsides (≈6–12 wks).</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“3 C’s after Cutting the Cervix”<br /></br>Cutting (hysterectomy) → Continuous, Clear urine from the Cavity (vagina) = think Fistula.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>B. Cystitis: Would cause dysuria, frequency, urgency, suprapubic pain ± hematuria, not large continuous leakage.<br /></br>C. Detrusor instability (urge incontinence): Presents with sudden urge followed by small volume leakage; patient describes “can’t make it to the bathroom,” not continuous soaking.<br /></br>D. Overflow incontinence: Constant dribbling + sensation of incomplete emptying, ↑post-void residual, often in diabetics or spinal cord disease—not in a healthy woman post-hysterectomy with large gushes.<br /></br>E. Stress incontinence: Small spurts with ↑intra-abdominal pressure (cough, laugh, sneeze); requires intact bladder neck but weak pelvic floor; timing linked to activity, not continuous flow.</p></br><p>Key Exam Pearl</br>Continuous watery vaginal discharge after pelvic surgery = vesicovaginal fistula until proven otherwise; evaluate with dye test and cystoscopy, then plan surgical repair.</p>
193. An asymptomatic 72-year-old woman comes for a follow-up examination after she was found to have an 80% stenosis of the right internal carotid artery. A new study has shown that endarterectomy for asymptomatic stenosis decreases the risk for stroke or death from 10% to 5% at 5 years (p<0.05). Which of the following is the best way to interpret this research for this patient?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Endarterectomy decreases the absolute risk for stroke or death by 50%</br>b. Endarterectomy decreases the relative risk for stroke or death by 5%</br>c. Endarterectomy prevents 5% of strokes or deaths</br>d. The number of endarterectomies needed to prevent one stroke or death is 20</br>e. The number of endarterectomies needed to prevent one stroke or death is 5</br>f. The number of endarterectomies needed to prevent one stroke or death is 50</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. The number of endarterectomies needed to prevent one stroke or death is 20  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key numbers: risk of stroke/death without surgery = 10% (0.10); with surgery = 5% (0.05).<br /></br>• Question stem: “best way to interpret this research for this patient” → they want a clinically meaningful translation of the statistics (Number Needed to Treat, NNT).<br /></br>• No additional clinical clues are needed; the problem is purely biostatistical.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Calculate Absolute Risk Reduction (ARR):<br /></br>   ARR = Riskcontrol – Risktreatment = 0.10 – 0.05 = 0.05 (5%).<br /></br>2. Convert the ARR into NNT:<br /></br>   NNT = 1 / ARR = 1 / 0.05 = 20.<br /></br>3. Interpret: 20 asymptomatic patients with an 80 % ICA stenosis must undergo carotid endarterectomy to prevent one stroke or death over 5 years.   </p></br><p>Core Take-away: When the exam gives two event rates, ALWAYS think:<br /></br>• ARR (difference) → NNT (1/ARR) for bedside relevance.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (brief because stat question)<br /></br>Carotid atherosclerosis → embolic/low-flow ischemic stroke. Endarterectomy physically removes plaque, lowering embolic risk. The benefit is small but clinically meaningful in carefully selected asymptomatic, high-grade stenosis (&gt;70 %).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• First-line stroke prevention in asymptomatic high-grade (≥70 %) stenosis: carotid endarterectomy + optimal medical therapy (statin, antiplatelet, BP control).<br /></br>• Carotid artery stenting is an alternative in high surgical-risk patients.<br /></br>• Pre-op imaging: Duplex ultrasound (initial), CTA/MRA for anatomic detail.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ARR is the d-ifference, NNT is one over d-ifference.”<br /></br>Visual: Think of 20 identical coins lined up; flip them with surgery—one coin (patient) doesn’t “stroke out.”  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. “Endarterectomy decreases the absolute risk for stroke or death by 50 %”<br /></br>   • 50 % is the RELATIVE risk reduction (0.05 / 0.10 = 0.5). Absolute drop is 5 %, not 50 %.  </p></br><p>b. “Endarterectomy decreases the relative risk for stroke or death by 5 %”<br /></br>   • Relative risk reduction is 50 %, not 5 %. Mix-up between absolute and relative values.  </p></br><p>c. “Endarterectomy prevents 5 % of strokes or deaths”<br /></br>   • Vague wording. ARR is 5 %, but doesn’t express how many patients must be treated. Lacks bedside meaning.  </p></br><p>e. “NNT is 5”<br /></br>   • Would correspond to an ARR of 20 % (0.20). Not supported by the data given.  </p></br><p>f. “NNT is 50”<br /></br>   • Would correspond to an ARR of 2 % (0.02). Also not supported.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Confusing absolute vs. relative differences; forgetting to invert ARR to get NNT.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>An ARR of 5 % translates to an NNT of 20: treat twenty similar patients to prevent one stroke or death within five years.</p>
194. An otherwise healthy 17-year-old girl is brought to the physician by her mother because of a 1-year history of monthly cramps that begin 3 days before the onset of menses and last 5 days. The cramps have increased in severity during the past 4 months, causing her to miss 1 to 2 days of school each month. Menses occur at regular 27- to 30-day intervals and last 4 to 5 days. Menarche was at the age of 14 years. She has never been sexually active. She is 165 cm (5 ft 5 in) tall and weighs 54 kg, or 118lb; BMI is 20 kg/m². Examination shows a nontender abdomen and normal external genitalia. Pelvic examination is limited because of a narrow vaginal introitus. Ultrasonography of the pelvis shows a normal uterus and ovaries. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Reassurance</br>b. CT scan of the pelvis</br>c. Leuprolide therapy</br>d. Oral contraceptive therapy</br>e. Diagnostic laparoscopy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Oral contraceptive therapy</p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Recurrent cyclic pain: “monthly cramps that begin 3 days before the onset of menses and last 5 days.”<br /></br>• Onset in adolescence, soon after menarche (menarche at 14, symptoms age 16).<br /></br>• Completely normal pelvic US and non-tender abdomen → no structural abnormality.<br /></br>• Pelvic exam limited (narrow introitus) but nothing on history suggests dyspareunia, infertility, chronic non-cyclic pain, bowel/bladder symptoms → argues against endometriosis.<br /></br>• Pain severe enough to cause school absenteeism → requires active therapy, not simple reassurance.</p></br><p>Pattern = classic primary dysmenorrhea (prostaglandin-mediated uterine hypercontractility in otherwise normal reproductive tract).</p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify primary dysmenorrhea.<br /></br>2. Management algorithm:<br /></br>   • 1st-line = NSAIDs (ibuprofen, naproxen) begun 1–2 days before menses.<br /></br>   • If inadequate/contraindicated → add or switch to combined estrogen-progestin oral contraceptive pills (OCPs).<br /></br>3. NSAID option is not offered, so next appropriate step is OCPs.</p></br><p>Take-home message: For primary dysmenorrhea that interferes with life, treat medically—NSAIDs first, then hormonal suppression with combined OCPs.</p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Excess endometrial prostaglandin F₂α → uterine muscle contractions &amp; uterine ischemia → crampy lower-abdominal pain.<br /></br>• Risk factors: Adolescence, early menarche, heavy flow, family history.<br /></br>• Pain confined to menses, no pelvic pathology on exam or imaging.</p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>1. NSAIDs (e.g., naproxen 500 mg q12h, ibuprofen 600–800 mg q6h) – inhibit cyclo-oxygenase &amp; ↓ prostaglandins.<br /></br>2. Combined OCPs (ethinyl estradiol + norgestimate/levonorgestrel, etc.) – thin endometrium &amp; suppress ovulation → ↓ prostaglandins, lighter flow, less uterine activity.<br /></br>3. Refractory cases → consider:<br /></br>   • Long-acting reversible contraception (DMPA, LNG-IUD)<br /></br>   • Diagnostic laparoscopy ± treatment for suspected endometriosis.</p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>• “NSAID before the FIRST day, OCP if pain will STAY.”<br /></br>• Primary Dysmenorrhea triad: Adolescent + Cyclic pain + Normal exam/US = “A-C-N” (think “ACe iN”).</p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Reassurance<br /></br>   – Fails to address significant pain &amp; missed school; standard of care is active treatment.<br /></br>b. CT scan of the pelvis<br /></br>   – Imaging already normal on ultrasound; CT adds radiation, cost, and will not change management for primary dysmenorrhea.<br /></br>c. Leuprolide therapy (GnRH agonist)<br /></br>   – Reserved for confirmed moderate–severe endometriosis or uterine fibroids; excessive for uncomplicated primary dysmenorrhea.<br /></br>e. Diagnostic laparoscopy<br /></br>   – Invasive. Indicated if medical therapy fails or if there are red flags for endometriosis/pelvic pathology (infertility, dyspareunia, adnexal mass). Not first-line here.</p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Key Takeaway<br /></br>Primary dysmenorrhea = cyclic menstrual pain in a patient with a normal exam/imaging. Treat sequentially: NSAIDs → combined OCPs → further evaluation if refractory.</p>
195. An 87-year-old man is transferred to the emergency department from a skilled nursing care facility because of progressive confusion during the past 3 weeks. He underwent resection of lung cancer 25 years ago and has a history of deep venous thrombosis. His only medication is warfarin. On arrival, he is not sure where he is or why he is in the emergency department. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows multiple small abrasions and ecchymoses over the body. Pupils are 4 mm and react slowly but symmetrically to light. There is a slight drift to the right upper extremity when the patient holds both upper extremities in front of him. He is unable to perform serial sevens. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Measurement of serum lipoprotein(a) concentration</br>b. Measurement of serum thyroid-stimulating hormone concentration</br>c. Measurement of serum vitamin B₁₂ (cobalamin) concentration</br>d. CT scan of the head</br>e. EEG</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. CT scan of the head  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “87-year-old” + “progressive confusion during the past 3 weeks” → subacute change in mental status.<br /></br>• “Multiple small abrasions and ecchymoses” → suggests recent minor, perhaps unwitnessed, trauma.<br /></br>• “Only medication is warfarin” → potent risk factor for intracranial bleeding.<br /></br>• Focal neurologic finding: “slight drift to the right upper extremity” (subtle hemiparesis) and “pupils react slowly” → localizing deficit, not a purely metabolic encephalopathy.<br /></br>• Timeline of days-to-weeks + age + anticoagulation + minor trauma + focal deficit = classic pattern for a chronic/subacute subdural hematoma.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Is this delirium vs. dementia vs. focal brain lesion? Presence of focal signs (hemiparesis, pupillary sluggishness) immediately raises concern for structural lesion.<br /></br>2. What structural lesion is most likely given age + warfarin + possible minor trauma? → Subdural hematoma.<br /></br>3. First diagnostic test for any suspected intracranial bleed = non-contrast CT head. It is rapid, widely available, and will detect acute and chronic blood collections.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In an elderly or anticoagulated patient with subacute mental status changes and any focal neurologic sign, rule out a subdural hematoma with an immediate non-contrast head CT.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Subdural hematoma = venous blood from torn bridging veins between dura and arachnoid.<br /></br>• Risk factors: advanced age (brain atrophy stretches veins), anticoagulation/antiplatelet therapy, alcoholism, repeated minor head trauma, falls.<br /></br>• Presentation: headache, confusion, personality change, gait instability, focal deficits, ± seizures. Symptoms may appear hours (acute) to weeks (subacute/chronic) after injury.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• Diagnosis: Non-contrast CT → crescent-shaped collection; acute blood = hyperdense, chronic = hypodense.<br /></br>• Management: Reverse anticoagulation (vitamin K, PCC), neurosurgical burr-hole or craniotomy if symptomatic or midline shift.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Old, on OAC (oral anticoagulant), Off-balance, Occult trauma → Order a CT.”<br /></br>Picture a crescent moon (subdural shape) over an elderly man’s head wearing a warfarin pill-cap.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Serum lipoprotein(a) concentration – Used to assess inherited CAD/stroke risk, not acute mental-status changes with focal deficits.<br /></br>b. Serum TSH – Hypothyroidism can cause cognitive slowing but develops insidiously and lacks focal neuro findings; does not explain bruising on warfarin.<br /></br>c. Serum vitamin B12 – Deficiency causes memory loss, peripheral neuropathy, and macrocytic anemia over months to years, not an acute 3-week decline with focal drift.<br /></br>e. EEG – Useful for seizure evaluation or encephalopathy of unclear etiology; a focal deficit mandates imaging first to rule out a mass/bleed before considering seizures.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Attributing confusion in the elderly to “metabolic” causes (thyroid, B12) without first excluding a surgically correctable bleed when focal signs exist.</p>
196. A 52-year-old woman comes to the office for a 6-month health maintenance examination. She has felt generally healthy. Medical history is remarkable for hypertension and mild arthritis. She takes hydrochlorothiazide daily and acetaminophen as needed. She does not smoke cigarettes or drink alcoholic beverages. Vital signs are temperature 36.8°C (98.2°F), pulse 76/min, respirations 18/min, and blood pressure 130/78 mm Hg. Physical examination discloses no abnormalities except for loss of her arch when standing; an arch is present when she stands on her toes. There is also increased eversion of the heels when standing and mild swelling over the lateral aspect of the ankles bilaterally. Which of the following is the most appropriate next diagnostic study?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. CT scan of the feet</br>b. MRI of the feet</br>c. Radionuclide bone scan</br>d. Standing AP x-rays of the feet</br>e. No additional diagnostic studies are indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>e.
