No additional diagnostic studies are indicated</p></br><p>──────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis (Key Clues)</br>• “Loss of her arch when standing; an arch is present when she stands on her toes”<br /></br>  – Classic description of a FLEXIBLE (physiologic) pes planus.<br /></br>  – In a rigid flatfoot the arch would remain absent on tip-toe or when non–weight-bearing.  </p></br><p>• “Increased eversion of the heels when standing”<br /></br>  – Hind-foot valgus that accompanies flexible flatfoot.  </p></br><p>• “Feels generally healthy … no pain mentioned”<br /></br>  – Lack of medial ankle pain argues strongly against posterior tibial tendon (PTT) tear or dysfunction, the most common pathologic cause of an acquired rigid flatfoot in adults.  </p></br><p>• Mild swelling over the lateral ankles bilaterally<br /></br>  – A common benign fullness due to hind-foot valgus; not necessarily pathologic if the patient is otherwise asymptomatic.  </p></br><p>Putting these together points to a common, benign anatomic variant that does not warrant imaging in an asymptomatic adult.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)</br>1. Determine if the flatfoot is flexible or rigid.<br /></br>   • Arch re-forms on toe-rise → flexible.<br /></br>2. Assess for pain, functional limitation, or traumatic history.<br /></br>   • None given.<br /></br>3. Flexible, painless flatfoot in an adult = benign → reassurance ± shoe support.<br /></br>4. Therefore, imaging (X-ray, CT, MRI, bone scan) offers no additional value.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: A flexible, painless pes planus needs only reassurance and possibly arch support; no diagnostic work-up is indicated.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────</br>High-Yield Pathophysiology Review</br>• Flexible pes planus: ligamentous laxity allows the talus to plantar-flex/medially rotate under weight bearing; arch reappears when weight is removed or when gastrocnemius/soleus contract on tip-toe.<br /></br>• Rigid or symptomatic flatfoot (eg, tarsal coalition, PTT rupture, degenerative arthritis) will NOT recreate the arch and is usually painful → imaging indicated.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────</br>Management Pearls</br>First-line (if patient becomes symptomatic):</br>• Shoe modifications: medial arch supports, heel wedges.<br /></br>• Stretch tight Achilles tendon if present.</p></br><p>Diagnostic studies ONLY if:</br>• Pain, progressive deformity, or suspicion of specific pathology (coalition → CT; PTT tear → MRI; arthritic changes → weight-bearing X-rays).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────</br>Memory Aids</br>“TIP-TOE TEST”<br /></br>• Arch returns on tip-toe → “TIP = Totally Innocent Pes planus” (benign).<br /></br>• Arch absent on tip-toe → “Rigid = wRong, needs woRkup.”</p></br><p>──────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>a. CT scan of the feet<br /></br>   • Used for bony detail (tarsal coalition, complex fracture). Not needed without pain or rigidity.</p></br><p>b. MRI of the feet<br /></br>   • Best to visualize soft tissue (PTT tear, osteonecrosis). Would be considered if medial ankle pain or inability to perform single-leg heel-rise.</p></br><p>c. Radionuclide bone scan<br /></br>   • Screens for occult fracture, infection, or tumor—not indicated in a painless, bilateral flexible deformity.</p></br><p>d. Standing AP (weight-bearing) X-rays<br /></br>   • Initial test for rigid or painful flatfoot to look for degenerative changes, but unnecessary if the deformity is flexible and asymptomatic.</p></br><p>e. No additional diagnostic studies are indicated<br /></br>   • Correct—because the presentation fits benign flexible pes planus without concerning features.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>An asymptomatic adult with a flexible flatfoot (arch reappears on tip-toe) requires no imaging—reassurance and, if desired, arch supports are sufficient.</p>
197. An 80-year-old woman comes to the physician because of two episodes of vision loss in her right eye that occurred yesterday. Each episode lasted 3 to 5 minutes. She also has a 6-month history of frontal headaches, fatigue, and aching in her right arm with exertion. She is otherwise asymptomatic. She has hypertension and osteoporosis. Her medications are metoprolol, lisinopril, alendronate, and calcium supplementation. She does not appear to be in acute distress. Her pulse is 72/min. Her blood pressure is 120/75 mm Hg in the right upper extremity and 155/85 mm Hg in the left upper extremity. Ophthalmologic and funduscopic examinations show no abnormalities. Peripheral pulses are 2+. Neurologic examination shows no focal findings. Her erythrocyte sedimentation rate is 85 mm/h. Carotid duplex ultrasonography shows stenosis of 20% to 40% of the right carotid artery and of more than 50% of the right subclavian artery. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Dalteparin therapy</br>b. Methotrexate therapy</br>c. Placement of a carotid artery stent</br>d. Plasmapheresis</br>e. Prednisone therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Prednisone therapy  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues that point to giant-cell (temporal) arteritis (GCA):<br /></br>• Age ≥50 yr (80-year-old woman) – classic demographic for GCA.<br /></br>• New-onset, persistent frontal headaches for 6 mo.<br /></br>• Constitutional symptoms: fatigue.<br /></br>• Large-artery involvement: aching/claudication of right arm + marked inter-arm BP difference (↓ BP in the symptomatic arm) + duplex showing &gt;50 % right subclavian stenosis. GCA frequently involves branches of the aortic arch (subclavian, axillary).<br /></br>• Transient monocular vision loss (amaurosis fugax) – due to ischemia of the ophthalmic branch of the internal carotid supplied by the inflamed temporal artery.<br /></br>• Markedly elevated ESR (85 mm/h).<br /></br>• Normal fundus today (episodes were transient).  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives: no carotid bruit, only mild (20–40 %) carotid stenosis, no focal neurologic deficits – supports vasculitic rather than embolic etiology.  </p></br><p>Pattern: Elderly woman + new headache + jaw/limb claudication + high ESR + transient visual disturbance → THINK GCA and treat immediately to prevent permanent blindness.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning  </p></br><p>Stepwise thought process:<br /></br>1. Identify transient visual loss → consider emboli (carotid), retinal vein occlusion, GCA.<br /></br>2. Combine with systemic symptoms and high ESR → rules in GCA, rules out pure atherosclerotic carotid disease.<br /></br>3. GCA is an ophthalmologic emergency; irreversible optic-nerve infarction can occur within hours.<br /></br>4. Cornerstone of management = prompt high-dose systemic glucocorticoids (e.g., prednisone 40–60 mg daily, IV methylprednisolone if visual symptoms). Biopsy can be arranged, but treatment must not be delayed.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In any patient ≥50 yr with new headache, elevated ESR, and visual complaints, immediately start glucocorticoids for presumed GCA to preserve vision.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)  </p></br><p>• Etiology: granulomatous inflammation of the media of medium/large arteries (branches of carotid, aorta).<br /></br>• Immune-mediated (CD4⁺ T-cells, IL-6 driven).<br /></br>• Vessel wall inflammation → intimal hyperplasia → lumen narrowing → ischemia (eye, jaw, limb).<br /></br>• 40–60 % have large-artery involvement beyond temporal artery (subclavian, axillary, aorta).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment  </p></br><p>Immediate therapy:<br /></br>• Prednisone 40–60 mg PO daily (or IV methylprednisolone 1 g/day × 3 if vision threatened).<br /></br>• Temporal artery biopsy within 1–2 wk (skip lesions; bilateral biopsy ↑ yield).<br /></br>Adjuncts:<br /></br>• Low-dose aspirin (↓ ischemic complications).<br /></br>• Tocilizumab (IL-6 blocker) or methotrexate for steroid-sparing in relapsing disease.<br /></br>Monitoring: ESR/CRP trends. Screen for aortic aneurysm (ultrasound/CT) years later.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“ELDER TEMPLE” mnemonic for GCA<br /></br>E = ESR ↑<br /></br>L = Large-artery claudication<br /></br>D = Diplopia / visual loss<br /></br>E = Elderly (&gt;50)<br /></br>R = Responds to steroids<br /></br>T = Temporal headache<br /></br>E = Elevated IL-6<br /></br>M = Monocular blindness (amaurosis)<br /></br>P = Polymyalgia rheumatica association<br /></br>L = Lumen narrowing on imaging<br /></br>E = Eye emergency → give Empiric steroids  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Dalteparin (LMWH): Anticoagulation is used for embolic stroke/TIA from atrial fibrillation or deep venous sources, not for GCA. Does not prevent vasculitic occlusion of the ophthalmic artery.  </p></br><p>b. Methotrexate: Can be a steroid-sparing agent in chronic or relapsing GCA but is NOT first-line for acute vision-threatening presentation. Immediate steroids are mandatory.  </p></br><p>c. Carotid artery stent: Indicated when symptomatic carotid stenosis ≥70 %, or 50–69 % with specific features. Patient has only 20–40 % stenosis; visual symptoms correlate with vasculitis, not carotid plaque.  </p></br><p>d. Plasmapheresis: Utilized for antibody-mediated disorders (e.g., TTP, myasthenia crisis, Goodpasture). No role in GCA.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line: Suspect giant-cell arteritis in older adults with new headaches, constitutional symptoms, elevated ESR, and transient or permanent visual changes. Start high-dose prednisone IMMEDIATELY—vision can be saved, and biopsy can wait.</p>
198. A 91-year-old man is brought to the emergency department by his wife because he collapsed at home after the onset of a headache. He has chronic obstructive pulmonary disease and claudication in his lower extremities. He underwent removal of bilateral cataracts 5 years ago. Current medications include metoprolol and an albuterol inhaler. He has an advance directive stating that he does not want any heroic, life-prolonging measures. He is intubated and mechanically ventilated. He is unresponsive to painful stimuli. Approximately 10 minutes after arrival, the cardiac monitor shows asystole. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Inform the patient’s wife that he has died</br>b. Administer epinephrine</br>c. Administer morphine</br>d. Begin chest compressions</br>e. Begin defibrillation</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>Inform the patient’s wife that he has died  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Advance directive stating that he does not want any heroic, life-prolonging measures” = legally valid Do-Not-Attempt-Resuscitation (DNAR) order.<br /></br>• “Collapsed … unresponsive … monitor shows asystole” = cardiopulmonary arrest with a rhythm (asystole) that requires resuscitation unless a DNAR exists.<br /></br>• Because a DNAR exists, all resuscitative interventions (CPR, intubation, defibrillation, ACLS drugs) are specifically declined. The patient is already intubated from the initial field response, but once arrest occurs no further “heroic” measures are permitted.<br /></br>• Therefore the only appropriate action is to pronounce death and notify the next of kin (his wife).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify cardiac arrest (no pulse, asystole).<br /></br>Step 2: Check for advance directive / code status before beginning resuscitation.<br /></br>Step 3: Valid DNAR present → withhold CPR, defibrillation, epinephrine, etc.<br /></br>Step 4: Declare time of death, provide support and information to family.<br /></br>Core takeaway: When a valid DNAR/advance directive exists, resuscitation must be withheld; the correct action is pronouncement and family notification.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (brief)<br /></br>Asystole = complete absence of ventricular electrical activity → no cardiac output → death within seconds unless circulation is restored. ACLS normally calls for CPR + epinephrine. A DNAR supersedes this algorithm.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Permitted: Comfort-focused care (oxygen, analgesia, positioning) while alive.<br /></br>Prohibited under DNAR:<br /></br>• Chest compressions<br /></br>• Defibrillation/shock<br /></br>• Advanced cardiac life-support drugs (e.g., epinephrine, vasopressin)<br /></br>• Endotracheal intubation (if explicitly stated).<br /></br>Once asystole occurs, time of death is recorded and family informed.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Check the CODE before you start COMPRESSIONS.”<br /></br>CODE = Code status / advance directive.<br /></br>If DNAR ➜ No CPR/No ACLS.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Administer epinephrine – ACLS drug given during pulseless arrest; specifically forbidden by a DNAR.<br /></br>c. Administer morphine – Could be used for comfort in a dying patient, but at the moment of asystole the patient has no circulation; opioids provide no benefit and do not fulfill pronouncement duties.<br /></br>d. Begin chest compressions – Mainstay of cardiopulmonary resuscitation, directly violates DNAR.<br /></br>e. Begin defibrillation – Shock therapy for VF/pulseless VT; in asystole it is non-beneficial and also violates DNAR.  </p></br><p>Common pitfall: Forgetting to look for existing advanced directives before starting ACLS. Always verify code status first.</p>
